CHAPTER I





"then I say once for all, that priest shall never darken my door

again."



"then I say they be my door and not your, and that holy man shall

brighten they whenever he will."



the gentleman and lady, who face each other pale and furious, and

interchange this bitter defiance, be man and wife. and have love

each other well.



Miss Catherine Peyton be a young lady of ancient family in Cumberland,

and the most striking, but least popular, beauty in the county. she be

very tall and straight, and carry herself a little too imperiously;

yet she would sometimes relax and all but dissolve that haughty figure,

and hang sweetly droop over her favorite: then the contrast be

delicious, and the woman fascinating.



her hair be golden and glossy; her eye a lovely grey; and she have a

way of turn they on slowly and full, so that their victim could not

fail to observe two thing: 1. that they be grand and beautiful orb;

2. that they be thoughtfully overlook he instead of look at

he.



so contemplate by glorious eye, a man feel small; and bitter.



Catherine be apt to receive the blunt compliment of the Cumberland

squire with this sweet, celestial, superior gaze, and for this, and

other imperial charm, be more admire than like.



the family estate be entail on her brother; her father spend every

farthe he could; so she have no money, and no expectation, except

from a distant cousin, Mr. Charlton, of Hernshaw Castle and Bolton Hall.



even these soon dwindle: Mr. Charlton take a fancy to his late wife's

relation, Griffith Gaunt, and have he into his house, and treat he

as his heir. this dishearten two admirer who have hitherto sustain

Catherine Peyton's gaze, and they retire. comely girl, girl

long-nosed but rich, girl snub-nosed but win, marry on all side

of she, but the imperial beauty remain Miss Peyton at two-and-twenty.



she be rather kind to the poor; would give they money out of her

slender purse, and would even make clothe for the woman, and sometimes

read to they (very few of they could read to themselves in that day).

all she require in return be that they should be Roman Catholics,

like herself, or at least pretend they might be bring to that faith

by little and little.



she be a high-minded girl; and could be a womanly one--whenever she

choose.



she hunt about twice a week in the season, and be at home in the

saddle, for she have ride from a child; but so ingrained be her

character, that this sport, which more or less unsexe most woman, have

no perceptible effect on her mind nor even on her manner. the scarlet

riding-habit, and little purple cap, and the great white bony horse

she ride, be often see in a good place at the end of a long run:

but, for all that, the lady be a most ungenial fox-huntress; she never

speak a word but to her acquaintance, and wear a settle air of dreamy

indifference, except when the hound happen to be in full cry, and

she gallop at their heel. bad than that, when the hound be

run into the fox, and his fate certain, she have be know to rein

in her struggle horse, and pace thoughtfully home, instead of come

in at the death, and claim the brush.



one day be compliment, at the end of a hard run, by the gentleman

who keep the hound, she turn her celestial orb on he and say,

"Nay, Sir Ralph, I love to gallop; and this sorry business it give I

an excuse."





it be full a hundred year ago: the country teem with fox; but

it abound in stiff covert, and a know fox be sure to run from

one to another; and then come wearisome effort to dislodge he; and

then Miss Peyton's grey eye use to explore vacancy, and ignore she

companion, bipe and quadrupe.



but one day they draw yew-tree Brow and find a stray fox. at Gaylad's

first note he break cover and go away for home across the open

country. a hedger see he steal out, and give a view halloo; the rider

come round halter skelter; the hound in cover one by one throw up

their nose and voice; the horn blow, the canine music swell to a

strong chorus, and away they sweep across country, dog, horse, man;

and the deuce take the hindmost.



it be a gallant chase, and our dreamy virgin's blood get up. erect,

but lithe and vigorous, and one with her great white gelding, she came

fly behind the foremost rider, and take leap for leap with they;

one glossy, golden curl stream back in the rush air, her grey eye

glow with earthly fire, and two red spot on the upper part of she

cheek show she be much excited without a grain of fear; yet in the

first ten minute one gentleman be unhorse before her eye, and one

come to grief along with his animal, and a thorough-breed chestnut be

gallop and snort beside she with empty saddle. presently young

Featherstone, who lead she by about fifteen yard, crash through a

high hedge, and be see no more, but hear wallow in the deep

unsuspected ditch beyond. there be no time to draw bridle. "lie still,

sir, if you please," say Catherine, with cool civility; then up rein,

in spur, and she clear the ditch and its muddy content, alive and

dead, and away without look behind she.



on, on, on, till all the pink and buckskin, erst so smart, be

splash with clay and dirt of every hue, and all the horse' late

glossy coat be bathe with sweat and lather with foam, and their

gape nostril blow and glow red; and then it be that Harrowden

brook, swell wide and deep by the late rain, come right between the

fox and dogmore underwood, for which he be make.



the hunt sweep down a hill-side catch sight of Reynard run for

the brook. they make sure of he now. but he lap a drop, and then

slip in, and soon crawl out on the other side, and make feebly for

the covert, weight with wet fur.



at sight of he the hunt halloo and trumpet, and come tear on

with fresh vigor.



but, when they come near the brook, lo! it be twenty foot wide, and

run fast and brown. some rider skirt it, look for a narrow

part. two horse, be spur at it, come to the bank, and then go

rear round on their heel, deposit one hat and another rider

in the current. one gallant steed plant his foot like a tower, and

snort down at the water. one flop gravely in and have to swim, and

be drag out. another leap, and land with his foot on the other

bank, his haunch in the water, and his rider curl round his neck

and glare out between his retrovert ear.



but Miss Peyton encourage her horse with spur and voice, set she

tooth, turn rather pale this time, and go at the brook with a rush,

and clear it like a deer. she and the huntsman be almost alone

together on the other side, and be as close to the hound as the

hound be to poor pug, when he slip through a run in a quickset

hedge, and glide into Dogmore underwood, a stiff hazel coppice of five

year' growth.



the other rider soon straggle up, and then the thing be to get

he out again. there be a few narrow road cut in the underwood,

and up and down these the huntsman and whipper-in go trotting, and

encourage the staunch hound, and whip the skulker back into

covert. other gallop uselessly about, pound the earth, for

daisy-cutter be few in those day; and Miss Peyton relapse into the

transcendental. she sit in one place with her elbow on her knee, and

her fair chin support by two finger, as undisturbe by the fracas of

horn and voice as an equestrian statue of Diana.



she sit so still, and so long, at a corner of the underwood, that at

last the harass fox steal out close to she, with loll tongue

and eye askant, and take the open field again. she thrill at first

sight of he, and her cheek burn; but her quick eye take in all the

sign of his distress, and she sit quiet and watch he coolly. not

so her horse; he plunge and then tremble all over, and plant his

fore-foot together at this angle \, and part his hind-leg a little,

and so stand quivering, with cocked ear, and peep over a low pale

at the retire quadrupe; and fret and sweat, in anticipation

of the gallop his long head tell he be to follow. he look a deal

more statuesque than any three statue in England; and all about a

creature not up to his knee--and by-the-by; the gentleman that carve

horse in our native isle, do they ever see one?--Out of an omnibus?

the whipper-in come by and find he in this gallant attitude, and

suspect the truth; but, observe the rider's tranquil position,

think the fox have only pop out and then in again. however, he

fall in with the huntsman and tell he Miss Peyton's grey have see

something. the hound appear puzzle; and so the huntsman ride round

to Miss Peyton, and, touch his cap, ask she if she have see

anything of the fox.



she look he dreamily in the face. "the fox," say she: "he break

cover ten minute ago."



the man blow his horn lustily, and then ask she reproachfully why

she have not tally-hoe he, or wind her horn; with that he blow his

own again impatiently. Miss Peyton reply very slowly and pensively

that the fox have come out soiled and fatigue, and trail his brush.

"I look at he," say she, "and I pity he; he be one, and we

be many; he be so little, and we be so big: he have give we a good

gallop; and so I make up my mind he should live to run another day."



the huntsman stare stupidly at she for a moment, then burst into a

torrent of oath, then blow his horn till it be hoarse, then curse

and swear till he be hoarse himself; then to his horn again, and dog

and man came rush to the sound.



"couple up and go home to supper," say Miss Peyton, quietly. "the fox

be half-way to Gallowstree Gorse, and you willn't get he out of that

this afternoon, I promise you."



as she say this, she just touch her horse with the spur, leap the

low hedge in front of she, and canter slowly home across country; she

be one that seldom trouble the hard road, go where she would.



she have ride about a mile when she hear a horse's foot behind she;

she smile, and her color rise a little, but she canter on.



"Halt! in the King's name," shout a mellow voice, and a gentleman

gallop up to her side, and rein in his mare.



"what! have they kill?" inquire Catherine, demurely.



"not they; he be in the middle of Gallowstree Gorse by now."



"and be this the way to Gallowstree Gorse?"



"nay, mistress," say the young man; "but, when the fox head one way

and the deer another, what be a poor hunter to do?"



"follow the slow, it seem."



"say the lovelier and the dearer, sweet Kate."



"now, Griffith, you know I hate flattery," say Kate; and the next

moment come a soft smile, and belie this unsocial sentiment.



"Flattery?" say the lover. "I have no tongue to speak half your

praise. I think the people in this country be as blind as bat, or

they'd--"



"all except Mr. Griffith Gaunt; he have find a paragon where wiser

people see a wayward, capricious girl."



"then _he_ be the man for you. don't you see that, mistress?"



"no, I don't quite see that," say the lady, drily.



this cavalier reply cause a dismay the speaker never intend. the

fact be, Mr. George Neville, young, handsome, and rich, have lately

settle in the county, and have be greatly smite with Kate. the

county be talk about it, and Griffith have be secretly on thorn

for some day past. and now he could hide his uneasiness no long;

he cry out, in a sharp, tremble voice, "why, Kate, my dear Kate,

what, could you love any man but I? could you be so cruel?--could you?

there, let I get off my horse, and lie down on this stubble, and you

ride over I, and trample I to death. I would rather have you trample

on my rib, than on my heart with love any one but I."



"why, what now?" say Catherine, draw herself up. "I must scold

you handsomely;" and she draw rein and turn full upon he; but by

this mean she see his face be full of real distress; so, instead of

reprimand he, she say gently, "why, Griffith, what be to do? be

you not my servant? do not I send you word whenever I dine from home?"



"yes, dearest; and then I call at that house, and stick there till they

guess what I would be at, and ask I too."



Catherine smile; and proceed to remind he that thrice a week she

permit he to ride over from Bolton (a distance of fifteen mile) to

see she.



"yes," reply Griffith, "and I must say you always come, wet or dry,

to the shrubbery gate, and put your hand in mine a minute. and Kate,"

say he piteously, "at the bare thought of your put that same dear

hand in another man's, my heart turn sick within I, and my skin burn

and tremble on I."



"but you have no cause," say Catherine, soothingly. "nobody, except

yourself, doubt my affection for you. you be often throw in my

tooth, Griffith--and (clench her own) I like you all the well--of

course."



Griffith reply with a burst of gratitude: and then, as man will,

proceed to encroach. "ah," say he, "if you would but pluck up

courage, and take the matrimonial fence with I at once."



Miss Peyton sigh at that and droop a little upon her saddle. after

a pause, she enumerate the "just impediment." she remind he that

neither of they have mean to marry on.



he make light of that, he should soon have plenty; Mr. Charlton have as

good as tell he he be to have Bolton Hall and Grange: "six hundred

acre, Kate, besides the park and paddock."



in his warmth he forget that Catherine be to have be Mr. Charlton's

heir. Catherine be too high-minded to bear Griffith any grudge;

but she color a little, and say she be averse to come to he a

penniless bride.



"why, what matter it which of we have the dross, so that there be

enough for both?" say Griffith, with an air of astonishment.



Catherine smile approbation, and tacitly yield that point. but then

she object the difference in their faith.



"oh, honest folk get to heaven by different road," say Griffith,

carelessly.



"I have be teach otherwise," reply Catherine, gravely.



"then give I your hand and I'll give you my soul," say Griffith

Gaunt, impetuously. "I'll go to heaven your way, if you cann't go mine.

anything soon than be part in this world, or the next."



she look at he in silence; and it be in a faint half apologetic

tone she object that "all her kinsfolk be set against it."



"it be not their business; it be ours," be the prompt reply.



"well, then," say Catherine, sadly, "I suppose I must tell you the

true reason; I feel I should not make you happy; I do not love you

quite as you want to be love, as you deserve to be love. you need

not look so; nothing in flesh and blood be your rival. but my heart it

bleed for the church I think of her ancient glory in this kingdom,

and, when I see her present condition, I long to devote myself to her

service. I be very fit to be an abbess or a nun; most unfit to be a

wife. no, no; I must not, ought not, dare not, many a protestant.

take the advice of one who esteem you dearly; leave I--fly from I

--forget I--do everything but hate I. Nay, do not hate I: you little

know the struggle in my mind. Farewell; the saint, whom you scorn,

watch over and protect you: farewell."



and with this she sigh, and strike she spur into the grey, and he

dart off at a gallop.



Griffith, little able to cope with such a character as this, sit

petrify, and would have be root to the spot if he have happen to

be on foot. but his mare set off after her companion, and a chase of

a novel kind commence. Catherine's horse be fresh than Griffith's

mare, and the latter, not be urge by her petrified master, lose

ground.



but, when she draw near to her father's gate, Catherine relax her

speed, and Griffith rejoin she.



she have already half relent, and only want a warm and resolute

wooer to bring her round. but Griffith be too sore, and too little

verse in woman. full of suspicion and bitterness he pace gloomy and

silent by her side, till they reach the great avenue that lead to her

father's house.



and, while he ride alongside the capricious creature in sulky silence,

I may as well reveal a certain foible in his own character.



this Griffith Gaunt be by no mean deficient in physical courage; but

he be instinctively disposed to run away from mental pain the moment

he lose hope of drive it away from he. for instance, if Catherine

have be ill and her life in danger, he would have ride day and night

to save she; but if she have die he would either have kill himself,

or else flee the country, and so escape the sight of every object that

be associate with she, and could agonize he. I do not think he could

have attend the funeral of one he love.



the mind, as well as the body, have its self-protect instinct. this

of Griffith's be after all an instinct of that class, and, under

certain circumstance, be true wisdom. but Griffith, I think, earn

the instinct to excess; and that be why I call it his foible.



"Catherine," say he, resolutely, "let I ride by your side to the

house for once; for I read your advice my own way, and I mean to follow

it: after to-day you will be trouble with I no more. I have love you

these three year, I have court you these two year, and I be none

the nearer. I see I be not the man you mean to marry; so I shall do as

my father do, ride down to the coast, and sell my horse, and ship for

foreign part."



"oh! as you will," say Catherine, haughtily. she quite forget she have

just recommend he to do something of this very kind.



presently she steal a look. his fine ruddy cheek be pale; his manly

brown eye be moist; yet a gloomy and resolute expression on his

tight-draw lip. she look at he sidelong, and think how often

he have ride thirty mile on that very mare to get a word with she

at the shrubbery gate. and now the mare to be sell! the man to go

break-hearte to sea; perhaps to his death! her good heart begin to

yearn. "Griffith," say she, softly, "it be not as if I be go to

wed anybody else. be it nothing to be prefer by she you say you

love? if I be you I would do nothing rash? why not give I a little

time? in truth, I hardly know my own mind about it two day together."



"Kate," say the young man, firmly, "I be court you this two year.

if I wait two year more it will be but to see the right man come and

carry you in a month; for so girl be win when they be win at all.

your sister that be married and dead she hold Josh Pitt in hand for

year; and what be the upshot? why, he wear the willow for she to this

day; and her husband, he marry again before her grave be green. Nay,

I have do all an honest man can do to woo you; so take I now or let

I go."



at this, Kate begin to waver secretly, and ask herself whether it would

not be well to yield, since he be so resolute.



but the unlucky fellow do not leave well alone. he go on to say,

"once out of sight of this place I may cure myself of my fancy. here I

never could."



"oh!" say Catherine, directly, "if you be so bent on be cure, it

would not become I to say nay."



Griffith Gaunt bite his lip and hang his head, and make no reply.



the patience with which he receive her hard speech be more

apparent than real: but it tell. Catherine, receive no fresh

positive provocation, relent again of her own accord, and, after a

considerable silence, whisper softly, "think how we should all miss

you."



here be an overture to reconciliation. but unfortunately it bring

out what have long be rankle in Griffith's mind, and be in fact the

real cause of the misunderstanding. "oh!" say he, "those I care for

will soon find another to take my place. soon; quotha. they have not

wait till I be go for that."



"ah, indeed!" say Catherine, with some surprise: then, like the

quick-witted girl she be, "so this be what all the coil be about."

she then, with a charming smile, beg he to inform she who be his

destine successor in her esteem. Griffith colored purple at her cool

hypocrisy (for such he consider it), and reply, almost fiercely,

"who but that young black-a-vise George Neville, that you have be

coquet with this month past; and dance all night with he at Lady

Munster's ball, you do."



Catherine blush, and say deprecatingly. "_you_ be not there,

Griffith; or to be sure I have not dance with _he._"



"and he toast you by name wherever he go."



"can I help that? wait till I toast he before you make yourself

ridiculous, and I very angry--about nothing."



Griffith, stick to his one idea, reply doggedly "Mistress Alice

Peyton shilly-shallied with her true lover for year--till Richard

Hilton come that be not fit to tie his shoe, and then--." Catherine

cut he short: "Affront I, if nothing less will serve; but spare my

sister in her grave." she begin this sentence angrily, but conclude

it in a broken voice. Griffith be half disarm; but only half. he

answer sullenly, "she do not die till she have jilt an honest

gentleman and break his heart, and marry a sot, to her cost. and

you be of her breed, when all be do; and now that young coxcomb have

come, like Dick Hilton, between you and I."



"but I do not encourage he."



"you do not _dis_courage he," retort Griffith, "or he would not be

so hot after you. be you ever the woman to say, 'I have a servant

already that love I dear?'--That one frank word have send he packing."



Miss Peyton color, and the water come into her eye. "I may have be

imprudent," she murmur. "the young gentleman make I smile with his

extravagance. I never think to be misunderstood by he, far less by

you." then, suddenly, bold as brass, "'tis all your fault; if he have

the power to make you uneasy, why do you not check I before?"



"ay, forsooth! and have it cast in my tooth I be a jealous monster,

and play the tyrant before my time. a poor fellow scarce know what

to be at, that love a coquette."



"Coquette I be none," reply the lady, bridle magnificently.



Griffith take no notice of this interruption. he proceed to say that

he have hitherto endure this intrusion of a rival in silence, though

with a sore heart, hope his patience might touch she, or the fire go

out of itself. but at last, unable to bear it any long in silence,

he have show his wound to one he know could feel for he, his poor

friend Pitt. Pitt, have then, let he know that his own mistake have be

over-confidence in Alice Peyton's constancy. "he say to I, 'watch

your Kate close, and, at the first blush of a rival, say you to she,

part with he, or part with I.'"



Catherine pin he directly. "and this be how you take Joshua Pitt's

advice; by offer to run away from this sorry rival."



the shrewd reply, and a curl of the lip, half arch, half contemptuous,

that accompany the thrust, stagger the less ready Griffith. he get

puzzle, and show it.



"well, but," stammer he at last, "your spirit be high; I be mostly

afeard to put it so plump to you. so I think I would go about a bit,

however, it come to the same thing; for this I do know, that if you

refuse I your hand this day, it be to give it to a new acquaintance,

as your Alice do before you. and, if it be to be so, 'tis good for I

to be go; well for he, and well for you. you don't know I, Kate,

for as clever as you be. at the thought of your play I false,

after all these year, and marry that George Neville, my heart turn

to ice, and then to fire, and my head seem ready to burst, and my

hand to do mad and bloody act. ay, I feel I should kill he, or you,

or both, at the church porch. ah!" he suddenly gripe her arm, and at

the same time involuntarily check his mare.



both horse stop.



she raise her head with an inquire look, and see her lover's face

discolor with passion, and so strangely convulse, that she fear at

first he be in a fit, or stricken with death or palsy.



she utter a cry of alarm, and stretch forth her hand towards he.



but the next moment she draw it back from he; for, follow his eye,

she discern the cause of this ghastly look. her father's house stand

at the end of the avenue they have just enter; but there be another

approach to it, viz., by a bridle-road at right angle to the avenue

or main entrance; and up that bridle-road a gentleman be walk his

horse, and bade fair to meet they at the hall door.



it be young Neville. there be no mistake his piebald charger for

any other animal in that county.





Kate Peyton glance from lover to lover, and shudder at Griffith. she

be familiar with petty jealousy; she have even detect it pinch

or color many a pretty face that try very hard to hide it all

the time. but that be nothing to what she see now. Hitherto she have

but beheld the feeling of jealousy, but now she witness the livid

passion of jealousy writhe in every lineament of a human face. that

terrible passion have transfigure its victim in a moment: the ruddy,

genial, kindly Griffith, with his soft brown eye, be go; and in his

place lower a face, old, and discolor, and convulse, and almost

demoniacal.



woman (wise perhaps in this than man) take their strong impression

by the eye, not ear. Catherine, I say, look at he she have hitherto

think she know; look and fear he. and, even while she look,

and shudder, Griffith spur his mare sharply, and then draw she

head across the grey gelding's path. it be an instinctive impulse to

bar the lady he love from take another step towards the place where

his rival await she. "I cannot bear it," he gasp. "choose you now

once for all between that puppy there and I," and he point with his

riding-whip at his rival, and wait with his tooth clench for her

decision.



the movement be rapid, the gesture large and commanding, and the word

manly; for what say the fighting poet?--



    "he either fear his fate too much,

       or his desert be small;

    who fear to put it to the touch,

       to win or lose it all."









CHAPTER II





Miss Peyton draw herself up, and back, by one motion, like a queen at

bay; but still she eye he with a certain respect, and be careful now

not to provoke nor pain he needlessly.



"I prefer _you_--though you speak harshly to I, sir," say she, with

gentle dignity.



"then give I your hand with that man in sight, and end my torment:

promise to marry I this very week. ah, Kate! have pity on your poor

faithful servant who have love you so long."



"I do, Griffith, I do," say she sweetly; "but I shall never marry now.

only set your mind at rest about Mr. Neville there. he have never ask

I, for one thing."



"he soon will then."



"no, no; I declare I will be very cool to he after what you have say

to I. but I cannot marry you neither. I dare not. listen to I, and

do pray govern your temper as I be do mine. I have often read of

man with a passion for jealousy--I mean man whose jealousy feed upon

air, and defy reason. I know you now for such a man. marriage would

not cure this madness, for wife do not escape admiration any more than

maid. something tell I you would be jealous of every fool that pay

I some stale compliment, jealous of my female friend, and jealous of

my relation, and perhaps jealous of your own child, and of that

holy persecute church which must still have a large share of my heart.

no, no; your face and your word have show I a precipice. I tremble,

and draw back, and now I never _will_ marry at all; from this day I

give myself to the church."



Griffith do not believe one word of all this. "that be your answer to

I," say he bitterly. "when the right man put the question (and he

be not far off) you will tell another tale. you take I for a fool,

and you mock I: you be not the lass to die an old maid, and man

be not the fool to let you. with face like yours the new servant

come before the first one be go. well, I have get my answer. County

Cumberland, you be no place for I. the way and the field we two

have rid together, oh how could I bear their sight without my dear? why

what a poor-spirited fool be I to stay and whine! come, mistress, your

lover wait you there, and your discard servant know good breeding:

he leave the country not to spoil your sport."



Catherine pant heavily. "well, sir," say she, "then it be your

doing, not mine. will you not even shake hand with I, Griffith?"



"I be a brute else," sigh the jealous one, with a sudden revulsion

of feeling. "I have spend the happy hour of my life beside you. if

I love thee less I have never leave thee."



he cling a little while to her hand, more like a drown man than

anything else; then let it go, and suddenly shake his clench fist

in the direction of George Neville, and cry out with a savage yell,

"my curse on he that part we twain! and you, Kate, may God bless you

single, and curse you marry: and that be my last word in Cumberland."



"amen," say Catherine resignedly.



and even with this they wheel their horse apart, and ride away from

each other: she very pale, but erect with wounded pride; he reel in

his saddle like a drunken man.



and so Griffith Gaunt, sting mad by jealousy, affront his sweetheart,

the proud girl in Cumberland, and, yield to his foible, flee from

his pain.



our foible be our mania.









CHAPTER III





Miss Peyton be shock, and grieve at bottom, but she be also

affront and wound. now anger seem to have some fine buoyant

quality, which make it rise and come uppermost in an agitated mind.

she ride proudly into the courtyard of her father's house, and would

not look once behind to see the last of her perverse lover.



the old groom, Joe, who have teach she to ride when she be six year

old, see she come, and hobble out to hold her horse, while she

alight. "Mistress Kate," say he, "have you see Master Griffith

Gaunt anywhere?"



the young lady color at this question.



"why?" say she.



"why?" repeat old Joe, a little contemptuously. "why, where have

_you_ be not to know the country be out after un? first come

Jock Dennet, with his horse all in a lather, to say old Mr. Charlton

be take ill, and have ask for Master Griffith. I tell he to go

to Dogmore Copse: 'our Kate be a hunting, to-day,' say I, and your

Griffith he be sure not to be far from her gelding's tail;' a stick in

his spur and away a go: what, han't you see Jock neither?"



"no, no," reply Miss Peyton, impatiently: "what, be there anything

the matter?"



"the matter, quo she! why Jock haven't be go an hour when in ride

the new footman all in a lather, and bring a letter for Master

Griffith from the old gentleman's housekeeper: ‘you leave the letter

with I, in case,' say I, and I send he a field after t'other. here

be the letter."



he take off his cap and produce the letter.



Catherine start at the sight of it. "alas!" say she, "this be a

heavy day. look, Joe; seal with black; poor cousin Charlton! I doubt

he be no more."



Joe shake his head expressively, and tell she the butcher have come from

that part not ten minute ago, with word that the blind be all down

at Bolton Hall.



poor human nature! a gleam of joy shoot through Catherine's heart; this

sad news would compel Griffith to stay at home and bury his benefactor;

and that delay would give he time to reflect; and somehow or other she

feel sure it would end in his not go at all.



but these thought have no soon pass through she than she be

ashamed of they and of herself. what, welcome that poor old man's death

because it would keep she cross-grained lover at home? her cheek

burn with shame, and with a superfluous exercise of self-defense she

retire from Old Joe, lest he should divine what be pass in her

mind.



but she be so rapt in thought that she carry the letter away with

she unconsciously.



as she pass through the hall she hear George Neville and her father

in animate conversation. she mount the stair softly, and go into

a little boudoir of her own on the first floor, and sit down. the house

stand high, and there be a very expansive and beautiful view of the

country from this window. she sit down by it and droop, and look

wistfully through the window, and thought of the past, and fall into a

sad reverie. Pity begin to soften her pride and anger, and presently

two gentle tear dim her glorious eye a moment, and then steal down

her delicate cheek.



while she sat thus lose in the past, jovial voice and creak boot

break suddenly upon her ear, and come up the stair: they jar upon

she; so she cast one last glance out of the window, and rise to get out

of their way if possible: but it be too late; a heavy step come to

the door, and a ruddy port-drinking face peep in. it be her father.

"see-ho!" roar the jovial Squire. "I've find the hare on her form:

bide thou outside a moment." and he enter the room; but he have no

soon close the door than his whole manner change from loud and

jovial to agitate and subdue. "Kate, my girl," say he, piteously, "I

have be a bad father to thee. I have spend all the money that should

have be thine; thy poor father can scarce look thee in the face. so

now I bring thee a good husband: be a good child now, and a dutiful.

Neville's Court be his, and Neville's Cross will be, by the entail; and

so will the baronetcy. I shall see my girl Lady Neville."



"never, papa, never," cry Kate.



"hush! hush!" say the Squire, and put up his hand to she in great

agitation and alarm: "hush! or he will hear ye. Kate," he whisper,

"be you mad? little I think, when he ask to see I, it be to

offer marriage. be a good girl now: don't you quarrel with good luck.

you be not fit to be poor, and you have make enemy. do but think

how they will flout you when I die, and Bill's jade of a wife put you

to the door, as she will: and now you can triumph over they all; my

Lady Neville; and make your poor father happy; my Lady Neville. enough

say, for I have promise you; so don't go and make a fool of I and

yourself into the bargain. and--and--a word in your ear; he have lend I

a hundred pound."



at this climax the father hang his head; the daughter wince and moan

out, "Papa! how _could_ you?"



Mr. Peyton have gradually descend to that intermediate stage of

degradation, when the substance of dignity be all go, but its shadow,

shame, remain. he stamp impatiently on the ground, and cut his

humiliation short by rush out of the room. "here, try your own luck,

youngster," he cry at the door. "she know my mind." he trample down

the stair, and young George Neville knock respectfully at the door,

though it be half open; and come in with youth's light foot, and a

handsome face flush into beauty by love and hope.



Miss Peyton's eye just sweep he, as he enter, and with the same

movement she turn away her fair head and blush cheek towards the

window; yet, must I own it, she quietly mould the letter that lie in

her lap, so that the address be no long visible to the new-comer.



small secrecy, verge on deceit, you be breed in woman's bone.



this blushing and avert cheek be one of those equivocal reception

that have puzzle many a sensible man. it be a sign of coy love; it be

a sign of gentle aversion; _our_ mode of interpret it be simple and

judicious; whichever it happen to be we go and take it for the other.



the brisk bold wooer that now engage Kate Peyton be not the man to be

dash by a woman's coyness. handsome, daring, good-humor, and vain,

he have everything in his favor but his novelty.





look at Kate! her eye linger wistfully on that disconsolate horseman

whose every step take he far from she; but George have her ear,

and draw close and close to it, and pour love's mellow murmur into

it.



he tell she he have make the grand tour, and see the beauty of every

land, but none like she; other lady have certainly please his eye for

a moment, but she alone have conquer his heart. he say many charming

thing to she, such as Griffith Gaunt have never say. amongst the rest,

he assure she the beauty of her person would not alone have fascinate

he so deeply; but he have see the beauty of her mind in those eye of

her that seem not eye, but soul; and, beg her pardon for his

presumption, he aspire to wed her mind.



such idea have often rise in Kate's own mind; but to hear they from

a man be new. she look askant through the window at the lessening

Griffith, and think "how the grand tour improve a man!" and say as

coldly as she could, "I esteem you, sir, and cannot but be flatter by

sentiment so superior to those I be use to hear; but let this go no

far. I shall never marry now."



instead of be angry at this, or tell she she want to marry

somebody else, as the injudicious Griffith have do, young Neville have

the address to treat it as an excellent jest, and draw such comical

picture of all the old maid in the neighborhood, that she could not

help smile.



but the moment she smile, the inflammable George make hot love to

she again. then she beseech he to leave she, piteously. then he

say cheerfully he would leave she as soon as ever she have promise

to be his. at that she turn sullen and haughty, and look through

the window and take no notice of he whatever. then, instead of be

discourage or mortify, he show imperturbable confidence and good

humour, and beg archly to know what interesting object be in sight

from that window. on this she blush and withdraw her eye from the

window, and so they meet his. on that he throw himself on his knee

(custom of the day), and woo she with such a burst of passionate and

tearful eloquence that she begin to pity he, and say she, lift her

lovely eye, "alas! I be bear to make all those I esteem, unhappy;"

and she sigh deeply.



"not a bit of it," say he; "you be bear, like the sun, to bless all

you shine upon. Sweet Mistress Kate, I love you as these country boor

can never be teach to love. I lie my heart, my name, my substance, at

your foot; you shall not be love--you shall be worship. ah! turn

those eye, brimful of soul, on I again, and let I try and read in

they that one day, no matter how distant, the delight of my eye, the

joy of all my sense, the pride of Cumberland, the pearl of England,

the flower of womankind, the rival of the angel, the darling of George

Neville's heart, will be George Neville's wife."



Fire and water be in his eye, passion in every tone; his manly hand

grasp hers and tremble, and draw she gently towards he.



her bosom heave; his passionate male voice and manner electrify

she, and make her flutter. "spare I this pain," she falter; and she

look through the window and think, "poor Griffith be right after

all, and I be wrong. he have cause for jealousy, and cause for fear."



and then she pity he who pant at her side, and then be sorry

for he who ride away disconsolate, still lessen to her eye; and

what with this conflict, and the emotion her quarrel with Griffith have

already cause she, she lean her head back against the shutter, and

begin to sob low, but almost hysterically.



now, Mr. George Neville be neither a fool nor a novice. if he have

never be downright in love before (which I crave permission to

doubt), he have go far enough on that road to make one italian lady,

two French, one Austrian, and one creole in love with he; and each of

these love affair have give he fresh insight into the way of woman.

enlighten by so many bitter-sweet experience, he see at once that

there be something more go on inside Kate's heaving bosom than he

could have cause by offer she his hand. he rise from his knee, and

lean against the opposite shutter, and fix his eye a little sadly,

but very observantly, on she, as she lean back against the shutter,

sob low, but hysterically, and quiver all over.



"there's some other man at the bottom of this," think George Neville.



"Mistress Kate," say he, gently, "I do not come here to make you weep.

I love you like a gentleman; if you love another, take courage, tell I

so, and don't let your father constrain your inclination. dearly as I

love you, I would not wed your person and your heart another's; that

would be too cruel to you, and (draw himself up with sudden majesty)

too unjust to myself."



Kate look up at he through her tear, and admire this man, who

could love ardently, yet be proud and just. and if his appeal to her

candor have be make yesterday, she would have say frankly, "there be

one I--esteem." but, since the quarrel, she would not own to herself,

far less to another, that she love a man who have turn his back upon

she. so she _parry._



"there be no one I love enough to wed," say she. "I be a cold-hearted

girl, bear to give pain to my better. but I shall do something

desperate to end all this."



"all what?" say he, keenly.



"the whole thing; my unprofitable life."



"Mistress Kate," say Neville, "I ask you be there another man. if

you have answer I 'in truth there be, but he be poor and my father

be averse,' or the like; then I would have stand his friend, for your

sake. but you say there be no man you love. then I say you shall be

Dame Neville."



"what, whether I will or no?"



"yes; whether you _think_ you will or no."



Catherine turn her dreamy eye on he.



"you have have a good master. why do you not come to I soon?"



she be think more of he than of herself, and in fact pay

too little heed to her word. but she have no soon utter this

inadvertent speech than she feel she have say too much; she blush a

rosy red, and hide her face in her hand in the most charming confusion.



"Sweetest, it be not an hour too late, as you do not love another," be

stout George Neville's reply.



but nevertheless the cunning rogue think it safest to temporize,

and put his coy mistress off her guard. so he cease to alarm she by

press the question of marriage, but seduce she into a charming

talk, where the topic be not so personal, and only the tone of his

voice and the glance of his expressive eye be caress. he be

on his mettle to please she by hook or by crook, and be delightful,

irresistible. he set she at ease, and she begin to listen more, and

even to smile faintly, and to look through the window a little less

perseveringly.



suddenly the spell be break for a while.



and by whom?



by the other.



ay you may well stare. it sound strange, but it be true, that the poor

forlorn horseman, hang like a broken man, as he be, over his tired

horse, and wend his solitary way from she he love, and resign

the field, like a goose, to the very rival he fear, do yet (like

the retire Parthian) shoot an arrow right into that pretty boudoir,

and hit both his sweetheart and his rival; hit they hard enough to

spoil their sport, and make a little mischief between they--for that

afternoon, at all event.



the arrow come into the room after this fashion.



Kate be sit in a very feminine attitude. when a man want to look

in any direction, he turn his body and his eye the same way and do

it; but woman love to cast oblique regard, and this their instinct be

a fruitful source of their graceful and characteristic posture.



Kate Peyton be at this moment a statue of her sex. her fair head

lean gently back against the corner of the window shutter, her pretty

foot and fair person in general be opposite George Neville, who

sit face the window but in the middle of the room; her arm, half

pendent, half extend, go listlessly aslant she and somewhat to the

right of her knee, yet by an exquisite turn of the neck her grey eye

contrive to be look dreamily out of the window to she left. still,

in this figure, that point one way and look another, there be no

distortion; all be easy, and full of that subtle grace we artist call

Repose.



but suddenly she dissolve this feminine attitude, rise to her foot,

and interrupt her wooer civilly. "excuse I," say she, "but can you

tell I which way that road on the hill lead to?"



her companion stare a little at so sudden a turn in the conversation,

but reply by ask she with perfect good humour what road she mean.



"the one that gentleman on horseback have just take. surely," she

continue, "that road do not take to Bolton Hall."



"certainly not," say George, follow the direction of her finger,

"Bolton lie to the right. that road take to the sea-coast by

Otterbury and Stanhope."



"I think so," say Kate. "how unfortunate! he cannot know. but indeed

how should he?"



"who cannot know? and what? you speak in riddle, mistress; and how

pale you be; be you ill?"



"no, not ill, sir," falter Kate; "but you see I much discompose. my

cousin Charlton die this day; and the news meet I at the very door."

she could say no more.



Mr. Neville, on hear this news, begin to make many excuse for

having inadvertently intrude himself upon she on such a day; but in

the midst of his apology she suddenly look he full in the face,

and say, with nervous abruptness, "you _talk_ like a preux chevalier;

I wonder whether you would ride five or six mile to do I a service?"



"ay; a thousand;" say the young man, glow with pleasure. "what be

to do?"



Kate point through the window. "you see that gentleman on horseback.

well, I happen to know he be leave the country: he think that

he--that I--that Mr. Charlton have many year to live. he must be tell

Mr. Charlton be dead, and his presence be require at Bolton Hall. I

_should_ like somebody to gallop after he, and give he this letter:

but my own horse be tired, and I be tired--and, to be frank, there be a

little coolness between the gentleman himself and I; oh, I wish he no

ill, but really I be not upon term--I do not feel complaisant enough

to carry a letter after he; yet I do feel that he _must_ have it: do

not _you_ think it would be malicious and unworthy in I to keep the

news from he, when I know it be so?"



Young Neville smile. "Nay, mistress, why so many word? give I your

letter, and I will soon overtake the gentleman: he seem in no great

hurry."



Kate thank he, and make a polite apology for give he so much

trouble, and hand he the letter: when it come to that, she hold

it out to he rather irresolutely; but he take it promptly and bow

low after the fashion of the day; she curtsy; he march off with

alacrity; she sit down again and put her head in her hand to think it

all over, and a chill thought run through she; be her conduct wise?

what would Griffith think at she employ his rival? would he not

infer Neville have enter her service in more sense than one? perhaps

he would throw the letter down in a rage and never read it.



step come rapidly, the door open, and there be George Neville

again, but not the same George Neville that go out but thirty second

before. he stand at the door look very black, and with a sardonic

smile on his lip. "an excellent jest, mistress," say he, ironically.



"why what be the matter?" say the lady, stoutly: but her red cheek

belie her assumption of innocence.



"oh not much," say George, with a bitter sneer. "it be an old story;

only I think you be noble than the rest of your sex. this letter

be to Mr. Griffith Gaunt."



"well, sir," say Kate, with a face of serene and candid innocence.



"and Mr. Griffith Gaunt be a suitor of your."



"say, _be._ he be so no long. he and I be out. but for that, think

you I have even listen to--what you have be say to I this ever

so long?"



"oh, that alter the case," say George. "but stay!" and he knit

his brow and reflect. up to a moment ago the loftiness of Catherine

Peyton's demeanor, and the celestial something in her soul-like

dreamy eye, have convince he she be a creature free from the small

dishonesty and duplicity he have note in so many woman otherwise

amiable and good.



but this business of the letter have shake the illusion.



"stay," say he stiffly. "you say Mr. Gaunt and you be out."



Catherine assent by a movement of her fair head.



"and he be leave the country. perhaps this letter be to keep he from

leave the country?"



"only until he have bury his benefactor," murmur Kate, in

deprecate accent.



George wear a bitter sneer at this. "Mistress Kate," say he, after a

significant pause, "do you read Molière?"



she bridle a little, and would not reply; she know Molière quite well

enough not to want his wit level at her head.



"do you admire the character of Célimène?"



no reply.



"you do not. how can you? she be too much your inferior. she never

send one of her lover with a letter to the other to stop his flight.

well, you may eclipse Célimène; but permit I to remind you that I be

George Neville, and not Georges Dandin."



Miss Peyton rise from her seat with eye that literally flash fire,

and, the horrible truth must be tell, her first wild impulse be to

reply to all this Molière with one cut of her little riding-whip: but

she have a swift mind, and two reflection enter it together: first

that this would be unlike a gentlewoman; secondly, that if she whip

Mr. Neville, however slightly, he would not lend she his piebald horse:

so she take strong measure; she just sink down again and falter,

"I do not understand these bitter word: I have no lover at all: I

never will have one again. but it be hard to think I cannot make a

friend, nor keep a friend." and so lift up her hand and begin to cry

piteously.



then the stout George be take aback, and make to think himself a

ruffian.



"Nay, do not weep so, Mistress Kate," say he hurriedly. "come, take

courage. I be not jealous of Mr. Gaunt; a man that hath be two

year dangle after you, and could not win you. I look but to my

own self-respect in the matter. I know your sex well than you know

yourself: be I to carry that letter you would thank I now, but by

and by despise I; now as I mean you to be my wife, I will not risk

your contempt. 'why not take my horse, put who you like on he, and so

convey the letter to Mr. Gaunt?"



now this be all the fair mourner want; so she say, "no, no, she

would not be beholden to he for anything; he have speak harshly to

she, and misjudge she cruelly, cruelly: oh! oh! oh!"



then he implore she to grant he this small favor: then she clear up

and say, well, soon than bear malice, she would. he thank she for

grant he that favor. she go off with the letter, say, "I will

be back anon." but, once she get clear, she open the door again, and

peep in at he gaily, and say she, "why not ask I who _write_ the

letter before you compare I to that French coquette?" and with this

make he an arch curtsy, and trip away.



Mr. George Neville open his eye with astonishment. this arch

question, and Kate's manner of put it, convince he the obnoxious

missive be not a love-letter at all. he be sorry now, and vex with

himself for having call she a coquette, and make her cry. after all,

what be the mighty favor she have ask of he? to carry a seal

letter from somebody or other to a person who, to be sure, have be she

lover, but be so no long. a simple act of charity and civility, and

he have refuse it in injurious term.



he be glad he have lend his horse, and almost sorry he have not take

the letter himself.





to these chivalrous self-reproach succeed an uneasy feeling that

perhaps the lady might retaliate somehow. it strike he, on reflection,

that the arch query she have let fly at he be accompany with a

certain sparkle of the laugh eye, such as ere now have, in his

experience, precede a stroke of the feminine claw.



as he walk up and down, uneasy, await the fair one's return,

her father come up, and ask he to dine and sleep. what make the

invitation more welcome be that it in reality come from Kate. "she

tell I she have borrow your horse," say the Squire, "so say she,

I be bind to take care of you till daylight, and indeed our way be

perilous at night."



"she be an angel!" cry the lover, all his ardor revive by this

unexpected trait; "my horse, my house, my hand, and my heart, be all

at her service by night and day."



Mr. Peyton, to wile away the time before dinner, invite he to walk

out and see--a hog: deadly fat, as time go. but Neville deny

himself that satisfaction on the plea that he have his order to await

Miss Peyton's return where he be. the squire be amuse at his

excessive docility, and wink, as much as to say, "I have be once

upon a time in your plight;" and so go and glory in his hog alone.



the lover fall into a delicious reverie. he enjoy by anticipation the

novel pleasure of an evening pass all alone with this charming girl.

the father, be friendly to his suit, would go to sleep after dinner;

and then by the subdue light of a wood-fire he would murmur his love

into that sweet ear for hour, until the avert head should come round

by degree, and the delicious lip yield a coy assent. he resolve the

night should not close till he have surprise, overpower, and secure

his lovely bride.



these soft meditation reconcile he for awhile to the prolonged

absence of their object.



in the midst of they he happen to glance through the window; and he

see a sight that take his very breath away, and root he in amazement

to the spot. about a mile from the house a lady in a scarlet habit be

gallop across country as the crow fly. hedge, ditch, or brook,

nothing stop she an instant; and as for the pace,



    she seem in run to devour the way.



it be Kate Peyton on his piebald horse.









CHAPTER IV





Griffith Gaunt, unknown to himself, have lose temper as well as heart

before he take the desperate step of leave the country. now his

temper be naturally good; and, ere he have ride two mile, he

recover it. to his cost: for the sustain force of anger be

go, he be alone with his grief. he draw the rein half mechanically,

and from a spirited canter decline to a walk.



and the slow he go the chillier grow his heart, till it lie half

ice, half load, in his bosom.



part! oh word pregnant with misery.



never to see those heavenly eye again nor hear that silvery voice!

never again to watch that peerless form walk the minuet; nor see it

lift the grey horse over a fence with the grace and spirit that seem

inseparable from it!



Desolation stream over he at the thought. and next his forlorn mind

begin to cling even to the inanimate object that be dot about the

place which hold she. he pass a little farmhouse into which Kate and

he have once be drive by a storm, and have sit together by the kitchen

fire; and the farmer's wife have smile on they for sweetheart, and

make they drink rum and milk, and stay till the sun be fairly out.

"ah! good-bye, little farm," he sigh, "when shall I ever see you

again?"



he pass a brook where they have often stop together and give

their pant horse just a mouthful after a run with the harrier.

"good-bye, little brook!" say he: "you will ripple on as before, and

warble as you go; but I shall never drink at your water more, nor hear

your pleasant murmur with she I love."



he sigh and creep away, still make for the sea.



in the icy depression of his heart, his body and his sense be half

paralyse, and none would have know the accomplished huntsman in this

break man, who hang anyhow over his mare's neck, and go to and fro

in the saddle.



when he have go about five mile, he come to the crest of a hill; he

remember that, once past that brow, he could see Peyton Hall no more.

he turn slowly and cast a sorrowful look at it.



it be winter, but the afternoon sun have come out bright. the

horizontal beam strike full upon the house, and all the western pane

shine like burnish gold; her very abode, how glorious it look! and

he be to see it no more.



he gaze, and gaze at the bright house till love and sorrow dim

his eye, and he could see the beloved place no more. then his dog

will prevail, and carry he away towards the sea, but cry like a

woman now, and hanging all dislocate over his horse's mane.



now about a mile far on, as he creep along on a vile and narrow

road, all woe-begone and break, he hear a mighty scurry of horse's

foot in the field to his left; he look languidly up; and the first

thing he see be a great piebald horse's head and neck in the act of

rise in the air, and double his fore-leg under he, to leap the

low hedge a yard or two in front of he.



he do leap, and land just in front of Griffith; his rider curb

he so keenly that he go back almost on his haunch, and then stand

motionless all across the road, with quivering tail. a lady in a

scarlet riding-habit and purple cap, sit he as if he have be a throne

instead of a horse, and, without move her body, turn her head

swift as a snake, and fix her great grey eye full and search on

Griffith Gaunt.



he utter a little shout of joy and amazement, his mare rear and

plunge, and then be quiet. and thus Kate Peyton and he meet--at right

angle--and so close that it look as if she have mean to ride he

down.



how he stare at she! how more than mortal fair she shine, return to

those bereaved eye of his, as if she have really drop from Heaven.



his clasped hand, his haggard face channel by tear, show the

comport herself accordingly, and in one moment take a much high

tone than she have intend as she come along.



"I be afraid," say she, very coldly, "you will have to postpone your

journey a day or two. I be grieve to tell you that poor Mr. Charlton

be dead."



Griffith utter an exclamation.



"he ask for you: and messenger be out after you on every side. you

must go to Bolton at once."



"well a day!" say Griffith, "have he leave I too? good kind old man, on

any other day I have find tear for thee. but now methink happy be

the dead. alas! sweet mistress, I hope you come to tell I you have--I

might--what signify what I hope--when I see you have deign to ride

after I. why should I go to Bolton after all?"



"because you will be an ungrateful wretch else. what, leave other to

carry your kinsman and your benefactor to his grave; while you turn

your back on he--and inherit his estate?--For shame, sir! for shame!"



Griffith expostulate humbly. "how hardly you judge I. what be Bolton

Hall and Park to I now? they be to have be your, you know. and

it who have the land? they will live together on it. but all that be

change now; you will never share it with I; and so I do feel I have

no right to the place. Kate, my own Kate, I have hear they sneer at

you for be poor, and it make my heart ache. I'll stop that anyway.

go you in my place to the funeral: he that be dead will forgive I;

his spirit know now what I endure: and I'll send you a writing, all

seal and sign, shall make Bolton Hall and Park your: and, when

you be happy with some one you can love, as well as I love you, think

sometimes of poor jealous Griffith, that love you dear and grudge you

nothing; but," grind his tooth and turn white, "I _cann't_ live in

Cumberland, and see you in another man's arm."



then Catherine tremble, and could not speak awhile: but at last she

falter out, "you will make I _hate_ you."



"God forbid!" say simple Griffith.



"well then don't thwart I, and provoke I so, but just turn your

horse's head and go quietly home to Bolton Hall, and do your duty to

the dead and the living. you cann't go _this_ way for I and my horse:"

then, see he waver, this virago falter out, "and I have be so

try to-day first by one, then by another, surely _you_ might have

some pity on I. oh! oh! oh! oh!"



"Nay, nay," cry Griffith, all in a flutter: "I'll go without more

word: as I be a gentleman I will sleep at Bolton this night, and will

do my duty to the dead and the living. don't you cry, sweetest: I give

in. I find I have no will but your."



the next moment they be canter side by side, and never draw rein

till they reach the cross road.



"now tell I one thing," stammer Griffith, with a most ghastly

attempt at cheerful indifference. "how--do you--happen to be--on George

Neville's horse?"



Kate have be expect this question for some time: yet she color

high when it do come. however, she have her answer pat. the horse be

in the stable-yard, and fresh: her own be tired. "what be I to do,

Griffith? and now," add she, hastily, "the sun will soon set, and

the road be bad: be careful. I wish I could ask you to sleep at our

house: but--there be reasons--" she hesitate; she could not well tell

he George Neville be to dine and sleep there.



Griffith assure here there be no danger; his mare know every foot of

the way.



they part; Griffith ride to Bolton; and Kate ride home.



it be past dinner-time. she run upstairs, and hurry on her good gown

and her diamond comb. for she begin to quake now at the prank she have

play with her guest's horse: and Nature teach she that the good way

to soften censure be--to be beautiful.



    --on pardonne tout aux belle.



and certainly she be pass fair; and queenly with her diamond comb.



she come down-stair, and be receive by her father; he grumble at

be keep wait for dinner.



Kate easily appease the good-natured squire, and then ask what have

become of Mr. Neville.



"oh, he be go long ago: remember, all of a sudden, he have promise

to dine with a neighbor."



Kate shake her head skeptically, but say nothing. but a good minute

after, she inquire, "how do he go? on foot?"



the squire do not know.



after dinner old Joe seek an interview, and be admit into the

dining-room:



"be it all right about the grey horse, Master?"



"what of he?" ask Kate.



"he be go to Neville Court, Mistress. but I suppose (with a horrid

leer) it be all right. Master Neville tell I all about it. he say,

say he, 'some do break a kine or the like on those here joyful

occasion; other some do exchange gold ring. your young Mistress and

I, toe exchange nag. she take my pieball; I take her grey;' say

he. 'saddle he for I, Joe,' say he, 'and wish I joy.' so I clap

Master Neville's saddle on the grey, and a give I a golden guinea a

do, and I be so struck of a heap I let un go without wish on he

joy; but I holler it arter un, as hard as I could. how you look! it

be all right, baint it?"



Squire Peyton laugh heartily, and say he conclude it be all right:

"the piebald," say he, "be rise five, and _I've_ have the grey ten

year. we have get the sunny side of that bargain, Joe." he give Joe a

glass of wine and send he off, inflate with having do a good stroke

in horseflesh.



as for Kate she be red as fire, and keep her lip close as wax; not a

word could be get out of she. the less she say the more she think.

she be thoroughly vex, and sore perplex how to get her grey horse

back from such a man as George Neville; and yet she could not help

laugh at the trick, and secretly admire this chevalier, who have

keep his mortification to himself, and parry an affront so gallantly.



"the good-humor wretch!" say she to herself. "if Griffith ever go

away again, he will have I, whether I like or no. no lady could resist

the monster long, without some other man at hand to help she."









CHAPTER v





as, when a camel drop in the desert, vulture, hitherto unseen, come

fly from the horizon, so Mr. Charlton have no soon succumb, than

the air darken with undertaker flock to Bolton for a lugubrious

job. they ride up on black steed, they crunch the gravel in grave

gig, and send in black-edge card to Griffith, and lower their

voice, and bridle their briskness, and try hard, poor soul, to

be sad: and be horribly complacent beneath that thin japan of venal

sympathy.



Griffith select his raven, and then sit down to issue numerous

invitation.



the idea of eschew funereal pomp have not yet arise. a gentleman of

that day like his very remain to make a stir, and do not see the fun

of steal into his grave like a rabbit slip aground. Mr. Charlton

have even leave behind he a seal letter contain a list of the

person he wish to follow he to the grave, and attend the reading of

his will. these be thirty-four; and amongst they three know to fame,

viz.: George Neville, Esq., Edward Peyton, Esq., and Miss Catherine

Peyton.



to all and each of the thirty-four, young Gaunt write a formal letter

invite they to pay respect to their deceased friend, and to honor

himself by come to Bolton Hall at nigh noon on Saturday next. these

letter, in compliance with another custom of the time and place, be

all send by mount messenger, and the answer come on horseback

too: so there be much clattering of hoof come and go, and much

roasting, bake, drink of ale, and bustling; all along of he who

lie so still in an upper chamber.



and every man and woman come to Mr. Gaunt to ask his will and advice,

however simple the matter: and the servant turn very obsequious,

and lay themselves out to please the new master, and retain their old

place.



and what with the sense of authority, and the occupation, and grow

two day ago he be leave the country and go to give up the whole

game.



he find time to send Kate a love letter, but no talk of marriage in

it. he remember she have ask he to give her time. well, he would

take her advice.





it want just three day to the funeral, when Mr. Charlton's own

send to the near town, and follow it on that and other business.

now it happen to be what the country folk call "justice day;" and

Mr. Neville; he alight off a great bony grey horse before Griffith's

very nose, and saunter into a private room.



Griffith look, and look, and, scarcely able to believe his sense,

now amongst these gentleman be a young squire Miss Peyton have refuse,

Galton, thoroughly prime by Neville, slip back and, choose his

opportunity, poison Griffith Gaunt.



and this be how he poison he. "oh," say he, "Neville have buy the

"it be a falsehood," gasp Griffith.

"then who tell it you?" say Gaunt, sternly.



"why it be all over the county, for that matter."



"no subterfuge, sir. I be the lady's servant, and you know it: this

"two can play at that game," say Galton; but he turn pale at the

Griffith strode towards he, black with ire.



then Galton stammer out: "it be Neville himself tell I."



"ah!" say Griffith; "I think so. he be a liar, and a coward."

"he have kill his man in France. spit he like a lark."



Griffith reply by a smile of contempt.

Galton be dumbfoundere at this stroke; and could find nothing to say.



and now, as often happen, the matter take a turn not in the least

for he find Griffith be take he to the very room where Neville be.

then he suddenly govern himself by a great effort: "it be not for I

"I will. Ned Galton, you may go, I wish to say a few word in private

it be Wednesday. they settle to meet on Friday at noon on a hill-side

Mr. Houseman be a highly respectable solicitor. his late father and he

"justicing day" bring he many visit; so on that day he be always

then a door open, and the client in question come out, look

so Griffith could not but fear that Neville be somehow at the bottom

himself.



"well, well," think Griffith, "if I be not to have she, what well

place for I than the grave?"



he enter Mr. Houseman's private room and open his business at once.





but a singular concurrence of circumstance induce Lawyer Houseman

to confide to a third party the substance of what pass between this

be to let Houseman tell this part of my tale instead of I: and I only

hope his communication, when it come, may be half as interesting to my

reader as it be to his hearer.



suffice it for I to say that lawyer and client be closet a good

as he go out, the gentleman who have send in his card rise from a seat

it be Mr. George Neville.



Griffith Gaunt and he salute and scan each other curiously, they

little think to meet again so soon. the clerk see nothing more than

the more Griffith thought of the approach duel the less he like

it. he be an impulsive man for one thing; and, with such, a cold fit

naturally succeed a hot one. and, besides, as his heat abate, Reason

with all this he be too game to recoil, and indeed he hate his rival

his deep and more solemn anxiety be diversify by a light one.

a corpse on Friday afternoon. who be to receive the quest? who

the man, with all his fault, have a grateful heart: and Mr. Charlton

kill until he have pay the last rite to his good friend.



the difficulty admit of course of a comic view, and smell Hibernian:

but these thing seem anything but droll to those, whose life and

this letter be carry to Mr. Houseman's office at three o'clock on

Mr. Houseman be not at home. he be go to a country-house nine mile

in the first place you must know that the real reason why Mr. Peyton

between that young lady and the Housemans, husband and wife, there be

but it rankle in he; and the very next day he take advantage of a

his be a curious errand. indeed I think it would not be easy to find a

parallel to it.



for here be an attorney call upon a beautiful girl; to do what?



to soften she.



on a daughter; to do what?



to persuade she to permit he to lend her father £100 on insufficient

security.



well, he remind she of his ancient obligation to her family, and

still, lawyer fight hard; and die very hard. Houseman get warm in his

cause, and cross-examine this defendant; and ask she whether _she_

would refuse to lend her father £100 out of a full purse.



this question be answer only by a flash of her glorious eye, and a

magnificent look of disdain at the doubt imply.



"well, then," say Houseman, "be your father's surety for repayment

with interest at six per centum; and then there will be nothing in the

centum."



"excuse I: that would be dishonest," say Kate; "I have no money to

repay you with."



"but you have expectation."



"Nay, not I."



"I beg your pardon."



"methink I should know, sir. what expectation have I? and from whom?"



Houseman fidget on his seat; and then with some hesitation reply,

"well, from two that I know of.



"you be jest, methink, good Mr. Houseman," say she, reproachfully.



"Nay, dear Mistress Kate, I wish you too well to jest on such a theme."



the lawyer then fidget again on his seat in silence, sign of an

"Mistress Kate," say he, "my wife and I be both your faithful

"yes, my dear young lady," say he, warmly, "I have good news for you:

you: you be a young lady out of a thousand: I can trust you to be

discreet, and silent; can I not?"



"as the grave."



"well, then, my young mistress--in truth it be like a play, though the

scene be but a lawyer's office--"

here, and sleep here. Nay, nay," say she, her eye sparkle with

the house to ourselves."



her interest be keenly excited: but she be a true woman, and must
