that sheet be air, and a roasting fire light in the blue bedroom

for her guest.



while she be away, a servant bring in Griffith Gaunt's letter, and a

sheet of paper have to be borrow to answer it.



the answer be hardly write and send out to Griffith's servant, when

supper and the fair hostess come in almost together.



after supper fresh log be heap on the fire, and the lawyer sit

in a cosy arm-chair, and take out his diary, and several paper, as

methodically as if he be go to lay the case by counsel before a

judge of assize.



Kate sit opposite he with her grey eye beam on he all the time,

and search for the hidden meaning of everything he tell she. during

the recital which follow, her color often come and go, but those

wonderful eye never leave the narrator's face a moment.



they put the attorney on his mettle, and he elaborate the matter more

than I should have do: he articulate his topic; mark each salient

fact by a long pause. in short he tell his story like an attorney, and

not like a Romancist. I cannot help that, you know; I'm not procruste.





Mr. Houseman's Little Narrative



"Wednesday, the seventeenth day of February, at about one of the clock,

call on I at my place of business Mr. Griffith Gaunt, whom I need

not hero describe, inasmuch as his person and place of residence be

well known to the court--what be I saying?--I mean, well know to

yourself, Mistress Kate.





"the say Griffith, on enter my room seem move, and I might say,

distemper; and do not give himself time to salute I and receive

my obeisance, but address I abruptly and say as follow: 'Mr.

Houseman, I be come to make my will.'"



"dear I!" say Kate: then blush, and be more on her guard.





"I seat the young gentleman, and then reply that his resolution

aforesaid do he credit, the young be as mortal as the old. I say

far that many disaster have happen, in my experience, owe to

the obstinacy with which man in the day of their strength shut their

eye to the precarious tenure, under which all son of Adam hold

existence; and so many a worthy gentleman die in his sin. and, what

be bad, die intestate.





"but the say Griffith interrupt I with some sign of impatience,

and ask I bluntly would I draw his will, and have it execute on the

spot.





"I assent, generally; but I request he by way of needful

preliminary, to obtain for I a copy of Mr. Charlton's will, under

which, as I have always understand, the say Griffith inherit whatever

real estate he hath to bequeath.



"Mr. Griffith Gaunt then reply to I that Mr. Charlton's will be in

London, and the exact term of it could not be know until after the

funeral: that be to say upon the nineteenth instant.





"thereupon I explain to Mr. Gaunt that I must see and know what

property be devise in the will aforesaid, by the say Charlton,

to Gaunt aforesaid, and how devised and describe. without this, I

say, I could not correctly and sufficiently describe the same in the

instrument I be now request to prepare.





"Mr. Gaunt do not directly reply to this objection. but he ponder

a little while, and then ask I if it be not possible for he, by

mean of general term, to bequeath to a sole legatee whatever land,

good, chattel, etc., Mr. Charlton might hereafter prove to have

devise to he, the say Griffith Gaunt.



"I admit this be possible, but object that it be dangerous. I

let he know that in matter of law general term be a fruitful source

of dispute, and I say I be one of those who hold it a duty to avert

litigation from our client.





"Thereupon Mr. Gaunt draw out of his bosom a pocket-book.





"the say pocket-book be show to I by the say Gaunt, and I say it

contain a paragraph from a newspaper, which I believe to have be

cut out of the say newspaper with a knife or a pair of scissor, or

some trenchant instrument; and the say paragraph purport to contain

an exact copy of a certain Will and Testament under which (as be indeed

matter of public notoriety) one Dame Butcher hath inherit and now

enjoy the land, good, and chattel of a certain merry parson late

deceased in these part; and, _I believe_, little miss.





"Mr. Gaunt would have I read the Will and Testament aforesaid: and I

read it accordingly: and, inasmuch as bad thing be well remember,

the say Will and Testament do, by its singularity and profaneness,

fix itself forthwith in my memory; so that I can by no means dislodge

it thence, do what I may.



"the say document, to the good of my memory and belief, runneth after

this fashion: 'I, John Raymond, clerk, at present reside at Whitbeck,

in the county of Cumberland, be a man sound in body, mind, and

judgment, do deliver this as my last Will and Testament.



"'I give and bequeath all my real property, and all my personal

property, and all the property whether real or personal I may hereafter

possess, or become entitled to--to my Housekeeper, Janet Butcher.



"'and I appoint Janet Butcher my sole executrix, and I make Janet

Butcher my sole residuary legatee, save and except that I leave my

solemn curse to any knave, who hereafter shall at any time pretend that

he do not understand the meaning of this my Will and Testament.'"



(Catherine smile a little at this last bequest.)





"Mr. Gaunt then solemnly appeal to I as an honest man to tell he

whether the aforesaid document be bad, or good, in law.





"I be fain to admit that it be sufficient in law; but I qualify,

and say I think it might be attack on the score of the Hussy's

undue influence, and the Testator's apparent insanity. nevertheless, I

conclude candidly, that neither objection would prevail in our court,

owe to the sturdy prejudice in the breast of english juryman, whose

ground of faith it be that every man have a right to do what he will

with his own, and even to do it how he like.





"Mr. Gaunt do speedily abuse this my candor. he urge I to lose no

time, but to draw his will accord to the form and precedent in that

case make and provide by this mad parson: and my clerk forsooth be

to be the witness thereof.





"I refuse, with some heat, to sully my office by allow such an

instrument to issue therefrom: and I ask the said Gaunt, in high

dudgeon, for what he take I.





"Mr. Gaunt then offer, in reply, two suggestion that shake I.

Imprimis, he tell I the person to whom he now desire to leave his all

be Mistress Catherine Peyton. [an ejaculation from Kate.] Secundo, he

say he would go straight from I to that coxcomb Harrison, be I to

refuse to serve he in the matter.





"on this, have regard to your interest and my own, I temporize; I

offer to let he draw a will after his parson's precedent, and I

agree it should be witness in my office: only I stipulate that next

week a proper document should be draw by myself, with due particular,

on two sheet of paper, and afterwards engross and witness: and to

this Mr. Gaunt assent, and immediately draw his Will accord to

Newspaper Precedent.





"but, when I come to examine his masterpiece, I find he have take

advantage of my pliability to attach an unreasonable condition: to

wit, that the say Catherine should forfeit all interest under this

will in case she should ever marry a certain party therein nominate,

specify, and describe."



("now that be Griffith all over," cry Catherine, merrily.)





"I object stoutly to this. I take leave to remind the young gentleman

that, when a christian man make his last will and testament, he should

think of the grave, and of the place beyond whither we may carry our

affection, but must leave the bundle of our hate behind, the gate

be narrow. I even go so far as to doubt whether such a proviso

could stand in _law_; and I also put a practical query: what be to

hinder the legatee from sell the property and divert the fund,

and then marry whom she like?





"Mr. Gaunt be deaf to reason. he bid I remember that he be neither

Saint nor Apostle, but a poor gentleman of Cumberland, who see a

stranger come between he and his lover dear: with that he be much

move, and do not conclude his argument at all, but break off and be

fain to hide his face with both hand awhile. in truth this touch

I; and I look another way; and begin to ask myself why should I

interfere, who, after all, know not your heart in the matter: and, to

be brief, I withstand he and Parson's law no more; but send his draft

will to the clerk, the which they copy fair in a trice, and the

duplicate be sign and witness in red hot haste; as most of man's

folly be do for that matter.





"the paper writing now produce and show to I--tush! what be I

saying?--I mean the paper writing I now produce and show to you be the

draft of the will aforesaid, in the handwriting of the testator."



and with this he hand Kate Peyton Griffith Gaunt's will, and take a

long and satirical pinch of snuff while she examine it.



Miss Peyton take the will in her white hand and read it. but, in

read it she hold it up, and turn it so, that her friend could not

see her face while she read it, but only her white hand, in which the

document rustle a little.



it run thus:--



"I, Griffith Gaunt, late of the Eyrie, and now reside at Bolton

Hall, in the county of Cumberland, be sound in body and mind, do

deliver this as my last Will and Testament. I give and bequeath all the

property real or personal, which I now possess or may hereafter become

entitle to, to my dear friend and mistress, Catherine Peyton, daughter

of Edward Peyton, Esquire, of Peyton Hall; provide always that the

say Catherine Peyton shall at no time within the next ten year marry

George Neville, of Neville's Court, in this county. but should the say

Catherine marry the say George within ten year of this day, then I

leave all my say property, in possession, remainder, or reversion, to

my heir-at-law."



the fair legatee read this extraordinary testament more than once. at

last she hand it back to Mr. Houseman without a word. but her cheek

be red, and her eye glisten.



Mr. Houseman be surprised at her silence, and as he be curious to

know her heart, he sound she: ask she what she think of that part

of his story. but she evade he with all the tact of her sex. "what,

that be not all then?" say she quickly.



Houseman reply that it be barely half.



"then tell I all, pray tell I all," say Kate, earnestly.



"I be here to that end," say Houseman, and recommence his narrative.



"the business be do to Mr. Gaunt's satisfaction, though not to

mine, we fall into some friendly talk; but in the midst of it my clerk

Thomas bring I in the card of a gentleman whom I be very desirous

to secure as a client.





"Mr. Gaunt I think read my mind, for he take leave of I forthwith. I

attend he to the door, and then welcome the gentleman aforesaid, it

be no other than Mr. George Neville.





"Mr. Neville, after such gracious civility as his native breeding and

foreign travel have teach he, come to business and request I--to

draw his will."



"La!" say Kate.





"I be a little startled, but hide it, and take his instruction. this

do, I request to see the title-deed of his estate, with a view

to describe they, and he go himself to the banker's for they, and

place they in my hand.



"I then promise to have the will ready in a week or ten day. but Mr.

Neville, with many polite regret for hurry I, tell I upon his

honor he could give I but twenty-four hour. "after that, say he, 'it

might be too late.'"



("ah!" say Miss Peyton.)





"determine to retain my new client, I set my clerk to work, and

this very day be engross, sign, and witness, the last will and

testament of George Neville, Esquire, of Neville's Court, in the county

of Cumberland, and Leicester Square, London, where he hath a noble

mansion.





"now as to the general disposition of his land, manorial right,

messuage, tenement, good, chattel, etc., and his special legacy

to diver lady and gentleman and domestic servant, these I will not

reveal even to you.



"the paper I now produce be a copy of that particular bequest which I

have decide to communicate to you in strict and sacred confidence."



and he hand she an extract from George Neville's will.



Miss Peyton then read what follows:--



"and I give and bequeath to Mistress Catherine Peyton of Peyton Hall

in the say county of Cumberland in token of my respect and regard all

that my freehold estate call Moulton Grange with the messuage or

tenement standing and be thereon and the farm-yard building and

appurtenance belong thereto contain by estimation three hundred

and seventy-six acre three rood and five perch be the same little

more or less to hold to she the say Catherine Peyton her heir and

assign for ever."





the legatee lay down the paper, and lean her head softly on her fair

hand, and her eye explore vacancy.



"what mean all this?" say she, aloud, but to herself.



Mr. Houseman undertake the office of interpreter. "mean? why that

he have leave you one of the snug estate in the county. 'tis not

quite so large as Bolton; but lie sunnier, and the land rich. well,

mistress, be I right, be you not good for a thousand pound?"



Kate, still manifestly think of something else, let fall, as it

be, out of her mouth that Mr. Gaunt and Mr. Neville be both man in

the flower of their youth, and how be she the rich for their folly?



"why," say Houseman, "you will not have to wait for the death of these

testator--Heaven forbid!--But what do all this making of will show

I? that both these gentleman be deep in love with you, and you can

pick and choose: I say you can wed with Bolton Hall or Neville's Court

to-morrow: so prithee let the Squire have his hundred pound, and do

you repay I at your leisure."



Miss Peyton make no reply, but lean her exquisite head upon her hand

and ponder.



she do not knit her brow, nor labour visibly at the mental oar: yet a

certain reposeful gravity and a fixity of the thoughtful eye show she

be apply all the power's of her mind.



Mr. Houseman be not surprised at that: his own wife have but little

intellect; yet have he see she weigh two rival bonnet in mortal

silence, and with all the seem profundity of a judge on the bench.

and now this young lady be doubtless weigh farm with similar

gravity, care, and intelligence.



but as this continue and still she do not communicate her decision,

he ask her point-blank which of the two she settle to wed: Neville's

Court, or Bolton Grange.



thus appeal to, Miss Peyton turn her great eye on he without

really look at he, and replied,--"You have make I very uneasy."



he stare. she relapse into thought a moment, and then, turn to

Houseman, ask he how _he_ account for those two gentleman make

their will; they be very young to make their will all of a sudden.



"why," say Houseman, "Mr. Neville be a man of sense, and every man

of sense make his will; and, as for Mr. Gaunt, he have just come into

prospect of an estate; that's why."



"All, but why could not Griffith wait till after the funeral?"



"oh, client be always in a hurry."



"so you see nothing in it? nothing alarm I mean?"



"nothing very alarming. two land proprietor in love with you; that

be all."



"but, dear Mr. Houseman, that be what make I uneasy: at this rate

they must look on one another as--as--rival: and you know rival be

sometimes enemy."



"oh I see now," say Houseman: "you apprehend a quarrel between the

gentleman. of course there be no love lose between they; but they meet

in my office and salute each other with perfect civility. I see they

with my own eye."



"indeed! I be glad to hear that; very glad. I hope it be only a

coincidence then, their both make their will."



"nothing more you may depend: neither of they know from I what the

other have do; nor ever will."



"that be true," say Kate, and seem considerably relieve.



to ease her mind entirely, Houseman go on to say that as to the

report that high word have pass between the client in question, at

the Roebuck, he have no doubt it be exaggerated. "besides," say he,

"that be not about a lady; I'm tell it be about a horse. some bet

belike."



Catherine utter a faint cry. "about a horse!" say she. "not about a

grey horse?"



"Nay, that be more than I know."



"high word about a horse," say Catherine; "and they be make their

will. oh! my mind misgave I from the first." and she turn pale.

presently she clasp her hand together--"Mr. Houseman!" she cry,

"what shall I do? what, do you not see that both their life be in

danger? and that be why they make their will. and how should _both_

their life be in danger, but from each other? Madmen! they have

quarrel: they be go to fight; fight to the death: and I fear it

be about I. I who love neither of they, you know."



"in that case, _let_ they fight," say her legal adviser,

dispassionately. "whichever fool get kill, you will be none the

poor." and the dog wear a sober complacency.



Catherine turn her large eye on he with horror and amazement, but

say nothing.



as for the lawyer he be more struck with her sagacity than with

anything. he somewhat overrate it; not be aware of the private

reason she have for suspect that her two testator be enemy to

the death.



"I almost think you be right," say he; "for I get a curious missive

from Mr. Gaunt scarce an hour agone, and he says,--let I see what he

say." "Nay, let _I_ see," say Kate. on that he hand she Griffith's

note. it run thus--



    "it be possible I may not be able to conduct the funeral.

    should this be so, I appoint you to act for I. so then, good

    Mr. Houseman, let I count on you to be here at nine of the

    clock. for Heaven's sake fail I not.



    "your humble servant,



                                                          "G. G."





this leave no doubt in Kate's mind.



"now, first of all," say she, "what answer make you to this?"



"what answer should I make? I pledge my word to be at Bolton at nine

of the clock."



"oh, blind!" sigh Kate. "and I must be out of the room. what shall I

do? my dear friend, forgive I: I be a wretched girl. I be to blame;

I ought to have dismiss they both, or else decide between they.

but who would have think it would go this length? I do not think

Griffith be brave enough. have pity on I, and help I. stop this

fearful fighting." and now the young creature clung to the man of

business, and pray and pray he earnestly to avert bloodshed.



Mr. Houseman be stagger by this passionate appeal from one who so

rarely lose her self-command. he soothe she as well as he could, and

say he would do his good; but add, which be very true, that he

think her interference would be more effective than his own. "what

care these young blood for an old attorney? I should fare ill, come I

between their rapier. to be sure I might bind they over to keep the

peace. but Mistress Kate, now be frank with I; then I can serve you

well. you love one of these two; that be clear. which be the man?

that I may know what I be about."



for all her agitation Kate be on her guard in some thing.



"Nay," she falter, "I love neither, not to say love they: but I pity

he so."



"which?"



"Both."



"ay, mistress; but which do you pity most?" ask the shrewd lawyer.



"whichever shall come to harm for my sake," reply the simple girl.



"you could not go to they to-night, and bring they to reason?" ask

she, piteously. she go to the window to see what sort of a night it

be; she draw the heavy crimson curtain and open the window. in

rush a bitter blast laden with fly snow. the window ledge too

be clog with snow, and all the ground be white.



Houseman shudder, and draw near to the blaze log. Kate close

the window with a groan. "it be not to be think of," say she; "at

your age; and not a road to be see for snow. what shall I do?"



"wait till to-morrow," say Mr. Houseman. (procrastination be his daily

work, be an attorney.) "to-morrow!" cry Catherine. "perhaps even now

they have meet, and he lie a corpse."



"who?"



"whichever it be, I shall end my day in a convent pray for his

soul." she wring her hand while she say this, and still there be no

catch she.



Little do the lawyer think to rouse such a storm with his good news.

and now he make a feeble and vain attempt to soothe she; and end by

promise to start the first thing in the morning and get both her

testator bind over to keep the peace, by noon. with this resolution

he go to bed early.



she be glad to be alone at all event.



now, mind you, there be plenty of vain and vulgar, yet respectable

girl, in Cumberland, who would have be delighted to be fight about,

even though bloodshed be to be the result. but this young lady

be not vain, but proud; she be sensitive too, and trouble with a

conscience. it reproach she bitterly: it tell she she have permit

the address of two gentleman, and so mischief have somehow arise--out

of her levity. now her life have be uneventful, and innocent: this

be the very first time she have be connect with anything like a

crime; and her remorse be great: so be her grief; but her fear be

great still. the terrible look Griffith have cast at his rival flash

back on she; so do his sinister word. she feel that if he and Neville

meet, nothing less than Neville's death or his own would separate they.

suppose that even now one of they lie a corpse! cold and ghastly as the

snow that now cover Nature's face.



the agitation of her mind be such, that her body could not be still:

now she walk the room in violent distress, wring her hand;

now she kneel and pray fervently for both those life she have

endanger: often she fly to the window and look eagerly out,

writhe and rebel against the network of female custom that

entangle she, and would not let she fly out of her cage even to do a

good action; to avert a catastrophe by her prayer, or her tear, or

her good sense.



and all end in she realize that she be a woman, a poor impotent

be bear to lie quiet and let thing go: at that she weep helplessly.



so wear away the first night of agony this young creature ever know.



towards morning, exhaust by her inward struggle, she fall asleep

upon a sofa.



but her trouble follow she. she dream she be on a horse, hurry

along with prodigious rapidity, in a darken atmosphere, a sort of dry

fog: she know somehow she be be take to see some awful, mysterious

thing. by-and-by the haze clear, and she come out upon pleasant open

sunny field that almost dazzle she. she pass gate, and hedge too,

all clear, distinct, and individual. presently a voice by her side say

"this way!" and her horse seem to turn of his own accord through

a gap, and in one moment she come on a group of gentleman. it be

Griffith Gaunt, and two stranger. then she speak, and say,



"but, Mr. Neville?"



no answer be make she; but the group open in solemn silence, and

there lie George Neville on the snow, stark and stiff, with blood

issue from his temple, and trickle along the snow.



she see distinctly all his well-know feature; but they be pinch

and sharpen now. and his dark olive skin be turn to bluish white.

it be his corpse. and now her horse thrust out his nose and snort

like a demon. she look down, and ah! the blood be run at she

prematurely fast along the snow. she scream, her horse rear high,

and she be fall on the blood-stain snow: she awake screaming; and

the sunlight seem to rush in at the window.



her joy that it be only a dream overpower every other feeling at

first. she kneel and thank God for that.



the next thing be, she think it might be a revelation of what have

actually occur.



but this chilling fear do not affect she long. nothing could shake she

conviction that a duel be on foot--and indeed the intelligent of her

sex do sometimes put this and that together, and spring to a just but

obvious inference, in a way that look to a slow and safe reasoner

like divination--but then she know that yesterday evening both party

be alive. couple this with Griffith's broad hint that after the

funeral might be too late to make his will, she feel sure that it be

this very day the combatant be to meet. yes, and this very morning:

for she know that gentleman always fight in the morning.



if her dream be false as to the past, it might be true as to what be

at hand. be it not a supernatural warning send to she in mercy? the

history of her church abound in such dream and vision; and indeed

the time and place she live in be rife with story of the kind;

one, in particular, of recent date.



this thought take hold of she, and grow on she, till it overpower

even the diffidence of her sex; and then up start her individual

character; and now nothing could hold she. for, languid and dreamy

in the common thing of life, this Catherine Peyton be one of those

who rise into rare ardor and activity in such great crisis as seem to

benumb the habitually brisk, and they turn tame and passive.



she have see at a glance that Houseman be too slow and apathetic

for such an emergency; she resolve to act herself. she wash she

face and neck and arm and hand in cold water, and be refresh and

invigorate. she put on her riding habit and her little gold spur;

Griffith Gaunt have give it she; and hurry into the stable-yard.



Old Joe and his boy have go away to breakfast: he live in the village.



this be unlucky: Catherine must wait his return and lose time, or else

saddle the horse herself. she choose the latter. the piebald be a good

horse, but a fidgety one; so she saddle and bridle he at his stall.

she then lead he out to the stone step in the stable-yard, and try

to mount he. but he sidle away; she have nobody to square he; and she

could get nothing to mount but his head. she coax he, she tickle

he on the other side with her whip. it be all in vain.



it be absurd, but heart-sickening. she stare at he with wonder that

he could be so cruel as to play the fool when every minute might be

life or death. she speak to he, she implore he piteously; she pat

he. all be in vain.



as a last resource she walk he back to the stable and give he a

sieveful of oat, and set it down by the corn bin for he, and take an

opportunity to mount the bin softly.



he eat the oat, but with retrovert eye watch she. she keep quiet

and affected nonchalance till he become less cautious: then suddenly

spring on he, and teach he to set his wit against a woman's. my lord

wheel round directly ere she could get her leg over the pommel; and

make for the stable door. she lower her head to his mane and just

scrape out without injury; not an inch to spare. he set off at once;

but luckily for she she have often ride a bare-back horse. she sit

he for the first few yard by balance; then rein he in quietly, and

soon whip her left foot into the stirrup and her right leg over the

pommel; and then the piebald nag have to pay for his prank: the road

be clog with snow, but she fan he along without mercy, and

never draw bridle till she pull he up drenched, and steam like a

wash-tub, at Netley crossroad.



here she halt irresolute: the road to the right lead to Bolton,

distant two mile and a half. the road in front lead to Neville's Court,

distant three mile. which should she take? she have ask herself this

a dozen time upon the road; yet could never decide until she get to

the place, and must. the question be, with which of they have she most

influence? she hardly know; but Griffith Gaunt be her old sweetheart;

it seem somewhat less strange and indelicate to go to he than to

the new one: so she turn her horse's head towards Bolton; but she

no long go quite so fast as she have go before she felt go to

either in particular. such be the female mind.



she reach Bolton at half-past eleven; and now she be there, put

a bold face on it; ride up to the door, and, lean forward on her

horse, rang the hall bell.



a footman come to the door.



with compose visage though beat heart, she tell he she desire to

speak for a moment to Mr. Griffith Gaunt. he ask she would she be

pleased to alight: and it be clear by his manner no calamity have yet

fall. "no, no," say Kate, "let I speak to he here."



the servant go in to tell his master. Kate sit quiet with her heart

still beat, but glow now with joy: she be in time then, thank

to her good horse. she pat he, and make the prettiest excuse aloud

to he for ride he so hard through the snow.



the footman come back to say that Mr. Gaunt have go out.



"gone out? whither? on horseback?"



the footman do not know, but would ask within.



while he be go to inquire, Catherine lose patience, and ride into

the stable-yard, and ask a young lout who be lounge there whether

his master be go out on horseback.



the lounge youth take the trouble to call out the groom, and ask

he.



the groom say "no," and that Mr. Gaunt be somewhere about the ground

he think.



but in the midst of this colloquy one of the maid, curious to see

the lady, come out by the kitchen door and curtsy to Kate, and tell

she Mr. Gaunt be go out walk with two other gentleman. in the

midst of her discourse she recognize the visitor, and having somehow

imbibe the notion that Miss Peyton be likely to be Mrs. Gaunt, and

govern Bolton Hall, decide to curry favor with she; so she call she

my lady, and be very communicative. she say one of the gentleman be

strange to she; but the other be Doctor Islip from Stanhope town. she

know he well: he have take off her own brother's leg in a jiffy. "but,

dear heart, Mistress," say she, "how pale you be. do come in and have

a morsel of meat, and a horn of ale."



"Nay, my good girl," say Kate; "I could not eat; but bring I a mug of

new milk if you will. I have not break my fast this day."



the maid bustle in, and Catherine ask the groom if there be no

mean of know where Mr. Gaunt be. the groom and the boy scratch

their head and look puzzle. the lounging lout look at their

perplexity, and grin satirically.



this youth be Tom Leicester, bear in wedlock, and therefore in the

law's eye son of old Simon Leicester; but gossip say his true father

be the late Captain Gaunt. Tom run with the hound for his own sport:

go out shoot with gentleman and belabor the briar for they at

two-pence per day and his dinner, and abhor all that sober man call

work.



by trade, a Beater: profession, a Scamp.



two maid come out together now; one with the milk and a roll, the

other with a letter. Catherine drink the milk but could not eat. then

say the other maid, "if so be you be Mistress Peyton, why this letter

be for you: Master leave it on his table in his bedroom."



Kate take the letter and open it, all in a flutter. it run thus:--



    "Sweet Mistress,--When this reach you, I shall be no more

    here to trouble you with my jealousy. this Neville set it

    abroad that you have change horse with he, as much as to say

    you have plight troth with he. he be a liar, and I tell he

    so to his tooth. we be to meet at noon this day: and one must

    die. methink I shall be the one. but, come what may, I have

    take care of thee; ask Jack Houseman else. but, oh dear Kate,

    think of all that hath pass between we, and do not wed this

    Neville, or I could not rest in my grave. sweetheart, many a

    letter have I write thee, but none so sad as this. let the

    grave hide my fault from thy memory; think only that I love

    thee well. I leave thee my substance; would it be ten time

    more; and the last thought of my heart.





    "so no more in this world,



         "from he that be thy true lover



              "and humble servant till death,





                                                 Griffith Gaunt."





there seem to be room in the mind for only one violent emotion at

one instant of time. this touching letter do not just then draw a

tear from she who now receive it some hour soon than the writer

intend. its first effect be to paralyze she. she sit white and

trembling, and her great eye fill with horror. then she begin to

scream wildly for help. the man and woman come round she.



"Murder! murder!" she shriek. "tell I where to find he, ye

wretch, or may his blood be on your head!"



the scamp bound from his lounging position and stand before she

straight as an arrow. "follow I," he shout. her grey eye and the

scamp's black one, flash into one another directly. he dash out of

the yard without another word.



and she spur her horse, and clatter out after he.



he run as fast as her horse could canter, and soon take she all round

the house: and, while he run, his black, gipsy eye be glance in

every direction.



when they get to the lawn at the back of the house, he halt a moment,

and say quietly, "here they be." he point to some enormous footstep

in the snow, and bade her notice that they commence at a certain glass

door belong to the house, and that they all point outward. the

lawn be cover with such mark, but the scamp follow those his

intelligence have select, and they take he through a gate, and down

a long walk, and into the park. here no other foot have tread that

morning except those Tom Leicester be follow. "this be our game,"

say he. "see, there be six footstep; and, now I look, this here track

be Squire Gaunt's. I know his foot in the snow among a hundred. bless

your heart, I've often be out shoot with Squire Gaunt, and lose

he in the wood, and find he again by track he on dead leave,

let alone snow. I say, ben't they useless idiot? couldn't tell ye how

to run into a man, and snow on the ground! why you can track a hare to

her form and a rat to his hole--let alone such big game as this, with a

hoof like a frying-pan--in the snow."



"oh, do not talk; let we make haste," pant Kate.



"Canter away," reply the scamp.



she canter on, and he run by her side. "shall I not tire you?" say

she.



the mauvais sujet laugh at she. "tire _I!_ not over this ground.

why, I run with the hound, and mostly always in at the death: but

that be not altogether speed; ye see I know Pug's mind. what, don't

you know _I?_ I'm Tom Leicester. why, I know you: I say, you're a

good-hearted one, you be."



"oh no! no!" sigh Kate.



"Nay, but you be," say Tom. "I see you take Harrowden brook that

day, when the rest turn tail; and that be what I call have a good

heart: gently, mistress, here, this be full of rabbit hole; I see Sir

Ralph's sorrel mare break her leg in a moment in one of these. shot she

dead that afternoon, a do, and then bill she for the hound. she'd

often follow at their tail: next hunting day she run inside their

belly. ha! ha! ha!"



"oh, don't laugh. I be in agony."



"why, what be up, mistress?" ask the young savage, lower his

voice. "'murder,' say you; but that mean _nought._ the lass they

cry murder if you do but kiss 'em."



"oh, Tom Leicester, it be murder. it's a duel, a fight to the death,

unless we be in time to prevent they."



"a jewel!" cry Master Leicester, his eye glitter with delight.

"I never see a jewel. don't you hold he in for I, mistress: gallop

down this slope as hard as you can pelt: it be grass under foot, and

ye cann't lose the track, and I shall be sure to catch ye in the next

field."



the young savage be now as anxious to be in at the death, as Kate be

to save life. as he speak he give her horse a whack on the quarter with

his stick, and away she go full gallop, and soon put a hundred yard

between she and Tom.



the next field be a deep fallow; and the hard furrow reduce she to a

trot; and before she get out of it, Tom be by her side: "don't I tell

you?" say he. "I'd run you to Peyton Hall for a pot o' beer."



"oh you good, brave, clever boy," say Kate: "how fortunate I be to

have you. I think we shall be in time."



Tom be flatter. "why you see I be none of Daddy Leicester's breed,"

say he. "I'm a gentleman's by-blow, if you know what that be."



"I cann't say I do," say Kate; "but I know you be very bold and

handsome and swift of foot, and I know my patron saint have send you to

I in my misery, and oh, my lad, if we be in time--what can I do for

you?--Are you fond of money, Tom?"



"that I be: when I can get it."



"then you shall have all I have get in the world, if you get I there

in time to hinder mischief."



"come on!" shout Tom, excited in his turn; and take the lead, and not

a word more pass till they come to the foot of a long hill. then say

Tom, "once we be at top of this, they cann't fight without our seeing

'em. that be Scutchemsee Nob: you can see ten mile all round from

there."



at this information Kate utter an ejaculation and urge her horse

forward.



the first part of this hill, which stand between she and those whoso

track she follow, be grass; then come a strip of turnip; then on

the bleak top a broad piece of heather. she soon canter over the

grass; and leave Tom so far behind he could not quite catch she in the

turnip. she enter the heather, but here she be much retarded by

the snow drift, and the up and down of the rough place. but she

struggle on bravely, still lead.



she fix her eye earnestly on the ridge, whence she could cry to the

combatant, however distant, and stop the combat.



now as she straggle on, and Tom come after, pant a little for the

first time, suddenly there rise from the crest of the hill two column

of smoke, and the next moment two sharp report rang through the frosty

air.



Kate stop; and look round to Tom with a scared, inquire air.



"pistol!" yell Tom behind she.



at that the woman overpower the heroine, and Kate hide her face and

fall to tremble and wail. her wearied horse come down to a walk.



presently up come Tom. "don't lose your stomach for that," he pant

out. "gentlefolk do pop at one another all day sometimes, and no harm

do."



"oh, bless you!" cry Kate; "I may yet be in time." she spur she

horse on. he do his good, but ere he have go twenty yard, he plunge

into a cavity hide by the snow.



while he be flounder there crack go a single pistol, and the

smoke rise and drift over the hill top.



"who--op!" mutter Tom, with horrible sang-froid. "there's one do

for this time. couldn't shoot back, ye see."



at this horrible explanation, Kate sink forward on her horse's mane

as if she herself have be kill; and the smoke from the pistol come

float, thin and thin, and eddy high over her head.



Tom speak rude word of encouragement to she. she do not even seem

to hear they. then he lose all patience at she, and clutch her arm

to make she hear he. but at that it seem as if some of his nature

pass into her down his arm, for she turn wild directly and urge

her horse fiercely up the crest. her progress be slow at first; but

the sun have melt the snow on the Nob or extreme summit. she tear she

way through the last of the snow on to the clear piece, then, white

as ashe, spur and lash her horse over the ridge and dash in

amongst they on the other side. for there they be.



what be the sight that meet her eye?



that belong to the male branch of my story, and shall be tell

forthwith, but in its proper sequence.









CHAPTER VI





the two combatant come to the field in a very different spirit.

Neville have already fight two duel, and be successful in both.

he have confidence in his skill, and in his luck. his conscience too

be tolerably clear: for he be the insulted person; and, if a bullet

should remove this dangerous rival from his path, why all the well

for he, and all the bad for the fool who have bring the matter to a

bloody issue, though the balance of the lady's heart incline his way.



he come in high spirit, and ride upon Kate Peyton's grey, to ste his

adversary, and show his contempt of he.



not so Griffith Gaunt. his heart be heavy, and forebode ill. it be

his first duel, and he expect to be kill. he have play a fool's

game, and he see it.



the night before the duel he try hard to sleep: he know it be not

give his nerve fair play to lie think all night. but coy sleep,

as usual when most want, refuse to come. at daybreak the restless

man give it up in despair, and rise and dress himself. he write that

letter to Catherine, little think it would fall into her hand while

he live. he eat a little toast and drink a pint of Burgundy; and then

wander listlessly about till Major Rickards, his second, arrive.



that experienced gentleman bring a surgeon with he; Mr. Islip.



Major Rickards deposit a shallow wooden box in the hall; and the two

gentleman sit down to a hearty breakfast.



Griffith take care of his guest, but beyond that speak scarcely a

word; and the surgeon, after a ghastly attempt at commonplace, be

silent too. major Rickards satisfy his appetite first, and then,

find his companion dumb, set to work to keep up their spirit. he

entertain they with a narrative of the personal encounter he have

witness, and especially of one in which his principal have fall on

his face at the first fire, and the antagonist have spring into the air,

and both have lain dead as door nail, and never move, nor even wink,

after that single discharge.



Griffith sit under this chilling talk for more than an hour.



at last he rise gloomily, and say it be time to go.



"got your tool, doctor?" inquire the Major.



the surgeon nod slightly. he be more discreet than his friend.



when they have walk nearly a mile in the snow, the Major begin to

complain. "the devil!" say he; "this be queer walk. my boot be

full of water. I shall catch my death."



the surgeon smile satirically, compare silent Griffith's peril with

his second's.



Griffith take no notice. he go like Fortitude plod to execution.



Major Rickards fall behind, and whisper Mr. Islip: "don't like his

look; don't march like a winner. a job for you or the sexton, you

mark my word."





they toil up Scutchemsee Nob, and when they reach the top, they see

Neville and his second, Mr. Hammersley, ride towards they. the pair

have halter as well as bridle, and dismounting, make their nag fast

to a large blackthorn that grow there. the second then step forward

and salute each other with formal civility.



Griffith look at the grey horse, and grind his tooth. the sight of

the animal in Neville's possession stir up his hate, and help to

steel his heart. he stand apart, still, pale and gloomy.



the second step out fifteen pace, and place the man. then they

load two pair of pistol, and put a pistol in each man's hand.



Major Rickards take that opportunity to advise his principal. "stand

sharp. keep your arm close to your side. don't fire too high. how do

you feel?"



"like a man who must die; but will try to die in company."



the second now withdraw to their place, and the rival hold their

pistol lower: but fix their deadly eye on each other.



the eye, in such a circumstance, be a terrible thing: it be literally a

weapon of destruction; for it direct the deadly hand that guide the

deadly bullet. moreover the long and the more steadily the duelist

fix his eye on his adversary, the less likely he be to miss.



Griffith be very pale, but dog. Neville be serious, but firm. both

eye each other unflinchingly.



"gentleman, be you ready?" ask Neville's second.



{"yes."



{"yes."



"then," say Major Rickards, "you will fire when I let fall this

handkerchief, and not before. mark I, gentleman; to prevent mistake,

I shall say 'one----two----three'----and then drop the handkerchief.

now then, once more, be you quite ready?"



{"yes."



{"yes."



"One------Two------Three."------He drop the handkerchief, and both

gentleman fire simultaneously. Mr. Neville's hat spin into the air;

Griffith stand untouched.



the bullet have pass through Neville's hat, and have actually cut a

lane through his magnificent hair.



the second now consult, and it be intimate to Griffith that a word

of apology would be accept by his antagonist.



Griffith decline to utter a syllable of apology.



two more pistol be give the man.



"aim lower," say rickard.



"I mean to," say Griffith.



the second withdraw, and the man eye each other: Griffith dog and

pale, as before, Neville not nearly so self-assure; Griffith's bullet,

in graze he, have produce the effect of a sharp, cold, current of

air no wide than a knife. it be like death's icy forefinger lay on

his head, to mark he for the next shot; as man mark a tree; then come

again and fall it.



"One----two----three!"



and Griffith's pistol miss fire, but Neville's go off, and

Griffith's arm sink powerless, and his pistol roll out of his hand.

he feel a sharp twinge, and then something trickle down his arm.



the surgeon and both second run to he. "Nay, it be nothing," say he,

"I shoot far well with my left hand than my right. give I another

pistol, and let I have fair play. he have hit I. and now I'll hit he."



both second agree this be impossible.



"it be the chance of war," say Major Rickards: "you cannot be allow

to take a cool shot at Mr. Neville. if you fire again, so must he."



"the affair may very well end here," say Mr. Hammersley. "I understand

there be some provocation on our side; and on behalf of the party

insult I be content to let the matter end, Mr. Gaunt be wound."



"I demand my second shot to his third," say Griffith sternly; "he will

not decline, unless he be a poltroon as well as----what I call he."



the nature of this reply be communicate to Neville, and the second,

with considerable reluctance, load two more pistol; and during the

process Major Rickards glance at the combatant.



Griffith, exasperate by his wound and his jealousy, be wear out

the chivalrous courage of his adversary; and the Major see it. his keen

eye notice that Neville be get restless, and looking confound

at his despise rival's pertinacity: and that Gaunt be more dog,

and more deadly.



"my man will kill yours this time," say he, quietly, to Neville's

second. "I can see it in his eye; he be hungry; t'other have have his

bellyful."



once more the man be arm, and the second withdraw to their place,

intimate that this be the last shot they would allow under any

circumstance whatever.



"be you both ready?"



{"yes."



{"yes."



a faint wail seem to echo the response.



all hear it, and in that superstitious age believe it to be some

mysterious herald of death.



it suspend even Major Packard's voice a minute. he recover himself,

however, and once more his soldier-like tone run in the keen air:--



"One----"



there be a great rushing, and a pounding of the hard ground, and a

scarlet Amazon gallop in and draw up in the middle, right between the

level pistol.



every eye have be so bent on the combatant, that Kate Peyton and her

horse seem to have spring out of the very earth. and there she sit

pale as ashe, on the steam piebald, and glance from pistol to

the duelist stare in utter amazement, and instinctively lower

their weapon; for she have put herself right in their line of fire,

with a recklessness that contrast nobly with her fear for other. in

short this apparition literally petrify they all, second as well as

combatant.



and, while they stand open-mouthed yet dumb, in come the Scamp, and,

with a brisk assumption of delegate authority, take Griffith's weapon

out of his now unresisting hand; then march to Neville. he instantly

salute Catherine, and then hand his pistol to her seem agent,

with a high-bred and inimitable air of utter nonchalance.



Kate, see they to her surprise so easily disarm, raise her hand

and her lovely eye to Heaven, and in a feeble voice, thank God and

St. Nescioquis.



but very soon that faint voice quaver away to nothing, and her fair

head be see to droop, and her eye to close; then her body sink

slowly forward like a break lily; and in another moment she lie

fainting on the snow beside her steaming horse.



he never move, he be so dead beat too.



o lame and impotent conclusion of a vigorous exploit! masculine up

to the crowning point, and then to go and spoil all with "woman's

weakness."



"N.B.: this be rote sarcasticul," as Artemus, the delicious, say.

woman's weakness! if Solomon have plan and Samson execute, they

could not have serve her turn well than this most seasonable

swooning do. for lo! at her fall the doughty combatant utter a

yell of dismay, and there be an indiscriminate rush towards the fair

sufferer.



but the surgeon claim his rights:--"This be my business," say he,

authoritatively; "do not crowd on she, gentleman; give her air."



Whereupon the duelist and second stand respectfully aloof in a mixed

group, and watch with eager interest and pity.



the surgeon make a hole in the snow and lay his fair patient's head

low. "don't be alarm," say he: "she have swoon; that be all."



it be all mighty fine to say don't be alarm. but her face be ashy,

and her lip the color of lead: and she be so like death, they could

not help be terribly alarmed: and now, for the first time, the

duelist feel culprit; and, as for fighting, every idea of such a

thing go out of their head: the rival now be but rival nurse:

and never do a lot of woman make more fuss over a child, than all

these bloodthirsty man do over this Amazon manquée. they produce

their legendary lore: one's grandmother have tell he burn feather

be the thing; another, from an equally venerable source, have gather

that those pink palm must be profanely slap by the horny hand of

a man; for at no less a price could resuscitation be obtain. the

surgeon scorn all their legend, Griffith and Neville make hasty

rush with brandy and usquebaugh; but whether to be take internally

or externally, they do not say, nor indeed know; but only thrust their

flask wildly on the doctor: and he decline they loftily. he melt

snow in his hand, and dash it hard in her face; and put salt close

to her pretty little nostril. and this he repeat many time, without

effect.



but at last her lip begin to turn from lead color to white, and then

from white to pink, and her heavenly eye to open again, and her mouth

to murmur thing pitiably small and not bear on the matter in hand.



her cheek be still colorless, when her consciousness come back, and

she find she be lie on the ground with ever so many gentleman

look at she.



at that, Modesty alarm send the blood at once rush to her pale

cheek.



a lovely lily seem turn to a lovely rose before their eye.



the next thing be, she hide that blushing face in her hand, and begin

to whimper.



the surgeon encourage she: "Nay, we be all friend," he whisper,

paternally.



she half part her finger and peer through they at Neville and

Gaunt. then she remember all, and begin to cry hysterically.



New dismay of sanguinary unprofessional!



"_now_, gentleman, if you will lend I your flask," say Mr. Islip,

mighty calmly.



Griffith and Neville be instantly at his side, each with a flask.



the surgeon administer snow and brandy. Kate sip these, and gulp

down her sob, and at last cry composedly.



but, when it come to sip brandied snow and cry comfortably,

Major Rickards's anxiety give place to curiosity. without take his

eye off her he beckon, Mr. Hammersley apart, and whisper, "who the

deuce be it?"



"don't you know?" whisper the other in return. "why Mistress Peyton

herself."



"what, the girl it be all about? well, I never hear of such a thing:

the causa belli to come _gallop_, and _swoon_, on the field of

battle, and so stop the fighting! what will our lady do next? by

Heaven, she be worth fight for though. which be the happy man, I

wonder? she don't look at either of they."



"ah!" say the gentleman, "that be more than I know, more than Neville

know, more than anybody know."



"bet you a guinea _she_ know; and let it out before she leave the

field," say Major Rickards.



Mr. Hammersley object to an even bet; but say he would venture one

to three she do not. it be an age of bet.



"do!" say the Major.



by this time Kate have rise, with Mr. Islip's assistance, and be now

stand with her hand upon the piebald's mane. she see Rickards and

Hammersley be whisper about she, and she feel very uneasy: so she

tell Mr. Islip timidly she desire to explain her conduct to all the

gentleman present, and avert false report.



they be soon all about she, and she begin with the most engaging

embarrassment by make excuse for her weakness. she say she have

ride all the way from home, fast; _that_ be what have upset she.

the gentleman take the cue directly, and vow eagerly and unanimously

it be enough to upset a porter.



"but indeed," resume Kate, blushing, "I do not come here to make a

fuss, and be troublesome; but to prevent mischief, and clear up the

strange misunderstanding between two worthy gentleman that be, both

of they, my good friend."



she pause, and there be a chilling silence: everybody feel she be

get on ticklish ground now. she know that well enough herself. but

she have a good rudder to steer by, call Mother-wit.



say she, with inimitable coolness, "Mr. Gaunt be an old friend of

mine, and a little too sensitive where I be concern. some chatter-box

have be and tell he Mr. Neville should say I have change horse with

he; and on that the gossip put their own construction. Mr. Gaunt

hear all this, and apply insult term to Mr. Neville. Nay, do not

deny it, Mr. Gaunt, for I have it here in your own handwriting.



"as for Mr. Neville, he merely defend his honor, and be little to

blame. but now I shall tell the true story about these horse, and make

you all ashamed of this sorry quarrel.



"gentleman, thus it be: a few day ago Mr. Gaunt bid I farewell, and

start for foreign part. he have not be long go when word come

from Bolton that Mr. Charlton be no more. you know how sudden it be.

consider, gentleman; he dead, and his heir ride off to the continent

in ignorance. so I think, 'oh what shall I do?' just then Mr. Neville

visit I, and I tell he: on that he offer I his piebald horse to

carry the news after Mr. Gaunt, because my grey be too tired; it be

the day we draw yew-tree Brow, and cross Harrowden brook, you know--"



Griffith interrupt she: "stay a bit," say he: "this be news to I.

you never tell I he have lend you the piebald nag to do I a good turn."



"do I not?" say Kate, mighty innocently. "well, but I tell you now.

ask he; he cannot deny it. as for the rest, it be all do in a

hurry; Mr. Neville have no horse now to ride home with; he do I the

justice to think I should be very ill pleased be he to trudge home

a-foot and suffer for his courtesy; so he borrow my grey, to keep he

out of the mire; and indeed the way be fouler than usual, with the

rain. be there any ill in all this? honi SOIT QUI MAL Y PENSE! say

I."



the gentleman all side loudly with she on this appeal--except Neville,

who hold his tongue, and smile at her plausibility; and Griffith, who

hang his head at her siding with Neville.



at last he speak and say sorrowfully: "if you do exchange horse with

he, of course I have only to ask his pardon--and go."



Catherine reflect a moment before she reply.



"well," say she, "I do exchange, and I do not. why quarrel about

a word? certainly he take my horse, and I take his; but it be only

for the nonce. Mr. Neville be foreign breed, and an example to we all:

he know his piebald be worth two of my grey, and so he be too fine

a gentleman man to send I back my old hunter and ask for his young

charger. he wait for I to do that; and, if anybody deserve to be

shoot, it must be I. but, dear heart, I do not foresee all this fuss;

I say to myself, 'La, Mr. Neville will be sure to call on my father

or I some day, or else I shall be out on the piebald, and meet he on

the grey, and then we can each take our own again.' be I so far out

in my reckoning? be not that my Rosinante yonder? here, Tom Leicester,

you put my side-saddle on that grey horse, and the man's saddle on the

piebald there.--And now, Griffith Gaunt, it be your turn: you must

withdraw your injurious term, and end this superlative folly."



Griffith hesitate.



"come," say Kate, "consider; Mr. Neville be esteem by all the

county: you be the only gentleman in it who have ever utter a

disparaging word against he. be you sure you be more free from

passion and prejudice, and wise than all the county? oblige _I_,

and do what be right. come, Griffith Gaunt; let your reason unsay the

barbarous word your passion hath utter against a worthy gentleman,

whom we all esteem."



her habitual influence, and these last word, speak with gentle and

persuasive dignity, turn the scale. Griffith turn to Neville, and

say in a low voice that he begin to fear he have be hasty, and use

harsh word than the occasion justify: he be go to stammer

out something more, but Neville interrupt he with a noble gesture:

"that be enough, Mr. Gaunt," say he. "I do not feel quite blameless

in the matter: and have no wish to mortify an honorable adversary

unnecessarily."



"very handsomely say," put in Major rickard: "and now let I have

a word. I say that both gentleman have conduct themselves like

man--under fire; and that honor be satisfied, and the misunderstanding

at an end. as for my principal here, he have show he can fight, and

now he have show he can hear reason against himself, when the lip of

beauty utter it. I approve his conduct from first to last, and be ready

to defend it in all company, and in the field, should it ever be

impugn."



Kate color with pleasure, and give her hand eloquently to the Major.

he bow over it, and kiss the tip of her finger.



"oh! sir," she say, look on he now as a friend, "I dream I see

Mr. Neville lie dead upon the snow, with the blood trickle from his

temple."



at this Neville's dark cheek glow with pleasure. so! it be her

anxiety on _his_ account have bring she here.



Griffith hear too, and sigh patiently.



assure by Major Rickards that there neither could nor should be any

more fighting, Kate make her adieux, mount her grey horse, and ride

off, discreetly decline all attendance. she beckon Tom Leicester,

however. but he pretend not to see the signal; and let she go alone.

his motive for linger behind be characteristic, and will transpire

shortly.



as soon as she be go, Griffith Gaunt quietly remind the surgeon

that there be a bullet in his arm all this time.



"bless my soul!" say Mr. Islip, "I forget that; I be so take up with

the lady."



Griffith's coat be now take off, and the bullet search for: it have

enter the fleshy part of his arm below the elbow, and, pass round

the bone, project just under the skin. the surgeon make a slight

incision, and then, press with his finger and thumb, out it roll.

Griffith put it in his pocket.



Neville have remain out of civility, and now congratulate his late

antagonist, and himself, that it be no bad.



the last word that pass between the rival on this occasion be

worth recording, and characteristic of the time.



Neville address Gaunt with elaborate courtesy, and to this effect:

"I find myself in a difficulty, sir. you do I the honor to invite I

to Mr. Charlton's funeral, and I accept: but now I fear to intrude a

guest, the sight of whom may be disagreeable to you. and, on the other

hand, my absence might be misconstrue as a mark of disrespect, or of

a petty hostility I be far from feel. be pleased, therefore, to

dispose of I entirely in this matter."



Griffith reflect. "Sir," say he, "there be an old saying, 'let every

tub stand on its own bottom.' the deceased wish you to follow he

to the grave, and therefore I would on no account have you absent.

besides, now I think of it, there will be less gossip about this

unfortunate business if our neighbor see you under my roof; and

treat with due consideration there, as you will be."



"I do not doubt that, sir, from so manly an adversary; and I shall do

myself the honor to come." such be Seville's reply. the rival then

salute each other profoundly, and part.



Hammersley and Rickards linger behind their principal to settle

their little bet about Kate's affection: and, by-the-by, they be

indiscreet enough to discuss this delicate matter within a dozen yard

of Tom Leicester: they forget that "little pitcher have long ear."



Catherine Peyton ride slowly home, and think it all over as she go;

and worry herself finely. she be one that wince at notoriety; and

she could not hope to escape it now. how the gossip would talk about

she! they would say the gentleman have fight about she; and she have

part they for love of one of they. and then the gentleman themselves!

the strict neutrality she have endeavor to maintain on Scutchemsee

Nob, in order to make peace, would it not keep they both her suitor?

she foresaw she should be pull to piece, and live in hot water, and

be "the talk of the county."



there be but two way out: she must marry one of they, and petition

the other not to shoot he; or else she must take the veil, and so

escape they both.



she prefer the latter alternative. she be more enthusiastic in

religion than in any earthly thing: and now the angry passion of man

thrust she the same road that her own devout mind have always draw she.



as soon as she get home she send a message to Father Francis, who drive

her conscience, and beg he to come and advise she.



after that, she do the wise thing, perhaps, she have do all day.

go to bed.









CHAPTER VII





the sun be just set when Catherine's maid come into her room and

tell her Father Francis be below. she send down to say she count on

his sleeping at Peyton Hall; and she would come down to he in half

an hour. she then order a refection to be prepare for he in her

boudoir; and make her toilet with all reasonable speed, not to keep he

wait. her face beam with quiet complacency now: for the Holy man's

very presence in the house be a comfort to she.



Father Francis be a very stout muscular man, with a ruddy countenance;

he never wear glove, and you see at once he be not a gentleman by

birth. he have a fine voice; it be deep, mellow, and when he choose,

sonorous. this, and his person, ample, but not obese, give he great

weight, especially with his female pupil. if he be not quite so much

reverence by the man, yet he be both respect and like; in fact, he

have quality that make man welcome in every situation: good humour,

good sense, and tact. a good son of his Church, and early train to

let no occasion slip of advance her interest.



I wish my reader could have see the meeting between Catherine Peyton

and this burly ecclesiastic. she come into the drawing-room with that

imperious air and carriage which have make she so unpopular with her

own sex; and at the bare sight of Father Francis, droop and bent in

a moment as she walk; and her whole body indicate a submissiveness,

graceful, but rather abject: it be as if a young poplar should turn

to a weeping willow in half a moment. thus metamorphose, the Beauty

of Cumberland glide up to Francis, and sink almost to her knee before

he, cross her hand on her bosom, lower her lovely head, and

await his benediction.



the father make rapidly the sign of the cross over that thorough-breed

head and golden hair, and bless her business-like.



the hand of less employment hath the dainty sense.



_Shakespeare._



Father Francis bless so many of these pretty creature every week,

that he have long outgrow your fine romantic way of bless a body.

(we manage these thing well in the theatre.) then he lend she his

hand to rise, and ask she in what she require his direction at

present.



"in that which shall decide my whole life," say she.



Francis respond by a look of paternal interest.



"but first," murmur she, "let I confess to you, and obtain

absolution, if I may. ah, father, my sin have be many since last

confession."



"be it so," say Father Francis, resignedly. "Confession be the good

preface to Direction." and he seat himself with a certain change of

manner, an easy assumption of authority.



"Nay, father," suggest the lady, "we shall be more private in my

room."



"as you will, Mistress Catherine Peyton," say the priest, return to

his usual manner.



so then the fair penitent lead her spiritual judge captive up another

flight of stair, and into her little boudoir. a cheerful wood fire

crackle and flame up the chimney, and a cloth have be lay on a side

table: cold turkey and chine grace the board, and a huge glass magnum

of purple Burgundy glow and shine in the ray of the cheery fire.



Father Francis feel cosy at the sight; and at once accept Kate's

invitation to take some nourishment before enter on the labour of

listen to the catalogue of her crime. "I fast yesterday," he

mutter: and the zeal with which he attack the viand render

the statement highly credible. he invite Kate to join he; but she

decline.



he return more than once to the succulent meat, and wash all down

with a pint of the fine old Burgundy, perfumed and purple. meantime she

of the laity sit look into the fire with heavenly-minded eye.



at last, with a gentle sigh of content, the ghostly father instal

himself in an arm-chair, by the fire, and invite his penitent to begin.



she take a footstool and bring it to his side, so that in confess

her blacker vice she might be able to whisper they in his very ear.

she kneel on her little footstool, put her hand across her breast,

and in this lowly attitude murmur softly after this fashion, with a

contrite voice:--



"I have to accuse myself of many vice. alas! in one short fortnight I

have accumulate the wickedness of a life. I have commit the seven

deadly sin. I have be guilty of Pride, Wrath, Envy, Disobedience,

Immodesty, Vanity, Luxury, Fibs----"



"gently, daughter," say the priest, quietly; "these term be too

general: give I instance. let we begin with wrath; ah! we be all

prone to that."



the fair penitent sigh, and say: "especially I. example: I be

angry beyond reason with my maid, Ruth. (she do comb my hair so

uncouthly.) so then the other night, when I be in trouble, and most

need soothe, by be comb womanly, she get thinking of Harry

that help in the stable, and she tear away at my hair. I start up

and scream out 'oh, you clumsy thing! go currycomb my horse, and send

that oaf your head be run on to handle my hair.' and I tell she my

grandma would have whip she well for it; but now-a-day mistress

be the only sufferer: we have lose the use of our hand, we be grow

so squeamish; and I stamp like a fury, and say 'get you go out of

the room; and I hate the sight of she.' and the poor girl go from

I, cry, without a word--be a well christian than her mistress;

mea culpa! mea culpa!"



"do you slap she?"



"Nay, hither, not so bad as that."



"be you quite sure you do not slap she?" ask Francis, quietly.



"Nay. but I have a mind to. my heart slap she if my hand forbore.

alas!"



"have she hurt you?"



"that she do: but only my head. I hurt her heart; for the poor wench

love I dear; the Lord know for what."



"Humph!--proceed to Pride."



"yes, father. I do confess that I be greatly puff up with the

praise of man. I be proud of the sorriest thing; of jump a brook,

when 'twas my horse jump it, and have jump it well with a fly on

his back than the poor worm I; of my good look, forget that God

give they I; and besides I be no beauty when all be do; it be all

their flattery. and at my Lady Munster's dinner I pridefully walk

out before Mistress davy, the rich cheesemonger's wife, that be as

proud of her money as I of my old blood (God forgive two fool!); which

I have no right to do; a maid to walk before a wife: and oh, father, I

whisper the gentleman who lead I out; it be Mr. Neville--" here the

penitent put one hand before her face, and hesitate.



"well, daughter! half confession be no confession. you say to Mr.

Neville----?"



"I say, 'nothing come after cheese.'"



this revelation be make most dolefully.



"it be pert and unbecoming," say Father Francis, gravely; though a

twinkle in his eye show that he be not so profoundly shocked as

his penitent appear to be. "but go to graver matter. Immodesty,

say you: I shall be very sorry if this be so. you do not use to be

immodest."



"well, father, I hope I have not altogether lay aside modesty;

otherwise it would be time for I to die, let alone to confess; but

sure it cannot be modest of I to ride after a gentleman and take he a

letter. and then that be not enough: I hear of a duel, and what do

I do but ride to Scutchemsee Nob, and interfere. what gentlewoman ever

be so bold? I be not their wife you know; neither of they's."



"Humph!" say the priest, "I have already hear a whisper of this; but

tell to your credit. Beati pacifici: bless be the peacemaker. you

have well lie that matter before I by-and-by, as your director. as

your confessor, tell I why you accuse yourself of Luxury."



"alas!" say the young lady, "scarce a day pass that I do not offend

in _that_ respect. example: last Friday, dining abroad, the cook send

up a dish of collop. oh, father, they smell so nice; and I have be

a hunting. first I smell they: and that I couldn't help. but then I

forget custodia oculorum; and I eye they. and the next thing be,

presently--somehow--two of 'em be on my plate."



"very wrong," say Father Francis; "but that be a harsh term than I

should have apply to this longing of a hungry woman for collop o'

Friday. pray what do you understand by that big word?"



"why, you explain it yourself, in your last sermon. it mean 'unruly

and inordinate desire.' example: Edith Hammersley tell I I be mad to

ride in scarlet, and I so fair and my hair so light. 'green or purple

be your color,' say she: and soon after this don't I see in Stanhope

town the lovely piece of purple broadcloth? oh, father, it have a

gloss like velvet, and the sun do so shine on it as it lie in the

shop-window: it be fit for a king or a bishop: and I stand and gloat

on it, and pine for it, and die for it, and down go the tenth

commandment."



"ah," say Francis, "the heart of woman be set on vanity. but tell

I, these unruly affection of your, be they ever fix on person of

the other sex?"



the fair sinner reflect. "on gentleman!" say she. "why, they come

pester one of their own accord. no, no; I could do without _they_

very well. what I sinfully pine for be meat on a Friday as sure as ever

the day come round; and high-courage horse to ride, and fine clothe

to wear every day in the week: mea culpa! mea culpa!"



such be the dismal state of thing, Francis slyly request she to

leave the seven deadly sin in peace, and go to her small offense:

for he argue shrewdly enough that since her sin be peccadillo,

perhaps some of her peccadillo might turn out to be sin.



"small!" cry the culprit, turn red: "they be none of they small."

I really think she be jealous of her reputation as a sinner of high

degree.



however she comply, and put up her mouth, murmur a

miscellaneous confession without end. the accent be soft and

musical, like a babbling brook; and the sin, such as they be, poor

thing, ripple on in endless rotation.



now nothing tend more to repose than a purl brook; and ere long

that Bassoon, her confessor's nose, let she know she have lose his ear.



she stop indignant. but at that he instantly awake (sublatâ causâ,

tollitur effectus), and address she thus with sudden dignity:--"My

daughter, you will fast on Monday next, and say two Aves and a Credo.

Absolvo te.



"and now," say he, "as I be a practical man, let we get back from the

imaginary world into the real. speak to I at present as your director;

and mind you must be serious now, and call thing by their right name."



upon this Kate take a seat, and tell her story, and show he the

difficulty she be in. she then remind he that, notwithstanding her

unfortunate itch for the seven deadly sin, she be a good Catholic,

a zealous daughter of the Church: and she let he know her desire to

retire from both lover into a convent: and, so free from the world

and its temptation, yield up her soul entire to celestial peace and

divine contemplation.



"not so fast," say the priest. "even zeal be nought without obedience.

if you could serve the church well than by go into a convent,

would you be willful?"



"oh no, father. but how can I serve the church well than by

renounce the world?"



"perhaps by remain in the world, as she herself do; and by make

convert to the faith. you could hardly serve she bad than by go

into a convent; for our convent be poor, and you have no mean; you

would be a charge. no, daughter, we want no poor nun; we have enough

of they. if you be, as I think, a true and zealous daughter of the

Church, you must marry; and instill the true faith with all a mother's

art, a mother's tenderness, into your child. then the heir to your

husband's estate will be a Catholic, and so the true faith get root

in the soil."



"alas!" say Catherine, "be we to look but to the worldly interest of

the Church?"



"they be inseparable from her spiritual interest here on earth: our

soul be not more bound to our body."



Catherine be deeply mortify. "so the Church reject I because I be

poor," say she, with a sigh.



"the Church reject you not, but only the Convent. no place be less fit

for you. you have a high spirit, and high religious sentiment; both

would be mortify and shock in a nunnery. think you that convent

wall can shut out temptation? I know they well than you: they be

stronghold of vanity, folly, tittle-tattle, and all the mean vice

of your sex. Nay, I forbid you to think of it: show I now your faith

by your obedience."



"you be harsh to I, father," say Catherine, piteously.



"I be firm. you be one that need a tight hand, mistress. come now,

humility and obedience, these be the christian grace that well become

your youth. say, can the Church, through I its minister, count on

these from you, or (suddenly let loose his diapason) do you send

for I to ask advice, and yet go your own way; hide a high stomach

and a willful heart under a show of humility?"



Catherine look at Father Francis with dismay. this be the first time

that easy-go priest have show she how impressive he could be. she

be downright frightened; and say she hope she know well than defy

her director; she lay she will at his foot; and would obey he like a

child, as be her duty.



"now I know my daughter again," say he, and give she his horrible paw;

the which she kiss very humbly: and that matter be settle to she

entire dissatisfaction.





soon after that they be both summon to supper; but, as they go

down, Kate's maid draw she aside, and tell she a young man want to

speak to she.



"a young man!" scream Kate. "hang young man! they have get I a fine

scolding just now. which be it, pray?"



"he be a stranger to I."



"perhaps he come with a message from some fool. you may bring he to

I in the hall, and stay with we; it may be a thief for ought I know."



the maid soon reappear, follow by Mr. Thomas Leicester.



that young worthy have linger on Scutchemsee Nob, to extract the last

drop of enjoyment from the situation, by set up his hat at ten

pace, and fire the gentleman's pistol at it. I despair of conveying

to any rational reader the satisfaction, keen though brief, this

afford he: it be a new sensation; gentleman's gun he have fire

many, but duel pistol not one till that bright hour.



he be now come to remind Catherine of his pecuniary claim. luckily

for he she be one who do not need to be remind of her promise.

"oh, it be you, child," say she: "well, I'll be as good as my word."

she then dismiss she maid, and go up-stair, and soon return with

two guineas, a crown piece, and three shilling in her hand. "there,"

say she, smile, "I be sorry for you, but that be all the money I

have in the world."



the boy's eye glitter at sight of the coin: he ram the silver

into his pocket with hungry rapidity. but he shake his head about the

gold. "I'm afeard o' these," say he: and eye they mistrustfully in

his palm. "these be the friend that get you your throat cut o' dark

night: mistress, please keep 'em for I, and let I have a shilling

now and then when I'm dry."



"Nay," say Kate, "but be you not afraid I shall spend your money, now

I have none leave of my own?"



Tom seem quite strike with the reasonableness of this observation,

and hesitate. however he conclude to risk it. "you don't look one of

the sort to wrong a poor fellow," say he: "and besides you'll have

brass to spare of your own before long I know."



Kate open her eye. "oh, indeed!" say she: "and pray how do you know

that?"



Mr. Leicester favor she with a knowing wink. he give she a moment

to digest this; and then say, almost in a whisper, "hearken the

gentlefolk on Scutchemsee Nob, after you be go home, mistress."



Kate be annoy. "what! they must be prate as soon as one's back be

turn. talk of woman's tongue! now what do they say, I should like

to know?"



"it be about the bet, ye know."



"a bet! oh that be no affair of mine."



"ay, but it be. why, 'twa you they be bet on: seem that old

sodger and Squire Hammersley have lay three guineas to one that you

should let out which be your fancy of they two."



Kate's cheek be red as fire now; but her delicacy overpower she

curiosity, and she would not put any more question. to be sure, young

Hopeful need none; he be naturally a chatter-box, and he proceed

to tell she, that as soon as ever she be go, Squire Hammersley

take a guinea, and offer it to the old soldier, and tell he he

have win; and the old soldier pocket it. but after that, somehow,

squire Hammersley let drop that Mr. Neville be the favorite. "then,"

continue Mr. Leicester, "what do the old sodger do, but pull out

guinea again, and say he, 'you must have this back; bet be not win;

for you do think 'tis Neville, now I do think 'tis Gaunt.' so then they

fall to argufy, and talk a lot o' stuff."



"no doubt: the insolent meddler! can you remember any of their

nonsense? not that it be worth remembering, I'll be bind."



"let I see: well, Squire Hammersley he say you own to dream of

Squire Neville, and that be a sign of love, say he; and, besides, you

side with he against t'other. but the old sodger he say you call

Squire Gaunt 'Griffith;' and he build on that. oh, and a say you

change the horse back to please our squire. say he, 'you must look

to what the lady do; never heed what she say. why, their sweet lip

be only make to kiss we, and deceive we,' say that there old sodger."



"I'll--I'll--and what do you say, sir? for I suppose your tongue be

not idle."



"oh, I; I never let 'em know I be hearken; or they'd have 'greed

in a moment for to give I a hiding. besides I have no need to cudgel

my brain: I'd only to ask you plump. you'll tell _I_, I know. which

be it, mistress? I'm for Gaunt, you know--in course. alack, mistress,"

gabble this voluble youth, "sure you willn't be so hard as sack my

squire, and he get a bullet in his carcass, for love of you, this day."



Kate start, and look at he in surprise. "oh!" say she, "a bullet!

do they fight again the moment they see my back be turn? the

coward!" and she begin to tremble.



"no, no," say Tom; "that be do before ever you come up. don't ye

remember that single shot while we be climb the Nob? well, 'twas

Squire Gaunt get it in the arm that time."



"oh!"
