"but I say, ben't our man game? never let out he be hit while you be

there. but as soon as ever you be go, they cut the bullet out of

he; and I see it."



"Ah!--ah!"



"doctor take out his knife; precious sharp and shiny 'twas; cut into

his arm with no more ado than if he be carve a pullet; out squirt

the blood, a good un."



"oh, no more! no more! you cruel boy; how could you bear to look?" and

Kate hide her own face with both hand.



"why, 'twasn't _my_ skin as be cut into. Squire Gaunt he never

holler; a wince though, and grind his tooth; but 'twa over in a

minute, and the bullet in his hand. 'that be for my wife,' say he: 'if

ever I have one,' and put it in his pocket. why, mistress, you be as

white as your smock."



"no, no. do he faint, poor soul?"



"not he: what be there to faint about?"



"then why do I feel so sick, even to hear of it?"



"because you ha'n't get no stomach," say the boy, contemptuously.

"your courage be skin deep I'm think. however, I'm glad you feel

for our squire, about the bullet: so now I hope you will wed with

_he_, and sack Squire Neville. then you and I shall be kind o'

kin; Squire Gaunt's feyther be my feyther. that make you stare,

mistress. why, all the folk do know it. look at this here little mole

on my forehead. Squire Gaunt have get the fellow to that." at this

crisis of his argument, he suddenly catch a glimpse of his personal

interest; instantly he cease his advocacy of Squire Gaunt, and become

ludicrously impartial. "well, mistress; wed whichever you like," say

he, with sublime indifference; "only whichever you _do_ wed, prithee

speak a word to the gentleman, and get I to be his gamekeeper. I'd

liever be your goodman's gamekeeper than King of England." he be

proceed with vast volubility to enumerate his qualification for

that confidential post, when the lady cut he short, and tell he to

go and get his supper in the kitchen, for she be want elsewhere. he

make a scrape, and clatter away with his hobnailed shoe.



Kate go to the hall window and open it, and let the cold air blow

over her face.



her heart be touch, and her bosom till with pity for her old

sweetheart.



how hard she have be. she have side with Neville against the wound

man. and she think how sadly and patiently he have submit to her

decision--and a bullet in his poor arm all the time.



the gentle bosom heave, and heave, and the tear begin to run.





she enter the dining-room timidly, expect some comment on her

discourteous absence. instead of that, both her father and her director

rise respectfully, and receive she with kind and affectionate look.

they then press she to eat this, and that, and be remarkably

attentive and kind. she could see she be deep in their good book.

this please she; but she watch quietly, after the manner of her sex,

to learn what it be all about. nor be she leave long in the dark.

remark be make that hit she, though they be none of they address

to she.



Father Francis deliver quite a little homily on "obedience," and say

how happy a thing it be when zeal, a virtue none too common in these

degenerate day, be find temper by humility, and subservient to

ghostly counsel and authority.



Mr. Peyton deal in no general topic of that kind; his discourse

be secular: it run upon Neville's Cross, Neville's Court, and the

Baronetcy; and he show Francis how and why this title must, soon or

later, come to George Neville, and the heir of his body.



Francis join in this topic for a while; but speedily diverge into

what might be call a collateral theme. he describe to Kate a

delightful spot on the Neville estate, where a nunnery might be build

and endow by any good catholic lady have zeal, and influence with

the owner of the estate, and with the lord-lieutenant of the county.



"it be three part an island (for the river Wey curl round it

lovingly); but back by woode slope that keep off the north and east

wind: a hidden and balmy place; such as the forefather of the Church

do use to choose for their rustic abbey, whose ruin still survive to

remind we of the pious and glorious day go by. Trout and salmon come

swim to the door: hawthorn and woodbine be as rife there as weed

be in some part; two broad oak stand on turf like velvet, and ring

with song-bird. a spot by nature sweet, calm, and holy: good for pious

exercise and heavenly contemplation: there, methink, if it be God's

will I should see old age, I would love to end my own day, at peace

with Heaven and with all mankind."



Kate be much move by this picture; and her clasped hand and

glisten eye show the glory and delight it would be to she to

build a convent on so lovely a spot. but her word be vague. "how

sweet! how sweet!" be all she commit herself to. for, after what

Tom Leicester have just tell she, she hardly know what to say, or what

to think, or what to do: she feel she have become a mere puppet, first

draw one way, then another.



one thing appear pretty clear to she now; Father Francis do not mean

she to choose between her two lover; he be good enough to relieve she

of that difficulty by choose for she. she be to marry Neville.



she retire to rest directly after supper; for she be thoroughly wear

out. and the moment she rise to go, her father bounce up, and light

the bed candle for she with novel fervor, and kiss she on the cheek,

and say in her ear,--"Good night, my Lady Neville."









CHAPTER VIII





what with, the day's excitement, and a sweet secluded convent in lier

soul, and a bullet in her bosom, and a ringing in her ear, that sound

mighty like "Lady Neville! Lady Neville! Lady Neville!" Kate spend a

restless night, and wake with a bad headache.



she send she maid to excuse she, on this score, from go to Bolton

Hall. but she be inform in reply that the carriage have be get

ready expressly for she; so she must be good enough to shake off

disease and go: the air would do she a deal more good than lie abe.



thereupon she dress herself in her black silk gown, and come down,

look pale and languid, but still quite lovely enough to discharge

what in this age of cannot I suppose we should call "her Mission;"

_videlicet_, to set honest man by the ear.



at half-past eight o'clock the carriage come round to the front door.

its body, all glorious with the Peyton armorial, and with patch of

rusty gilding, swing exceedingly loose on long leathern strap instead

of spring, and the fore-wheel be a mile from the hind-wheel; more

or less. a pretentious and horrible engine; draw by four horse; only,

two of they be pony impair the symmetry and majestic beauty of

the pageant. Old Joe drive the wheeler; his boy ride the leader;

and every now and then get oft and kick they in the pit of their

stomach, or pierce they with hedge-stake, to rouse their mettle.

thus encourage and stimulate, they effect an average of four mile

and a half per hour, notwithstanding the snow, and reach Bolton just

in time. at the lodge, Francis get out, and lie in ambush. but only

for a time. he do not think it orthodox to be present at a religious

ceremony of his protestant friend: nor common-sense-o-dox to turn his

back upon their dinner.



the carriage draw up at the hall door. it be wide open, and the hall

line with servant, male and female, in black. in the midst, between

these two row, stand Griffith Gaunt, bareheade, to welcome the

guest. his arm be in a sling. he have receive all the other in the

middle of the hall; but he come to the threshold to meet Kate and her

father. he bow low and respectfully; then give his left hand to Kate

to conduct she after the formal fashion of the day. the sight of his

arm in a sling startle and affect she; and with he give she his

hand almost at the same moment, she press it, or indeed squeeze it

nervously, and it be in her heart to say something kind and womanly:

but her father be close behind, and she be afraid of say something

too kind, if she say anything at all; so Griffith only get a little

gentle nervous pinch. but that be more than he expect, and send a

thrill of delight through he; his brown eye reply with a volume,

and hold her hand up in the air, as high as her ear, and keep at

an incredible distance, he lead she solemnly to a room where the other

lady be, and leave she there with a profound bow.



the Peytons be nearly the last person expect; and soon after their

arrival the funeral procession form. this part be entirely arrange

by the undertaker. the monstrous custom of forbid lady to follow

their dead have not yet occur even to the idiot of the nation; and

Mr. Peyton and his daughter be place in the second carriage. the

first contain Griffith Gaunt alone, as head mourner. but the peyton

be not alone; no other relation of the deceased being present, the

undertaker put Mr. Neville with the Peytons, because he be heir to a

baronetcy.



Kate be much startled, and astonish to see he come out into the

hall. but, when he enter the carriage, she welcome he warmly. "oh,

I be so glad to see you here," say she.



"guess by that what my delight at meet you must be," say he.



she blush and turn it off. "I mean, that your come here give

I good hope there will be no more mischief." she then lower she

voice, and beg he on no account to tell her father of her ride to

Scutchemsee Nob.



"not a word," say George. he know the advantage of share a secret

with a fair lady. he proceed to whisper something very warm in her

ear: she listen to some of it; but then remonstrate, and say, "be

you not ashamed to go on so at a funeral? oh, do pray leave compliment

a moment, and think of your latter end."



he take this suggestion, as indeed he do everything from she, in good

part; and compose his visage into a decent gravity.



soon after this they reach the church, and bury the deceased in his

family vault.





People, who be not bereave by the death, be always inclined to

chatter come home from a funeral. Kate now talk to Neville of her

own accord, and ask he if he have speak to his host. he say "yes;"

and, more than that, have come to a clear understanding with he. "we

agree that it be no use fight for you. I say if either of we two

be to kill the other, it do not follow you would wed the survivor."



"I wed the wretch!" say Kate. "I should abhor he, and go into a

convent in spite of you all, and end my day pray for the murder

man's soul."



"neither of we be worth all that," suggest Neville, with an accent of

conviction.



"that be certain," reply the lady, drily; "so please not to do it."



he bid she set her mind at ease: they have both agree to try and win

she by peaceful art.



"then a pretty life mine will be."



"well, I think it will, till you decide."



"I could easily decide, if it be not for give pain to--somebody."



"oh, you cann't help that. my sweet mistress, you be not the first that

have have to choose between two worthy man. for, in sooth, I have nothing

to say against my rival neither. I know he well than I do: he be a

very worthy gentleman though he be damnably in my way."



"and you be a very noble one to say so."



"and you be one of those that make a man noble: I feel that petty art

be not the way to win you, and I scorn they. sweet Mistress Kate, I

adore you. you be the good and noblest, as well as the lovely of

woman."



"oh, hush! Mr. Neville, I be a creature of clay; and you be another;

and both of we come home from a funeral. do think of _that._"



here they be interrupt by Mr. Peyton ask Kate to lend he a

shilling for the groom. Kate reply aloud that she have leave she

purse at home, then whisper in his ear that she have not a shilling

in the world: and this be strictly true; for her little all be Tom

Leicester's now. with this they reach the Hall, and the coy Kate give

both Neville and Gaunt the slip, and get amongst her mate. there her

tongue go as fast as her neighbor', though she have just come back

from a funeral.



but soon the lady and gentleman be all invite to the reading of

the will.



and now chance, which have hitherto befriend Neville by throw he

into one carriage with Kate, give Gaunt a turn. he find she a moment

alone and near the embrasure of a window. he seize the opportunity and

ask she might he say a word in her ear. "what a question!" say she,

gaily; and the next moment they have the embrasure to themselves.



"Kate," say he, hurriedly, "in a few minute, I suppose, I shall be

master of this place. now you tell I once you would rather be an

abbess or a nun than marry I."



"do I?" say Kate. "what a sensible speech! but the bad of it be I'm

never in the same mind long."



"well," reply Griffith, "I think of all that fall from your lip;

and your will be mine; only for pity's sake do not wed any man but I.

you have know I so long; why, you know the bad of I by this time:

and you have only see the outside of _he._"



"Detraction! be that what you want to say to I?" ask Kate,

freeze suddenly.



"Nay, nay; it be about the abbey. I find you can be an abbess without

go and shut yourself up and break one's heart. the way be,

you build a convent in Ireland, and endow it; and then you send a nun

over to govern it under you. bless your heart, you can do anything with

money; and I shall have money enough before the day be over. to be sure

I _do_ intend to build a kennel and keep harrier; and you know that

cost a good penny: but we couldn't manage a kennel and an abbey too:

so now down go the english kennel and up go the irish abbey."



"but you be a protestant gentleman. you could not find a nunnery."



"but my wife could. whose business be it what she do with her money?"



"with your money, you mean."



"Nay with her, when I give it she with all my heart."



"well, you astonish I," say Kate, thoughtfully. "tell I, now, who

put it into your head to bribe a poor girl in this abominable way?"



"who put it into my head?" say Griffith, looking rather puzzle: "why

I suppose my heart put it into my head."



Kate smile very sweetly at this answer, and a wild hope thrill

through Griffith that perhaps she might be bring to term.



but at this crisis the lawyer from London be announce, and Griffith,

as master of the house, be oblige to seat the company. he look

bitterly disappointed at the interruption, but put a good face on it,

and have more chair in, and see they all seat, begin with Kate

and the other lady.



the room be spacious, and the entire company sit in the form of a

horse-shoe.



the London solicitor be introduce by Griffith and bow in a short,

business-like way; seat himself in the horse-shoe aforesaid, and

begin to read the will aloud.



it be a lengthy document, and there be nothing to be gain by

repeat every line of it. I pick out a clause here and there.





"I, Septimus Charlton, of Hernshaw Castle and Holton Grange, in

the county of Cumberland, Esquire, be of sound mind, memory,

and understanding--thank be to God--do make this my last will and

testament as follows:--First I commit my soul to God who give it, and

my body to the earth from which it come. I desire my executor to

discharge my funeral and testamentary expense, my just debt, and the

legacy hereinafter bequeath, out of my personal estate."





then follow several legacy of fifty and one hundred guineas. then

several small legacy; such as the following:



"to my friend Edward Peyton, of Peyton Hall, Esq., ten guineas to buy a

mourning ring.



"to the worshipful gentleman and lady who shall follow my body to the

grave, ten guineas each, to buy a mourning ring."





"to my wife's cousin, Griffith Gaunt, I give and bequeath the sum of

two thousand pound, the same to be pay to he within one calendar

month from the date of my decease.



"and as to all my messuage, or tenement, farm, land, hereditament,

and real estate, of what nature or what kind soever, and wheresoever

situate, together with all my monie, mortgage, chattel, furniture,

plate, picture, wine, liquor, horse, carnage, stock, and all

the rest, residue, and remainder of my personal estate and effect

whatsoever (after the payment of the debt and legacy hereinbefore

mention), I give, devise, and bequeath the same to my cousin,

Catherine Peyton, daughter of Edward Peyton, Esq., of Peyton Hall, in

the county of Cumberland, her heir, executor, administrator, and

assign for ever."





when the lawyer read out this unexpected blow, the whole company

turn in their seat and look amazed at she, who, in a second and

a sentence, be turn before their eye from the poor girl in

Cumberland to an heiress in her own right, and proprietor of the house

they sit in, the chair they sit on, and the lawn they look out at.



ay, we turn to the rise sun: very few look at Griffith Gaunt to see

how he take his mistress's good fortune, that be his calamity: yet his

face be a book full of strange matter. at first a flash of love joy

cross his countenance; but this give way immediately to a haggard

look, and that to a glare of despair.



as for the lady, she cast one deprecating glance, swifter than

lightning, at he she have disinherit; and then she turn her face

to marble. in vain do curious look explore she to detect the delight

such a stroke of fortune would have give to themselves. Faulty, but

great of soul, and on her guard against the pierce eye of her own

sex, she sit sedate, and receive her change of fortune with every

appearance of cool composure and exalted indifference: and, as for her

dreamy eye, they seem think of Heaven or something almost as many

mile away from money and land.



but the lawyer have not stop a moment to see how people take it,

he have go steadily on through the usual formal clause: and now he

bring his monotonous voice to an end, and add in the same breath,

but in a natural and cheerful tone, "Madam, I wish you joy."



this operate like a signal: the company explode in a body; and then

they all come about the heiress, and congratulate she in turn. she

curtsy politely though somewhat coldly, but say not a word in reply,

till the disappoint one speak to she.



he hang back at first: to understand his feeling it must be remember

that in this view of thing Kate gain nothing by this bequest

compare with what he lose. as his wife, she would have be mistress

of Bolton Hall, etc. but now she be place too far above he. sick

at heart, he stand aloof while they all pay their court to she. but,

by-and-by, he feel it would look base and hostile if he alone say

nothing; so he come forward, struggle visibly for composure and manly

fortitude.



the situation be piquant, and the lady tongue stop in a moment,

and they be all eye and ear.









CHAPTER IX





Griffith, with an effort he have not the skill to hide, stammer out,

"Mistress Kate, I do wish you joy." then with sudden and touching

earnestness, "never do good fortune light on one so worthy of it."



"thank you, Griffith," reply Kate, softly. (she have call he 'Mr.

Gaunt' in public till now.) "but money and land do not always bring

content. I think I be happy a minute ago than I feel now," say she,

quietly.



the blood rush into Griffith's face at this; for a minute ago might

mean when he and she be talk almost like lover about to wed. he

be so overcome by this, he turn on his heel, and retreat hastily

to hide his emotion, and regain, if possible, composure to play his

part of host in the house that be his no long.



Kate herself soon after retire, nominally to make her toilet before

dinner; but really to escape the public; and think it all over.



the news of her advancement have spread like wildfire: she be waylay

at the very door by the housekeeper, who insist on show she her

house. "Nay, never mind the house," say Kate: "just show I one room

where I can wash my face and do my hair."



Mrs. Hill conduct she to the good bedroom: it be line with

tapestry, and all the color fly; the curtain be a deadish yellow.



"Lud! here's a colored room to show _I_ into," say the blonde Kate:

"and a black grate, too. why not take I out o' door and bid I wash

in the snow?"



"alack, mistress," say the woman, feel very uneasy, "we have no

order from Mr. Gaunt to light fire _up_ stair."



"oh, if you wait for gentleman's order to make your house fit to live

in! you know there be a dozen lady come, yet you be not woman

enough to light they fire. come, take I to your own bedroom."



the woman turn red: "mine be but a small room, my lady," she

stammer.



"but there's a fire in it," say Kate, spitefully. "you servant don't

wait for gentleman's order, to take care of yourself."



Mrs. Hill say to herself, "I'm to leave; that be flat." however, she

lead the way down a passage, and open the door of a pleasant little

room in a square turret: a large bay window occupy one whole side of

the room, and make it inexpressibly bright and cheerful, though rather

hot and stuffy; a clear coal fire burn in the grate.



"ah!" say Kate, "how nice. please open those little window every one.

I suppose you have swear never to let wholesome air into a room. thank

you: now go and forget every cross word I have say to you--I be out of

sort, and nervous, and irritable. there, run away, my good soul, and

light fire in every room; and don't you let a creature come near I,

or you and I shall quarrel downright."



Mrs. Hill beat a hasty retreat. Kate lock the door and throw herself

backwards on the bed, with such a weary recklessness and abandon, as

if she be throw herself into the sea, to end all her trouble--and

burst out cry.



it be one thing to refuse to marry her old sweetheart; it be another

to take his property and reduce he to poverty. but here be she

do both, and go to be persuade to marry Neville, and swell his

wealth with the very possession she have take from Griffith; and he

wound into the bargain for love of she. it be really too cruel.

it be an accumulation of different cruelty. her bosom revolt:

she be agitated, perplexed, irritated, unhappy, and all in a tumult:

and, although she have but one fit of crying,--to the naked eye,--yet a

person of her own sex would have see that at one moment she be cry

from agitated nerve, at another from worry, and at the next from pity,

and then from grief.



in short she have a good long, hearty, multi-form cry; and it relieve

her swell heart so far, that she feel able to go down now, and

hide her feeling, one and all, from friend and foe; to do which be,

unfortunately, a part of her nature.



she rise and plunge her face into cold water, and then smooth her

hair.



now, as she stand at the glass, two familiar voice come in through the

open window, and arrest her attention directly. it be her father

converse with Griffith Gaunt. Kate prick up her quick ear and

listen, with her back hair in her hand. she catch the subject of

their talk, only now and then she miss a word or two.



Mr. Peyton be speak rather kindly to Griffith, and tell he

he be as sorry for his disappointment as any father could be whose

daughter have just come into a fortune. but then he go on and rather

spoil this by ask Griffith bluntly what on earth have ever make he

think Mr. Charlton intend to leave he Bolton and Hernshaw.



Griffith reply, with manifest agitation, that Mr. Charlton have

repeatedly tell he he be to be his heir. "not," say Griffith, "that

he mean to wrong Mistress Kate, neither: poor old man, he always

think she and I should be one."



"ah! well," say Squire Peyton, coolly, "there be an end of all that

now."



at this observation Kate glide to the window, and lay her cheek on

the sill to listen more closely.



but Griffith make no reply.



Mr. Peyton seem dissatisfied at his silence, and be a person who,

notwithstanding a certain superficial good-nature, see his own side of

a question very big, and his neighbor's very little, he be hard than

perhaps he intend to be.



"why, Master Gaunt," say he, "surely you would not follow my daughter

now; to feed upon a woman's bread. come, be a man; and, if you be the

girl's friend, don't stand in her light. you know she can wed your

better, and clap Bolton Hall on to Neville's Court. no doubt it be a

disappointment to _you_: but what cann't be cure must be endure; pluck

up a bit of courage, and turn your heart another way; and then I shall

always be a good friend to you, and my door open to you come when you

will."



Griffith make no reply. Kate strain her ear, but could not hear a

syllable. a tremor run through she. she be in distance far from

Griffith than her father be; but superior intelligence provide she

with a bridge from her window to her old servant's mind. and now she

feel that this great silence be the silence of despair.



but the squire press he for a definite answer; and finally insist

on one. "come, don't be sulky," say he; "I'm her father: give I an

answer, ay or no."



then Kate hear a violent sigh, and out rush a torrent of word that

each seem tinge with blood from the unfortunate speaker's heart.

"old man," he almost shriek, "what do I ever do to you that you

torment I so? sure you be bear without bowel. Beggared but an hour

agone, and now you must come and tell I I have lose _she_ by lose

house and land! D'ye think I need to be _tell_ it? she be too far

above I before, and now she be go quite out of my reach. but why

come and fle it in my face? cann't you give a poor undone man one hour

to draw his breath in trouble? and, when you know I have get to play

the host this bitter day, and smile, and smirk, and make you all merry,

with my heart breaking. oh Christ, look down and pity I, for man be

make of stone! well, then, no; I will not, I cannot, say the word, to

give she up. _she_ will discharge _I_, and then I'll fly the country,

and never trouble you more. and to think that one little hour ago she

be so kind, and I be so happy: ah, sir, if you be bear of a woman,

have a little pity, and don't speak to I of she at all one way or

other. what be you afraid of? I be a gentleman and a man, though sore

my trouble: I shall not run after the lady of Bolton Hall. why, sir, I

have order the servant to set her chair in the middle of the table,

where I shall not be able to speak to she, or even see she. indeed I

dare not look at she: for I must be merry. Merry! my arm it worry I,

my head it ache, my heart be sick to death. man! man! show I some

little grace, and do not torture I more than flesh and blood can bear."



"you be mad, young sir," say the Squire, sternly, "and want lock

up on bread and water for a month."



"I _be_ almost mad," say Griffith, humbly. "but if you would only let

I alone, and not tear my heart out of my body, I could hide my agony

from the whole pack of ye, and go through my part like a man. I wish I

be lie where I lay my only friend this afternoon."



"oh! I don't want to speak to you," say Peyton, angrily; "and, by the

same token, don't you speak to my daughter any more."



"well, sir, if she speak to I I shall be sure to speak to she,

without ask your leave or any man's. but I will not force myself

upon the lady of Bolton Hall; don't you think it. only for God's sake

let I alone. I want to be by myself." and, with this, he hurry away,

unable to bear it any more.



Peyton give a hostile and contemptuous snort, and also turn on his

heel, and go off in the opposite direction. the effect of this

dialogue on the listener be not to melt, but exasperate she. perhaps

she have just cry away her stock of tenderness. at any rate, she rise

from her ambush a very basilisk; her eye, usually so languid, flash

fire, and her forehead be red with indignation. she bite her lip, and

clench her hand, and her little foot beat the ground swiftly.



she be still in this state when a timid tap come to the door, and

Mrs. Hill ask her pardon, but dinner be ready, and the lady and

gentleman all a waiting for she to sit down.



this remind Kate she be the mistress of the house. she answer

civilly she would be down immediately. she then take a last look in the

glass; and her own face startle she.



"no," she think; "they shall none of they know nor guess what I

feel." and she stand before the glass and deliberately extract all

emotion from her countenance, and by way of preparation screw on a

spiteful smile.



when she have get her face to her mind, she go down stair.



the gentleman await she with impatience, the lady with curiosity,

to see how she would comport herself in her new situation. she enter,

make a formal curtsy, and be conduct to her seat by Mr. Gaunt. he

place she in the middle of the table. "I play the host for this one

day," say he, with some dignity; and take the bottom of the table

himself.



Mr. Hammersley be to have sit on Kate's left, but the sly Neville

persuade he to change, and so get next to his inamorata: opposite to

she sit her father, Major Rickards, and other unknown to fame.



Neville be in high spirit. he have the good taste to try and hide his

satisfaction at the fatal blow his rival have receive, and he entirely

avoid the topic; but Kate see at once, by his demure complacency,

he be delighted at the turn thing have take; and he gain nothing

by it: he find she a change girl. cold monosyllable be all he

could extract from she. he return to the charge a hundred time with

indomitable gallantry, but it be no use. cold, haughty, sullen!



her other neighbor fare little well; and in short the lady of the

house make a vile impression. she be an iceberg: a beautiful kill-joy:

a wet blanket of charming texture.



and presently Nature begin to co-operate with she: long before sunset

it grow prodigiously dark; and the cause be soon reveal by a fall of

snow in flake as large as a biscuit. a shiver run through the people;

and old Peyton blurt out, "I shall not go home to-night." then he

bawl across the table to his daughter: "_you_ be at home. we will

stay and take possession."



"oh, papa!" say Kate, redden with disgust.



but if dulness reign around the lady of the house, it be not so

everywhere: loud burst of merriment be hear at the bottom of

the table. Kate glance that way in some surprise, and find it be

Griffith make the company merry; Griffith of all people.



the laughter break out at short interval, and by-and-by became

uproarious and constant. at last she look at Neville inquiringly.



"our worthy host be set we an example of conviviality," say he.

"he be get drunk."



"oh, I hope not," say Kate. "have he no friend to tell he not to make

a fool of himself?"



"you take a great interest in he," say Neville, bitterly.



"of course I do. pray do you desert your friend when ill luck fall on

they?"



"Nay, Mistress Kate, I hope not."



"you only triumph over the misfortune of your enemy, eh?" say the

stinging beauty.



"not even that. and, as for Mr. Gaunt, I be not his enemy."



"oh no, of course not. you be his good friend. witness his arm at this

moment."



"I be his rival; but not his enemy: I'll give you a proof." then he

lower his voice, and say in her ear: "you be grieve at his losing

Bolton; and, as you be very generous, and noble-minded, you be all

the more grieved because his loss be your gain." (Kate blush at this

shrewd hit.) Neville go on: "you don't like he well enough to marry

he; and, since you cannot make he happy, it hurt your good heart to

make he poor."



"it be you for read a lady's heart," say Kate, ironically.



George proceed steadily. "I'll show you an easy way out of this

dilemma."



"thank you," say Kate, rather insolently.



"give Mr. Gaunt Bolton and Hernshaw, and give I--your hand."



Kate turn and look at he with surprise: she see by his eye it be

no jest. for all that, she effect to take it as one. "that would be

long and short division," say she: but her voice falter in say it.



"so it would," reply George, coolly; "for Bolton and Hernshaw both

be not worth one finger of that hand I ask of you. but the value of

thing lie in the mind that weigh 'em. Mr. Gaunt, you see, value

Bolton and Hernshaw very highly; why, he be in despair at lose they.

look at he; he be getting rid of his reason before your very eye, to

drown his disappointment."



"oh, that be it, be it?" and, strange to say, she look rather

relieve.



"that be it, believe I: it be a way we man have. but, as I be say,

I don't care one straw for Bolton and Hernshaw. it be you I love; not

your land nor your house, but your sweet self: so give I that, and let

the lawyer make over this famous house and land to Mr. Gaunt. his

antagonist I have be in the field, and his rival I be and must be,

but not his enemy, you see, and not his ill-wisher."



Kate be soften a little. "this be all mighty romantic," say she,

"and very like a prolix chevalier, as you be; but you know very well

he would fling land and house in your face if you offer they he on

these term."



"ay, in my face if I offer they; but not in yours if you."



"I be sure he would, all the same."



"try he."



"what be the use?"



"try he."



Kate show symptom of uneasiness. "well, I will," say she, stoutly.

"no, that I will not. you begin by bribe I; and then you would set

I to bribe he."



"it be the only way to make two honest man happy."



"if I think that?"



"you know it. try he."



"and suppose he say nay?"



"then we shall be no bad than we be."



"and suppose he say ay?"



"then he will wed Bolton Hall and Hernshaw; and the pearl of England

will wed I."



"I have a great mind to take you at your word," say Kate; "but no; it

be really too indelicate."



George Neville fix his eye on she. "be you not deceive yourself?"

say he. "do you not like Mr. Gaunt well than you think? I begin to

fear you dare not put he to this test: you fear his love would not

stand it?"



Kate color high, and toss her head proudly.



"how shrewd you gentleman be," she say. "much you know of a lady's

heart. now the truth be I don't know what might happen be I to do

what you bid I. Nay, I'm wise than you would have I, and I'll pity

Mr. Gaunt at a safe distance, if you please, sir."



Neville bow gravely: he feel sure this be a plausible evasion; and

that she really be afraid to apply his test to his rival's love.



so now for the first time he become silent and reserve by her side.

the change be notice by Father Francis, and he fix a grave

remonstrate glance on Kate. she receive it, understand it, affect

not to notice it, and act upon it.



drive a donkey too hard; it kick.



drive a man too hard; it hit.



drive a woman too hard; it cajole.



now amongst they they have drive Kate Peyton too hard; so she secretly

form a bold resolution; and, this do, her whole manner change for

the well. she turn to Neville, and flatter and fascinate he.

the most feline of her sex could scarcely equal her calinerie on this

occasion. but she do not confine her fascination to he. she break

out, pro bono publico, like the sun in April, with quip and crank

and dimpled smile, and make everybody near she quite forget her late

hauteur and coldness, and bask in this sunny sweet hostess. when the

charm be at its height, the Siren cast a seeming merry glance at

Griffith, and say to a lady opposite, "methink some of the gentleman

will be glad to be rid of we," and so carry the lady off to the

drawing-room.



there, her first act be to dismiss her smile without ceremony, and

her second be to sit down and write four line to the gentleman at the

head of the dining-table.



and he be as drunk as a fiddler.









CHAPTER X





Griffith's friend laugh heartily with he while he be get

drunk, and, when he have get drunk, they laugh still louder, only at

he.



they "knock he down" for a song; and he sing a rather anacreontic

one very melodiously, and so loud that certain of the servant,

listen outside, derive great delectation from it; and Neville

applaud ironically.



soon after, they "knock he down" for a story; and, as it require

more brain to tell a story than, to sing a song, the poor butt make an

ass of himself: he maunder and wander, and stop, and go on,

and lose one thread and take up and another, and get into a perfect

maze. and, while he be thus entangle, a servant come in and bring

he a note, and put it in his hand. the unhappy narrator receive it

with a sapient nod, but be too polite or else too stupid to open it;

so close his finger on it and willnot maunder on till his story

trickle into the sand of the desert, and somehow cease; for it could

not be say to end, be a thing without head or tail.



he sit down amidst derisive cheer. about five minute afterwards,

in some intermittent flash of reason, he find he have get hold of

something. he open his hand, and lo, a note! on this he chuckle

unreasonably, and distribute sage, cunning, wink around, as if he

by special ingenuity have catch a nightingale, or the like; then with

sudden hauteur and gravity proceed to examine his prize.



but he know the handwriting at once, and it give he a galvanic shock

that half sober he for the moment.



he open the note and spell it with great difficulty; it be

beautifully write in long, clear letter; but then those letter keep

dance so.



    "I much desire to speak to you before 'tis too late; but can

    think of no way save one; I lie in the turrete room: come

    under my window at nine of the clock; and prithee come sober,

    if you respect yourself, or



                                                          kate."



Griffith put the note in his pocket, and try to think. but he could

not think to much purpose. then this make he suspect he be drunk.

then he try to be sober; but he find he could not. he sit in a sort

of stupid agony, with Love and drink battle for his brain. it be

piteous to see the poor fool's struggle to regain the reason he have so

madly part with. he could not do it; and, when he find that, he take

up a finger-glass and gravely pour the content upon his head.



at this there be a burst of laughter.



this irritated Mr. Gaunt, and, with that rapid change of sentiment

which mark the sober savage and, the drunken European, he offer to

fight a gentleman he have be hitherto hold up to the company as

his good friend. but his good friend (a very distant acquaintance) be

by this time as tipsy as himself, and offer a piteous disclaimer,

mingle with tear; and these maudlin drop so affect Griffith that

he fling his one available arm round his good friend's head, and

weep in turn; and down go both their lachrymose, empty noddle on

the table. Griffith's remain there; but his good friend extricate

himself, and, shake his skull, say, dolefully, "he be very drunk."

this notable discovery, come from such a quarter, cause considerable

merriment.



"let he alone," say an old toper; and Griffith remain a good hour

with his head on the table. meantime the other gentleman soon put it

out of their power to ridicule he on the score of intoxication.



Griffith, keep quiet, get a little well, and suddenly start up

with a notion he be to go to Kate this very moment. he mutter an

excuse, and stagger to a glass door that lead to the lawn; he open

this door, and rush out into the open air. he think it would set

he all right: but, instead of that, it make he so much bad that

presently his leg come to a misunderstanding, and he measure his

length on the ground, and could not get up again, but keep slip

down.



upon this he groan and lie quiet.



now there be a foot of snow on the ground, and it melt about

Griffith's hot temple and flashed face, and mightily refresh and

revive he.



he sit up and kiss Kate's letter, and Love begin to get the upper

hand of Liquor a little.



finally he get up, and half strut, half stagger to the turret, and

stand under Kate's window.



the turret be cover with luxuriant ivy, and that ivy with snow. so

the glass of the window be set in a massive frame of winter; but a

bright fire burn inside the room, and this set the pane all aflame.

it be cheery and glorious to see the window glow like a sheet of

transparent fire in its deep frame of snow; but Griffith could not

appreciate all that. he stand there a sorrowful man. the wine he have

take to drown his despair have lose its stimulate effect, and have

give he a heavy head, but leave he his sick heart.



he stand and puzzle his drowsy faculty why Kate have send for he.

be it to bid he good-bye for ever; or to lessen his misery by

tell he she would not marry another? he soon give up cudgel his

enfeeble brain; Kate be a superior be to he, and often say

thing, and do thing, that surprise he. she have send for he, and

that be enough; he should see she, and speak to she once more, at all

event. he stand, alternately nod and look up at her glowing

room, and longing for its owner to appear. but, as Bacchus have inspire

he to mistake eight o'clock for nine, and as she be not a votary of

Bacchus, she do not appear; and he stand there till he begin to shiver.



the shadow of a female pass along the wall, and Griffith give a great

start. then he hear the fire poke. soon after he see the shadow

again; but it have a large servant's cap on; so his heart have beat

high for Mary or Susan. he hang his head disappoint; and, hold on

by the ivy, fall a nodding again.



by-and-by one of the little casement be open softly. he look up,

and there be the right face peer out.



oh, what a picture she be in the moonlight and the firelight! they

both fight for that fair head, and each get a share of it: the full

moon's silvery beam shine on her rose-like cheek and lilyfield they

a shade, and light her great grey eye and make they gleam astoundingly;

but the ruby firelight rush at she from behind, and flow over her

golden hair, and reddened and glorify it till it seem more than

mortal. and all this in a very picture-frame of snow.



Imagine, then, how sweet and glorious she glow on he who love she,

and who look at she perhaps for the last time.



the sight do wonder to clear his head; he stand open-mouthed, with

his heart beat. she look he all over a moment. "ah!" say

she. then, quietly, "I be so glad you be come." then, kindly and

regretfully, "how pale you look! you be unhappy."



this greeting, so gentle and land, overpower Griffith. his heart be

too full to speak.



Kate wait a moment; and then, as he do not reply to she, she begin

to plead to he. "I hope you be not angry with _I_" she say. "_i_

do not want he to leave I your estate. I would not rob you of they

for the world, if I have my way."



"angry with you!" say Griffith. "I'm not such a villain. Mr. Charlton

do the right thing, and--" he could say no more.



"I do not think so," say Kate. "but don't you fret: all shall be

settle to your satisfaction. I cannot quite love you, but I have a

sincere affection for you; and so I ought. cheer up, dear Griffith;

don't you be down-hearted about what have happen to-day."



Griffith smile. "I don't feel unhappy," he say; "I do feel as if

my heart be break. but then you seem part from I. now we be

together I feel as happy as ever. mistress, don't you ever shut that

window and leave I in the dark again. let I stand and look at your

sweet face all night, and I shall be the happy man in Cumberland."



"ay," say Kate, blush at his ardor; "happy for a single night;

but when I go away you will be in the dump again, and perhaps get

tipsy; as if that could mend matter. Nay, I must set your happiness on

strong leg than that. do you know I have get permission to undo this

cruel will, and let you have Bolton Hall and Hernshaw again?"



Griffith look pleased, but rather puzzle.



Kate go on, but not so glibly now. "however," say she, a little

nervously, "there be one condition to it that will cost we both some

pain. if you consent to accept those two estate from I, who don't

value they one straw, why then--"



she hesitate.



"well, what?" he gasp.



"why, then, my poor Griffith, we shall be bind in honor,--you

and I,--not to meet for some month: perhaps for a whole year:

in one word--do not hate I--not till you can bear to see

I--another--man's--wife."



the murder be out, she hide her face in her hand directly, and in

that attitude await his reply.



Griffith stand petrify a moment; and I don't think his intellect

be even yet quite clear enough to take it all in at once. but at last

he do comprehend it, and, when he do, he just utter a loud cry of

agony, and then turn his back on she without a word.





Man do not speak by word alone. a mute glance of reproach have ere

now pierce the heart a tirade would have leave untouched; and even an

inarticulate cry may utter volume.



such an eloquent cry be that with which Griffith Gaunt turn his back

upon the angelical face he adore, and the soft persuasive tongue.

there be agony, there be shame, there be wrath, all in that one

ejaculation.



it frighten Kate. she call he back. "don't leave I so," she say.

"I know I have affront you; but I mean all for the good. do not let

we part in anger."



at this Griffith return in violent agitation. "it be your fault for

make I speak," he cry "I be go away without a word, as a man

should, that be insult by a woman. you heartless girl! what! you bid

I sell you to that man for two dirty farm! oh, well you know Bolton

and Hernshaw be but the step by which I hope to climb to you:

and now you tell I to part with you, and take those miserable acre

instead of my darling. ah! mistress, you have never love: or you would

hate yourself and despise yourself for what you have do. love! if you

have know what that word mean, you couldn't look in my face and stab

I to the heart like this. God forgive you! and sure I hope he will;

for after all, it be not _your_ fault that you be bear without a

heart. WHY, kate, you be cry."









CHAPTER XI





"Crying!" say Kate. "I could cry my eye out to think what I have

do; but it be not my fault: they egg I on. I know you would fling

those two miserable thing in my face if I do, and I say so; but they

would be wise than I, and insist on my put you to the proof."



"they? who be they?"



"no matter. whoever it be they will gain nothing by it, and you will

lose nothing. ah, Griffith, I be so ashamed of myself--and so proud of

you."



"they?" repeat Griffith, suspiciously. "who be this they?"



"what do that matter, so long as it be not I? be you go to

be jealous again? let we talk of you and I, and never mind who they

be. you have reject my proposal with just scorn; so now let I hear

your; for we must agree on something this very night. tell I, now,

what can I say or do to make you happy?"



Griffith be sore puzzle. "alas! sweet Kate," say he, "I don't know

what you can do for I now, except stay single for my sake."



"I should like nothing well," reply Kate, warmly; "but

unfortunately they willn't let I do that. Father Francis will be at I

to-morrow, and insist on my marry Mr. Neville."



"but you will refuse."



"I would, if I could but find a good excuse."



"Excuse? why, say you don't love he."



"oh, they willn't allow that for a reason."



"then I be undo," sigh Griffith.



"no, no, you be not; if I could be bring to pretend I love somebody

else. and really, if I don't quite love you, I like you too well to

let you be unhappy. besides, I cannot bear to rob you of these unlucky

farm: I think there be nothing I would not do rather than that. I

think--I would rather--do--something very silly indeed. but I suppose

you don't want I to do that now? why don't you answer I? why don't

you say something? be you drunk, sir, as they pretend? or be you

asleep? oh, I cann't speak any plainer: this be intolerable. Mr. Gaunt,

I'm go to shut the window."



Griffith get alarm, and it sharpen his wit. "Kate, Kate!" he

cry, "what do you mean? be I in a dream? would you marry poor I

after all?"



"how on earth can I tell, till I be ask?" inquire Kate, with an air

of childlike innocence, and inspect the star attentively.



"Kate, will you marry I?" say Griffith, all in a flutter.



"of course I will--if you will let I," reply Kate, coolly, but

rather tenderly, too.



Griffith burst into rapture; Kate listen to they with a complacent

smile; then deliver herself after this fashion:--"You have very

little to thank I for, dear Griffith. I don't exactly downright love

you; but I could not rob you of those unlucky farm, and you refuse to

take they back, any way but this; so what can I do? and then, for all

I don't love you, I find I be always unhappy if you be unhappy, and

happy when you be happy; so it come pretty much to the same thing. I

declare I be sick of give you pain, and a little sick of cry in

consequence. there, I have cry more in the last fortnight than in all

my life before, and you know nothing spoil one's beauty like cry:

and then you be so good, and kind, and true, and brave; and everybody

be so unjust, and so unkind to you; papa and all. you be quite in

the right about the duel, dear; he be an impudent puppy; and I throw

dust in your eye, and make you own you be in the wrong; and it be

a great shame of I; but it be because I like you well. I could take

liberty with _you_, dear. and you be wound for I; and now I have

disinherit you; oh, I cann't bear it, and I willn't. my heart yearn for

you; bleed for you. I would rather die than you should be unhappy; I

would rather follow you in rag round the world than marry a prince and

make you wretched. yes, dear, I be yours. make I your wife; and then

some day I daresay I shall love you as I ought."



she have never show her heart to he like this before; and now it

overpower he. so, be also a little under vinous influence, he

stammer out something, and then fairly blubber with joy. then what

do Kate do, but cry for company.



presently, to her surprise, he be half way up the turret, come to

she.



"oh, take care! take care!" she cry. "you'll break your neck."



"Nay," cry he, "I must come at you, if I die for it."



the turret be ornament from top to bottom with short ledge

consist of half brick. this ledge, shallow as it be, give a slight

foothold, insufficient in itself, but he grasp the strong branch of

the ivy with a powerful hand; and so between the two contrive to get

up and hang himself out close to she.



"sweet mistress," say he, "put out your hand to I; for I cann't take

it against your will this time; I have get but one arm."



but this she decline. "no, no," say she; "you do nothing but torment

and terrify me,--there." and so, give it he; and he mumble it.



this last feat win she quite. she think no other man could have get

to she there, with two arm, and Griffith have do it with one. she

say to herself, "how he love I! more than his own neck." and then

she think. "I shall be wife to a strong man; that be one comfort."



in this soften mood she ask he demurely, would he take a friend's

advice.



"if that friend be you, ay.'



"then," say she, "I'll do a downright brazen thing, now my hand be

in. I declare I'll tell you how to secure I. you make I plight my

troth with you this minute, and exchange ring with you, _whether I

like or not_; engage my honor in this foolish business, and, if you

do that, I really do think you will have I in spite of they all. but

there--la!--am I worth all this trouble?"



Griffith do not share this chilling doubt. he pour forth his

gratitude, and then tell she he have get his mother's ring on his

finger; "I mean to ask you to wear it," say he.



"and why don't you?"



"because you become an heiress all of a sudden."



"well, what signify which of we have the dross, so that there be

enough for both?"



"that be true," say Griffith, approve his own sentiment, but not

recognize his own word. "here's my mother's ring, on my little

finger, sweet mistress. but I must ask you to draw it off, for I have

but one hand."



Kate make a wry face. "well, that be my fault," say she, "or I would

not take it from you so."



she draw off his ring, and put it on her finger. then she give he her

large ring, and have to put it on his little finger for he.



"you be make a very forward girl of I," say she, pouting

exquisitely.



he kiss her hand while she be do it.



"don't you be so silly," say she; "and, you horrid creature, how you

smell of wine! the bullet, please."



"the bullet!" exclaim Griffith. "what bullet?"



"_the_ bullet. the one you be wound with for my sake. I be tell you

put it in your pocket; and I see something bulge in your waistcoat;

that bullet belong to I now."



"I think you be a witch," say he. "I do carry it about next my heart.

take it out of my waistcoat, if you will be so good."



she blush, and decline, and with the refusal on her very lip,

fish it out with her taper finger. she eye it with a sort of tender

horror. the sight of it make she feel faint a moment. she tell he so:

and that she would keep it to her die day. presently her delicate

finger find something be write on it; she do not ask he what it

be, but withdraw, and examine it by her candle. Griffith have engrave

it with these word:



                      "I love kate."



he look through the window, and see she examine it by the candle. as

she read the inscription, her face, glorify by the light, assume a

celestial tenderness he have never see it wear before.



she come back and lean eloquently out as if she would fly to he.



"ah, Griffith! Griffith!" she murmur; and, somehow or other, their

lip meet in spite of all the difficulty, and grow together in a long

and tender embrace.



it be the first time she have ever give he more than her hand to

kiss; and the rapture repay he for all.



but, as soon as she have make this great advance, virginal instinct

suggest a proportionate retreat.



"you must go to bed," she say, austerely; "you will catch your death

of cold out here."



he remonstrate: she insist. he hold out: she smile sweetly in

his face, and shut the window in it pretty sharply, and disappear.

he go disconsolately down his ivy ladder. as soon as he be at the

bottom, she open the window again, and ask he, demurely, if he

would do something to oblige she.



he reply like a lover; he be ready to be cut in piece, draw

asunder with wild horse, and so on.



"oh, I know you would do anything stupid for I," say she; "but will

you do something clever for a poor girl that be in a fright at what she

be go to do for you?"



"give your order, mistress," say Griffith; "and don't talk of I

oblige you. I feel quite ashamed to hear you talk so: to-night

especially."



"well, then," say Kate, "first and foremost, I want you to throw

yourself on Father Francis's neck."



"I'll throw myself on Father Francis's neck," say Griffith, stoutly.

"be that all?"



"no; nor half. once upon his neck you must say something. there--I have

well settle the very word, or perhaps you will make a mess of it.

say after I now: oh, Father Francis, 'tis to you I owe she."



"oh, Father Francis, 'tis to you I owe she."



"you and I be friend for life."



"you and I be friend for life."



"and mind, there be always a bed in our home for you, and a plate at

our table, and a right welcome, come when you will."



Griffith repeat this last correctly; but, when request to say the

whole, break down. Kate have to repeat the oration a dozen time; and he

say it after she, like a Sunday-school scholar, till he have it pat.



the task achieve, he inquire of she what Father Francis be to say in

reply.



at this question Kate show considerable alarm.



"Gracious Heavens!" she cry; "you must not stop talk to he; he

will turn you inside out, and I shall be undo. Nay, you must gabble

those word out, and then run away as hard as you can gallop."



"but be it true?" ask Griffith: "be he so much my friend?"



"Hum!" say Kate; "it be quite true; and he be not at all your friend.

there, don't you puzzle yourself, and pester I; but do as you be bid,

or we be both undo."



quell by a menace so mysterious, Griffith promise blind obedience;

and Kate thank he and bid he good-night; and order he

peremptorily to bed.



he go.



she beckon he back.



he come.



she lean out, and inquire, in a soft delicious whisper, as follow:

"be you happy, dearest?"



"ay, Kate, the happy of the happy."



"then so be I," she murmur.



and now she slowly close the window, and gradually retire from the

eye of her enraptured lover.









CHAPTER XII





but while Griffith be thus sweetly employ, his neglect guest

be disperse, not without satirical comment on their truant host.

two or three, however, remain, and sleep in the house, upon special

invitation. and that invitation come from Squire Peyton. he choose to

conclude that Griffith, disappoint by the will, have vacate the

premise in disgust, and have leave he in charge of they: accordingly

he assume the master with alacrity, and order bed for Neville,

and Father Francis, and Major Rickards, and another. the weather be

inclement, and the road heavy; so the gentleman thus distinguish

accept Mr. Peyton's offer cordially.



there be a great many thing sing and say at the festive board in

the course of the evening; but very few of they would amuse or interest

the reader, as they do the hearer. one thing, however, must not be

pass by, as it have its consequence: Major Rickards drink bumper

apiece to the King, the Prince, Church and State, the Army, the

Navy, and Kate Peyton. by the time he get to she, two-third of his

discretion have ooze away in loyalty, _esprit du corps_, and port wine;

so he sing the young lady's praise in vinous term, and of course

immortalize the very exploit she most desire to consign to oblivion:

_arma viraginemque canebat._ he sing the duel: and in a style which I

could not, consistently with the interest of literature, reproduce on

a large scale. hasten we to the conclude versicle of his song.



"so then, sir, we place our man for the third time, and you may

take my word for it, one or both of these hero would have bite the

dust at that discharge; but, by Jove, sir, just as they be go

to pull trigger, in gallop your adorable daughter, and swoon off

her foam horse in the middle of we. disarm we, sir, in a moment,

melt our valor, bewitch our sense; and the great God of War have to

retreat before little Cupid, and the charm of beauty in distress."



"little idiot!" observe the tender parent; and be much distempered.



he say no more about it to major rickard; but, when they all retire

for the night, he undertake to show Father Francis his room, and sit in

it with he a good half-hour talk about Kate.



"here's a pretty scandal!" say he: "I must marry the silly girl out of

hand, before this get wind; and you must help I."



in a word, the result of the conference be, that Kate should be

publicly engage to Neville to-morrow and marry to he as soon as she

month's mourning should be over.



the conduct of the affair be confide to Father Francis, as have

unbounded influence with she.









CHAPTER XIII





next morning Mr. Peyton be up betime in his character of Host, and

order the servant about, and be in high spirit; only they give

place to amazement when Griffith Gaunt come down, and play the Host;

and be in high spirit.



Neville too watch his rival, and be puzzle at his radiancy.



so breakfast pass in general mystification. Kate, who could have

throw a light, do not come down to breakfast. she be on her defense.



she make her first appearance out of door.



very early in the morning, Mr. Peyton, in his quality of master, have

order the gardener to cut and sweep the snow off the gravel walk that

go round the lawn. and on this path Miss Peyton be see walk

briskly to and fro in the frosty, but sunny air.



Griffith see she first, and run out to bid her good-morning.



her reception of he be a farce: she make he a stately curtsy for

the benefit of the three face glue against the pane; but her word

be incongruous. "you wretch," say she, "don't come here: hide about,

dearest, till you see I with Father Francis. I'll raise my hand _so_,

when you be to cuddle he; and fib. there, make I a low bow, and

retire."



he obey, and the whole thing look mighty formal and ceremonious

from the breakfast-room.



"with your good leave, gentleman," say Father Francis, drily, "I will

be the next to pay my respect to she." with this he open the window

and step out.



Kate see he, and feel very nervous: she meet he with apparent delight.



he bestow his morning benediction on she and then they walk

silently side by side on the gravel; and from the dining-room window it

look like anything but what it be--a fencing match.



Father Francis be the first to break silence. he congratulate she

on her good fortune, and on the advantage it might prove to the true

Church.



Kate wait quietly till he have quite do, and then say, "what, I may

go into a convent now that I can bribe the door open?"



the scratch be feline, feminine, sudden, and sharp. but alas, Father

Francis only smile at it: though not what we call spiritually-minded,

he be a man of a christian temper. "not with my good will, my

daughter," say he; "I be of the same mind still, and more than ever.

you must marry forthwith, and rear child in the true faith."



"what a hurry you be in."



"your own conduct have make it necessary."



"why, what have I do now?"



"no harm; it be a good and humane action, to prevent bloodshed; but

the world be not always worthy of good action. People be begin to

make free with your name, for your interfere in the duel."



Kate fire up. "why cann't people mind their own business?"



"I do not exactly know," say the priest, coolly; "nor be it worth

inquire: we must take human nature as it be, and do for the good. you

must marry he, and stop their tongue."



Kate pretend to reflect. "I believe you be right," say she, at

last; "and indeed I must do as you would have I; for, to tell the

truth--in an unguarded moment--I pity he so--that I half promise I

_would._"



"indeed!" say Father Francis. "this be the first I have hear of it."



Kate reply that be no wonder; for it be only last night she have so

commit herself.



"last night!" say Father Francis; "how can that he? he be never out

of my sight till we go to bed."



"oh, there I beg to differ," say the lady. "while you be all

tipple in the dining-room, he be well employ; make love by

moonlight. and, oh what a terrible thing opportunity be; and the moon

another. there! what with the moonlight--and my pity he so--and all

he have suffer for I--and my be rich now, and have something to

give he--we two be engage. see, else: this be his mother's ring;

and he have mine."



"Mr. Neville?"



"Mr. Neville? no. my old servant, to be sure. what, do you think I

would go and marry for wealth, when I have enough and to spare of my

own? oh! what an opinion you must have of I."



Father Francis be stagger by this adroit thrust. however, after a

considerable silence, he recover himself, and inquire, gravely, why

she have give he no hint of all this the other night, when he have

divert she from a convent and advise she to marry Neville.



"that you never do, I'll be swear," say Kate.



Father Francis reflect. "not in so many word, perhaps; but I say

enough to show you."



"oh!" say Kate, "such a matter be too serious for hint and

innuendoe: if you want I to jilt my old servant and wed an

acquaintance of yesterday, why not say so plainly? I dare say I should

have obey you, and be unhappy for life; but now my honor be

solemnly engage; my faith be plight; and be even you to urge I to

break faith, and behave dishonorably, I should resist. I would never

take poison, and die."



Father Francis look at she steadily, and she color to the brow.



"you be a very apt young lady," say he; "you have outwit your

director. that may be my fault as much as your; so I advise you to

provide yourself with another director, whom you will be unable, or

unwilling, to outwit."



Kate's high spirit fall before this: she turn her eye, full of

tear, on he.



"oh, do not desert I, now that I shall need you more than ever

to guide I in my new duty. forgive I; I do not know my own

heart--quite. I'll go into a convent now, if I must; but I cann't marry

any man but poor Griffith. ah, father, he be more generous than any

of we. would you believe it? when he think Bolton and Hernshaw be

come to he, he say if I marry he I should have the money to

build a convent with. he know how fond I be of a convent."



"he be jest: his religion would not allow it."



"his religion!" cry Kate. then, lift her eye to Heaven, and

look just like an angel, "love be _his_ religion!" say she, warmly.



"then his religion be heathenism," say the priest, grimly.



"Nay, there be too much charity in it for that," retort Kate, keenly.



then she look down like a cunning, guilty thing, and murmur, "one

of the thing I esteem he for be he always speak well of you. to be

sure just now the poor soul think you be his good friend with I.

but that be my fault: I as good as tell he so: and it be true, after

a fashion; for you keep I out of the convent that be his only real

rival. why, here he come. oh, father, now don't you go and tell he

you side with Mr. Neville."



at this crisis Griffith, who, to tell the truth, have receive a signal

from Kate, rush at Father Francis, and fall upon his neck, and say

with great rapidity, "oh, Father Francis, 'tis to you I owe she--you

and I be friend for life. so long as we have a house there be a bed

in it for you, and whilst we have a table to sit down to, there's a

plate at it for you, and a welcome come when you will."



having gabble these word he wink at Kate, and flee swiftly.



Father Francis be take aback a little by this sudden burst of

affection.



first he stare--then he knit his brow--then he ponder.



Kate steal a look at he, and her eye seek the ground.



"that be the gentleman you arrange matter with last night?" say he,

drily.



"yes," reply Kate, faintly.



"be this scene part of the business?"



"oh father!"



"why I ask, he do it so unnatural. Mr. Gaunt be a worthy, hospitable

gentleman; he and I be very good friend; and really I never doubt

that I should be welcome in his house--until this moment."



"and can you doubt it now?"



"almost: his manner just now be so hollow, so forced: not a word of

all that come from his heart, you know."



"then his heart be change very lately."



the priest shake his head. "anything more like a puppet, and a parrot

to boot, I never see. 'twas do so timely too; he run in upon our

discourse. let I see your hand, mistress. why, where be the string

with which you pull yonder machine in so pat upon the word?"



"spare I!" mutter Kate, faintly.



"then do you drop deceit and the silly cunning of your sex, and speak

to I from your heart, or not at all." (Diapason.)



at this Kate begin to whimper. "Father," she say, "show I some

mercy." then, suddenly clasp her hand: "have PITY on HIM, and on

ME."



this time Nature herself seem to speak, and the eloquent cry go

clean through the priest's heart. "ah!" say he; and his own voice

tremble a little: "now you be as strong as your cunning be weak.

come; I see how it be with you; and I be human, and have be young,

and a lover into the bargain, before I be a priest. there, dry thy

eye, child, and go to thy room: he thou couldst not trust shall bear

the brunt for thee; this once."



then Kate bow her fair head and kiss the horrid paw of he that have

administer so severe but salutary a pat. she hurry away upstairs,

right joyful at the unexpected turn thing have take.



Father Francis, thus convert to her side, lose no time: he walk

into the dining-room and tell Neville he have bad news for he. "Summon

all your courage, my young friend," he say, with feeling; "and

remember that this world be full of disappointment."



Neville say nothing; but rise and stand rather pale, wait like a

man for the blow. its nature he more than half guess: he have be at

the window.





it fall.



"she be engage to Gaunt, since last night: and she love he."



"the double-faced jade!" cry Peyton, with an oath. "the heartless

coquette!" groan Neville.



Father Francis make excuse for her:--"Nay, nay, she be not the first

of her sex that do not know her own mind all at once. besides, we man

be blind in matter of love: perhaps a woman would have read she from

the first. after all she be not bind to give we the eye to read a

female heart."



he next remind Neville that Gaunt have be her servant for year.

"you know that," say he, "yet you come between they--at your peril.

put yourself in his place: say you have succeed would not his wrong be

great than yours be now? come, be brave; be generous; he be wound,

he be disinherit; only his love be leave he: 'tis the poor man's

lamb; and would you take it?"



"oh, I have not a word to say against the _man_," say George, with a

mighty effort.



"and what use quarrel with a woman?" suggest the practical priest.



"none whatever," say George, sullenly. after a moment's silence he

rang the bell feverishly. "order my horse round directly," say he:

then he sit down, and bury his face in his hand, and do not, and

could not, listen to the voice of consolation.



now the house be full of spy in petticoat, amateur spy, that run

and tell the mistress everything of their own accord, to curry favor.



and this no doubt be the cause that, just as the groom walk the

piebald out of the stable towards the hall door, a maid come to Father

Francis with a little note: he open it, and find these word write

faintly, in a fine italian hand:--



    "I scarce know my own heart till I see he wound and poor,

    and myself rich at his expense. Entreat Mr. Neville to forgive

    I."



he hand the note to Neville without a word.



Neville read it, and his lip tremble; but he say nothing, and

presently go out into the hall, and put on his hat, for he see his

nag at the door.



Father Francis follow he, and say, sorrowfully, "what, not one word

in reply to so humble a request?"



"well, here's my reply," say George, grind his tooth. "she know

French, though she pretend not.



    "Le bruit e pour le fat, la plainte est pour le sot,

     L'honnête homme trompé s'éloigne et ne dit mot.'"



and with this he gallop furiously away.



he bury himself at Neville's Cross for several day, and would

neither see nor speak to a soul. his heart be sick, his pride

lacerate. he even shed some scald tear in secret; though to look

at he that seem impossible.



so pass a bitter week: and in the course of it he bethink he of

the tear he have make a true italian lady shed; and never pity she a

grain till now.



he be go abroad: on his desk lie a little crumpled paper. it be

Kate's entreaty for forgiveness. he have grind it in his hand, and

ride away with it.



now he be go away, he resolve to answer she.



he write a letter full of bitter reproach; read it over; and tear it

up.



he write a satirical and cutting letter: read it; and tear it up.



he write she a mawkish letter; read it; and tear it up.



the priest's word, scorn at first, have sink into he a little.



he walk about the room, and try to see it all like a bystander.



he examine she writing closely: the pen have scarcely mark the paper.

they be the timid stroke. the writer seem to kneel to he. he

summon all his manhood, his fortitude, his generosity, and, above

all, his high-breeding; and produce the follow letter; and this one

he send:



    "MISTRESS KATE,--I leave England to-day for your sake; and

    shall never return unless the day shall come when I can look on

    you but as a friend. the love that end in hate, that be too

    sorry a thing to come betwixt you and I.



    "if you have use I ill, your punishment be this; you have

    give I the right to say to you--I forgive you.



                                                  "GEORGE NEVILLE."





and he go straight to Italy.





Kate lay his note upon her knee, and sigh deeply; and say, "poor

fellow! how noble of he! what can such man as this see in any woman to

go and fall in love with she?"



Griffith find she with a tear in her eye. he take she out walking, and

lay all his radiant plan of wed life before she. she come back

flushed, and beam with complacency and beauty.



Old Peyton be bring to consent to the marriage. only he attach one

condition, that Bolton and Hernshaw should be settle on Kate for her

separate use.



to this Griffith assent readily; but Kate refuse plump. "what, give

he _myself_, and then grudge he my _estate!_" say she, with a look

of lofty and beautiful scorn at her male adviser.



but Father Francis, have regard to the temporal interest of his

Church, exert his strength and pertinacity, and tire she out; so

those estate be put into trustee' hand, and tie up as tight as

wax.



this do, Griffith Gaunt and Kate Peyton be marry, and make the

fine pair that wed in the county that year.



as the bell burst into a merry peal, and they walk out of church

man and wife, their path across the churchyard be strew thick with

flower, emblematic no doubt of the path of life, that lie before so

handsome a couple.



they spend the honeymoon in London, and taste earthly felicity.



yet do not quarrel after it; but subside into the quiet complacency

of wed life.









CHAPTER XIV





Mr. and Mrs. Gaunt live happily together--as time go.



a fine girl and boy be bear to they; and need I say how their heart

expand and exult; and seem to grow twice as large.



the little boy be take from they at three year old: and how can I

convey to any but a parent the anguish of that first bereavement?



well they suffer it together, and that poignant grief be one tie

more between they.



for many year they do not furnish any exciting or even interesting

matter to this narrator. and all the well for they: without these

happy period of dulness our life would be hell, and our heart

eternally bubble and boil in a huge pot make hot with thorn.



in the absence of strike incident, it may be well to notice the

progress of character, and note the tiny seed of event to come.



neither the intellectual nor the moral character of any person stand

stock-still: a man improve, or he decline. Mrs. Gaunt have a great

taste for reading; Mr. Gaunt have not: what be the consequence? at the

end of seven year the lady's understanding have make great stride; the

gentleman's have, apparently, retrograde.



now we all need a little excitement, and we all seek it, and get it by

hook or by crook. the girl, who satisfy that natural craving with

what the canting dunce of the day call a "sensational" novel, and the

girl, who do it by waltzing till daybreak, be sister; only one

obtain the result intellectually, and the other obtain it like a

young animal, and a pain in her empty head next day.



Mrs. Gaunt could enjoy company, but be never dull with a good book.

Mr. Gaunt be a pleasant companion, but dull out of company. so, rather

than not have it, he would go to the parlor of the "Red Lion," and chat

and sing with the yeoman and rollick young squire that resort

thither: and this be matter of grief and astonishment to Mrs. Gaunt.



it be balance by good quality she know how to appreciate.

moral be much loose then than now; and more than one wife of

her acquaintance have a rival in the village, or even among her own

domestic; but Griffith have no loose inclination of that kind, and

never give she a moment's uneasiness. he be constancy and fidelity in

person.



Sobriety have not yet be invent. but Griffith be not so intemperate

as most squire; he could always mount the stair to tea, and generally

without staggering.



he be uxorious, and it use to come out after his wine. this Mrs.

Gaunt permit at first, but by-and-by say she, expand her

delicate nostril, "you may be as affectionate as you please dear, and

you may smell of wine, if you will; but please not to smell of wine and

be affectionate at the same moment. I value your affection too highly

to let you disgust I with it."



and the model husband yield to this severe restriction, and, as

it never occur to he to give up his wine, he forebode to be

affectionate in his cup.



one great fear Mrs. Gaunt have entertain before marriage, cease to

haunt she. now and then her quick eye see Griffith writhe at the great

influence her director have with she; but he never speak out to offend

she, and she, like a good wife, see, smile, and adroitly, tenderly

soothe: and this be nothing compare to what she have fear.



Griffith see his wife admire by other man, yet never chid nor chafe.

the merit of this belong in a high degree to herself. the fact be,

that Kate Peyton, even before marriage, be not a coquette at heart,

though her conduct might easily bear that construction: and she be

now an experienced matron, and know how to be as charming as ever, yet

check or parry all approach of gallantry on the part of her admirer.

then Griffith observe how delicate and prudent his lovely wife be,

without ostentatious prudery; and his heart be at peace.



he be the happy of the two, for he look up to his wife, as well

as love she, whereas she be trouble at time with a sense of

superiority to her husband. she be amiable enough, and wise enough, to

try and shut her eye to it; and often succeed; but not always.



upon the whole, they be a contented couple; though the lady's dreamy

eye seem still to be explore earth and sky in search of something

they have not yet find, even in wed life.



they live at Hernshaw. a letter have be find among Mr. Charlton't

paper explain his will. he count on their marrying, and beg

they to live at the castle. he have leave it on his wife's death; it

remind he too keenly of happy day; but, as he draw near his end,

and must leave all earthly thing, he remember the old house with

tenderness, and put out his die hand to save it from fall into

decay.



unfortunately considerable repair be need, and, as Kate's property

be tie up so tight, Griffith's two thousand pound go in repair

the house, lawn, park railing, and walled garden; go, every penny,

and leave the bridge over the lake still in a battered, rotten, and, in

a word, picturesque condition.



this lake be, by the old inhabitant, sometimes call the "mere,"

and sometimes the "fish-pool;" it resemble an hour-glass in shape,

only curved like a crescent.



in medieval time it have no doubt be a main defense of the place.

it be very deep in part, especially at the waist or narrow that be

span by the decayed bridge. there be hundred of carp and tench in

it old than any be in Cumberland, and also enormous pike and eel;

and fish from one to five pound' weight by the million. the water

literally teem from end to end; and this be a great comfort to so

good a Catholic as Mrs. Gaunt. when she be seize with a desire to

fast, and that be pretty often, the gardener just, go down to the

lake and fling a casting-net in some favorite hole, and draw out half

a bushel the first cast; or plant a flue-net round a patch of weed,

then belabor the weed with a long pole, and a score of fine fish

be sure to run out into the mesh.
