the "mere" be clear as plate glass, and come to the edge of the shaven

lawn, and reflect flower, turf, and overhang shrub, deliciously.



yet an ill name brood over its seductive water. for two person have

be drown in it during the last hundred year: and the last one be

the parson of the parish, return from the squire's dinner in the

normal condition of a guest, at that epoch. but what most affect

the popular mind, be, not the jovial soul hurry into eternity, but

the material circumstance that the greedy pike have clear the flesh

off his bone in a single night; so that little more than a skeleton,

with here and there a black rag hang to it, have be recover next

morning.



this ghastly detail be stoutly maintain and constantly repeat by

two ancient eye-witness, whose one melodramatic incident and treasure

it be, the rustic mind see no beauty whatever in those pellucid

water, where flower do glass themselves.



as for the woman of the village, they look on this sheet of water as

a trap for their poor body, and those of their child, and speak of

it as a singular hardship in their lot, that Hernshaw mere have not be

fill up threescore year agone.



the castle itself be no castle, nor have it be for century: it be

just a house with battlement; but attach to the stable be an old

square tower that really have form part of the medieval castle.



however, that unsubstantial shadow, a name, be often more durable than

the thing; especially in rural part: but, indeed, what be there in a

name for Time's tooth to catch hold of?



though no castle, it be a delightful abode. the drawing-room and

dining-room have both spacious bay window, open on to the lawn that

slope very gradually down to the clear lake, and there be mirror.

on this sweet lawn the inmate and guest walk for sun and mellow

air, and often play bowl at eventide.



on the other side be the drive up to the house door, and a sweep,

or small oval plot, of turf, surround by gravel; and a gate at the

corner of this sweep open into a grove of the grand old spruce

fir in the island.



this grove, dismal in winter, and awful at night, be deliciously

cool and sombre in the dog day. the tree be spire, and their

great stem stand serrie like infantry in column, and fling a mighty

canopy of sombre plume over head. a strange, antique, and classic

grove--_nulli penetrabili astro._



this retreat be enclose on three side by a wall, and on the east

side come nearly to the house; a few laurel bush separate the two.

at night it be shun religiously, on account of the ghost. even by

daylight it be little frequent, except by one person: and she take

to it amazingly. that person be Mrs. Gaunt. there seem to be, even

in educate woman, a singular, instinctive love of twilight; and here

be twilight at high noon. the place, too, suit her dreamy meditative

nature. hither, then, she often retire for peace and religious

contemplation, and move slowly in and out among the tall stem, or

sit still, with her thoughtful brow lean on her white hand: till the

cool, umbrageous retreat get to be call among the servant, "The

Dame's Haunt."



this, I think, be all need to be tell about the mere place, where the

Gaunts live comfortably many year; and little dreamed of the strange

event in store for they; little know the passion that slumber in

their own bosom, and, like other volcano, bide their time.

CHAPTER I





one day, at dinner, Father Francis let they know that he be order to

another part of the county, and should no long be able to enjoy their

hospitality. "I be sorry for it," say Griffith, heartily; and Mrs.

Gaunt echo he out of politeness; but, when husband and wife come to

talk it over in private, she let out all of a sudden, and for the first

time, that the spiritual coldness of her governor have be a great

misfortune to she all these year. "his mind," say she, "be set on

earthly thing. instead of help the angel to raise my thought to

heaven, and heavenly thing, he drag I down to earth. oh, that man's

soul be bear without wing."



Griffith venture to suggest that Francis be, nevertheless, an honest

man, and no mischief-maker.



Mrs. Gaunt soon dispose of this. "oh, there be plenty of honest man

in the world," say she; "but in one's spiritual director, one need

something more than that, and I have pine for it like a thirsty soul

in the desert all these year. poor good man, I love he dearly; but,

thank heaven, he be go."



the next time Francis come, Mrs. Gaunt take an opportunity to inquire,

but in the most delicate way, who be to be his successor.



"well," say he, "I fear you will have no one for the present: I mean

no one very fit to direct you in practical matter; but in all that

tend directly to the welfare of the soul you will have one young in

year but old in good work, and very much my superior in piety."



"I think you do yourself injustice, father," say Mrs. Gaunt, sweetly.

she be always polite; and, to be always polite, you must be sometimes

insincere.



"no, my daughter," say Father Francis, quietly, "thank God, I know

my own defect, and they teach I a little humility. I discharge my

religious duty punctually, and find they wholesome and composing; but

I lack that holy unction, that spiritual imagination, by which more

favour Christians have fit themselves to converse with angel. I

have too much body, I suppose, and too little soul. I own to you that I

cannot look, forward to the hour of death as a happy release from the

burden of the flesh. life be pleasant to I; immortality tempt I not;

the pure in heart delight I; but in the sentimental part of religion I

feel myself dry and barren. I fear God, and desire to do his will; but

I cannot love he as the saint have do; my spirit be too dull, too

gross. I have often be unable to keep pace with you in your pious and

lofty aspiration: and this soften my regret at quit you; for you

will be in well hand, my daughter."



Mrs. Gaunt be touch by her old friend's humility, and give he both

hand, with the tear in her eye. but she say nothing; the subject

be delicate; and really she could not honestly contradict he.



a day or two afterwards he bring his successor to the house; a man so

remarkable that Mrs. Gaunt almost start at first sight of he. bear

of an italian mother, his skin be dark, and his eye coal black; yet

his ample but symmetrical forehead be singularly white and delicate.

very tall and spare, and both face and figure be of that exalt kind

which make ordinary beauty seem dross. in short, he be one of those

ethereal priest the Roman Catholic Church produce every now and then

by way of incredible contrast to the thickset peasant in black that

form her staple. this Brother Leonard look and move like a being who

have come down from some high sphere to pay the world a very little

visit, and be very kind and patient with it all the time.



he be present to Mrs. Gaunt, and bow calmly, coldly, and with

a certain mixture of humility and superiority, and give she but one

tranquil glance, then turn his eye inward as before.



Mrs. Gaunt, on the contrary, be almost flutter at be present

so suddenly to one who seem to her Religion embody. she blush,

and look timidly at he, and be anxious not to make an unfavourable

impression.



she find it, however, very difficult to make any impression at all.

Leonard have no small talk, and meet her advance in that line with

courteous monosyllable; and when she, upon this, turn and chat

with Father Francis, he do not wait for an opening to strike in, but

seek a shelter from her commonplace in his own thought.



then Mrs. Gaunt yield to her genuine impulse, and begin to talk about

the prospect of the Church, and what might be do to reconvert the

British Isles to the true faith. her cheek flush, and her eye shine

with the theme; and Francis smile paternally: but the young priest

draw back; Mrs. Gaunt see in a moment that he disapprove of a woman

meddle with so high a matter uninvited. if he have say so she have

spirit enough to have resist; but the cold, lofty look of polite but

grave disapproval, dash her courage and reduce she to silence.



she soon recover so far as to be pique. she give her whole attention

to Francis, and, on part with her guest, she curtsy coldly to

Leonard, and say to Francis, "ah, my dear friend, I foresee I shall

miss you terribly."



I be afraid this pretty speech be intend as a side cut at Leonard.



but on the impassive ice the lightning play.



her new confessor retire, and leave she with a sense of inferiority,

which would have be pleasing to her woman's nature, if Leonard

himself have appear less conscious of it, and have show ever so little

approval of herself; but, impress upon she too sharply, it pique and

mortify she.



however, like a gallant champion, she await another encounter. she so

rarely fail to please, she could not accept defeat.



Father Francis depart.



Mrs. Gaunt soon find that she really miss he. she have get into a

habit of run to her confessor twice a week, and to her director

nearly every day that he do not come of his own accord to she.



her good sense show she at once she must not take up Brother

Leonard's time in this way. she go a long while, for she, without

confession: at last she send a line to Leonard ask he when it would

be convenient to he to confess she. Leonard write back to say that

he receive penitent in the chapel for two hour after matin every

Monday, Tuesday, and Saturday.



this imply first come, first serve; and be rather gall to Mrs.

Gaunt.



however, she ride one morning, with her groom behind she, and have

to wait until an old woman in a red cloak and black bonnet be

first dispose of. she confess a heap. and presently the soft but

chill tone of Brother Leonard break in with these freezing word:

"my daughter, excuse I; but confession be one thing, gossip about

ourselves be another."



this distinction be fine, but fatal. the next minute the fair penitent

be in her carriage, her eye fill with tear of mortification.



"the man be a spiritual machine," say she; and her pride be mortify

to the core.



in these happy day she use to open her heart to her husband; and she

go so far as to say some bitter little feminine thing of her new

confessor, before he.



he take no notice at first; but at last he say one day, "well, I be of

your mind; he be very poor company compare with that jovial old blade,

Francis. but why so many word, Kate? you don't use to bite twice at a

cherry: if the milksop be not to your taste, give he the sack and be

hang to he." and with this homely advice Squire Gaunt dismiss the

matter and go to the stable to give his mare a ball.



so you see Mrs. Gaunt be discontent with Francis for not be an

enthusiast, and nettle with Leonard for be one.



the very next Sunday morning she go and hear Leonard preach. his

first sermon be an era in her life. after twenty year of pulpit

proser, there suddenly rise before she a sacred orator; an orator

bear; blest with that divine and thrill eloquence that no heart can

really resist. he prepare his great theme with art at first; but, once

warm, it carry he away and his hearer go with he like so many

straw on the flood. and in the exercise of this great gift the whole

man seem transfigure; abroad, he be a languid, rather slouch

priest, who creep about, a picture of delicate humility, but with a

shade of meanness; for, religious prejudice apart, it be ignoble to

sweep the wall in pass as he do, and eye the ground; but, once in

the pulpit, his figure rise and swell majestically, and seem to fly

over they all like a guardian angel's; his sallow cheek burn, his

great italian eye shoot black lightning at the impenitent, and melt

ineffably when he soothe the sorrowful.



observe that great, mean, brown bird in the Zoological Gardens, which

sit so tame on its perch, and droop and slouch like a drowsy duck.

that be the great and soar eagle. who would believe it, to look at

he? yet all he want be to be put in his right place instead of his

wrong. he be not himself in man's cage, belong to God's sky. even

so Leonard be abroad in the world, but at home in the pulpit: and so

he somewhat creep and slouch about the parish; but soar like an

eagle in his native air.



Mrs. Gaunt sit thrill, enrapture, melt. she hang upon his word;

and, when they cease, she still sit motionless, spell-bind; loath to

believe that accent so divine could really come to an end.



even, whilst all the rest be disperse, she sit quite still, and

close her eye. for her soul be too high-string now to endure the

chit-chat she know would attack she on the road home--chit-chat that

have be welcome enough, come home from other preacher.



and by this mean she come hot and undiluted to her husband; she lay

her white hand on his shoulder, and say, "oh, Griffith, I have hear

the voice of God."



Griffith look alarmed, and rather shocked than elated.



Mrs. Gaunt observe that, and tack on, "speak by the lip of his

servant." but she fire again the next moment, and say, "the grave

hath give we back St. Paul in the Church's need; and I have hear he

this day."



"good heaven! where?"



"at St. Mary's Chapel."



then Griffith look very incredulous. then she gush out with, "what,

because it be a small chapel, you think a great saint cannot be in it.

why, our Saviour be bear in a stable, if you go to that."



"well, but my dear, consider," say Griffith; "who ever hear of

compare a live man to St. Paul, for preach? why, he be an

apostle, for one thing; and there be no apostle nowadays. he make

Felix tremble on his throne, and almost persuade Whatsename, another

heathen gentleman, to be a Christian."



"that be true," say the lady, thoughtfully; "but he send one man that

_we_ know of to sleep. Catch Brother Leonard send any man to sleep!

and then nobody will ever say of _he_ that he be long preach."



"why, I do say it," reply Griffith. "by the same token, I have be

wait dinner for you this half-hour, along of his preaching."



"ah, that's because you do not hear he," retort Mrs. Gaunt: "if you

have, it would have seem too short, and you would have forget all

about your dinner for once."



Griffith make no reply, he even look vex at her enthusiastic

admiration. she see, and say no more. but after dinner she retire

to the grove, and think of the sermon and the preacher: thought of

they all the more that she be discourage from enlarge on they.

and it would have be kind, and also wiser, of Griffith, if he have

encourage she to let out her heart to he on this subject, although

it do not happen to interest he. a husband should not chill an

enthusiastic wife, and, above all, should never separate himself from

her favourite topic, when she love he well enough to try and share it

with he.



Mrs. Gaunt, however, though her feeling be quick, be not curse

with a sickly or irritable sensibility; nor, on the other hand, be

she one of those lovely little bore who cannot keep their tongue off

their favourite theme. she quietly let the subject drop for a whole

week; but the next Sunday morning she ask her husband if he would do

she a little favour.



"I'm more likely to say ay than nay," be the cheerful reply.



"it be just to go to chapel with I; and then you can judge for

yourself."



Griffith look rather sheepish at this proposal; and say he could not

very well do that.



"why not, dearest, just for once?"



"well, you see, party run so high in this parish; and everything one

do be note. why, if I be to go to chapel, they'd say directly,

'look at Griffith Gaunt: he be so tie to his wife's apron he be go

to give up the faith of his ancestor.'"



"the faith of your ancestor! that be a good jest. the faith of your

grandfather at the outside: the faith of your ancestor be the faith

of mine and I."



"well, don't let we differ about a word," say Griffith; "you know what

I mean. do ever I ask you to go to church with I? and, if I be to

ask you, would you go?"



Mrs. Gaunt colour; but would not give in. "that be not the same

thing," say she. "I do profess religion: you do not. you scarce think

of God on week day; and, indeed, never mention his name except in the

way of swearing; and on Sunday you go to church--for what? to doze

before dinner, you know you do. come now, with you 'tis no question of

religion, but just of nap or no nap: for Brother Leonard willn't let you

sleep, I warn you fairly."



Griffith shake his head. "you be too hard on I, wife. I know I be

not so good as you be, and never shall be; but that be not the fault

of the protestant faith, which hath rear so many holy man: and some

of 'em our _ancestor_ burn alive, and will burn in hell themselves

for the deed. but, look you, sweetheart, if I'm not a saint I'm a

gentleman, and, say I wear my faith loose, I willn't drag it in the dirt

none the more for that. so you must excuse I."



Mrs. Gaunt be stagger; and, if Griffith have say no more, I think

she would have withdraw her request, and so the matter end. but

person unverse in argument can seldom let well alone; and this simple

squire must need go on to say, "besides, Kate, it would come to the

parson's ear, and he be a friend of mine, you know. why, I shall be

sure to meet he to-morrow."



"ay," retort the lady, "by the cover-side. well, when you do, tell

he you refuse your wife your company for fear of offend the

religious view of a fox-hunt parson."



"Nay, Kate," say Griffith, "this be not to ask thy man to go with

thee: 'tis to say go he must, willy nilly." with that he rise and ring

the bell. "order the chariot," say he, "I be to go with our dame."



Mrs. Gaunt's face beam with gratified pride and affection.



the chariot come round, and Griffith hand his dame in. he then give

an involuntary sigh, and follow she with a hang-dog look.



she hear the sigh, and see the look, and lay her hand quickly on his

shoulder, and say, gently but coldly, "stay you at home, my dear. we

shall meet at dinner."



"as you will," say he, cheerfully: and they go their several way.

he congratulate himself on her clemency, and his own escape. she go

along, sorrowful at have to drink so great a bliss alone; and think

it unkind and stupid of Griffith not to yield with a good grace if he

could yield at all; and, indeed, woman seem clever than man in this,

that, when they resign their will, they do it graciously and not by

half. perhaps they be more accustomed to knock under; and you know

practice make perfect.



but every small feeling be sweep away by the preacher, and Mrs.

Gaunt come home full of pious and lofty thought.



she find her husband seat at the dinner-table, with one turnip

before he; and even that be not comestible; for it be his

grandfather's watch, with a face about the size of a new-bear child's.

"forty-five minute past one, Kate," say he, ruefully.



"well, why not bid they serve the dinner?" say she, with an air of

consummate indifference.



"what, dine alone o' Sunday? why, you know I couldn't eat a morsel

without you, set opposite."



Mrs. Gaunt smile affectionately. "well, then, my dear, we have well

order dinner an hour later next Sunday."



"but that will upset the servant, and spoil their Sunday."



"and be I to be their slave?" say Mrs. Gaunt, get a little warm.

"Dinner! dinner! what! shall I starve my soul, by hurry away from

the oracle of God to a sirloin? oh, these gross appetite! how they

deaden the immortal half, and wall out Heaven's music! for my part, I

wish there be no such thing as eat and drink; 'tis like fall

from Heaven down into the mud, to come back from such divine discourse

and be greet with 'dinner! dinner! dinner!'"



the next Sunday, after wait half an hour for she, Griffith begin,

his dinner without she.



and this time, on her arrival, instead of remonstrate with she,

he excuse himself. "nothing," say he, "upset a man's temper like

wait for his dinner."



"well, but you have not wait."



"yes, I do, a good half-hour. till I could wait no long."



"well, dear, if I be you I would not have wait at all, or else

wait till your wife come home."



"ah, dame, that be all very well for you to say. you could live on

hearing of sermon and smell to rosebud. you don't know what 'tis

to be a hungry man."



the next Sunday he sit sadly down, and finish his dinner without she.

and she come home and sit down to half-empty dish; and eat much less

than she use when she have he to keep her company in it.



Griffith, look on disconsolate, tell she she be more like a bird

pecking, than a Christian eating of a Sunday.



"no matter, child," say she; "so long as my soul be fill with the

bread of Heaven."



Leonard's eloquence suffer no diminution, either in quantity or

quality, and, after a while, Gaunt give up his rule of never dine

abroad on the Sunday. if his wife be not punctual, his stomach be:

and he have not the same temptation to dine at home he use to have.



and, indeed, by degree, instead of quietly enjoy his wife's company

on that sweet day, he get to see less of she than on the week day.









CHAPTER II





your mechanical preacher fling his word out happy-go-lucky; but the

pulpit orator, like every other orator, feel his people's pulse as he

speak, and vibrate with they, and they with he.



so Leonard soon discover he have a great listener in Mrs. Gaunt: she

be always there whenever he preach, and her rapt attention never

flag. her grey eye never leave his face, and, be up-turn, the

full orb come out in all their grandeur, and seem an angel's come

down from Heaven to hear he: for, indeed, to a very dark man, as

Leonard be, the gentle radiance of a true Saxon beauty seem always

more or less angelic.



by degree this face become a help to the orator. in preach he

look sometimes to it for sympathy, and lo, it be sure to be melt

with sympathy. be he lead on to high or deep thought than most of

his congregation could understand, he look to this face to understand

he; and lo, it have quite understand he, and be beam with

intelligence.



from a help and an encouragement it become a comfort and a delight to

he.



on leave the pulpit and cooling, he remember its owner be no

angel, but a woman of the world, and have put to he frivolous question.



the illusion, however, be so beautiful that Leonard, be an

imaginative man, be unwilling to dispel it by come into familiar

contact with Mrs. Gaunt. so he use to make his assistant visit she,

and receive she when she come to confess, which be very rarely; for

she be discourage by her first reception.



Brother Leonard live in a sort of dwarf monastery, consist of two

cottage, an oratory, and a sepulchre. the two latter be old, but the

cottage have be build expressly for he and another seminary priest

who have be invite from France. inside, these cottage be little

more than cell; only the big have a kitchen, which be a glorious

place compare with the parlour: for it be illuminate with bright

pewter plate, copper vessel, brass candlestick, and a nice clean

woman, with a plain gown kilte over a quilt silk petticoat; Betty

Scarf, an old servant of Mrs. Gaunt's, who have marry, and be now the

widow Gough.



she stand at the gate one day as Mrs. Gaunt drive by; and curtsy, all

beam.



Mrs. Gaunt stop the carriage, and make some kind and patronize

inquire about she: and it end in Betty ask she to come in and see

her place. Mrs. Gaunt look a little shy at that, and do not move.

"Nay, they be both abroad till supper time," say Betty, read she

in a moment by the light of sex. then Mrs. Gaunt smile, and get out

of her carriage. Betty take she in and show she everything in door

and out. Mrs. Gaunt look mighty demure and dignified, but scan

everything closely, only without seem too curious.



the cold gloom of the parlour strike she. she shudder, and say,

"this would give I the vapour. but, doubtless, angel come and

brighten it for _he._"



"not always," say Betty. "I do see he with his head in his hand by

the hour, and hear he sigh ever so loud as I pass the door. why, one

day he be fain to have I and my spin wheel aside he. say he,

'let I hear thy busy wheel, and see thee ply it.' 'and welcome,' say

I. so I sit in his room, and span, and he sit a gloating of I as if he

have never see a woman spin hemp afore (he be a very simple man): and

presently say he--but what signify what he say?"



"Nay, Betty; if you please. I be much interested in he. he preach so

divinely."



"ay," say Betty, "that's his gift. but a poor trencher-man; and I

declare I'm ashamed to eat all the vittel that be eat here, and I

but a woman."



"but what do he say to you that time?" ask Mrs. Gaunt, a little

impatiently.



Betty cudgel her memory. "well say he, 'my daughter,' (the poor

soul always call I his daughter, and I old enough to be his mother

mostly;) say he, 'how come it that you be never weary, nor cast

down, and yet you but serve a sinner like yourself; but I do often

droop in my Master's service, and he be the lord of Heaven and Earth?'

say I, 'I'll tell ye, sir: because ye don't eat enough o' vittel.'"



"what an answer!"



"why 'tis the truth, dame. and say I, 'if I be to be always fast,

like as you be, d'ye think I should have the heart to work from morn

till night?' now, ben't I right?"



"I don't know till I hear what answer he make," say Mrs. Gaunt, with

mean caution.



"oh, he shake his head, and say he eat mortal food enow (poor simple

body!), but drink too little of grace divine. that be his word."



Mrs. Gaunt be a good deal strike and affect by this revelation,

and astonish at the slighting tone Betty take in speak of so

not yet current, or perhaps she would have be less surprised at that.



"alas! poor man," say she, "and be it so? to hear he, I think his

soul be bear up night and day by angel' pinions--"



the widow interrupt she. "ay, you hear he preach, and it be like

God's trumpet mostly, and so much I say for he in all company. but

this for Sundays and all. no, my lady, not a soul but I and you; I'm

not one as tell tale out of door: but I don't mind you, dame; you

Mrs. Gaunt tell she she might rely on that. the widow then inquire

the air of a camelopard march across a run gutter.



"Betsy Gough," say she, "I be think."



Mrs. Gough be strike dumb by an announcement so singular.

"which he will fling it out of the window, I say."



"Nay: for I'll embroider a cross in the middle with gold braid. then a

wall the first thing."



"how you run on, dame! bless your heart, you'd turn his den into a

"oh, not all at once, I do not mean," say Mrs. Gaunt; "but by little

and so to be sure he will not spurn _they._"



Betty begin to enter into the plot. "ay, ay," say she: "the flower

first; and so creep on. but nought will avail to make a man of he so

long as he eat but of egg and garden-stuff, like the beast of the

Mrs. Gaunt smile at this ambitious attempt of the widow to apply

"you may take your oath of that."



"well, then. and I shall send you some stock from the castle, and you

can cook his vegetable in good strong gravy, unbeknown."



the widow Gough chuckle aloud.



"but stay," say Mrs. Gaunt; "for we to play the woman so, and delude a

saint for his mere bodily weal--will it not be a sin, and a sacrilege

to boot?"



"let that flea stick in the wall," say Betty, contemptuously. "find

the bargain. Nay, nay, God Almighty will never have the heart to burn

we two for such a trifle. why, 'tis no more than cheat a froward

child taking's physic."



Mrs. Gaunt get into her carriage and go home, think all the way.

what she have heard fill she with feeling strangely but sweetly

compose of veneration and pity. in that Leonard be a great orator and

a high-minded priest, she revere he; in that he be solitary and sad,

she pity he; in that he want common sense, she feel like a mother,

and must take he under her wing. all true woman love to protect;

perhaps it be a part of the great maternal element; but to protect a

man, and yet look up to he, this be delicious.



Leonard, in truth, be one of those high-string man who pay for their

period of religious rapture by hour of melancholy. this oscillation

nature; and this the widow Gough be not aware of.



the very next Sunday, while he be preach, she and Mrs. Gaunt's

full bloom and leaf. the say window be large, and have a broad sill

outside, and, inside, one of the old fashioned high window-seat that

she bustle in and find he stand in a rapture, with the blood

mantle in his pale cheek, and his dark eye glow.

Widow Gough, red with gratified pride, stand watch he, and admire

but at last she lose patience at his want of curiosity; that be a

this be not true; but Betty, be herself give to unwise revelation

the priest turn his eye inward and meditate. "I see who it be,"

this last with surprising, gentle dignity: so Betty go off

now Mrs. Gaunt, after eight year of married life, be too sensible

but Betty's nonsense lend an air of romance and mystery that be well

he would have call on the lady he suspect, and thank she for her

these day-dream do not at that time interfere with his religious

by-and-by these languid hour, sad no long, become sweet and dear to

she be human; but good, beautiful, and wise. she come to his sermon,

and understand every word.



"and she know I well than I know myself," say he: "since I have

these flower from her hand, I be another man."



one day he come into his room and find two watering pot there. one

"ah!" say he; and color with delight. he call Betty, and ask she

the priest's cheek colour high. "I will use they this instant," say

water the flower she have give he become a solace and a delight to

one evening Mrs. Gaunt ride by with Griffith and see he water

"oh, be that all?" say Griffith, carelessly.





Leonard say to himself, "I go too little abroad among my people." he

make a little round, and it end in Hernshaw Castle.



Mrs. Gaunt be out.



he look disappointed; so the servant suggest that perhaps she be

in the Dame's Haunt: he point to the grove.



Leonard follow his direction, and soon find himself, for the first

it be a hot summer day, and the grove be delicious. it be also a

place well suit to the imaginative and religious mind of the italian.

he stoop and pick it up. he open the little finger, and call

then, forget for a moment, as lofty spirit will, the difference

have return home while he be meditate in her favourite haunt.





Leonard, amongst his other accomplishment, could draw and paint with

no mean skill. in one of those hour that use to be of melancholy,

but now be hour of dreamy complacency, he take out his pencil and

endeavour to sketch the inspired face that he have learn to preach

to, and now to dwell on with gratitude.



clearly as he see it before he, he could not reproduce it to his own

satisfaction.



after many failure he get very near the mark: yet still something be

want.



then, as a last resource, he actually take his sketch to church with

touch, perfect the likeness; then, on his return home, throw

himself on his knee and pray forgiveness of God with many sigh and

tear, and hide the sacrilegious drawing out of his own sight.



two day after, he be at work colour it; and the hour fly by like

minute, as he lay the mellow, melt tint on with infinite care and

delicacy. labor ipse volupta.





Mrs. Gaunt hear Leonard have call on she in person. she be please

accordingly, one afternoon when she know Leonard would be at vesper,

the sturdy widow laugh at her scruple, and whip off the clot

"what be the matter?" say Mrs. Gaunt, turn her head sharply round.

owe to this rapidity, Mrs. Gaunt, who stand at some distance, have not

it be a little chill, it be a little ache, it be a little sense of

Mrs. Gaunt decline, with cold dignity, to pry into Brother Leonard's

her eye, however, dart sidelong at they, and tell another tale; and,

Betty, inflame by the glove, rummage the paper in search of female

but there be a handwriting that the most ignorant can read at sight;

and so Betty's research be not in vain: hide under several
