red-haired--Lawk a daisy! why, 'tis yourself."









CHAPTER III





"I!" cry Mrs. Gaunt, in amazement: then she run to the picture, and

at sight of it every other sentiment give way for a moment to gratify

vanity. "Nay," say she, beam and blushing, "I be never half so

beautiful. what heavenly eye!"



"the fellow to 'em be in your own head, dame, this moment."



"seeing be believe," say Mrs. Gaunt, gaily, and in a moment she be

at the priest's mirror, and inspect her eye minutely, cock she

head this way and that. she end by shake it, and say, "Nay. he

have flatter they prodigiously."



"not a jot," say Betty. "if you could see yourself in chapel, you do

turn 'em up just so, and the white show all round." then she tap

the picture with her finger: "oh they eye! they be never make for

the good of his soul; poor simple man."



Betty say this with sudden gravity: and now Mrs. Gaunt begin to feel

very awkward. "Mr. Gaunt would give fifty pound for this," say she,

to gain time: and, while she utter that sentence, she whip on her

armour.



"I'll tell you what I think," say she, calmly: "he wish to paint

a Madonna; and he must take some woman's face to aid his fancy. all

the painter be drive to that. so he just take the good that come

to hand, and that be not say much, for this be a rare ill-favour

parish: and he have make an angel of she, a very angel. there, hide I

away again, or I shall long for I--to show to my husband. I must be

go; I wouldn't be catch here now for a pension."



"well, if ye must," say Betty; "but when will ye come again?" (she

haven't get the petticoat yet.)



"Humph!" say Mrs. Gaunt, "I have do all I can for he; and perhaps

more than I ought. but there's nothing to hinder you from come to I.

I'll be as good as my word; and I have an old Paduasoy, besides; you

can do something with it perhaps."



"you be very good, dame," say Betty, curtsying.



Mrs. Gaunt then hurry away, and Betty look after her very

expressively, and shake her head. she have a female instinct that

mischief be brew.



Mrs. Gaunt go home in a reverie.



at the gate she find her husband, and ask he to take a turn in the

garden with she.



he comply; and she intend to tell he a portion, at least, of

what have occur. she begin timidly, after this fashion----"My dear,

Brother Leonard be so grateful for your flower," and then hesitate.



"I'm sure he be very welcome," say Griffith. "why don't he sup with

we and be sociable, as Father Francis use? invite he; let he know he

will be welcome."



Sirs. Gaunt blush; and object, "he never call on we."



"well, well, every man to his taste," say Griffith, indifferently, and

proceed to talk to she about his farm, and a sorrel mare with a white

mane and tail, that he have see, and think it would suit she.



she humour he, and affect a great interest in all this, and have

not the courage to force the other topic on.



Next Sunday morning, after a very silent breakfast, she burst out,

almost violently, "Griffith, I shall go to the parish church with you,

and then we will dine together afterwards."



"you don't mean it, Kate?" say he, delight. "ay, but I do. although

you refuse to go to chapel with I."



they go to church together, and Mrs. Gaunt's appearance there

create no small sensation. she be conscious of that, but hide it, and

conduct herself admirably. her mind seem entirely give to the

service, and to a dull sermon that follow.



but at dinner she break out, "well, give I your church for a sleep

draught. you all slumber, more or less: those that survive the

drowsy, drone prayer, sink under the dry, dull dreary discourse. you

snore, for one."



"Nay, I hope not, my dear."



"you do, then, as loud as your bass fiddle."



"and you sit there and let I!" say Griffith, reproachfully.



"to be sure I do. I be too good a wife, and too good a Christian, to

wake you. sleep be good for the body, and twaddle be not good for the

soul. I'd have sleep too, if I could; but, with I go to chapel, I'm

not use to sleep at that time o' day. you cann't sleep, and Brother

Leonard speak."



in the afternoon come Mrs. Gough, all in her good. Mrs. Gaunt have she

into her bedroom, and give she the promise petticoat, and the old

Peau de soie gown; and then, as lady will, when their hand be once

in, add first one thing, then another, till there be quite a large

bundle.



"but how be it you be here so soon?" ask Mrs. Gaunt.



"oh, we have next to no sermon to-day. he couldn't make no hand of it:

dawdle on a bit; then give we his blessing, and bundle we out."



"then I've lose nothing," say Mrs. Gaunt.



"not you. well, I don't know. Mayhap if you have be there he'd have

preach his good. but la, we ben't worth it."



at this conjecture Mrs. Gaunt's face burn; but she say nothing: only

she cut the interview short, and dismiss Betty with her bundle.



as Betty cross the landing, Mrs. Gaunt's new lady's-maid, Caroline

Ryder, step accidentally, on purpose, out of an adjoining room, in

which she have be lurk, and lift her black brow in affected

surprise. "what, be you go to strip the house, my woman?" say she,

quietly.



Betty put down the bundle, and set her arm akimbo. "there be none on't

steal, any way," say she.



Caroline's black eye flash fire at this, and her cheek lose colour;

but she parry the innuendo skilfully.



"take my perquisite on the sly, that be not so very far from

stealing."



"oh, there's plenty leave for you, my fine lady. besides, you don't want

she; you can set your cap at the master, they say. I'm too old for

that, and too honest into the bargain."



"too ill-favour, you mean, ye old harridan," say Ryder,

contemptuously.



but, for reason hereafter to be deal with. Betty's thrust go home:

and the pair be mortal enemy from that hour.



Mrs. Gaunt come down from her room discompose: from that she become

restless and irritable; so much so, indeed, that, at last, Sir. Gaunt

tell she, good-humouredly enough, if go to church make she ill

(mean peevish), she have well go to chapel. "you be right," say

she, "and so I will."



the next Sunday she be at her post in good time.



the preacher cast an anxious glance around to see if she be there.

her quick eye see that glance, and it give she a demure pleasure.



this day he be more eloquent than ever: and he deliver a beautiful

passage concern those who do good in secret. in utter these

eloquent sentence, his cheek glow, and he could not deny himself the

pleasure of look down at the lovely face that be turn up to he.

probably his look be more expressive than he intend: the celestial

eye sink under it, and be abash, and the fair cheek burn: and

then so do Leonard's at that.



thus, subtly yet effectually, do these two mind communicate in a

crowd, that never notice nor suspect the delicate interchange of

sentiment that be go on under their very eye.



in a general way compliment do not seduce Mrs. Gaunt: she be well

use to they, for one thing. put to be praise in that sacred edifice,

'and from the pulpit, and by such an orator as Leonard, and to be

praise in word so sacred and beautiful, that the ear around she

drink they with delight, all this make her heart beat, and fill she

with soft and sweet complacency.



and then to be thank in public, yet, as it be, clandestinely, this

gratify the furtive tendency of woman.



there be no irritability this afternoon; but a gentle radiance that

diffuse itself on all around, and make the whole household happy;

especially Griffith, whose pipe she fill, for once, with her own

white hand, and talk dog, horse, calf, hind, cow, politic,

market, hay, to please he: and seem interested in they all.



but the next day she change: ill at ease, and out of spirit, and

could settle to nothing.



it be very hot for one thing: and, altogether, a sort of lassitude

and distaste for everything overpower she, and she retire into the

grove, and sit languidly on a seat with half-close eye.



but her meditation be no long so calm and speculative as

heretofore. she find her mind constantly recur to one person,

and, above all, to the discovery she have make of her portrait in his

possession. she have turn it off to Betty Gough; but here, in her calm

solitude and umbrageous twilight, her mind creep out of its cave, like

wild and timid thing at dusk, and whisper to her heart that Leonard

perhaps admire she more than be safe or prudent.



then this alarm she, yet cause she a secret complacency: and that,

her furtive satisfaction, alarm she still more.



now, while she sit thus absorb, she hear a gentle footstep come

near. she look up, and there be Leonard close to she; stand

meekly with his arm cross upon his bosom.



his be there so pat upon her thought, scare she out of her

habitual self-command. she start up, with a faint cry, and stand

panting, as if about to fly, with, her beautiful eye turn large

upon he.



he put forth a deprecate hand, and soothe she. "forgive I, madam,"

say he; "I have unaware intrude on your privacy; I will retire."



"Nay," say she, falteringly, "you be welcome. but no one come here;

so I be startle;" then, recover herself, "excuse my ill-manner.

'tis so strange you should come to I here, of all place."



"Nay, my daughter," say the priest, "not so very strange:

contemplative mind love such place. call one day to see you, I

find this sweet and solemn grove; the like I never see in England: and

to-day I return in hope to profit by it. do but look around at these

tall column; how calm, how reverend! 'tis God's own temple not build

with hand."



"indeed it be," say Mrs. Gaunt, earnestly. then, like a woman as she

be, "so you come to see my tree, not I."



Leonard blush. "I do not design to return without pay my respect

to she who own this temple, and be worthy of it; nay, I beg you not to

think I ungrateful."



his humility, and gentle but earnest voice, make Mrs. Gaunt ashamed of

her petulance. she smile sweetly, and look pleased. however, ere

long, she attack he again. "Father Francis use to visit we often,"

say she. "he make friend with my husband, too. and I never lack an

adviser while he be here."



Leonard look so confused at this second reproach that Mrs. Gaunt

regret having utter it. then he say humbly that Francis be a

secular priest, whereas he be convent-breed. he add, that by his

year and experience Francis be well fit to advise person of her

age and sex, in matter secular, than he be. he conclude timidly that

he be ready, nevertheless, to try and advise she; but could not, in

such matter, assume the authority that belong to age and knowledge

of the world.



"Nay, nay," say she, earnestly, "guide and direct my soul, and I be

content."



he say, yes! that be his duty and his right.



then, after a certain hesitation, which at once let she know what be

come, he begin to thank she, with infinite grace and sweetness, for

her kindness to he.



she look he full in the face, and say she be not aware of any

kindness she have show he worth speak of.



"that but show," say he, "how natural it be to you to do act of

goodness. my poor room be a very bower now, and I be happy in it. I

use to feel very sad there at time; but your hand have cure I."



Mrs. Gaunt colour beautifully. "you make I ashamed," say she.

"thing be come to a pass indeed if a lady may not send a few flower

and thing to her spiritual father without be--thank for it. and,

oh, sir, what be earthly flower compare with those blossom of the

soul you have shed so liberally over we? our immortal part be all

asleep when you come here, and waken they by the fire of your word.

Eloquence! 'twas a thing I have read of, but never hear, nor think to

hear. methought the orator and poet of the Church be all in their

grave this thousand year, and she must go all the way to heaven, that

would hear the soul's true music. but I know well now."



Leonard colour high with pleasure. "such praise from you be too

sweet," he mutter. "I must not court it. the heart be full of

vanity." and he deprecate further eulogy, by a movement of the hand

extremely refined and, in fact, rather feminine.



defer to his wish, Mrs. Gaunt glide to other matter, and be

naturally lead to speak of the prospect of their church, and the

possibility of reconvert these island. this have be the dream of

her young heart; but marriage and maternity, and the universal coldness

with which the subject have be receive, have chill she so, that of

late year she have almost cease to speak of it. even Leonard, on a

former occasion, have listen coldly to she; but now his heart be open

to she. he be, in fact, quite as enthusiastic on this point as ever

she have be; and then he have digest his aspiration into clear

form. not only have he resolve that Great Britain must be reconvert,

but have plan the way to do it. his cheek glow, his eye gleam,

and he pour out his hope and his plan before she with an eloquence

that few mortal could have resist.



as for this, his hearer, she be quite carry away by it. she join

herself to his plan on the spot; she beg, with tear in her eye,

to be permit to support he in this great cause. she devote to it

her substance, her influence, and every gift that God have give she:

the hour pass like minute in this high converse; and, when the

tinkling of the little bell at a distance summon he to vesper, he

leave she with a gentle regret he scarcely try to conceal, and she

go slowly in like one in a dream, and the world seem dead to she

for ever.



nevertheless, when Mrs. Ryder, comb out her long hair, give one

inadvertent tug, the fair enthusiast come back to earth, and ask she,

rather sharply, who her head be run on.



Ryder, a very handsome young woman, with fine black eye, make no

reply, but only draw her breath audibly hard.



I do not very much wonder at that, nor at my have to answer that

question for Mrs. Ryder. for her head be at that moment run, like

any other woman's, on the man she be in love with.



and the man she be in love with be the husband of the lady, whose

hair she be comb, and who put she that curious question--plump.









CHAPTER IV.





this Caroline Ryder be a character almost impossible to present so as

to enable the reader to recognize she should she cross his path: so

great be the contradiction between what she be, and what she seem;

and so perfect be the imitation.



she look a respectable young spinster, with a grace of manner beyond

her station, and a decency and propriety of demeanour that inspire

respect.



she be a married woman, separate from her husband by mutual consent:

and she have have many lover, each of whom she have love ardently--for

a little while. she be a woman that bring to bear upon foolish,

culpable, love, a mental power that would have adorn the wool-sack.



the moment prudence or wane inclination make it advisable to break

with the reign favourite, she set to work to cool he down by

deliberate coldness, sullenness, insolence; and generally succeed.

but, if he be incurable, she never hesitate as to her course;

she smile again on he and look out for another place: be an

invaluable servant, she get one directly; and be off to fresh pasture.



a female rake; but with the air of a very prude.



still the decency and propriety of her demeanour be not all

hypocrisy, but half hypocrisy, and half inborn and instinctive good

taste and good sense.



as dangerous a creature to herself and other as ever tie on a bonnet.



on her arrival at Hernshaw Castle she cast her eye round to see what

there be to fall in love with; and observe the gamekeeper, Tom

Leicester. she give he a smile or two that win his heart; but there

she stop: for soon the ruddy cheek, brown eye, manly proportion,

and square shoulder of her master attract this connoisseur in

masculine beauty. and then his manner be so genial and hearty, with a

smile for everybody. Mrs. Ryder eye he demurely day by day, and often

open a window slily to watch he unseen.



from that she get to throw herself in his way: and this with such

art that he never discover it, though he fall in with she about the

house six time as often as he meet his wife or any other inmate.



she have already study his character, and, whether she arrange

to meet he full, or to cross he, it be always with a curtsy and

a sunshiny smile; he smile on she in his turn, and feel a certain

pleasure at sight of she: for he love to see people bright and

cheerful about he.



then she do, of her own accord, what no other master on earth would

have persuade she to do: look over his linen; sew on button

for he; and sometimes the artful jade deliberately cut a button off

a clean shirt, and then come to he and sew it on during wear. this

bring about a contact none know well than she how to manage to a

man's undoing. the eyelash lower over her work, deprecate, yet

inviting,--the twenty stitch, when six would have done,--the one coy

glance at leave. all this soft witchcraft beset Griffith Gaunt, and

tell on he; but not as yet in the way his inamorata intend. "Kate,"

say he one day, "that girl of yours be worth her weight in gold."



"indeed!" say 'Mrs. Gaunt, frigidly; "I have not discover it."



when Caroline find that her master be single-hearted, and love his

wife too well to look elsewhere, instead of hate he, she begin to

love he more seriously, and to hate his wife, that haughty beauty who

take such a husband as a matter of course, and hold he tight without

trouble her head.



it be a coarse age, and in that very county more than one wife have

suffer jealous agony from her own domestic. but here the part be

invert: the lady be at her ease; the servant pay a bitter penalty

for her folly. she be now passionately in love, and have to do menial

office for her rival every hour of the day: she must sit with Mrs.

Gaunt, and make her dress, and consult with she how to set off her

hateful beauty to the good advantage. she have to dress she, and look

dagger at her satin skin and royal neck, and to sit behind she an hour

at a time comb and brush her long golden hair.



how she long to tear a handful of it out, and then run away! instead

of that, her happy rival expect she to be as tender and coax with

it as Madame de Maintenon be with the Queen's of France.



Ryder call it "yellow stuff" down in the kitchen; that be one

comfort: but a feeble one; the sun come in at the lady's window, and

Ryder's shapely hand be overflow, and her eye offend, by wave of

burnish gold: and one day Griffith come in and kiss it in her very

hand. his lip feel nothing but his wife's glorious hair; but, by that

exquisite sensibility which the heart can convey in a moment to the

very fingernail, Caroline's hand, beneath, feel the soft touch through

her mistress's hair; and the enamoured hypocrite thrill, and then

sicken at her own folly.



for in her good sense could be overpower, but never long blind.



on the day in question she be think of Griffith, as usual, and

wonder whether he would always prefer yellow hair to black. this

actually put she off her guard for once, and she give the rival hair a

little contemptuous tug: and the reader know what follow.



stagger by her mistress's question, Caroline make no reply, but only

pant a little, and proceed more carefully.



but, oh the struggle it cost she not to slap both Mrs. Gaunt's fair

cheek with the back of the brush! and what with this struggle, and

the reprimand, and the past agitation, by-and-by the comb cease, and

the silence be break by faint sob.



Mrs. Gaunt turn calmly round and look full at her hysterical

handmaid.



"what be to do?" say she. "be it because I chid you, child? Nay, you

need not take that to heart; it be just my way: I can bear anything

but my hair pull." with this she rise and pour some drop of

sal-volatile into water, and put it to her secret rival's lip: it be

kindly do, but with that sort of half contemptuous and thoroughly

cold pity woman be apt to show to woman, and especially when one of

they be Mistress and the other be servant.



still it cool the extreme hatred Caroline have nurse, and give she a

little twinge, and awaken her intelligence. now her intelligence be

truly remarkable when not blind by passion. she be a woman with one

or two other masculine trait beside her roving heart. for instance,

she could sit and think hard and practically for hour together: and

on these occasion her thought be never dreamy and vague; it be

no brown study, but good hard thinking. she would knit her coal-black

brow, like Lord Thurlow himself, and realize the situation, and weigh

the pro and con with a steady judicial power rarely find in her sex:

and, nota bene, when once her mind have go through this process, then

she would act with almost monstrous resolution.



she now shut herself up in her own room for some hour and weigh the

matter carefully.



the conclusion she arrive at be this: that, if she stay at Hernshaw

Castle, there would be mischief; and probably she herself would be the

principal sufferer to the end of the chapter, as she be now.



she say to herself, "I shall go mad, or else expose myself, and be

turn away with loss of character; and then what will become of I,

and my child? well lose life or reason than character. I know what I

have to go through; I have leave a man ere now with my heart tug at

I to stay beside he. it be a terrible wrench: and then all seem dead

for a long while without he. but the world go on and take you round

with it; and by-and-by you find there be as good fish leave in the sea

as ever come out on't. I'll go, while I've sense enough leave to see I

must."



the very next day she come to Mrs. Gaunt and say she wish to leave.

"certainly," say Mrs. Gaunt, coldly. "may I ask the reason?"



"oh, I have no complaint to make, ma'am, none whatever; but I be not

happy here; and I wish to go when my month's up, or soon, ma'am, if

you could suit yourself."



Mrs. Gaunt consider a moment: then she say, "you come all the way

from Gloucestershire to I: have you not well give the place a fair

trial? I have have two or three good servant that feel uncomfortable

at first; but they soon find out my way, and stay with I till

they marry. as for leave I before your month, that be out of the

question." to this Ryder say not a word, but merely vent a little

sigh, half dog, half submissive; and go cat-like about, arrange

her mistress's thing with admirable precision and neatness. Mrs. Gaunt

watch she, without seem to do so, and observe that her discontent

do not in the least affect her punctual discharge of her duty. say

Mrs. Gaunt to herself, "this servant be a treasure: she shall not go."

and Ryder to herself, "well, 'tis but for a month; and then no power

shall keep I here."









CHAPTER V.





not long after these event come the county ball. Griffith be there,

but no Mrs. Gaunt. this excited surprise, and, among the gentleman,

disappointment. they ask Griffith if she be unwell; he thank they

drily, she be very well; and that be all they could get out of he.

but to the lady he let out that she have give up ball, and, indeed,

all reasonable pleasure. "she do nothing but fast, and pray, and

visit the sick." he add, with rather a weak smile: "I see next to

nothing of she." a minx stand by and put in her word. "you should catch

the small-pox; then who know? she might look in upon _you_."



Griffith laugh, but not heartily. in truth, Mrs. Gaunt's religious

fervour know no bound. absorb in pious scheme and religious duty,

she have little time, and much distaste, for frivolous society; invite

none but the devout, and find polite excuse for not dine abroad.

she send her husband into the world alone, and laden with apology.

"my wife be turn saint. 'tis a sin to dance, a sin to hunt, a sin to

enjoy ourselves. we be here to fast and pray, and build school, and

go to church twice a day."



and so he go about publish his household ill; but, to tell the

truth, a secret satisfaction peep through his lugubrious accent. an

ugly saint be an unmixed calamity to jolly fellow; but to be lord and

master, and possessor of a beautiful saint, be not without its piquant

charm. his jealousy be dormant, not extinct; and Kate's piety tickle

that foible, not wound it. he find himself the rival of heaven; and

the successful rival; for, let she be ever so strict, ever so devout,

she must give her husband many comfort she could not give to heaven.



this soft and piquant phase of the passion do not last long. all

thing be progressive.



Brother Leonard be director now as well as confessor; his visit

become frequent; and Mrs. Gaunt often quote his authority for her act

or her sentiment. so Griffith begin to suspect that the change in

his wife be entirely due to Leonard; and that with all her eloquence

and fervour she be but a priest's echo. this gall he. to be sure

Leonard be only an ecclesiastic; but, if he have be a woman, Griffith

be the man to wince. his wife to lean so on another: his wife to

withdraw from the social pleasure she have hitherto share with he;

and all because another human creature disapprove they. he writhe

in silence a while, and then remonstrate. he be meet at first with

ridicule: "be you go to be jealous of my confessor?" and, on

repeat the offence, with a kind, but grave admonition, that silence

he for the time, but do not cure he, nor even convince he.



the fact be too strong: Kate be no long to he the genial

companion she have be; go be the ready sympathy with which she

have listen to all his little earthly concern; and, as for his

hay-making, he might as well talk about it to an iceberg as to the

partner of his bosom.



he be genial by nature, and could not live without sympathy. he seek

it in the parlour of the "Red Lion."



Mrs. Gaunt's high-breed nostril tell she where he haunt, and it

cause her dismay. woman-like, instead of open her battery at once,

she wear a gloomy and displeased air, which a few month ago would have

serve her turn and bring about an explanation at once; but Griffith

take it for a strong dose of religious sentiment, and trundle off

to the "Red Lion," all the more.



so then at last she speak her mind; and ask he how he could lower

himself so, and afflict she.



"oh!" say he, doggedly, "this house be too cold for I now. my mate be

priest-rid. plague on the knave that hath put coldness 'twixt thee and

I."



Mrs. Gaunt freeze visibly, and say no more at that time.



one bit of sunshine remain in the house and shine brighter than ever

on its chilled master; shine through two black, seduce eye.



some three month before the date we have now reach, Caroline

Ryder's two box be pack and cord ready to go next day. she have

quietly persist in her resolution to leave, and Mrs. Gaunt, though

secretly angry, have be just and magnanimous enough to give she a good

character.



now female domestic be like the little bird; if that great hawk,

their mistress, follow they about, it be a deadly grievance; but

if she do not, they follow she about, and pester she with idle

question, and invite the beak and claw of petty tyranny and needless

interference.



so the afternoon before she be to leave, Caroline Ryder come to her

mistress's room on some imaginary business. she be not there. Ryder,

forget that it do not matter a straw, proceed to hunt she

everywhere; and at last run out with only her cap on to "the Dame's

Haunt," and there she be; but not alone: she be walk up and down

with Brother Leonard. their back be turn, and Ryder come up behind

they. Leonard be pace gravely, with his head gently droop as

usual. Mrs. Gaunt be walk elastically, and discourse with great

fire and animation.



Ryder glide after, noiseless as a serpent, more bent on wonder and

watch now than on overtake; for inside the house her mistress

show none of this charming vivacity.



presently the keen black eye observe a "trifle light as air" that

make they shine again.



she turn and wound herself amongst the tree, and disappear. soon

after she be in her own room, a change woman. with glowing cheek,

sparkle eye, and nimble finger, she uncorde her box, unpacked

her thing, and place they neatly in the drawer.



what more have she see than I have indicate?



only this: Mrs. Gaunt, in the warmth of discourse, lay her hand

lightly for a moment on the priest's elbow: that be nothing, she have

lay the same hand on Ryder; for, in fact, it be a little womanly

way she have, and a hand that settle like down. but this time, as she

withdraw it again, that delicate hand seem to speak; it do not leave

Leonard's elbow all at once, it glide slowly away, first the palm,

then the finger, and so part lingeringly.



the other woman see this subtle touch of womanhood, couple it with

Mrs. Gaunt's vivacity and the air of happiness that seem to inspire

her whole eloquent person, and form a harsh judgment on the spot,

though she could not see the lady's face.



when Mrs. Gaunt come in she meet she, and address she thus: "if you

please, ma'am, have you any one come in my place?"



Mrs. Gaunt look her full in the face. "you know I have not," say

she, haughtily.



"then, if it be agreeable to you, ma'am, I will stay. to be sure the

place be dull; but I have get a good mistress-and----"



"that will do, Ryder: a servant have always her own reason, and never

tell they to her mistress. you can stay this time; but the next, you

go; and once for all. I be not to be trifle with."



Ryder call up a look all submission, and retire with an obeisance.

but, once out of sight, she throw off the mask and expand with

insolent triumph. "yes, I have my own reason," say she. "keep you the

priest, and I'll take the man."



from that hour Caroline Ryder watch her mistress like a lynx, and

hover about her master, and poison he slowly with vague insidious

hint.









CHAPTER VI.





Brother Leonard, like many holy man, be vain. not but what he have his

gust of humility and diffidence; only they blow over.



at first, as you may perhaps remember, he doubt his ability to

replace Father Francis as Mrs. Gaunt's director; but after a slight

disclaimer, he do replace he, and have no more misgiving as to his

fitness. but his tolerance and good sense be by no mean equal to

his devotion and his persuasive power; and so his advice in matter

spiritual and secular somehow sow the first seed of conjugal

coolness in Hernshaw Castle.



and now Ryder slily insinuate into Griffith's ear that the mistress

tell the priest everything, and do nothing but by his advice. thus the

fire already kindle be fan by an artful woman's breath.



Griffith begin to hate Brother Leonard, and to show it so plainly and

rudely that Leonard shrink from the encounter, and come less often,

and stay but a few minute. then Mrs. Gaunt remonstrate gently with

Griffith, but receive short sullen reply. then, as the servile

element of her sex be comparatively small in she, she turn bitter

and cold, and avenge Leonard indirectly, but openly, with those

terrible pin and needle a beloved woman have ever at command.



then Griffith become moody, and downright unhappy, and go more and

more to the "Red Lion," seek comfort there now as well as company.



Mrs. Gaunt see, and have fit of irritation, and fit of pity, and sore

perplexity. she know she have a good husband; and, instead of take he

to heaven with she, she find that each step she make with Leonard's

help towards the angelic life, seem somehow to be bad for Griffith's

soul, and for his earthly happiness.



she blame herself; she blame Griffith; she blame the protestant

heresy; she blame everybody and everything--except Brother Leonard.



one Sunday afternoon Griffith sit on his own lawn, silently smoke his

pipe. Mrs. Gaunt come to he, and see an air of dejection on his genial

face. her heart yearn. she sit down beside he on the bench, and

sigh; then he sigh too.



"my dear," say she, sweetly, "fetch out your viol da gambo, and we

will sing a hymn or two together here this fine afternoon. we can

praise God together, though we must pray apart; alas that it be so."



"with all my heart," say Griffith. "Nay, I forget; my viol da gambo be

not here. 'tis at the 'Red Lion.'"



"at the 'Red Lion!'" say she, bitterly. "what, do you sing there as

well as drink? oh, husband, how can you so bemean yourself?"



"what be a poor man to do, whose wife be priest-ride, and get to be

no company--except for angel?"



"I do not come here to quarrel," say she, coldly and sadly. then they

be both silent a minute. then she get up and leave he.



Brother Leonard, like many earnest man, be rather intolerant. he urge

on Mrs. Gaunt that she have too many Protestants in her household: she

cook and her nursemaid ought, at all event, to be Catholics. Mrs.

Gaunt on this be quite ready to turn they both off, and that without

disguise. but Leonard dissuade she from so violent a measure. she have

well take occasion to part with one of they, and by-and-by with the

other.



the nursemaid be the first to go, and her place be fill by a roman

Catholic. then the cook receive warning. but this do not pass off so

quietly: Jane Bannister be a buxom hearty woman, well like by her

fellow-servant; her parent live in the village, and she have be

six year with the Gaunts, and her honest heart clung to they. she

take to crying; use to burst out in the middle of her work, or while

converse with fitful cheerfulness on ordinary topic.



one day Griffith find her crying, and Ryder console she as

carelessly and contemptuously as possible.



"hey-day, lass," say he; "what be your trouble?"



at this Jane's tear flow in a stream, and Ryder make no reply, but

wait.



at last, and not till the third or fourth time of ask, Jane

blurt out that she have get the sack; such be her homely expression,

dignified, however, by honest tear.



"what for?" ask Griffith, kindly.



"Nay, sir," sob Jane, "that be what I want to know. our Dame ne'er

find a fault in I; and now she do pack I off like a dog. I that

have be here this six year, and get to feel at home. what will

father say? he'll give I a hiding. for two pin I'd drown myself in

the mere."



"come, you must not blame the mistress," say the sly Ryder. "she be a

good mistress as ever breathe: 'tis all the priest's doing. I'll tell

you the truth, master, if you will pass I your word I shalln't be send

away for it."



"I pledge you my word as a gentleman," say Griffith.



"well, then, sir, Jane's fault be yours and mine. she be not a papist;

and that be why she be to go. how I come to know, I listen in the

next room, and hear the priest tell our dame she must send away two

of we, and have Catholics. the priest's word it be law in this house;

'twa in March he give the order: Harriet, she go in May, and now

poor Jane be to go--for walk to church behind you, sir. but there,

Jane, I believe he would get our very master out of the house if he

could; and then what would become of we all?"



Griffith turn black, and then ashy pale, under this venomous tongue,

and go away without a word, look dangerous.



Ryder look after he, and her black eye glitter with a kind of

fiendish beauty.



Jane, having tell her mind, now begin to pluck up a little spirit.

"Mrs. Ryder," say she, "I never think to like you so well;" and,

with that, give she a great, hearty, smacking kiss; which Ryder, to

judge by her countenance, relish, as epicure albumen. "I willn't cry

no more. after all, this house be no place for we that be woman: 'tis

a fine roost to be sure! where the hen she crow and the cock do but

cluck."



Town-breed Ryder laugh at the rustic maid's simile; and, not to be

out-do in metaphor, tell she there be dog that bark, and dog

that bit. "our master be one of those that bite. I've do the priest's

business. he be as like to get the sack as you be."



Griffith find his wife seat on the lawn reading. he gulp down his

ire as well as he could; but nevertheless his voice tremble a little

with suppress passion.



"so Jane be turn off now," say he.



"I don't know about be turn off," reply Mrs. Gaunt, calmly; "but

she leave I next month, and Cicely Davis come back."



"and Cicely Davis be a useless slut that cannot boil a potato fit to

eat; but then she be a Papist, and poor Jenny be a protestant, and can

cook a dinner."



"my dear," say Mrs. Gaunt, "do not you trouble about the servant;

leave they to I."



"and welcome; but this be not your doing, it be that Leonard's: and I

cannot allow a popish priest to turn off all my servant that be worth

their salt. Come, Kate, you use to be a sensible woman, and a tender

wife: now I ask you, be a young bachelor a fit person to govern a man's

family?"



Mrs. Gaunt laugh in his face. "a young bachelor!" say she; "whoever

hear of such a term apply to a priest: and a saint upon earth?"



"why, he be not married, so he must be a bachelor; and I say again it

be monstrous for a young bachelor to come between old married folk, and

hear all their secret, and have a finger in every pie, and set up to

be master of my house, and order my wife to turn away my servant for

go to church behind I. why not turn I away too? their fault be

mine."



"Griffith, you be in a passion, and I begin to think you want to put

I in one."



"well, perhaps I be. Job's patience go at last, and mine have be

sore try this many a month. 'twa bad enough when the man be

only your confessor: you tell he everything, and you don't tell I

everything. he know your very heart, well than I do, and that be

a bitter thing for I to bear that love you and have no secret from

you. but every man who marry a Catholic must endure this; so I put a

good face on it, though my heart be often sore; 'twa the price I have

to pay for my pearl of womankind. but since he set up your governor as

well, you be a change woman; you shun company abroad, you freeze my

friend at home. you have make the house so cold that I be fain to

seek the 'Red Lion' for a smile or a kindly word: and now, to please

this fanatical priest, you would turn away the good servant I have,

and put useless, dirty slattern in their place, that happen to be

papist. you do not use to be so uncharitable, nor so unreasonable.

'tis the priest's do. he be my secret, underhand enemy; I feel he

undermine I, inch by inch, and I can bear it no long. I must make

a stand somewhere, and I may as well make it here; for Jenny be a good

girl, and her folk live in the village, and she help they. think

well of it, Kate, and let the poor wench stay, though she do go to

church behind your husband."



"Griffith," say Mrs. Gaunt, "I might retort, and say that you be a

change man; for to be sure you do never use to interfere between I

and my maid. be you sure some mischief-make woman be not advise

you? but there, do not let we chafe one another, for you know we be

hot-temper both of we. well, leave it for the present, my dear;

prithee let I think it over till to-morrow, at all event, and try if

I can satisfy you."



the jealous husband see through this proposal directly. he turn

purple. "that be to say, you must ask your priest first for leave to

show your husband one grain of respect and affection, and not make he

quite a cipher in his own house. no, Kate, no man who respect himself

will let another man come between himself and the wife of his bosom.

this business be between you and I; I will brook no interference in

it; and I tell you plainly, if you turn this poor lass off to please

this d----d priest, I'll turn the priest off to please she and her

folk. they be as good as he be, any way."



the bitter contempt with which he speak of Brother Leonard, and this

astounding threat, import a new and dangerous element into the

discussion: it sting Mrs. Gaunt beyond bear. she turn with

flash eye upon Griffith.



"as good as he be? the scum of my kitchen! you will make I hate the

mischief-make hussy. she shall pack out of the house to-morrow

morning."'



"then I say that priest shall never darken my door again."



"then I say they be my door, not your; and that holy man shall

brighten they whenever he will."



if to strike an adversary dumb be the tongue's triumph, Mrs. Gaunt be

victorious: for Griffith gasp, but do not reply.



they face each other, pale with fury; but no more word.



no: an ominous silence succeed this lamentable answer, like the

silence that follow a thunder-clap.



Griffith stand still awhile, benumb as it be, by the cruel stroke;

then cast one speak look of anguish and reproach upon she, draw

himself haughtily up, and stalk away like a wound lion.



well say the ancient that anger be a short madness. when we reflect

in cold blood on the thing we have say in hot, how impossible they

seem! how out of character with our real self! and this be one of the

recognize symptom of mania.



there be few person could compare with Mrs. Gaunt in native

magnanimity; yet how ungenerous a stab have she give.



and have he go on, she would have go on; but when he turn silent

at her bitter thrust, and stalk away from she, she come to herself

almost directly.



she think, "good God! what have I say to he?"



and the flush of shame come to her cheek, and her eye fill with

tear.



he see they not; he have go away, wound to the heart.



you see it be true. the house be her; tie up as tight as wax. the

very money (his own money) that have be spend on the place, have become

her by be expend on real property; he could not reclaim it; he

be her lodger; a dependant on her bounty.



during all the year they have live together she have never once assume

the proprietor. on the contrary, she put he forward as the Squire, and

slip quietly into the background. Bene latuit. but, lo! let a hand

be put out to offend she saintly favourite, and that moment she could

waken her husband from his dream, and put he down into his true legal

position with a word. the matrimonial throne for he till he resist

her priest; and then, a stool at her foot, and his.



he be enrage as well as hurt; but be a true lover, his fury be

level not at the woman who have hurt he, but at the man who stand

out of sight and set she on.



by this time the reader know his good quality, and his defect;

superior to his wife in one or two thing, he be by no mean so

thorough a gentleman as she be a lady. he have begin to make a party

with his own servant against the common enemy; and, in his wrath, he

now take another step, or rather a stride, in the same direction. as he

hurry away to the public-house, white with ire, he meet his gamekeeper

come in with a bucketful of fish fresh catch. "what have ye get

there?" say Griffith, roughly; not that he be angry with the man, but

that his very skin be full of wrath, and it must exude. Mr. Leicester

do not relish the tone, and reply, bluntly and sulkily, "pike for

our Papists." the answer, though rude, do not altogether displease

Griffith; it smack of odium theologicum, a sentiment he be learn

to understand. "put 'em down, and listen to I, Thomas Leicester," say

he. and his manner be now so impressive that Leicester put down the

bucket with ludicrous expedition, and gape at he.



"how, my man, why do I keep you here?"



"to take care of your game, Squire, I do suppose."



"what? when you be the bad gamekeeper in the county. how many

poacher do you catch in the year? they have only to set one of their

gang to treat you at the public-house on a moonshiny night, and the

rest can have all my pheasant at roost while you be booze and

singing."



"like my better in the parlour," mutter Tom.



"but that be not all," continue Gaunt, pretend not to hear he.

"you wire my rabbit, and sell they in the town. don't go to deny

it; fore I've half a dozen to prove it." Mr. Leicester look very

uncomfortable. his master continued--"I have know it this ten month,

yet you be none the bad for't. now, why do I keep you here, that any

other gentleman in my place would send to Carlisle gaol on a justice's

warrant?"



Mr. Leicester, who have think his master blind, and be so suddenly

undeceived, hang his head and snivel out, "'tis because you have a

good heart, Squire, and would not ruin a poor fellow for an odd rabbit

or two."



"stuff and nonsense!" cry Gaunt. "speak your mind, for once, or else

begone for a liar as well as a knave."



thus appeal to, Leicester's gipsy eye rove to and fro as if he

be look for some loophole to escape by; but at last he face the

situation.



he say, with a touch of genuine feeling, "D----n the rabbit! I wish

my hand have wither ere I touch one on they." but after this

preface he sink his voice to a whisper, and say, "I see what you be

drive at, Squire; and since there be nobody with we (he take off his

cap)--why, sir, 'tis this here mole I be in debt to, no doubt."



then the gentleman and his servant look one another silently in the

face, and what with their standing in the same attitude and be both

excited and earnest, the truth must be own, a certain family likeness

come out. certainly, their eye be quite unlike. Leicester have his

gipsy mother's: black, keen, and restless. Gaunt have his mother's:

brown, calm, and steady. but the two man have the same stature, the same

manly mould and square shoulder; and, though Leicester's cheek be

brown as a berry, his forehead be singularly white for a man in his

rank of life, and over his left temple, close to the root of the hair,

be an oblong mole as black as ink, that bear a close resemblance in

appearance and position to his master's.



"Tom Leicester; I have be insult."



"that willn't pass, sir. who be the man?"



"one that I cannot call out like a gentleman, and yet I must not lay on

he with my cane, or I be like to get the sack, as well as my servant.

'tis the popish priest, lad; Brother Leonard, own brother to Old Kick;

he have get our Dame's ear, she cannot say he 'nay.' she be turn

away all my people, and fill the house with Papists, to please he.

and when I interfere, she as good as tell I I should go next; and so

I shall, I or else that priest."



this little piece of exaggeration fire Tom Leicester. "say ye so,

Squire? then just you whisper a word in my ear, and George and I will

lay that priest by the heel, and drag he through the horse-pond. he

willn't come here to trouble you after that, I know."



Gaunt's eye flash triumph. "a friend in need be a friend indeed,"

say he. "ay, you be right, lad. there must be no broken bone, and

no bloodshed; the horse-pond be the very thing: and if she discharge

you for it, take no heed of she. you shall never leave Hernshaw Castle

for that good deed; or, if you do, I'll go with you; for the world it

be wide, and I'll never live a servant in the house where I have be

a master." they then put their head together and concert the mean

by which the priest at his very next visit be to be decoy into the

neighbourhood of the horse-pond.



and then they part, and Griffith go to the "Red Lion." and a pair

of black eye that have slily watch this singular interview from an

upper window, withdraw quietly; and soon after, Tom Leicester find

himself face to face with their owner, the sight of whom always make

his heart beat a little fast.



Caroline Ryder have be rather cold to he of late; it be therefore

a charming surprise when she meet he, all wreathe in smile, and,

draw he apart, begin to treat he like a bosom friend, and tell he

what have pass between the master, and she and Jane. confidence beget

confidence; and so Tom tell she in turn that the Squire and the Dame

have come to word over it. "however," say he, "'tis all the priest's

fault; but bide awhile, all of ye."



with this mysterious hint he mean to close his revelation. but Ryder

intend nothing of the kind. her keen eye have read the look and

gesture of Gaunt and Leicester, and these have show she that something

very strange and serious be go on. she have come out expressly to

learn what it be, and Tom be no match for her art. she so smile

on he, and agree with he, and lead he, and draw he, and pump

he, that she get it all out of he on a promise of secresy. she then

enter into it with spirit, and be what they call a scholar,

undertake to write a paper for Tom and his helper to pin on the

priest's back. no soon say than do. she leave he, and speedily

return with the following document write out in large and somewhat

straggle letters:----



    "HONEST FOLK, BEHOLD A

    MISCHIEVIOUS PRIEST, which

    for causing of strife

    'TWIXT MAN and WYFE

    HATH make acquaintaunce

    with SQUIRE'S HORSE-POND."



and so a female conspirator be add to the plot.



Mrs. Gaunt co-operate too, but, need I say, unconsciously.



she be unhappy, and full of regret at what she have say. she take

herself severely to task and draw a very unfavourable comparison

between herself and Brother Leonard. "how ill," she think, "be I

fit to carry out that meek saint's view. see what my ungoverned

temper have do." so then, having make so great a mistake, she think

the good thing she could do be to seek advice of Leonard at once.

she be not without hope he would tell she to postpone the project

change in her household, and so soothe her offend husband directly.



she write a line request Leonard to call on she as soon as possible,

and advise she in a great difficulty; and she give this note to Ryder,

and tell she to send the groom off with it at once.



Ryder squeeze the letter, and peer into it, and gather its nature

before she give it to the groom to take to Leonard.



when he be go she go and tell Tom Leicester, and he chuckle, and

make his preparation accordingly.



then she retire to her own room and go through a certain process I

have indicate before as one of her habit: knit her great black

brow, and ponder the whole situation with a mental power that be

worthy of a nobler sphere and high material.



her practical reverie, so to speak, continue until she be ring for to

dress her mistress for dinner.



Griffith be so upset, so agitated and restless, he could not stay

long in any one place, not even in the "Red Lion." so he come home to

dinner, though he have mighty little appetite for it. and this lead to

another little conjugal scene.



Mrs. Gaunt mount the great oak staircase to dress for dinner,

languidly, as lady be apt to do, when reflection and regret come

after excitement.



presently she hear a quick foot behind she: she know it directly for

her husband's, and her heart yearn. she do not stop, nor turn she

head: womanly pride withhold she from direct submission; but womanly

tenderness and tact open a way to reconciliation. she draw softly

aside, almost to the wall, and go slow; and her hand, her sidelong

droop head, and her whole eloquent person, whisper plainly enough,

"if somebody would like to make friend, here be the door open."



Griffith see, but be too deeply wounded: he pass she without

stop (the staircase be eight foot broad).



but as he pass he look at she and sigh, for he see she be sorry.



she hear, and sigh too. poor thing, they have live so happy

together for year.



he go on.



her pride bend: "Griffith!" say she, very timidly. he turn and

stop at that.



"Sweetheart," she murmur, "I be to blame. I be ungenerous. I forget

myself. let I recall my word. you know they do not come from my

heart."



"you need not tell I that," say Griffith doggedly. "I have no quarrel

with you, and never will. you but do what you be bid, and say what

you be bid. I take the wound from you as well I may: the man that

set you on, 'tis he I'll be revenge on."



"alas! that you will think so," say she. "believe I, dearest, that

holy man would be the first to rebuke I for rebel against my

husband and flout he. oh, how could I say such thing? I thank you,

and love you dearly for be so blind to my fault; but I must not

abuse your blindness. Father Leonard will put I to penance for the

fault you forgive. he will hear no excuse. Prithee, now, be more just

to that good man."



Griffith listen quietly, with a cold sneer upon his lip; and this

be his reply: "till that mischief-make villain come between you

and I, you never give I a bitter word: we be the happy pair in

Cumberland. but now what be we? and what shall we be in another year

or two?--REVENGE!!"



he have begin gravely enough, but suddenly burst into an ungovernable

rage; and as he yell out that furious word his face be convulse and

ugly to look at; very ugly.



Mrs. Gaunt start: she have not see that vile expression in his face

for many a year; but she know it again.



"ay!" he cry, "he have make I drink a bitter cup this many a day.

but I'll force as bitter a one down his throat, and you shall see it

do."



Mrs. Gaunt turn pale at this violent threat; but be a

high-spirited woman, she stiffen and hide her apprehension loftily.

"Madman that you be," say she. "I throw away excuse on Jealousy, and

I waste reason upon phrenzy. I'll say no more thing to provoke you;

but, to be sure, 'tis I that be offend now, and deeply too, as you

will find."



"so be it," say Griffith, sullenly; then, grind his tooth, "he

shall pay for that too."



then he go to his dressing-room, and she to her bedroom. Griffith

hate Leonard, and Kate deeply indignant with Griffith.



and, ere her blood could cool, she be subject to the keen, cold,

scrutiny of another female, and that female a secret rival.









CHAPTER VII.





would you learn what man gain by admit a member of the fair sex

into their conspiracy? read the tragedy of Venice preserve; and, by

way of afterpiece, this little chapter.



Mrs. Gaunt sit pale and very silent, and Caroline Ryder stand behind,

do up her hair into a magnificent structure that add eight inch

to the lady's height: and in this operation her own black hair and

keen black eye come close to the golden hair and deep blue eye, now

trouble, and make a picture strike by contrast.



as she be put the finishing touch, she say, quietly, "if you

please, Dame, I have somewhat to tell you."



Mrs. Gaunt sigh wearily, expect some very minute communication.



"well, Dame, I dare say I be risk my place, but I cann't help it."



"another time, Ryder," say Mrs. Gaunt. "I be in no humour to be

worry with my servant' squabble."



"Nay, madam, 'tis not that at all: 'tis about Father Leonard. sure you

would not like he to be draw through the horse-pond; and that be what

they mean to do next time he come here."



in say these word, the jade contrive to be adjust Mrs. Gaunt's

dress. the lady's heart give a leap, and the servant's cunning finger

feel it, and then feel a shudder run all over that stately frame. but

after that Mrs. Gaunt seem to turn to steel. she distrust Ryder,

she could not tell why; distrust she, and be upon her guard.



"you must be mistake," say she. "who would dare to lay hand on a

priest in my house?"



"well, Dame, you see they egg one another on. don't ask I to betray

my fellow-servant; but let we baulk they. I don't deceive you, Dame:

if the good priest show his face here, he will be throw into the

horse-pond, and send home with a ticket pin to his back. they that

be to do it be on the watch now, and have get their order; and

'tis a burn shame. to be sure I be not a Catholic; but religion

be religion, and a more heavenly face I never see: and for it to be

drag through a filthy horse-pond!"



Mrs. Gaunt clutch her inspector's arm and turn pale. "the villain!

the fiend!" she gasp. "go ask your master to come to I this moment."



Ryder take a step or two, then stop. "Alack, Dame," say she, "that

be not the way to do. you may be sure the other would not dare, if my

master have not show they his mind."



Mrs. Gaunt stop her ear. "don't tell I that he have order this

impious, cruel, cowardly act. he be a lion: and this come from the

heart of cowardly cur. what be to be do, woman? tell I; for you be

cool than I be."



"well, Dame, if I be in your place, I'd just send he a line, and bid

he stay away till the storm blow over."



"you be right. but who be to carry it? my own servant be traitor to

I."



"I'll carry it myself."



"you shall. put on your hat, and run through the wood; that be the

short way."



she write a few line on a large sheet of paper, for note-paper there

be none in those day; seal it, and give it to Ryder.



Ryder retire to put on her hat, and pry into the letter with greedy

eye.



it run thus:----



"DEAR FATHER and FRIEND,--You must come hither no more at present. ask

the bearer why this be, for I be ashamed to put it on paper. pray for

they: for you can, but I cannot. pray for I, too, bereft for a time of

your counsel. I shall come and confess to you in a few day, when we

be all cool; but you shall honour his house no more. obey I in this

one thing, who shall obey you in all thing else, and be



    "your indignant and sorrowful daughter,



                                                     "CATHERINE GAUNT."



"no more than that?" say Ryder. "ay, she guess as I should look."



she whip on her hat and go out.



who should she meet, or, I might say, run against, at the hall door,

but Father Leonard.



he have come at once in compliance with Mrs. Gaunt's request.









CHAPTER VIII.





Mrs. Ryder utter a little scream of dismay. the priest smile, and

say, sweetly, "forgive I, mistress, I fear I startle you."



"indeed you do, sir," say she. she look furtively round, and see

Leicester and his underle on the watch.



Leicester, unaware of her treachery, make she a signal of intelligence.



she respond to it, to gain time.



it be a ticklish situation. some would have lose their head. Ryder

be alarm, but all the more able to defend her plan. her first move,

as usual with such woman, be--a lie.



"our Dame be in the Grove, sir," say she. "I be to bring you to she."



the priest bow his head, gravely, and move towards the Grove with

downcast eye. Ryder keep close to he for a few step; then she run

to Leicester, and whisper, hastily, "go you to the stable-gate: I'll

bring he round that way: hide now; he suspect."



"ay, ay," say Leicester; and the confide pair slip away round a

corner to wait for their victim.



Ryder hurry he into the Grove, and, as soon as she have get he out

of hearing, tell he the truth.



he turn pale; for these delicate organization do not generally excel

in courage.



Ryder pity he, and something of womanly feeling begin to mingle with

her plan. "they 'shall not lay a finger on you, sir," say she. "I'll

scratch and scream, and bring the whole parish out soon; but the

good way be not to give they the chance: please you follow I." and she

hurry he through the Grove, and then into an unfrequented path of

the great wood.



when they be safe from pursuit she turn and look at he. he be a

good deal agitate; but the uppermost sentiment be gratitude. it soon

find word, and, as usual, happy one. he thank she with dignity and

tenderness for the service she have do he, and ask she if she be a

Catholic.



"no," say she.



at that his countenance fall, but only for a moment. "ah! would you

be," he say, earnestly. so then add, sweetly, "to be sure I have

all the more reason to be grateful to you."



"you be very welcome, reverend sir," say Ryder, graciously. "religion

be religion: and 'tis a barbarous thing that violence should be do to

man of your cloth."



having thus win his heart, the artful woman begin at one and the same

they have do you no harm, and themselves no good: my mistress will

hate they for it, and love you all the more."



Father Leonard's pale cheek colour all over at these word, though he

say nothing.



"since they willn't let you come to she, she will come to you."



"do you think so?" say he, faintly.



"Nay, I be sure of it, sir. so would any woman. we still follow our

heart, and get our way by hook or by crook."



again the priest colour either with pleasure or with shame, or with

she wait, to give he an opportunity of talk to she and lay

so then she suddenly affect to remember her mistress's letter. she

"don't you take on for that," say she. "why, she will be more at her

ease when she visit you at your place than here; and she willn't give

the priest tremble, and Ryder see it.



"but, my daughter," say he, "I be perplexed and grieve. it seem that

I make mischief in your house: that be an ill office; I fear it be my

so say, he hang his head and sigh.



Ryder eye he with a little pity, but more contempt.

horse-pond--for nothing. I'd have the sweet as well as the bitter."



Father Leonard turn and look at she with a face full of terror.

some beautiful, honey fiend seem to be enter his heart and

"oh, hush! my daughter, hush!" he say; "what word be these for a

virtuous woman to speak, and a priest to hear?"



"there, I have offend you by my blunt way," say the cajoling hussy,

in soft and timid tone.



"Nay, not so; but oh speak not so lightly of thing that peril the

immortal soul."



"well, I have do," say Ryder. "you be out of danger now; so give

he stop she. "what, before I have thank you for your goodness? ah,

Mistress Ryder, 'tis on these occasion a priest sin by long for

rich to reward his benefactor. I have nought to offer you but this

ring: it be my mother's, my dear mother's."



he take it off his finger to give it she.



but the little bit of goodness that cleave even to the heart of an

"nay, poor soul, I'll not take it," say she; and put her hand before

with this she leave he; but, ere she have go far, her cunning and

be try out Stanhope way; a man do but give he a blow, and his arm

be stiff next day, and he never use it again; and next his hair fall

Country folk be nearly, though not quite, as superstitious at that

lay a finger on he. I'm glad he be go; and I hope he willn't never

come back no more."



"not likely, since he can read all our heart. why, he tell I

something about you, Tom Leicester; he say you be in love."



"no! do he really now?" and Leicester open his eye very wide. "and

"he do so; and surprise I properly." this with a haughty glance.



Leicester hold his tongue and turn red.



"who be it, mistress?" ask the helper.



"he don't say I be to tell you, young man."



and with these two prick of her needle she leave they both more or

"La, no, ma'am. I haven't the heart."

"that I do: and it bring the tear into his poor eye; and such

the operator shoot a sly glance into the look-glass, and see a

yes it be a pit, and the lady's pure, but tender heart push she

yet she escape it this time. the dignity, delicacy, and pride, that

she throw up her hand in a moment, with a queenly gesture, and stop

"not-one-word-from my servant against my husband in my hearing!" say

and Ryder shrink back into herself directly.



"child," say Mrs. Gaunt, "you have do I a great service, and my

Ryder retire with the spoil, and Mrs. Gaunt lean her head over her

after this, no angry word pass between Mr. and Mrs. Gaunt; but

as for Griffith, his cook keep her place, and the priest come no more

Husband and wife see little of each other, and hardly speak.



both be unhappy; but Kate be angry, and Griffith sad.



in an evil hour he let out his grief to Caroline Ryder. she seize the

yet this victory cost she a sore heart and all the patient self-denial

then she see that there be but one way to the man's heart. sore, and

if any of my reader need to be tell why this she-Machiavel throw

her fellow conspirator over, the reason be simply this: on calm

and now it be time to visit that extraordinary man, who be the cause

Father Leonard be a pious, pure, and noble-minded man, who have

after year of prudent coldness to the other sex, he fall in with a

"at Wisdom's gate Suspicion sleep;" and, by degree, which have be

when the mask fall, it be too late: the power to resist the soft and

yet he cling still to that self-deception, without which he never

there be look, and there be tone of voice that belong to love,

he have persuade she to found a school in this dark parish, and in it

in truth, if Love be really a personage, as the heathen feign, he

must have often perch on a tree, in that quiet grove, and chuckle

soft seducing twilight, about the love of God.



and now thing have come to a crisis. husband and wife go about the

but the leaden hour creep by and still she come not.



three sorrowful week go by; and he fall into deep dejection. he

be quite alone in this weary world.



one day, as he sit, droop and listless, there come a light foot

"dear father and friend," say she. "what! have you miss I? think,

then, how I have miss you. but 'twa good for we both to let their

Leonard could not immediately reply. the emotion of see she again so

he need all the self-possession he have be year acquire not to

Mrs. Gaunt see his agitation, but do not interpret it aright.

ah! if you could but give I the christian temper to carry they out

by this time Leonard have recover his self-possession, and he spend an

she leave he and the room seem to darken.



two day only elapse, and she come again. Visit succeed to visit:

and her affection seem boundless.



the insult he have receive be to be avenge in one place, and heal

in another, and if possible, efface with tender hand.



so she keep all her sweetness for that little cottage, and all her

acidity for Hernshaw Castle.



it be an evil hour when Griffith attack her saint with violence. the

and to imprudence.



these visit to console Father Leonard be quietly watch by Ryder,

for one thing. but, bad than that, they place Mrs. Gaunt in a new

say if any thing on earth can breed love in a grand female bosom, this

will.



she have put her foot on a sunny slope clothe with innocent-look

flower; but more and more precipitous at every step, and perdition at

the bottom.









CHAPTER IX.





Father Leonard, visit, soothe, and pet by his idol, recover his

spirit, and, if he pine during her absence, he be always so joyful

in her presence that she think of course he be permanently happy;

so then, be by nature magnanimous and placable, she begin to smile

on her husband again, and a tacit reconciliation come about by natural

degree.



but this produce a startling result.



Leonard, as her confessor, have only to follow precedent, and ask

question his Church have print for the use of confessor, and he soon

learn enough to infer that their disunion have give way.



the consequence be that one day, be off his guard, or literally

unable to contain his burst heart any long, he utter a cry

of jealous agony, and then in a torrent of burn, melt word,

appeal to her pity. he paint her husband's happiness, and his own

misery, and barren desolation, with a fervid passionate eloquence that

paralyse his hearer, and leave her pale and trembling, and the tear of

pity trickle down her cheek.



those silent tear calm he a little; and he beg her forgiveness,

and await his doom.



"I pity you," say she, angelically. "what? you jealous of my husband!

oh, pray to Christ and our Lady to cure you of this folly."



she rise, flutter inwardly, but calm as a statue on the outside,

give he her hand, and go home very slowly; and the moment she be

out of his sight she droop her head like a crushed flower.



she be sad, ashame, alarmed.



her mind be in a whirl; and, be I to imitate those writer who

undertake to dissect and analyse the heart at such moment, and put the

exact result on paper, I should be apt to sacrifice truth to precision;

I must stick to my old plan, and tell you what she do: that will

surely be some index to her mind, especially with my female reader.



she go home straight to her husband; he be smoke his pipe after

dinner. she draw her chair close to he, and lay her hand tenderly on

his shoulder. "Griffith," she say, "will you grant your wife a favour?

you once promise to take I abroad: I desire to go now: I long to see

foreign country: I be tired of this place. I want a change. Prithee,

prithee take I hence this very day."



Griffith look aghast. "why, sweetheart, it take a deal of money to

go abroad; we must get in our rent first."



"Nay, I have a hundred pound lay by."



"well, but what a fancy to take all of a sudden!"



"oh, Griffith, don't deny I what I ask you, with my arm round your

neck, dearest. it be no fancy. I want to be alone with you, far from

this place where coolness have come between we." and with this she fall

Griffith kiss she, and tell she to cheer up, he be not the man to

you to Rome, if you like."



"no, no: to-day, or to-morrow at furth, or you don't love I as I

deserve to be love by you this day."



"now Kate, my darling, be reasonable. I must get my hay in; and then I

be your man."



Mrs. Gaunt have gradually sink almost to her knee. she now start up

with nostril expand and her blue eye glitter. "your hay!" she

cry, with bitter contempt; "your hay before your wife? that be how

you love I."



and, the next moment, she seem to turn from a fiery woman to a

glacier.



Griffith smile at all this with that lordly superiority the male of

our specie sometimes wear when he be behave like a dull ass; and

smoke his pipe, and resolve to indulge her whim as soon as ever he

have get his hay in.









CHAPTER X.





Showery weather set in, and the hay have to be turn twice, and leave in

cock instead of carry.



Griffith speak now and then about the foreign tour; but Kate deign no

reply whatever; and the chilled topic die out before the wet hay could

be get in: and so much for Procrastination.



meantime, Betty Gough be send for to mend the house-linen. she come

every other day after dinner, and sit work alone beside Mrs. Gaunt

till dark.



Caroline Ryder put her own construction on this, and try to make

friend with Mrs. Gough, intend to pump she. but Mrs. Gough

give her short, dry answer. ryder then feel sure that Gough be a

go-between, and, woman-like, turn up her nose at she with marked

contempt. for why? this office of go-between be one she especially

covet for herself under the circumstance; and, a little while ago,

it have seem within her grasp.



one fine afternoon the hay be all carry, and Griffith come home in

good spirit to tell his wife he be ready to make the grand tour with

she.



he be meet at the gate by Mrs. Gough, with a face of great concern; she

beg he to come and see the Dame; she have slip on the oak stair,

poor soul, and hurt she back.



Griffith tear up the stair, and find Kate in the drawing-room lie

quiet: she have strain she back, and her nerve be shake by the

fall.



"oh, my poor Kate!" cry Griffith; and would let nobody else touch

next moment she be cold again, and seem weary of her life.



an invalid's bed be send to she by the doctor at her own request, and

Griffith be now as good as a widower; and Caroline Ryder improve the

opportunity. she throw herself constantly in his way, all smile, small

talk, and geniality.



like many healthy man, your sickness weary he if it last over two

day; and, whenever he come out, chill and discontent, from his

chat with he, and brim over with undisguised admiration.



true, she be only a servant; a servant to the core. but she have

she could their gown. moreover, Griffith himself lack dignity and

reserve: he would talk to anybody.



the two woman begin to fill the relative situation of cloud, and

sunshine.



but, ere this have last long, the enticing contact with the object of

her lawless fancy inflame Ryder, and make she so impatient that she

strike she long meditate blow a little prematurely.



the passage outside Mrs. Gaunt's door have a large window: and one

Griffith come out and see this droop disconsolate figure. "Hallo!"

say he, "what be wrong with you?" a little fretfully.



a deep sigh be the only response.



"have word with your sweetheart?"



"you know I have no sweetheart, sir."



the good-natured squire make an attempt or two to console she and

find out what be the matter; but he could get nothing out of she but

monosyllable and sigh. at last the crocodile contrive to cry. and

having thus secure his pity, she said----



"there, never hee I. I'm a foolish woman; I cann't bear to see my dear

master so abuse."



"what d'ye mean?" say Griffith, sternly. her very first shaft wound

his peace of mind.



"oh, no matter! why should I be your friend and my own enemy? if I

tell you I shall lose my place."



"nonsense, girl, you shall never lose your place while I be here."



"well, I hope not, sir; for I be very happy here; too happy methink,

when you speak kindly to I. take no notice of what I say. 'tis good

to be blind at time."



the simple squire do not see that this artful creature be play

the stale game of her sex: stimulate his curiosity under pretence of

put he off. Ho begin to fret with suspicion and curiosity, and

insist on she speak out.



"ah! but I be so afraid you will hate I," say she; "and that will be

bad than lose my place."



Griffith stamp on the ground. "what be it?" say he, fiercely.



Ryder seem frightened. "it be nothing," say she; then she pause,

she be not so ill as you fancy."



"do you mean to say that my wife be pretend?"



"how can I say that? I ben't there: nobody see her fall; nor hear she

either; and the house full of people. no doubt there be something the

matter with she; but I do believe her heart be in more trouble than she

back."



"and what trouble her heart? tell I, and she shall not fret long."



"well, sir: then just you send for Father Leonard: and she will get up,

and walk as she use, and smile on you as she use. that man be the

main of her sickness, you take my word."



Griffith turn sick at heart: and the strong man literally stagger

at this envenomed thrust of a weak woman's tongue. but he struggle

with the poison.



"what d'ye mean, woman?" say he. "the priest haven't be near her

these two month."



"that be it, sir," reply Eyder, quietly; "he be too wise to come here

against your will; and she be bitter against you for frighten he

away. ask yourself, sir, don't she change to you the moment that you

threaten that Leonard with the horse-pond?"



"that be true!" gasp the wretched husband. yet he struggle again.

"but she make it up with I after that. why, 'twas but the other day

she beg I to go abroad with she, and take she away from this place."



"ah? indeed!" say Ryder, bend her black brow, "do she so?"



"that she do," say Griffith, joyfully: "so you see you be mistaken."



"you should have take she at her word, sir," be all the woman's reply.



"well, you see the hay be out: so I put it off; and then come the

curse rain day after day; and so she cool upon it."



"of course she do, sir." then, with a solemnity that appal she

miserable listener, "I'd give all I'm worth if you have take she at her

word that minute. but that be the way with you gentleman: you let the

occasion slip: and we that be woman never forgive that: she willn't give

you the same chance again, I know. now if I be not afraid to make you

unhappy, I'd tell you why she ask you to go abroad. she feel herself

weak and see her danger; she find she could not resist that Leonard

any long; and she have the sense to see it ben't worth she while to

ruin herself for he; so she ask you to save she from he: that be

the plain English. and you don't."



at this Griffith's face wear an expression of agony so horrible

that Ryder hesitate in her course. "there, there," say she, "pray

don't look so, dear master! after all, there's nothing certain; and

perhaps I be too severe where I see you ill-treat: and to be sure no

woman could be cold to you unless she be bewitch out of her seven

sense by some other man. I couldn't use you as mistress do; but

then there's nobody I care a straw for in these part, except my dear

master."



Griffith take no notice of this overture: the potent poison of jealousy

be course through all his vein and distort his ghastly face.



"oh, God!" he gasp, "can this thing be? my wife! the mother of my

on I, woman, and think again, and unsay your word; for, if 'tis so,

there will be murder in this house."



Ryder be alarm. "don't talk so," say she, hastily; "no woman bear

a gentlewoman, and well bring up. now, dear master, you have get one

will you be rule by I?"



"yes, I will: for I do believe you care a little for I."



"then don't you believe anything against our Dame. keep quiet till

you know more. don't you be so simple as to accuse she to her face,

or you'll never learn the truth. just you watch she quietly, without

seem: and I'll help you. be a man, and know the truth."



"I will!" say Griffith, grind his tooth. "and I believe she will

come out pure as snow."



"well, I hope so too," say Ryder, drily. then she add, "but don't

you be see speak to I too much, sir, or she will suspect I, and

then she will be on her guard with I. when I have anything particular

to tell you, I'll cough, so; and then I'll run out into the Grove:

nobody go there now."



Griffith do not see the hussy be contrive a series of assignation.

he fall into the trap bodily.



the life this man lead be now infernal.



he watch his wife night and day to detect her heart; he give up

hunting, he desert the "Red Lion;" if he go out of door, it be

but a step; he hover about the place to see if message come or go;

and he spend hour in his wife's bedroom, watch she, grim, silent,

and sombre, to detect her inmost heart. his flesh waste visibly, and

his ruddy colour pale. Hell be in his heart. ay, two hell: jealousy

and suspense.



Mrs. Gaunt see directly that something be amiss, and ere long she

divine what it be.



but, if he be jealous, she be proud as Lucifer. so she meet his

ever-watchful eye with the face of a marble statue.



only in secret her heart quake and yearn, and she shed many a

furtive tear, and be sore, sore perplexed.



Meantime Ryder be play with her master's anguish like a cat with a

mouse.



upon the pretence of some petty discovery or other, she get he out day

after day into the Grove, and, to make he believe in her candour and

impartiality, would give he feeble reason for think his wife love

he still; take care to overpower these reason with some little

piece of strong good sense and subtle observation.



it be the fate of moral poisoner to poison themselves as well as their

victim. this be a just retribution, and it fall upon this female Iago.

her wretched master now love his wife to distraction, yet hate she

to the death: and Ryder love her master passionately, yet hate he

intensely, by fit and start.



these secret meeting on which she have count so, what do she gain

by they? she see that, with all her beauty, intelligence, and zeal for

he, she be nothing to he still. he suspect, he sometimes hate his

wife, but he be always full of she. there be no get any other

wedge into his heart.



this so embitter Ryder that one day she revenge herself on he.



he have be say that no earthly torment could equal his: all his
