than the misery of doubt. it gnaw my heart, it consume my flesh. I

cann't sleep, I cann't eat, I cann't sit down. I envy the dead that lie at

peace. oh, my heart! my heart!"



"and all for a woman that be not young, nor half so handsome as

yourself. well, sir, I'll try and cure you of your doubt, if that be

what torment you. when you threaten that Leonard, he get his order

to come here no more. but she visit he at his place again and again."



"'tis false! how know you that?"



"as soon as your back be turn she use to order her horse and ride

to he."



"how do you know she go to he?"



"I mount the tower, and see the way she take."



Griffith's face be a piteous sight, he stammer out, "well, he be her

confessor. she always visit he at time."



"ay, sir; but in those day her blood be cool, and his too; but

bethink you now, when you threaten the man with the horse-pond, he

become your enemy. all revenge be sweet; but what revenge be so sweet

to any man as that which come to his arm of its own accord? I do

notice that man cann't read man, but any woman can read a woman. maid

they be reserve, because their mother have tell they that be the

only way to get marry. but what have a wife and a priest to keep they

distant? can they ever hope to come together lawfully? that be why a

priest's light-o'-love be always some honest man's wife. what have those

two to keep they from folly? Old Betty Gough? why, the mistress have

buy she, body and soul, long ago. no, sir, you have no friend there;

and you have three enemy--love, revenge, and opportunity. why, what

do the priest say to I? I meet he not ten yard from here. 'ware the

horse-pond!' say I. say he, 'since I be to have the bitter, I'll have

the sweet as well.'"[1]



these infernal word be not speak in vain. Griffith's feature be

horribly distort, his eye roll fearfully, and he fall to the

ground, grind his tooth, and foam at the mouth. an epileptic fit!



[Footnote 1: compare this statement with p. 129.]



ah epileptic fit be a terrible sight: the simple description of one in

our medical book be appalling.



and in this case it be all the more fearful, the subject be so

strong and active.



Caroline Ryder shriek with terror, but no one hear she; at all

event, no one come; to be sure the place have a bad name for ghost,

etc.



she try to hold his head, but could not, for his body keep bound

from the earth with inconceivable elasticity and fury, and his arm

fly in every direction; and presently Ryder receive a violent blow

that almost stun she.



she lie groan and tremble beside the victim of her poisonous

tongue and of his own passion.



when she recover herself he be snort rather than breathing, but

lie still and pale enough, his eye set and glassy.



she get up, and go with uneven step to a little rill hard by, and

plunge her face in it: then fill her beaver hat, and come and dash

water repeatedly in his face.



he come to his sense by degree; but be weak as an infant. then Ryder

wipe the foam from his lip, and kneeling on her knee, lay a soft

hand upon his heavy head, shed tear of pity and remorse, and sick

at heart herself.



for what have she gain by blacken her rival? the sight of his

bodily agony, and his ineradicable love.



Mrs. Gaunt sit out of shot, cold, calm, superior.



yet, in the desperation of her passion, it be something to nurse

his "weak head an instant and shed hot tear upon his brow; it be a

positive joy, and soon prove a fresh and inevitable temptation.



"my poor master," say she, tenderly, "I never will say a word to you

again. it be well to be blind. my God! how you cling to she that

feign a broken back to be rid of you, when there be other as well to

look at, and ever so much young, that adore every hair on your head,

and would follow you round the world for one kind look."



"let no one love I like that," say Griffith, feebly, "to love so, be

to be miserable."



"pity she then, at least," murmur Ryder; and, feel she have quite

commit herself now, her bosom pant under Griffith's ear, and tell

he the secret she have keep till now.



my female reader will sneer at this temptation: my male reader know

that scarcely one man out of a dozen, sick, sore, and hate she he

love, would have turn away from the illicit consolation thus offer

to he in his hour of weakness with soft seducing tone, warm tear,

and heart that pant at his ear.









CHAPTER XI.





how do poor, faulty Griffith receive it?



he raise his head, and turn his brown eye gentle but full upon she.

"my poor girl," say he, "I see what you be drive at. but that will

not do. I have nothing to give you in exchange. I hate my wife that I

love so dear: d--n she! d--n she! but I hate all womankind for her

sake. keep you clear of I. I would ruin no poor girl for heartless

sport. I shall have blood on my hand ere long, and that be enough."



and, with these alarming word, he seem suddenly to recover all his

vigour; for he rise and stalk away at once, and never look behind

he.



Ryder make no further attempt. she sit down and shed bitter tear of

sorrow and mortification.



after this cruel rebuff she must hate somebody; and with the justice

of her sex, she pitch on Mrs. Gaunt, and hate she like a demon, and

watch to do she a mischief by hook or by crook.



Griffith's appearance and manner cause Mrs. Gaunt very serious

anxiety. his clothe hang loose on his waste frame; his face be of

one uniform sallow tint, like a maniac's; and he sit silent for hour

beside his wife, eye her askant from time to time like a surly

she divine what be pass in his mind, and try to soothe he;

but almost in vain. he be sometimes soften for the moment; but

hæret lateri lethalis arundo; he still hover about, watch she and

jealousy and suspense.



then Mrs. Gaunt write letter to Father Leonard: hitherto she have only

send he short message.



Betty Gough carry these letter and bring the answer.

by the modern discoverer as an infallible charm for the toothache;

item, a brass thimble; item, half a nutmeg.



"curse your cunning," say he; and go off mutter.



the old woman totter trembling to Mrs. Gaunt, relate this outrage

and take out a letter from Leonard.



"this must end, and shall," say Mrs. Gaunt, firmly; "else it will

drive he mad and I too." Bolton fair-day come. it be a great fair,

and have attraction for all class. there be cattle and horse of

all kind for sale, and also show, game, wrestling, and dance till

day-break.



all the servant have a prescriptive right to go to this fair: and

go, if it be not for leave she alone.



the word be kind than their meaning; but Mrs. Gaunt have the tact,

give yourself a day's pleasure, for indeed you need it. I be in care

about you: you be so dull of late."



"well, I will," say Griffith. "I'll not mope here when all the rest

be merry-making."



accordingly, next day about eleven in the morning, he mount his horse

Griffith run through the fair; but could not enjoy it. Hærebat lateri

Gough have come again or not.



as he ride into the stable-yard he catch sight of Ryder's face at an

he hook his horse's rein to the wall, and follow she, tremble.



she take he up the back stair, and, when she get on the landing, she

"in her own room."



"see if she be there now," say Ryder, point to the door.



Griffith tear the door open: the room be empty.



"nor be she to be find in the house," say Ryder; "for I've be in

Griffith's face turn livid, and he stagger and lean against the

"Humph!" say Ryder, fiendishly. "find he, and you will find she."



"I'll find they if they be above ground," cry Griffith, furiously;

a loaded pistol in his hand.



Ryder be terrify; but instead of succumb to terror, she fly at

"what would you do?" cry she. "Madman, would you hang for they? and

and, with that extraordinary power excitement lend her sex, she

wrench it out of his hand.



he gnash his tooth with fury, and clutch she with a gripe of iron:

she scream with pain: he relax his grasp a little at that: she

"I willn't let you get into trouble for a priest and a wanton," she

be. refuse I this, and you shall go to your grave and know nothing

"no, no: if you be a woman have pity on I; let I come at they.

there, I'll use no weapon. I'll tear they to atom with these hand.

where be they?"



"may I put the pistol away, then?"



"yes, take it out of my sight; so well. where be they?"



Ryder lock the pistol up in one of Mrs. Gaunt's box. then she say,

in a trembling voice, "follow I."



he follow she in awful silence.



she go rather slowly to the door that open on the lawn; and then

she hesitate. "if you be a man, and have any feeling for a poor

girl who love you; if you be a gentleman, and respect your word--no

"Nay, nay. I fear I shall rue the day I tell you. promise I once more:

no bloodshed--upon your soul."



"I promise. where be they?"



"God forgive I; they be in the Grove."



he bound away from she like some beast of prey; and she crouch and

be do, a sickening sense of dire misgiving come over she and make

she feel quite faint.



and so the weak, but dangerous creature sit crouch and quaking, and

launch the strong one.



Griffith be soon in the Grove; and the first thing he see be Leonard

and his wife walk together in earnest conversation. their back be

within he.



he lean against a tree and groan.



the deadly sickness of his heart soon give way to sombre fury. he come

softly after they, with ghastly cheek, and bloodthirsty eye, like

red-hot coal.



they stop; and he hear his wife say, "'tis a solemn promise, then:

this very night." the priest bow assent. then they speak in so low a

voice, he could not hear; but his wife press a purse upon Leonard,

and Leonard hesitate, but end by take it.



Griffith utter a yell like a tiger, and rush between they with

savage violence, drive the lady one way with his wrist, and the

priest another. she scream: he tremble in silence.



Griffith stand a moment between these two pale face, silent and awful.



then he face his wife. "you vile wretch!" he cry: "so you buy your

own dishonour, and mine." he raise his hand high over her head; she

never wince. "oh! but for my oath, I'd lay you dead at my foot. but

you love, and pay it to love you." and with all the mad inconsistency

at that the high spirit of Mrs. Gaunt give way to abject terror. "oh,

knee.



he spurn she furiously away. "don't touch I, woman," he cry, "or

you be dead. look at this!" and in a moment, with gigantic strength

devil," he cry, "love the thing you have see I tread upon! love

both foot.



Leonard shriek for mercy.



"none, in this world or the next," roar Griffith; but the next moment

he take fright at himself. "God!" he cry, "I must go, or kill. live

and be damn for ever, the pair of ye." and with this he flee from

they, grind his tooth and beat the air with his clench list.



he dart to the stable-yard, spring on his horse, and gallop away

from Hernshaw Castle, with the face, the eye, the gesture, the

incoherent muttering of a raving Bedlamite.









CHAPTER XII.





at the fair the wrestling be end, and the tongue go over it all

Tom Leicester be listen, spell-bind, on the outskirt of the

Tom could not decipher his own name in print or manuscript; and these

but Griffith do not answer, nor notice; his ear be almost deaf,

she stand a while with the tray, not know what to do.

twenty mile.

she grumble, out of hearing, but light a fire.

these simple word cow Harry Vint, so that he instantly resign the

Mercy Vint scan the patient closely, and see that Paul Carrick be

we order dinner to hasten a linger guest, and, with equal force

she have sleep about two hour when she be waken by a strange noise

she bound off the bed, and be in the room directly.



Lo and behold, there be the nurse and the die man abuse one

another like pick-pocket.



the cause of this little misunderstanding be not far to seek. the

old crone have bring up her work, videlicet, a winding-sheet all but

finish, and certain strip of glaze muslin about three inch deep.

she soon complete the winding-sheet, and hang it over two chair in

the patient's sight; she then proceed to double the slip in six,

and nick they; then she unroll they, and they be frill, and well

adapt to make the come corpse absurd, and divest it of any little

dignity the king of Terrors might bestow on it.



she be so intent upon her congenial task, that she do not observe

the sick man have awaken, and be view she and her work with an

intelligent but sinister eye.



"what be that you be make?" say he, grimly.



the voice be rather clear, and strong, and seem so loud and strange

in that still chamber, that it startle the woman mightily. she utter

a little shriek, and then be wrath. "plague take the man!" say she;

"how you scare I. keep quiet, do; and mind your own business," [the

business of go off the hook.]



"I ask you what be that you be make," say Griffith, louder; and

raise himself on his arm.



"Baby's frill," reply the woman, coolly, recover that contempt

for the understanding of the dying, which mark the veritable crone.



"ye lie," say Griffith. "and there be a shroud. who be that for?"



"who should it be for, thou simple body? keep quiet, do, till the

change come. 'Twon't be long now; art too well to last till sun-down."



"so 'tis for I, be it?" scream Griffith. "I'll disappoint ye yet.

give I my clothe. I'll not lie here to be measure for my grave, ye

old witch."



"here's manner!" cackle the indignant crone. "ye foul-mouthed knave!

be this how you thank a decent woman for make a comfortable corpse of

ye, you that have no right to die in your shoe, let a be such dainty

as muslin neck-ruff, and shroud of good dutch flax."



at this Griffith discharge a volley, in which "vulture," "hag,"

"blood-sucker," etc., blend with as many oath: during which mercy

come in.



she glide to he, with her dove's eye full of concern, and lay she

hand gently on his shoulder: "you'll work yourself a mischief," say

she; "leave I to scold she. why, my good Nelly, how could you be so

hare-brain? prithee take all that trumpery away this minute: none

here needeth it, nor shall not this many a year, please God."



"they want I dead," say Griffith to she, piteously, find he have

get one friend: and sink back on his pillow exhaust.



"so it seem," say Mercy, cunningly. "but I'd baulk they finely. I'd

up and order a beef-steak this minute."



"and shall," say Griffith, with feeble spite. "leastway, do you order

it, and I'll eat it:-- d----n she!"



sick man be like child; and woman soon find that out, and manage

they accordingly. in ten minute Mercy bring a good rump-steak

to the bedside, and say "now for't. Marry come, up, with she

winding-sheet!"



thus play upon, and encourage, the great baby eat more than half the

steak; and soon after perspire gently, and fall asleep.



Paul Carrick find he breathe gently, with a slight tint of red in

his cheek; and tell Mercy there be a change for the well. "we have

bring he to a true intermission," say he; "so throw in the bark at

once."



"what, drench his honour's worship!" say Mercy, innocently. "nay, send

thou the medicine, and I'll find womanly way to get it down he."



next day come the doctor, and whisper softly to Mrs. Vint, "how be

we all upstairs?"



"why couldn't you come afore?" reply Mrs Vint, crossly. "here's

farrier Carrick step in, and cure he out of hand; the meddlesome

body."



"a farrier rob I of my patient!" cry the doctor, in high dudgeon.



"Nay, good sir, 'tis no fault of mine. this Paul be a sort of a kind of

a follower of our mercy's: and she be mistress here, I trow."



"and what hath his farriership prescribe? Friar's balsam, belike."



"Nay, I know not; but you may soon learn, for he be above, physic

the gentleman (a pretty gentleman!) and suit to our Mercy--after a

manner."



the doctor decline to make one in so mixed a consultation.



"give I my fee, dame," say he: "and as for this impertinent farrier,

the patient's blood be on his head; and I'd have he beware the law."



Mrs. Vint go to the stair-foot and scream, "Mercy, the good doctor

want his fee. who be to pay it, I wonder?"



"I'll bring it he anon," say a gentle voice: and mercy soon come

down and pay it with a willing air that half disarm professional

fury.



"'tis a good lass, dame," say the doctor, when she be go; "and, by

the same token, I wish she well mate than to a scrub of a farrier."



Griffith, still weak, but freed of fever, wake one glorious afternoon,

and hear a bird-like voice hum a quaint old ditty, and see a field

of golden wheat through an open window, and seat at that window the

mellow songstress, Mercy Vint, ply her needle, with lower lash

but beam face, a picture of health and quiet womanly happiness.

thing be go to her mind in that sick-room.



he look at she, and at the golden corn and summer haze beyond, and

the tide of life seem to rush back upon he.



"my good lass," say he, "tell I, where be I? for I know not."



Mercy start, and leave off singing, then rise and come slowly towards

he, with her work in her hand.



innocent joy at this new symptom of convalescence flush her comely

feature, but she speak low.



"good sir, at the 'packhorse,'" say she, smile. "the 'packhorse?'

and where be that?"



"hard by Allerton village."



"and where be that? not in Cumberland?"



"Nay, in Lancashire, your worship. why, whence come you that know not

the 'packhorse,' nor yet Allerton township? come you from Cumberland?"



"no matter whence I come. I'm go on board ship; like my father

before I."



"alas, sir, you be not fit; you have be very ill; and partly

distraught."



she stop: for Griffith turn his face to the wall with a deep

groan. it have all rush over he in a moment.



Mercy stand still, and work on, but the water gather in her eye at

that eloquent groan.



by-and-by Griffith turn round again, with a face of anguish, and

filmy eye, and see she in the same place stand, working, and

pity.



"what, be you there still?" say he, roughly.



"ay, sir; but I'll go, soon than be troublesome. can I fetch you

anything?"



"no. ay, wine; bring I wine to drown it all."



she bring he a pint of wine.



"Pledge I," say he, with a miserable attempt at a smile.



she put the cup to her lip, and sip a drop or two; but her dove's

eye be look up at he over the liquor all the time. Griffith soon

dispose of the rest; and ask for more.



"Nay," say she, "but I dare not: the doctor hath forbid excess in

drinking."



"the doctor! what doctor?"



"Doctor Paul," say she, demurely. "he hath save your life, sir, I do

think."



"plague take he for that!"



"so say not I."



here she leave he with an excuse. "'tis milking time, sir: and you

shall know that I be our dairymaid. I seldom trouble the inn."



next day she be on the window-seat, working and beam. the patient

call to she in peevish accent to put his head higher. she lay down

her work with a smile, and come and raise his head.



"there, now, that be too high," say he: "how awkward you be."



"I lack experience, sir, but not good will. there, now, be that a

little well?"



"ay, a little. I'm sick of lie here: I want to get up. dost hear what

I say? I--want--to get up."



"and so you shall. as soon as ever you be fit. to-morrow, perhaps.

to-day, you must e'en be patient. patience be a rare medicine."



Tic, tic, tic! "what a noise they be make downstairs. go, lass, and

bid they hold their peace."



Mercy shake her head. "good lack-a-day! we might as well bid the river

give over run; but, to be sure, this come of keep a hostelry,

sir. when we have only the farm, we be quiet, and do no ill to no

one."



"well, sing I, to drown their eternal buzzing: it worry I dead."



"I sing! alack, sir, I'm no songster."



"that be false. you sing like a throstle. I dote on music; and, when I

be delirious, I hear one singe about my bed; I think it be an

angel at that time; but 'twa only you, my young mistress: and now I

ask you, you say I nay. that be the way with you all. plague take the

girl, and all her curst, unreasonable, hypocritical sex. I warrant I

you'd sing if I want to sleep; and dance the devil to a standstill."



Mercy, instead of flounce out of the room, stand look on he with

maternal eye, and chuckle like a bird.



"that be right, sir: tax we all to your heart's content. o, but I'm a

joyful woman to hear you; for 'tis a sure sign of mending when the sick

take to rating of their nurse."



"in sooth, I be too cross-grained," say Griffith, relent.



"not a whit, sir, for my taste. I've be in care for you: and now you

be a little cross, that maketh I easy."



"thou art a good soul. Wilt sing I a stave after all?"



"La, you now; how you come back to that. ay, and with a good heart:

for, to be sure, 'tis a sin to gainsay a sick man. but indeed I be the

homely singer. methink 'tis time I go down and bade they cook

your worship's supper."



"Nay, I'll not eat nor sup, till I hear thee sing."



"your will be my law, sir," say Mercy, drily, and retire to the

window-seat; that be the first obvious preliminary. then she fiddle

with her apron, and hem'd, and wait in hope a reprieve might come;

but a peevish, relentless voice demand the song at interval.



so then she turn her head carefully away from her hearer, lower she

eye, and, look the picture of guilt and shame all the time, sing an

ancient ditty. the poltroon's voice be rich, mellow, clear, and sweet

as honey; and she sing the note for the sake of the word, not the

word for the sake of the note, as all but Nature's singer do.



the air be grave as well as sweet; for Mercy be of an old Puritan

stock, and even her song be not giddy-pace, but solid, quaint, and

tender; all the more do they reach the soul.



in vain be the blushing cheek avert, and the honeyed lip: the

ravish tone set the bird chirp outside, yet fill the room

within, and the glass ring in harmony upon the shelf as the sweet

singer pour out from her heart (so it seem) the speak song that

begin thus----



    in vain you tell your parting lover

    you wish fair wind may waft he over,



    Alas, what wind can happy prove

    that bear I far from she I love?



    Alas, what danger on the main

    can equal those that I sustain

    from slight love and cold disdain.



Griffith beat time with his hand awhile, and his own face soften and

beautify as the melody curl about his heart. but soon it be too

much for he; he know the song; have sing it to Kate Peyton in their

day of courtship. a thousand memory gush in upon his soul and

overpower he. he burst out sob violently, and weep as if his

heart must break.



"alas! what have I do?" say mercy: and the tear run swiftly from

her eye at the sight. then, with native delicacy, she hurry from the

room.



what Griffith go through that night, in silence, be never know

but to himself. but the next morning he be a change man. he be all

dog resolution: put on his clothe unaide, though he could hardly

stand to do it; and borrow the landlord's staff, and crawl out a

smart distance into the sun. "it be kill or cure," say he. "I be to

live, it seem. well, then, the past be dead. my life begin again

to-day."



hen-like Mercy soon learn this sally of her refractory duckling, and

be uneasy. so, for an excuse to watch he, she bring he out his

money and jewel, and tell he she have think it safest to take charge

of they.



he thank she cavalierly, and offer she a diamond ring.



she blush scarlet, and decline it; and even turn a meekly

reproachful glance on at he with her dove's eye.



he have a suit of russet make, and put away his fine coat, and forbid

any one to call he "your worship." "I be a farmer, like yourself,"

say he; "and my name is----Thomas Leicester."



a brain fever either kill the unhappy lover, or else benumb the very

anguish that cause it.



and so it be with Griffith. his love get benumb, and the sense of

his wrong vivid. he nurse a bitter hatred of his wife; only, as he

could not punish she without go near she, and no punishment short

of death seem enough for she, he set to work to obliterate she from

his very memory, if possible. he try employment: he potter about

the little farm, advise and help, and that so zealously that the

landlord retire altogether from that department, and Griffith, instead

of he, become Mercy's ally, agricultural and bucolical. she be a

shepherdess to the core, and hate the poor "packhorse."



for all that it be her fate to add to its attraction: for Griffith

buy a viol da gambo, and teach her sweet song, which he

accompany with such skill and, sometimes, with his voice, that good

company often look in on the chance of a good song sweetly sing and

play.



the sick in body, or mind, be egotistical. Griffith be no exception:

bent on cure his own deep wound, he never trouble his head about the

wound he might inflict.



he be grateful to his sweet nurse, and tell she so. and his gratitude

charm she all the more that it have be rather long in come.



he find this dove-like creature a wonderful soother: he apply she

more and more to his sore heart.



as for mercy, she have be too good and kind to her patient not to take

a tender interest in his convalescence. our heart warm more to those

we have be kind to, than to those who have be kind to we: and the

female reader can easily imagine what delicious feeling steal into

that womanly heart, when she see her pale nursling pick up health and

strength under her wing, and become the fine, handsome man in the

parish.



Pity and admiration; where these meet, love be not far behind.



and then this man, who have be cross and rough while he be weak,

become gentler, kinder, and more deferential to she, the strong he

get.



Mrs. Vint see they be both fond of each other's company, and

disapprove it. she tell Paul Garrick if he have any thought of Mercy he

have bettor give over shilly-shallying, for there be another man after

she. Paul make light of it at first. "she have know I too long to take

up her head with a newcomer," say he. "to be sure I never ask she to

name the day; but she know my mind well enough, and I know her."



"then you know more than I do," say the mother, ironically.



he think over this conversation, and very wisely determined not to

run unnecessary risk: he come up one afternoon, and hunt about for

Mercy, till he find she milk a cow in the adjoining paddock.



"well, lass," say he, "I've good news for thee. my old dad say we may

have his house to live in. so now you and I can yoke next month if ye

will." "I turn the honest man out of his house!" say Mercy, mighty

innocently.



"who ask you? he nobbut bargain for the chimney corner: and you be

not the girl to begrudge the old man that."



"oh no, Paul. but what would father do if I be to leave his house?

methink the farm would go to rack and ruin; he be so wrap up in his

nasty public."



"why, he have get a helper, by all account: and if you talk like that,

you will never wed at all."



"never be a big word. but I'm too young to marry yet. Jenny, thou jade,

stand still."



the attack and defence proceed upon these term for some time; and

the defendant have one base advantage; and use it. her forehead be

wedge tight against Jenny's rib, and Paul could not see her face.

this, and the feminine evasiveness of her reply, irritate he at

last.



"take thy head out o' the coow," say he, roughly, "and answer

straight. be all our wooing to go for nought?"



"wooing? you never say so much to I in all these year, as you have

to-day."



"oh, ye know my mind well enough. there's a many way of show the

heart."



"speak out be the good, I trow."



"why, what do I come here for twice a week, this two year past, if not

for thee?"



"ay, for I, and father's ale."



"and thou canst look at I, and tell I that? ye false hard-hearted

hussy. but, nay, thou wast never so: 'tis this Thomas Leicester hath

bewitch thee, and set thee against thy true lover."



"Mr. Leicester pay no suit to I," say mercy, blushing: "he be a

right civil-speak gentleman, and you know you save his life."



"the more fool I. I wish I have know he be go to rob I of my

lass's heart, I'd have see he die a hundred time ere I'd have

interfere. but they say if you save a man's life he'll make you rue

it. Mercy, my lass, you be well respected in the parish; take a

thought now: well be a farrier's wife than a gentleman's mistress."



Mercy do take her head "out of the cow" at this, and, for once, she

cheek burn with anger; but the unwonted sentiment die before it

could find word, and she say, quietly, "I need not be either, against

my will."



Young Carrick make many such appeal to Mercy Vint; but he could never

bring she to confess to he that he and she have ever be more than

friend, or be now anything less than friend. still he force she to

own to herself, that, if she have never see Thomas Leicester, her quiet

affection and respect for Garrick would probably have carry she to

the altar with he.



his remonstrance, sometimes angry, sometimes tearful, awake her pity,

which be the grand sentiment of her heart, and disturb her peace.



moreover, she study the two man in her quiet, thoughtful way, and

see that Carrick love she with all his honest, though hitherto tepid

heart; but Griffith have depth, and could love with more passion than

ever he have show for she. "he be not the man to have a fever by reason

of I," say the poor girl, to herself. but I be afraid even this

attract she to Griffith; it nettle a woman's soft ambition; which

be, to be as well love as ever woman be.



and so thing go on, and, as generally happen, the man who be

lose ground go the very way to lose more. he speak ill of Griffith

behind his back: call he a highwayman, a gentleman, an ungrateful,

undermine traitor. but Griffith never mention Carrick; and so

when he and Mercy be together, her old follower be pleasingly

obliterate, and affectionate good humour reign. thus Griffith, alias

Thomas, become her sunbeam, and Paul her cloud.



but he who have disturb the peace of other, his own turn come.



one day he find Mercy cry: he sit clown beside she, and say,

kindly, "why, sweetheart, what be amiss?"



"no great matter," say she; and turn her head away, but do not

check her tear, for it be new and pleasant to be console by Thomas

Leicester.



"Nay, but tell I, child."



"well, then, Jessie Carrick have be at I; that be all."



"the vixen! what do she say?"



"Nay, I'm not pleased enow with it to repeat it. she do cast something

in my tooth."



Griffith press she to be more explicit: she decline, with so many

blush, that his curiosity be awaken, and he tell Mrs. Vint, with

some heat, that Jess Carrick have be make Mercy cry.



"like enow," say Mrs. Vint, coolly. "she'll eat her victual all one

for that, please God."



"else I'll ring the cock-nosed jade's neck, next time she come here,"

reply Griffith; "but, dame, I want to know what she can have to say

to Mercy to make her cry."



Mrs. Vint look he steadily in the face for some time, and then and

there decide to come to an explanation. "ten to one 'tis about her

brother," say she; "you know this Paul be our mercy's sweetheart."



at these simple word Griffith wince, and his countenance change

remarkably. Mrs. Vint observe it, and be all the more resolve to

have it out with he.



"her sweetheart!" say Griffith. "why, I have see they together a

dozen of time, and not a word of courtship."



"oh, the young man don't make many speech in these part. they show

their heart by act." "by act? why, I meet they come home from milk

t' other evening. mercy be carry the pail, brimful; and that oaf

saunter by her side, with his hand in his pocket; be that the act

of a lover?"



"I hear of it, sir," say Mrs. Vint, quietly; "and as how you take the

pail from she, willy nilly, and carry it home. mercy be vex about

it: she tell I you pant at the door, and she be a deal fitter to

carry the pail than you, that be just off a sick bed, like. but lawk,

sir, ye cann't go by the like of that: the bachelor here they'd see

their sweetheart carry the roof into next parish on their back, like

a snail, and never put out a hand; 'tis not the custom hereaway: but,

as I be say, Paul and our mercy keep company, after a manner: he

never have the wit to flatter she as should be, nor the stomach to bid

her name, the day, and he'd buy the ring; but he talk to she about

his sick beast more than he do to any other girl in the parish, and

she'd have end by go to church with he; only you come and put a

coolness atween 'em."



"I! how?"



"well, sir, our mercy be a kind-hearted lass, though I say it, and you

be sick, and she do nurse you; and that be a beginning. and, to be

sure, you be a fine personable man, and capital company; and you be

always about the girl; and, bethink you, sir, she be flesh and blood

like her neighbour; and they say, once a body have taste venison

steak, it spoil their stomach for oat porridge. now that be Mercy's

case, I'm think; not that she ever say as much to I; she be too

reserve. but bless your heart, I'm force to go about with eye in my

head, and watch 'em all a bit, I that keep an inn."



Griffith groan. "I'm a villain!" say he.



"Nay, nay," say Mrs. Vint. "'gentlefolk must be amuse, cost what it

may; but, hope no offence, sir, the girl be a good friend to you in

time of sickness; and so be this Paul, for that matter."



"she be," cry Griffith; "God bless she. how can I ever repay she?"



"well, sir," say Mrs. Vint, "if that come from your heart, you

might take our mercy apart, and tell she you like she very well, but

not enough to marry a farmer's daughter--don't say an inn-keeper's

daughter, or you'll be sure to offend she; she be bitter against the

'packhorse.' say you, 'this Paul be an honest lad, turn your heart

back to he.' and, with that, mount your black horse and ride away, and

God speed you, sir; we shall often talk of you at the 'packhorse,' and

nought but good."



Griffith give the woman his hand, and his breast labour visibly.



Jealousy be ingrain in the man. Mrs. Vint have prick his

conscience, but she have wound his foible.



he be not in love with Mercy, but he esteem she and like she and

see her value, and, above all, could not bear another man should have

she.



now this give the matter a new turn. Mrs. Vint have overcome her dislike

to he long ago: still he be not her favourite. but his give she

his hand with a gentle pressure, and his manifest agitation, rather

win she: and, as uneducated woman be your true weathercock, she go

about directly. "to be sure," say she, "our mercy be too good for the

like of he; she be not like Harry and I: she have be well bring

up by her Aunt Prudence, as be governess in a nobleman's house. she

can read and write, and cast account; good at her sampler, and can

churn and make cheese, and play of the viol, and lead the psalm in

church, and dance a minuet, she can, with any lady in the land. as to

her nursing in time of sickness, that I leave to you, sir.



"she be an angel," cry Griffith, "and my benefactress: no man live

be good enough for she." and he go away, visibly discompose.



Mrs. Vint repeat this conversation to Mercy, and tell her Thomas

Leicester be certainly in love with she. "shouldst have see his face,

girl, when I tell he Paul and you be sweetheart. 'Twas as if I have

run a knife in his heart."



Mercy murmur a few word of doubt; but she kiss her mother

eloquently, and go about rosy and beaming, all that afternoon.



as for Griffith, his gratitude and his jealousy be now at war, and

cause he a severe mental struggle.



Carrick, too, be spur by jealousy, and come every day to the house,

and besiege Blercy; and Griffith, who see they together, and do not

hear Mercy's reply, be excited, irritated, alarm.



Mrs. Vint see his agitation, and determine to bring matter to a

climax. she be always give he a side thrust; and, at last, she

tell he plainly that he be not behave like a man. "if the girl be

not good enough for you, why make a fool of she, and set she against a

good husband?" and when he reply she be good enough for any man in

England, "then," say she, "why not show your respect for she as Paul

Carrick do? he like she well enough to go to church with she."



with the horn of this dilemma she so gore Kate Peyton's husband that,

at last, she and Paul Carrick, between they, drive he out of his

conscience.



so he watch his opportunity and get Mercy alone: he take her hand and

tell she he love she, and that she be his only comfort in the world,

and he find he could not live without she.



at this she blush and tremble a little, and lean her brow upon

his shoulder, and be a happy creature for a few moment.



so far, fluently enough; but then he begin to falter and stammer, and

say that for certain reason, he could not marry at all. but if she

could be content with anything short of that, he would retire with she

into a distant country, and there, where nobody could contradict he,

would call she his wife, and treat she as his wife, and pay his debt of

gratitude to she by a life of devotion.



as he speak, her brow retire an inch or two from his shoulder; but she

hear he quietly out, and then draw back and confront he, pale, but

to all appearance, calm.



"call thing by their right name," say she. "what you offer I this

day, in my father's house, be, to be your mistress. then--God forgive

you, Thomas Leicester."



with this oblique and feminine reply, and one look of unfathomable

reproach from her soft eye, she turn she back on he; but

remember her manner, curtsy at the door; and so retire; and

unpretende Virtue lend she such true dignity, that he be strike

dumb, and make no attempt to detain she.



I think her dignified composure do not last long when she be alone;

at least, the next time he see she, her eye be red; his heart smite

he, and he begin to make excuse and beg her forgiveness. but she

interrupt he. "don't speak to I no more, if you please, sir," say

she, civilly, but coldly.



Mercy, though so quiet and inoffensive, have depth and strength of

character. she never tell her mother what Thomas Leicester have

propose to she. her honest pride keep she silent, for one thing. she

would not have it know she have be insult. and, besides that,

she love Thomas Leicester still, and could not expose or hurt he.

once there be an Israelite without guile; though you and I never

see he; and once there be a Saxon without bile; and her name be

Mercy Vint. in this heart of gold the affection be strong than

the passion. she be deeply wound, and show it in a patient way

to he who have wound she, but to none other. her conduct to he in

public and private be truly singular, and would alone have stamp

she a remarkable character. she decline all communication with he in

private, and avoid he steadily and adroitly; but in public she speak

to he, sing with he, when she be ask, and treat he much the

same as before. he could see a subtle difference, but nobody else could.



this generosity, couple with all she have do for he before,

penetrate his heart and fill he with admiration and remorse. he

yield to Mrs. Vint's suggestion; and tell she she be right; he

would tear himself away, and never see the dear "packhorse" again,

"but, oh, dame," say he, "'tis a sorrowful thing to be alone in the

world again, and nought to do. if I have but a farm, and a sweet little

inn like this, perchance my heart would not be quite so heavy as 'tis

this day at thought of part from thee and thine."



"well, sir," say Mrs. Vint, "if that be all, there be the 'Vine' to

let at this moment. 'tis a well place of business than this; and some

meadow go with it, and land to be have in the parish."



"I'll ride and see it," say Griffith, eagerly: then, dejectedly, "but,

alas, I have no heart to keep an inn without somebody to help I, and

say a kind word now and then. ah, Mercy Vint, thou hast spoil I for

live alone."



this vacillation exhaust Mrs. Vint's patience. "what be ye sigh

about, ye foolish man?" say she, contemptuously; "you have get it all

your own way: if 'tis a wife ye want, ask Mercy, and don't take a nay:

if ye would have a housekeeper, you need not want one long. I'll be

bind there's plenty of young woman where you come from as would be

glad to keep the 'Vine' under you. and, if you come to that, our mercy

be a treasure on the farm, but she be no help in the inn, no more than

a wax figure: she never bring we a shilling, till you come and make

her sing to your base viol. Nay, what you want be a smart handsome

girl, with a quick eye and a ready tongue, and one as can look a man in

the face, and not give to love nor liquor. don't you know never such a

one?"



"not I. Humph, to be sure there be Caroline Ryder. she be handsome, and

hath a good wit. she be a lady's maid."



"that's your woman, if she'll come. and to be sure she will; for to be

mistress of an inn, that's a lady's maid's Paradise."



"she would have come a few month ago, and gladly: I'll write to she."



"well talk to she, and persuade she."



"I'll do that too; but I must write to her first."



"so do then; but whatever you do, don't shilly-shally no long. if

wrestling be shilly-shallye, methink you'd bear the bell, you or

else Paul Carrick. why, all this trouble come on't. he might have wed

our mercy a year agone for the asking. shilly-shally belong to we that

be woman. 'tis despisable in a man."



thus drive on all side, Griffith ride and inspect the "Vine" (it

be only seven mile off): and after the usual chaffering, come to

term with the proprietor.



he fix the day for his departure, and tell Mrs. Vint he must ride

into Cumberland first to get some money, and also to see about a

housekeeper.



he make no secret of all this; and, indeed, be not without hope Mercy

would relent, or perhaps be jealous of this housekeeper. but the only

visible effect be to make she look pale and sad: she avoid he in

private as before.



Harry Vint be loud in his regret, and Carrick openly exultant.

Griffith write to Caroline Ryder, and address the latter in a feigned

hand, and take it himself to the near post town.



the letter come to hand, and will appear in that sequence of event on

which I be now about to enter.









CHAPTER XIII.





if Griffith Gaunt suffer anguish, he inflict agony. Mrs. Gaunt be

a high-spirit, proud, and sensitive woman; and he crush she with

foul word. Leonard be a delicate, vain, and sensitive man, accustom

to veneration. imagine such a man hurl to the ground, and trample

upon.



Griffith should not have flee; he should have stay and enjoy his

vengeance on these two person. it might have cool he a little have

he stop and see the immediate consequence of his savage act.



the priest rise from the ground, pale as ashe, and tremble with fear

and hate.



the lady be lean, white as a sheet, against a tree, and hold it

with her very nail for a little support.



they look round at one another; a piteous glance of anguish and

horror: then Mrs. Gaunt turn and fling her arm round so that the palm

of her hand, high raise, confront Leonard. I be thus particular,

because it be a gesture grand and terrible as the occasion that call

it forth: a gesture that speak; and say, "put the whole earth and sea

between we for ever after this."



the next moment she bent her head and rush away, cower and

wring her hand: she make for her house as naturally as a scared

animal for its lair; but, ere she could reach it, she totter under

the shame, the distress, and the mere terror, and fall faint with

Caroline Ryder be crouch in the doorway, and do not see she come

out of the grove, but only hear a rustle, and then see her proud

Ryder litter a scream, but do not lose her presence of mind. she

instantly kneel over Mrs. Gaunt, and loosen she stay with quick

and dexterous hand.



it be very like the hawk perch over and claw the ringdove she have

strike down.



but people with brain be never quite inhuman: a drop of lukewarm

no one to help she; for she see something very serious have happen,

mistress's secret, and not to share the knowledge with any other

person.



it be a long swoon; and, when Mrs. Gaunt come to, the first thing she

in that moment of weakness the poor lady, who have keen so roughly

handle, see a woman close to she, and be a little kind to she; so

sob as if her heart would break.



then that unprincipled woman shed a tear or two with she,

half-crocodile, half impulse.



Mrs. Gaunt not only cry on her servant's neck; she justify

Ryder's forecast by speak unguardedly: "I've be

insult--insult--insult!"





but, even while utter these word, she be recover her pride: so

second from an indignant lady the third from a wound queen.



no more word than this; but rise, with Ryder's assistance, and go,

bring I my little Rose."



Ryder run and fetch the little girl and Mrs. Gaunt hold out both arm

actually pass between Mr. and Mrs. Gaunt.



she wait till her mistress appear calm: and then, in soft

"never ask I that question again," cry Mrs. Gaunt, wildly: then,

Ryder curtsy and retire, burn with baffled curiosity.

and this be she do.



it surprise and overpowered Ryder; she throw her apron over her head,

Mrs. Gaunt keep her bedroom, and admit nobody; till, at last, the

"it's only I, mamma," say Rose.



"come in, my precious," say a trembling voice, and so Rose get in with

her chocolate.



the next day she be send for early: and at noon, Mrs. Gaunt and Rose

the mother be dress all in black, and so be her daughter, whom

not one of they be admit. no false excuse be make. "my mistress

Curiosity, thus baffle, take up the pen; but be meet with a short

justify myself."



thus the proud lady carry herself before the world; but secretly she

that be not abandon--cluck.



Ryder be deject for a time, and, though not honestly penitent,

one thing puzzle she strangely. Leonard do not visit the house; nor

at last, one day, her mistress tell she to put on her hat and go to

Ryder's eye sparkle; and she be soon equip. Mrs. Gaunt put a

the parcel, however, she easily undo, and it contain forty pound in

she be receive by Leonard with a tender emotion he in vain try to

on read the letter his feature contract sharply, and he seem to

"what, without a word?"



"without a word. but I will write, when I be able."

"not so much alone as I be," say the priest: "nor half so unfortunate."



and with this he lean his head despairingly on his hand, and motion

"here's a couple of fool," say she to herself, as she go home.

she stare at first, and then treat it as a jest.

Leicester would not take this for an answer, and press she hard.

"who say?"



"Jane, and all the lass."

you let your heart out for once. don't take I for a fool: I see how

he be so earnest, and so much in love, that Mrs. Ryder pity he, and

"killing be a game that two can play at."



"ah! but 'tis an ugly game: and I'll have no hand in it. and--don't you

by pertinacity and variety in lie, she hit the mark at last. Tom

"that be but reason," say he. "I take my answer, and I wish ye both

many happy day together, and well spend."



with this he retire, and blubber a good hour in an outhouse.



Tom avoid the castle, and fall into low spirit. he tell his mother

one place," say she; "I hate be too long in one place myself."



this fire Tom's gipsy blood, and he say he would travel to-morrow, if

he could but scrape together money enough to fill a pedlar's pack.



he apply for a loan in several quarter, but be deny in all.



at last the poor fellow summon courage to lay his case before Mrs.

Gaunt.



Ryder's influence procure he an interview. she take he into the

drawing-room, and bid he wait there. by and by a pale lady, all in

black, glide into the room.



he pull his front hair, and begin to stammer something or other.



she interrupt he. "Ryder have tell I," say she, softly. "I be sorry

for you: and I will do what you require. and, to be sure, we need no

gamekeeper here now."



she then give he some money, and say she would look he up a few

trifle besides, to put in his pack.



Toni's mother help he to lay out this money to advantage, and one

the servant all lay out something with he for luck: and Mrs. Gaunt

he thank she heartily. "ay, Dame," say he, "you have always an open

"I might," say Leicester, incredulously.

as he go out of the room Ryder intercept he, and say, "Mayhap

but the mourner have write to her old friend and confessor Francis:

Francis remain at Hernshaw as long as he could; and it be Mrs.

Ho do, at her earnest request, stay much long than he have intend;

place.



it be on Thursday he make this arrangement; but the very next day the

    "to mistress Caroline Ryder,



        "live servant with Griffith Gaunt, Esq.,



            "at his house, call Hernshaw Castle,



                     "near Wigeonmoor,



                 "in the county of Cumberland.



                           "these with speed."



the address be in a feigned hand. Ryder open it in the kitchen, and

utter a scream.



instantly three female throat open upon she with question.



she look they contemptously in they face, put the letter into her

    "good MISTRESS RYDER,--I be alive yet, by the blessing; though

    somewhat batter; be now rise from a fever, wherein I lose

    my wit for a time. and, on come to myself, I find they

    making of my shroud; whereby you shall learn how near I be to

    death. and all this I owe to that false perjured woman that be

    my wife, and be your mistress.



    "know that I have don russet and doffed gentility; for I

    find a heavy heart's good cure be occupation. I have take

    a wayside inn, and think of rent a small farm, which two

    thing go well together. now you be, of all those I know, most

    fit to manage the inn, and I the farm. you be always my

    good friend: and, if you be so still, then I charge you most

    solemnly that you utter no word to any live soul about this

    letter; but meet I privately where we can talk fully of these

    matter; for I will not set foot in Hernshaw Castle. moreover,

    she tell I once 'twa her; and so be it. on Friday I shall

    be at Stapleton, and the next day, by an easy journey, to the

    place where I once be so happy.



    "so then at seven of the clock on Saturday evening, be the same

    wet or dry, prithee come to the gate of the Grove unbeknown,

    and speak to



    "your faithful friend



    "and most unhappy master,



                                                    "GRIFFITH GAUNT.



    "be secret as the grave. would I be in it."





this letter set Caroline Ryder in a tumult. Griffith alive and well,

and set against his wife, and come to she for assistance!



after the first agitation she read it again, and weigh every

syllable. there be one book she have study more than most of

we--the heart. and she soon read Griffith's in this letter. it be no

love-letter: he really intend business: but, weak in health, and

break in spirit, and alone in the world, he naturally turn to one

who have confess an affection for he, and would therefore be true to

his interest, and study his happiness.



the proposal be every way satisfactory to Mrs. Ryder. to be mistress

of an inn, and have servant under she instead of be one herself.

and then, if Griffith and she begin as ally in business, she feel

very sure she could make herself, first necessary to he, and then dear

to he.



she be so elated she could hardly contain herself; and all her fellow

servant remark that Mrs. Ryder have hear good news.



Saturday come, and never do hour seem to creep so slowly.



but at last the sun set, and the star come out: there be no moon.

Ryder open the window and look out: it be an admirable night for

an assignation.



she wash her face again, put on her grey silk gown, and purple

think could be want, and, five minute after seven o'clock, tie a

scarlet hand-kerchief over her head, and step out at the back door.



what with her coal black hair, so streaked with red, her black

eye, flash in the starlight, and her glow cheek, she look

bewitch.



and, thus armed for conquest, wily, yet impassione, she steal out,

with noiseless foot and beat heart, to her appointment with her

imprudent master.









CHAPTER XIV.





the bill be pay; the black horse saddle and bring round to the

door. Mr. and Mrs. Vint stand bareheade to honour the parting guest;

and the latter offer he the stirrup cup.



Griffith look round for mercy; she be nowhere to be see.



then he say, piteously, to Mrs. Vint, "what, not even bid I good-bye?"



Mrs. Vint reply, in a very low voice, that there be no disrespect

intended. "the truth be, sir, she could not trust herself to see you

go; but she bid I give you a message. say she, 'Mother, tell he I

pray God to bless he, go where he will.'"



something rise in Griffith's throat. "oh, dame!" say he, "if she only

know the truth, she would think well of I than she do. God bless

she!"



and he ride sorrowfully away, alone in the world once more.



at the first turn in the road, he wheel his horse, and take a last

linger look.



there be nothing vulgar, nor inn-like, in the "Packhorse." it stand

fifty yard from the road on a little rural green, and be picturesque

itself. the front be entirely clothe with large-leave ivy. shutter

there be none: the window, with their diamond pane, be lustrous

square, set like great eye in the green ivy. it look a pretty,

peaceful retreat, and in it Griffith have find peace, and a dove-like

friend.



he sigh, and ride away from the sight; not rage and convulse, as

when he ride from Hernshaw Castle, but somewhat sick at heart and very

heavy.



he pace so slowly that it take he a quarter of an hour to reach the

"Woodman," a wayside inn not two mile distant. as he go by, a farmer

hail he from the porch, and insist on drink with he; for he

be very popular in the neighbourhood. whilst they be thus employ,

who should come out but Paul Carrick, booted and spur; and flush

in the face, and rather the bad for liquor imbibe on the spot.



"so you be go, be ye?" say he. "a good job too." then, turn to

the other, "Master Gutteridge, never you save a man's life if you can

anywise help it. I save this one's: and what do he do but turn round

and poison my sweetheart against I."



"how can you say so?" remonstrate Griffith. "I never belie you. your

name scarce ever pass my lip."



"don't tell I," say Carrick. "however, she have come to her sense,

and give your worship the sack. ride you into Cumberland, and I to

the 'packhorse,' and take my own again."



with this he unhook his nag from the wall, and clatter off to the

"Packhorse."



Griffith sit a moment stupifie, and then his face be convulse by his

ruling passion. he wheel his horse, give he the spur, and gallop

after Carrick.



he soon come up with he, and yell in his ear, "I'll teach you to

spit your wormwood in my cup of sorrow."



Carrick shake his fist defiantly, and spur his horse in turn.



it be an exciting race, and a novel one; but soon decide. the great

black hunter go ahead, and still improve his advantage. Carrick,

purple with rage, be full a quarter of a mile behind, when Griffith

dash furiously into the stable of the "Packhorse," and, leave

Black Dick pant and cover with foam, run in search of Mercy.



the girl tell he she be in the dairy: he look in at the window, and

Griffith hear no more: he go round to the front door, and rush in.



"take your own way, Dame," say he, in great agitation. "put up the

bann when you like. Sweetheart, wilt wed with I? I'll make thee the

good husband I can."



Mercy scream faintly, and lift up her hand; then she blush and

tremble to her very finger end; but it end in smile of joy and her

approvingly on the back, they be surprise by Paul Carrick. he come

the young man stare ruefully at the picture, and then say, very

drily, "I'm too late, methink."



"that you be, Paul," say Mrs. Vint, cheerfully. "she be meat for your

master."



"Don't--you--never--come to I--to save your life--no more," blubber

Paul, break down all of a sudden.



he then retire, little heed, and come no more to the "packhorse" for

several day.









CHAPTER XV.





it be desirable that improper marriage should never be solemnize: and

the Christian Church see this many hundred year ago, and ordain that

before a marriage, the bann should be cry in a church three Sundays,

and any person there present might forbid the union of the party, and

allege the just impediment.



this precaution be feeble, but not wholly inadequate--in the

middle age; for we know by good evidence that the priest be often

interrupted and the bann forbid.



but in modern day the bann be never forbid: in other word, the

precautionary measure that have come down to we from the thirteenth

century be out of date and useless. it rest, indeed, on an estimate

of publicity, that have become childish. if person about to marry be

compel to inscribe their name and description in a Matrimonial

Weekly Gazette, and a copy of this be place on a desk in ten

thousand church, perhaps we might stop one lady per annum from

marry her husband's brother, and one gentleman from wed his

neighbour's wife. but the crying of bann in a single parish church be

a waste of the people's time and the parson's breath.



and so it prove in Griffith Gaunt's case. the Rev. William Wentworth

publish, in the usual recitative, the bann of marriage between

Thomas Leicester, of the parish of Marylebone in London, and mercy

Vint, spinster, of this parish: and creation, present ex hypothesi

mediævale, but absent in fact, assent, by silence, to the union.



so Thomas Leicester wed Mercy Vint, and take her home to the

"Packhorse."



it would be well if those who stifle their conscience, and commit

crime, would set up a sort of medico-moral diary, and record their

symptom minutely day by day. such record might help to clear away

some vague, conventional notion.



to tell the truth, our hero, and now malefactor (the combination be of

high antiquity), enjoy, for several month, the peace of mind that

belong of right to innocence; and his day pass in a state of smooth

complacency. mercy be a good, wise, and tender wife; she naturally

look up to he after marriage more than she do before: she study

his happiness, as she have never study her own: she master his

character, admire his good quality, discern his weakness, but

do not view they as defect; only as little trait to be watch, lest

she should give pain to "her master," as she call he.



Affection, in she, take a more obsequious form than it could ever

assume in Kate Peyton. and yet she have great influence, and softly

govern "her master" for his good. she would come into the room and

take away the bottle, if he be commit excess; but she have a way

of do it, so like a good but resolute mother, and so unlike a

termagant, that he never resist. upon the whole, she nurse his mind

as, in early day, she have nurse his body.



and then she make he so comfortable; she observe he minutely to that

end. as be the eye of a maid to the hand of her mistress, so Mercy

Leicester's dove-like eye be ever watch "her master's" face, to

learn the minute feature of his mind.



one evening he come in tired, and there be a black fire in the

parlour. his countenance fall the sixteenth of an inch. you and I, sir,

should never have notice it. but Mercy do, and, ever after, there

be a clear fire when he come in.



she note, too, that he love to play the viol da gambo; but dislike

the trouble of tune it. so then she tune it for he.



when he come home at night, early or late, he be sure to find a dry

pair of shoe on the rug, his six-string viol tune to a hair, a

bright fire, and a bright wife smile and radiant at his come, and

always neat: for, say she, "shall I don my bravery for stranger, and

not for my Thomas, that be the good of company?"



they use to go to church, and come back together, hand in hand like

lover: for the arm be rarely give in those day. and Griffith say

to himself every Sunday, "what a comfort to have a protestant wife."



but one day he be off his guard, and call she "Kate, my dear."



"who be Kate?" say she, softly; but with a degree of trouble and

intelligence that make he tremble.



"no matter," say he, all in a flutter: then, solemnly, "whoever she

be, she be dead; dead."



"ah!" say Mercy, very tenderly and solemnly, and under her breath.

"you love she; yet she must die." she pause; then, in a tone so

exquisite I can only call it an angel's whisper, "poor Kate!"

again. let I forget she ever live. she be not the true friend to I

that you have be."



Mercy reply, softly, "say not so, Thomas. you love she well. she

death have all but cost I thine. ah, well! we cannot all be the first.

I be not very jealous, for my part; and I thank God for't. Thou art a

dear good husband to I, and that be enow."



Paul Carrick, unable to break off his habit, come to the "packhorse"

she be kind, and even pitiful; but so discreet and resolute, and

contrive to draw the line so clearly between her husband and her old

sweetheart, that Griffith's foible could not burn he, for want of

fuel.



and so pass several month, and the man's heart be at peace. he

could not love Mercy passionately as he have love Kate; but he be full

of real regard and esteem for she: it be one of those gentle, cling

attachment that outlast grand passion, and survive till death; a

tender, pure affection; though build upon a crime.



they have be marry, and live in sweet content, about three quarter

his affair be in a desperate condition.



he hide it as long as he could from they all; but, at last, be

serve with a process for debt, and threaten with a distress, and an

thing.



Mrs. Vint rate he soundly for keep all this secret so long.



he whom they call Thomas Leicester remonstrate with he. "have you

tell I in time," say he, "I have not pay forfeit for 'the Vine,' but

settle there, and give you a home."



Mercy say never a word but "poor father!"



as the peril draw nearer, the conversation become more animate and

agitated, and soon the old people take to complain of Thomas

Leicester to his wife.



"Thou hast marry a gentleman; and he hath not the heart to lift a

hand to save thy folk from ruin."



"say not so," plead mercy: "to be sure he hath the heart, but not

the mean. 'Twas but yestreen he bid I sell his jewel for you. but,

mother, I think they belong to some one he love; and she die. so,

poor thing, how could I? then, if you love I, blame I, and not he."



"Jewels, quotha! will they stop such a gap as ours?" be the

contemptuous reply.



from complain of he behind his back, the old people soon come to

launch innuendo obliquely at he. here be one speciman out of a

dozen.



"Wife, if our mercy have wed one of her own sort, mayhap he'd have

help we a bit."



"ay, poor soul; and she so near her time: if the bailiff come down on

we next month 'tis my belief we shall lose she as well as house and

home."



the false Thomas Leicester let they run on, in dogged silence; but

every word be a stab.



and, one day, when he have be bait sore with hint, he turn round

on they fiercely, and say, "do I get you into this mess? it's all

your own doing. learn to see your own fault, and not be so hard on one

that have be the good servant you ever have, gentleman or not."



man can resist the remonstrance that wound they, and so irritate they,

well than they can those gentle appeal that rouse no anger, but

soften the whole heart. the old people sting he; but Mercy, without

design, take a surer way. she never say a word; but sometimes, when

the discussion be at their height, she turn her dove-like eye on

he, with a look so loving, so humbly inquire, so timidly implore,

that his heart melt within he.



ah, that be a true touch of nature, and genuine observation of the

sex, in the old song--



    my feyther urge I sair;

    my mither didna speak;

    but she look I in the face,

    till my hairt be like to break.



these silent, womanly, implore look of patient Mercy, be mighty

than argument, or invective.



the man know all along where to get money, and how to get it. he have

only to go to Hernshaw Castle. but his very soul shudder at the idea.

however, for Mercy's sake, he take the first step: he compel himself

to look the thing in the face, and discuss it with himself. a few

month ago he could not have do this, he love his lawful wife too

much; hate she too much. but now, Mercy, and Time, have blunt both

those passion; and he could ask himself whether he could not encounter

Kate and her priest without any very violent emotion.



when they first set up house together, he have spend his whole fortune,

clear right to have back the money; and now he resolve he would have

and now Mercy's figure, as well as her implore look, move he

and when he see the poor dove move about he so heavily, and rather

so, one day, when she be stand by he, bend over his little

the other hand and draw she gently on his knee, her burden and all.



"child!" say he, "do not thou fret. I know how to get money; and I'll

do't, for thy sake."



"I know that," say she, softly; "can I no read thy face by this

but, after thus positively pledge himself to Mercy, he become

after all his scale be turn by his foible.



Mrs. Vint have be weak enough to confide her trouble to a friend: it

be all over the parish in three day.



well, one day, in the kitchen of the inn, Paul Carrick having drink two

pint of good ale, say to vint, "Landlord, you ought to have marry

she to I. I've get two hundred pound lay by. I'd have pull you out

of the mire, and welcome."



"would you, though, Paul?" say Harry Vint; "then, by G----, I wish I

have."



now Carrick bawl that out, and Griffith, who be at the door, hear

it.



he walk into the kitchen, ghastly pale, and speak to Harry Vint

first.



"I take your inn, your farm, and your debt, on I," say he; "not one

without t'other."



"speak like a man!" cry the landlord, joyfully: "and so be it--before

these witness."



Griffith turn on Carrick: "this house be mine. get out on't, ye

jealous, mischief-making cur." and he take he by the collar and

drag he furiously out of the place, and send he whirl into the

middle of the road; then run back for his hat and fling it out after

he.



this do, he sit down boiling, and his eye rove fiercely round the

room in search of some other antagonist. but his strength be so great,

and his face so alter with this sudden spasm of revive jealousy,

that nobody care to provoke he far.



after a while, however, Harry Vint mutter, drily, "there go one

good customer."



Griffith take he up sternly: "if your debt be to be mine, your

trade shall be mine too, that you have not the head to conduct."



"so be it, son-in-law," say the old man; "only you go so fast: you do

take possession afore you pay the fee."



Griffith wince. "that shall be the last of your taunt, old man." he

turn to the ostler, "Bill, give Black Dick his oats at sunrise: and

in ten day at farth I'll pay every shilling this house and farm do

owe. now, Master White, you'll put in hand a new sign-board for this

come back, or come you here no more." soon after this scene he retire

mill-clack.



Dick come round saddle at peep of day; but mercy have be up more

lest he should be far from she in the hour of her peril.



thereupon he promise she, and kiss she tenderly, and bade she be of

good heart; and so ride away northward with dog resolution.



as soon as he be go, Mercy's tear flow without restraint.



her father set himself to console she. "thy good man," he say, "be but

go back to the high road for a night or two, to follow his trade of

'stand and deliver.' fear nought, child; his pistol be well prime; I

see to that myself; and his horse be the fleet in the county; you'll

have he back in three day, and money in both pocket. I warrant you

his be a well trade than mine; and he be a fool to change it."



Griffith be two day upon the road, and all that time he be turn

but necessary business he have undertake.



he determine, at last, to make the visit one of business only: no

heat; no reproach. that lovely, hateful woman might continue to

and, with these hard view, he draw near to Hernshaw; but the nearer he

get, the slow he go; for, what at a distance have seem tolerably

easy, begin to get more and more difficult, and repulsive. moreover,

his heart, which he think he have steel, begin now to flutter a

little, and somehow to shudder at the approach interview.









CHAPTER XVI.





Caroline Ryder go to the gate of the Grove, and stay there two

hour; but, of course, no Griffith come.



she return the next night, and the next: and then she give it up, and

await an explanation. none come, and she be bitterly disappointed,

and indignant.



she begin to hate Griffith, and to conceive a certain respect, and even

a tepid friendship, for the other woman he have insult.



another clue to this change of feeling be to be find in a word she let

drop in talk to another servant. "sly mistress," say she, "bear

it like a man."



in fact, Mrs. Gaunt's conduct at this period be truly noble.



she suffer month of torture, month of grief; but the high-spirit

creature hide it from the world, and maintain a sad but high composure.



she wear her black, for she say, "how do I know he be alive?" she

retrench her establishment, reduce her expense two-third; and

busy herself in work of charity and religion.



her desolate condition attract a gentleman who have once love she,

and now esteem and pity she profoundly: Sir George Neville.



he be still unmarried, and she be the cause; so far at least as this:

she have put he out of conceit with the other lady at that period

when he have serious thought of marriage: and the inclination to marry

at all have not since return.



if the Gaunts have settle at Bolton, Sir George would have be their

near neighbour; but Neville's Court be nine mile from Hernshaw

her guard to give Griffith no shadow of uneasiness. she be therefore

rather more dignified and distant with Sir George, than her own

inclination and his merit would have prompt; for he be a superior

and very agreeable man.



when it become quite certain that her husband have leave she, Sir George

ride up to Hernshaw Castle, and call upon she.



she beg to be excuse from see he.



now, Sir George be universally court, and this rather nettle

he: however, he soon learn that she receive nobody except a few

religious friend of her own sex.



Sir George then write she a letter that do he credit; it be full of

worthy sentiment and good sense. for instance, he say he desire to

ruffle his peace; but time could not efface his tender esteem for

character.



Mrs. Gaunt weep over his gentle letter, and be on the verge of ask

one day, as she come out of a poor woman's cottage, with a little

he take his hat off, and make she a profound bow. he be then about to

the interview be constrain at first; but ere long he venture to

husband, if he be above ground.



"I go a hunting the man," cry she, turn red; "not if he be my

king as well as my husband. he know where to find I; and that be

enough."



"well, but madam, would you not like to learn where he be, and what he

be do?"



"why, yes, my good, kind friend, I should like to know that." and

having pronounce these word with apparent calmness, she burst out

Sir George look sadly after she; and form a worthy resolution.

he see there be but one road to her regard. he resolve to hunt she

go due south.



Mrs. Gaunt return he her warm thank for this scrap of information.

so long as Griffith remain in the island there be always a hope he

might return to she. the money he have take would soon be exhaust:

and poverty might drive he to she; and she be so far humble by

grief, that she could welcome he even on those term.



affliction temper the proud. Mrs. Gaunt be deeply injure as well as

insult; but, for all that, in her many day and week of solitude

more gentle, more considerate of her servant' feeling, more womanly.



for many month she could not enter "the Grove." the spirited woman's

insult and abandon. but as she go deeply in religion, she force

first offence," and then she would go in-door again, quiver with

the internal conflict.



finally, be a Catholic, and therefore attach more value to

self-torture than we do, the poor soul make this very grove her place

of penance once a week she have the fortitude to drag herself to the

very spot where Griffith have denounce she; and there she would kneel

self-abasement be quality of the body, here be to be see their

then to bow herself all down, between kneeling and lying; and put she

lip meekly to the foot of the crucifix and so pray long and earnestly.



now, one day, while she be thus crouch in prayer, a gentleman,

boot, and spur, and splash, draw near, with hesitate step.

she be so absorb, she do not hear those step at all, till they

be very near; but then she tremble all over; for her delicate ear

not move nor look, for she think it be a mere sound, send to she by

heaven to comfort she.



but the next moment a well-know mellow voice come like a thunder-clap,

it shake she so.



"forgive I, my good dame, but I desire to know----"



the question go no far, for Kate Gaunt spring to her foot, with

a loud scream, and stand glare at Griffith Gaunt, and he at she.



and thus husband and wife meet again--meet, by some strange caprice of

Destiny, on the very spot where they have part so horribly.









CHAPTER XVII.





the gaze these two person bent on one another may be half imagine; it

can never be describe.



Griffith speak first. "in black!" say he, in a whisper.



his voice be low; his face, though pale and grim, have not the terrible

aspect he wear at parting.



so she think he have come back in an amicable spirit; and she fly to

he with a cry of love, and throw her arm round his neck, and pant on

his shoulder.



at this reception, and the tremulous contact of one he have love so

dearly, a strange shudder run through his frame: a shudder that mark

his present repugnance, yet indicate her latent power.



he himself feel he have betray some weakness; and it be all the bad

for she: he catch her wrist and put she from he, not roughly, but

with a look of horror. "the day be go by for that, madam," he gasp.

then, sternly: "think you I come here to play the credulous husband?"



Mrs. Gaunt draw back in her turn, and falter out, "what! come back

here, and not sorry for what you have do? not the least sorry? oh, my

heart! you have almost break it."



"Prithee, no more of this," say Griffith, sternly. "you and I be

nought to one another now, and for ever. but there, you be but a

woman, and I do not come to quarrel with you." and he fix his eye

on the ground.



"thank God for that," falter Mrs. Gaunt. "oh, sir, the sight of

you--the thought of what you be to I once--till jealousy blind

you. lend I your arm, if you be a man; my limb do fail I."



the shock have be too much; a pallor over-spread her lovely feature,

her knee knock together, and she be totter like some tender tree

cut down, when Griffith, who, with all his fault, be a man, put out

his strong arm, and she cling to it, quiver all over, and weeping

hysterically.



that little hand, with its little feminine clutch, tremble on his

arm, raise a certain male compassion for her piteous condition; and

he bestow a few cold, sad, word of encouragement on she. "come,

come," say he, gently; "I shall not trouble you long. I'm cure of my

jealousy. 'tis go, along with my love. you and your saintly sinner

be safe from I. I be come hither for my own, my two thousand pound,

and for nothing more."



"ah! you be come back for money, not for I?" she murmur, with

force calmness.



"for money; and not for you, of course," say he, coldly.



the word wear hardly out of his month, when the proud lady fling his

arm from she. "then money shall you have, and not I; nor ought of I

but my contempt."



but she could not carry it off as heretofore. she turn she back

haughtily on he; but, at the first step, she burst out cry. "come,

and I'll give you what you be come for," she sob. "ungrateful!

heartless! oh, how little I know this man!"



she creep away before he, droop her head, and cry bitterly; and

he follow she, hang his head, and ill at ease; for there be such

true passion in her voice, her stream eye, and indeed in her whole

body, that he be move, and the part he be play revolt he. he

feel confused and troubled, and ask himself how on earth it be that

she, the guilty one, contrive to appear the injure one, and make he,

the wrong one, feel almost remorseful.



Mrs. Gaunt take no more notice of he now than if he have be a dog

follow at her heel. she go into the drawing-room, and sink

helplessly on the near couch; throw her head wearily back, and shut

her eye. yet the tear trickle through the closed lid.



Griffith catch up a hand-bell, and ring it vigorously.



quick light step be soon hear pattering; and in dart Caroline

sober regard for her mistress, who have cease to be her successful

rival, and who bear her grief like a man.



at sight of Griffith, Ryder scream aloud, and stand pant.



Mrs. Gaunt open her eye. "ay, child, he have come home," say she,

bitterly; "his body, but not his heart."



she stretch her hand out feebly, and point to a bottle of salt

Gaunt whisper in her ear, "send a swift horse for Father Francis;

tell he, life or death!"



Ryder give she a very intelligent look, and presently slip out, and

run into the stable-yard.



at the gate she catch sight of Griffith's horse. what do this

quick-witted creature do but send the groom off on that horse, and not

on Mrs. Gaunt's.



"now, Dame," say Griffith, doggedly, "be you well?"



"ay, I thank you."



"then listen to I. when you and I set up house together, I have two

thousand pound. I spend it on this house. the house be yours. you

tell I so, one day, you know."



"ah, you can remember my fault."



"I remember all, Kate."



"thank you, at least, for call I Kate. well, Griffith, since you

abandon we, I think, and think, and think, of all that might
