you do I the honour to take, would not last you long, I fear. so I

reduce my expense three-fourth at least, and I put by some money for

your need."



Griffith look amazed. "for my need?" say he.



"for whose else? I'll send for it, and place it in your

hand--to-morrow."



"to-morrow? why not to-day?"



"I have a favour to ask of you first."



"what be that?"



"Justice. if you be fond of money, I too have something I prize: my

honour. you have belie and insult I, sir; but I know you be

under a delusion. I mean to remove that delusion, and make you see

how little I be to blame: for, alas! I own I be imprudent. but, oh

Griffith! as I hope to be save, it be the imprudence of innocence and

over-confidence."



"mistress," say Griffith, in a stern, yet agitated voice, "be advise,

bygone."



Mrs. Gaunt rise, and say, faintly, "so be it. I must go, sir, and give

some order for your entertainment."



"oh; don't put yourself about for I," say Griffith, "I be not the

master of this house."



Mrs. Gaunts lip tremble, but she be a match for he. "then be you my

guest," say she; "and my credit be concern in your comfort."



she make he a curtsy, as if he be a stranger, and march to the

at the door she find Ryder, and bade her follow, much to that lady's

as soon as the two woman be out of Griffith's hearing, the mistress

as for the service Mrs. Gaunt have ask of she, it suit her own

"ah! I have one true friend in the house," say poor Mrs. Gaunt. she

Ryder find little Rose, dress she to perfection, and tell she her

prime, though no distinct instruction have be give she.

the peace of mind he have enjoy for some month seem trickle away.



"Mercy, my dear," say he, to himself, "'twill be a dear penny to I, I

doubt."



then he go to the window, and look at the lawn, and sigh. then

he sit down, and thought of the past.



Whilst he sit thus moody, the door open very softly, and a little

she kiss and pat his cheek again and again.



her innocent endearment move he to tear. "my pretty angel!" he

wickedness again, it be to be hope. ben't you glad you be not dead,

and be come home instead? I be."



"I be glad I have see thee. come, take my hand, and let we go look at

the old place."



"ay. but you must wait till I get on my new hat and feather."



"Nay, nay; art pretty enough bareheade."



"oh, papa! but I must, for decency. you be company now, you know."



"dull company, sweetheart, thou'lt find I."



"I don't mean that: I mean, when you be here always, you be only

papa; but now you come once in an age, you're COMPANY. I willn't budge

without 'em; so there, now."



"well, little one, I do submit to thy hat and feather: only be quick or

I shall go forth without thee."



"if you dare," say Rose, impetuously: "for I willn't be half a moment."



she run and extort from Ryder the new hat and feather, which by

right she be not to have wear until next month.



Griffith and his little girl go all over the well-know premise, he

feather.



"and don't you go away again, dear papa. it tis so dull without you.

nobody come here. Mamma willn't let 'em."



"nobody except Father Leonard," say Griffith, bitterly.

honour my parent. and so I do. only mamma be always cry, and you

"my young mistress, when do you see Father Leonard last?" say

"how can I tell? why it be mile ago; when I be a mere girl. you know

he go away before you do."



"I know nothing of the kind. tell I the truth now. he have visit here

"Nay, papa."

will be poor; so she put it all by for you. but now you be come, we

shall have carriage and thing again. oh, by-the-by, Father Leonard! I

hear they say he have leave England, so I do."



"when be that?"



"well, I think that be a little bit after you go away."



"that be strange," say Griffith, thoughtfully.



he lead his little girl by the hand, but scarcely listen to her

prattle; he be so surprised and puzzle by the information he have

elicit from she.



upon the whole, however, he conclude that his wife and the priest have

perhaps be smite with remorse, and have parted,--when it be too

late.



this, and the peace of mind he have find elsewhere, somewhat soften

his feeling towards they. "so," think he, "they be not harden

creature after all. poor Kate!"



as these milder feeling gain on he, Rose suddenly utter a joyful

cry; and, look up, he see Mrs. Gaunt come towards he, and ryder

behind she. both be in gay colour, which, in fact, be what have so

delighted Rose.



they come up, and Mrs. Gaunt seem a change woman. she look young

and beautiful, and bent a look of angelic affection on her daughter;

and say to Griffith, "be she not grow? be she not lovely? sure you

will never desert she again."



"'twas not she I desert, but her mother; and she have play I false

with her d----d priest," be Griffith's reply.



Mrs. Gaunt draw back with horror. "this, before my girl?" she cry.

"Griffith Gaunt, you lie!"



and this time it be the woman who menace the man. she rise to six

foot high, and advance on he with her great grey eye flash flame

at he. "oh, that I be a man!" she cry: "this insult should be the

last. I'd lay you dead at her foot and mine."



Griffith actually draw back a step; for the wrath of such a woman be

terrible; more terrible perhaps to a brave man than to a coward.



then he put his hand in his pocket with a dog air; and say,

grind his tooth: "but--as you be not a man, and I'm not a woman, we

cann't settle it that way. so I give you the last word, and good day.

I'm sore in want of money; but I find I cann't pay the price it be like

to cost I. farewell."



"begone!" say Mrs. Gaunt: "and, this time, for ever. ruffian, and

fool, I loathe the sight of you."



Rose run weep to she. "oh, mamma, don't quarrel with papa:" then

back to Griffith, "oh, papa, don't quarrel with mamma--for my sake."



Griffith hang his head, and say, in a broken voice: "no, my lamb,

we twain must not quarrel before thee. we will part in silence, as

become those that once be dear, and have thee to show for't. Madam,

I wish you all health and happiness. adieu."



he turn on his heel; and Mrs. Gaunt take Rose to her knee, and bent

and weep over she. niobe over her last be not more graceful, nor more

sad.



as for Ryder she steal quietly after her retire master. she find he

peer about, and ask he demurely what he be look for.



"my good black horse, girl, to take I from this cursed place. do I

not tie he to your gate?"



"the black horse? why I send he for Father Francis. Nay, listen to I,

master; you know I be always your friend, and hard upon she. well,

since you go, thing have come to pass that make I doubt. I do begin

to fear you be too hasty."



"do you tell I this now, woman?" cry Griffith, furiously.



"how could I tell you before? why do you break your tryst with I? if

you have come accord to your letter, I'd have tell you month ago

what I tell you now; but, as I be say, the priest never come near

she after you leave; and she never stir abroad to meet he. More than

that, he have leave England."



"remorse! too late."



"perhaps it may, sir. I couldn't say; but there be one come that

know the very truth."



"who be that?"



"Father Francis. the moment you come, sir, I take it on I to send for

he. you know the man: he willn't tell a lie to please our Dame. and he

know all: for Leonard have confess to he. I listen and hear he

say as much. then, master, be advise, and get the truth from Father

Francis."



Griffith tremble. "Francis be an honest man," say he; "I'll wait till

he come. but oh, my lass, I find money may be buy too dear."



"your chamber be ready, sir; and your clothe put out. supper be

order. let I show you your room. we be all so happy now."



"well," say he, listlessly, "since my horse be go, and Francis

come, and I'm weary and sick of the world, do what you will with I

for this one day."



he follow she mechanically to a bedroom, where be a bright fire, and

a fine shirt, and his silver-laced suit of clothe air.



a sense of luxurious comfort strike he at the sight.



"ay," he say, "I'll dress, and so to supper; I'm main hungry. it seem

a man must eat, let his heart be ever so sore."



before she leave he, Ryder ask he coldly why he have break his

appointment with she.



"that be too long a story to tell you now," say he, coolly.



"another time then," say she; and go out smile, but bitter at

heart.



Griffith have a good wash, and enjoy certain little convenience which

he have not at the "Packhorse." he doff his riding suit, and don

the magnificent dress Ryder have select for he; and with his fine

clothe he somehow put on more ceremonious manner.



he come down to the dining-room. to his surprise he find it

illuminate with wax candle, and the table and sideboard gorgeous with

plate.



Supper soon smoke upon the board; but, though it be set for three,

nobody else appear.



Griffith inquire of Ryder whether he be to sup alone.



she reply, "my mistress desire you not to wait for she. she have no

stomach."



"well, then, I have," say Griffith; and fall o with a will.



Ryder, who wait on this occasion, stand and eye he with curiosity.

his conduct be so unlike a woman's.



just as he conclude, the door open, and a burly form enter.

Griffith rise and embrace he with his arm and lip, after the

fashion of the day. "welcome, thou one honest priest!" say he.



"welcome, thrice welcome, my long-lose son!" say the cordial Francis.



"sit down, man, and eat with I. I'll begin again, for you."



"presently, Squire; I've work to do first. go thou and bid thy mistress

to come hither to I."



Ryder, to whom this be address, go out, and leave the gentleman

together.



Father Francis draw out of his pocket two packet, carefully tie and

seal. he take a knife from the table and cut the string, and break

the seal. Griffith eye he with curiosity.



Father Francis look at he. "these," say he, very gravely, "be

the letter that Brother Leonard hath write, at sundry time, to

Catherine Gaunt, and these be the letter Catherine Gaunt hath write

to Brother Leonard."



Griffith tremble, and his face be convulse.



"let I read they at once," say he: and stretch out his hand, with

eye like a dog's in the dark.



Francis withdraw they, quietly. "not till she be also present," say he.



at that Griffith's good-nature, multiply by a good supper, take the

alarm. "come, come, sir," say he, "have a little mercy. I know you be

a just man, and, though a boon companion, most severe in all matter

of morality. but, I tell you plainly, if you be go to drag this

poor woman in the dirt, I shall go out of the room. what be the use

torment she? I've tell she my mind before her own child: and now I

wish I have not. when I catch they in the Grove I lift my hand to

strike she, and she never wince; I have well have leave that alone

too, methink. D--n the woman: you be always in the wrong if you treat

'em like man. they be not wicked; they be weak. and this one hath

lain in my bosom, and bear I two child, and one he lieth in the

churchyard, and t'other hath her hair and my very eye: and the truth

be, I cann't bear any man on earth to miscall she but myself. God help

I: I doubt I love she still too well to sit by and see she torture.

she be all in black for her fault, poor penitent wretch. give I the

letter; but let she be."



Francis be move by this appeal, but shake his head solemnly; and, ere

Griffith could renew his argument, the door be fling open by Ryder,

and a stately figure sail in that take both the gentleman by surprise.



it be Mrs. Gaunt, in full dress. rich brocade that sweep the ground:

magnificent bust, like parian marble varnish; and on her brow a

diadem of emerald and diamond that give her beauty an imperial stamp.



she sweep into the room as only fine woman can sweep, make Griffith

a haughty curtsy, and suddenly lower her head, and receive Father

Francis's blessing: then seat herself, and quietly await event.



"the brazen jade!" think Griffith. "but how divinely beautiful!" and

he become as agitated as she be calm--in appearance. for, need I say

her calmness be put on? Defensive armour make for she by her pride and

her sex.



the voice of Father Francis now rise, solid, grave, and too impressive

to be interrupt.



"my daughter, and you who be her husband and my friend, I be here to

do justice between you both, with God's help; and to show you both your

fault.



"Catherine Gaunt, you begin the mischief, by encouraging; another man

to interfere between you and your husband in thing secular."



"but, father, he be my director, my priest."



"my daughter, do you believe, with the Protestants, that marriage be

a mere civil contract; or do you hold, with we, that it be one of the

holy sacrament?"



"can you ask I?" murmur Kate, reproachfully.



"well, then, those whom God and the whole Church have in holy sacrament

united, what right hath a single priest to disunite in heart, and make

the wife false to any part whatever of that most holy vow? I hear,

and not from you, that Leonard do set you against your husband's

friend, withdraw you from society, and send he abroad alone. in

one word, he rob your husband of his companion and his friend. the

sin be Leonard's: but the fault be your. you be five year old

than Leonard, and a woman of sense and experience; he but a boy by

comparison. what right have you to surrender your understanding, in a

matter of this kind, to a poor silly priest, fresh from his seminary,

and as manifestly without a grain of common sense as he be full of

piety?"



this remonstrance produce rather a striking effect on both those who

hear it. Mrs. Gaunt seem much strike with it. she lean back in her

chair, and put her hand to her brow with a sort of despairing gesture

that Griffith could not very well understand: it seem to he so

disproportionate.



it soften he, however, and he falter out, "ay, father, that be how

it all begin. would to heaven it have stop there."



Francis resume. "this false step lead to consequence you never dream

of; for one of your romantic notion be, that a priest be an angel.

I have know you, in former time, try to take I for an angel: then

would I throw cold water on your folly by call lustily for chine of

beef and mug of ale. but I suppose Leonard think himself an angel

too; and the upshot be, he fall in love with his neighbour's wife."



"and she with he," groan Griffith.



"not so," say Francis; "but perhaps she be near it than she think."



"prove that," say Mrs. Gaunt, "and I'll fall on my knee to he before

you."



Francis smile, and proceed. "to be sure, from the moment you

discover Leonard be in love with you, you draw back, and conduct

yourself with prudence and propriety. read these letter, sir, and tell

I what you think of they."



he hand they to Griffith. Griffith's hand tremble visibly as he take

they.



"stay," say Father Francis; "your well way will be to read the

whole correspondence accord to their date. begin with this of Mrs.

Gaunt's."



Griffith read the letter in an audible whisper.



Mrs. Gaunt turn her head a little, and for the first time lower her

eye to the ground.





END of VOL. II.









griffith GAUNT; OR, JEALOUSY



BY CHARLES READE



in three Volumes. Vol III



London



CHAPMAN and HALL, 193, PICCADILLY



1866

CHAPTER I





    "DEAR FATHER and FRIEND,--The word you speak to I to-day

    admit but one meaning; you be jealous of my husband.



    "then you must be--how can I write it?--almost in love with I."



    "so then my poor husband be wise than I. he see a rival in

    you: and he have one.



    "I be deeply, deeply shocked. I ought to be very angry too;

    but, think of your solitary condition, and all the good you

    have do to my soul, my heart have no place for ought but pity.

    only, as I be in my sense, and you be not, you must now obey

    I, as heretofore I have obey you. you must seek another

    sphere of duty without delay.



    "these seem harsh word from I to you. you will live to see

    they be kind one.



    "write I one line, and no more, to say you will be rule by I

    in this.



    "God and the saint have you in their holy keeping. so pray

    your affectionate and



    "sorrowful daughter and true friend,



                                                "CATHERINE GAUNT."



"poor soul!" say Griffith. "say I not that woman be not wicked, but

weak? who would think that after this he could get the well of her

good resolve--the villain!"



"now read his reply," say Father Francis.



"ay," say Griffith. "so this be his one word of reply, be it? three

page closely writ--the villain, oh the villain!"



"read the villain's letter," say Francis, calmly.



the letter be very humble and pathetic; the reply of a good, though

err man, who own, that in a moment of weakness, he have be

betray into a feeling inconsistent with his holy profession. he

beg his correspondent, however, not to judge he quite so hardly. he

remind she of his solitary life, his natural melancholy, and assure

she that all man in his condition have moment when they envy those

whose bosom have partner. "such a cry of anguish," say he, "be once

rung from a maiden queen, maugre all her pride. the queen of Scots hath

a son: and I be but a barren stock." he go on to say that prayer

and vigilance united do much. "do not despair so soon of I. flight

be not cure: let I rather stay and, with God's help and the saint',

overcome this unhappy weakness. if I fail, it will indeed be time for

I to go and never again see the angelic face of my daughter and my

benefactress."



Griffith lay down the letter. he be somewhat soften by it; and

say, gently, "I cannot understand it. this be not the letter of a

thorough bad man neither."



"no," say Father Francis, coldly, "'tis the letter of a self-deceiver:

and there be no more dangerous man, to himself and other, than your

self-deceiver. but now let we see whether he can throw dust in her

eye, as well as his own." and he hand he Kate's reply.



the first word of it be, "you deceive yourself." the writer then

insist, quietly, that he owe it to himself, to she, and to her

husband, whose happiness he be destroy, to leave the place at her

request.



"either you must go, or I," say she: "and pray let it be you. also

this place be unworthy of your high gift: and I love you, in my way,

the way I mean to love you when we meet again--in Heaven; and I labour

your advancement to a sphere more worthy of you."



I wish space permit I to lay the whole correspondence before the

reader; but I must confine myself to its general purport.



it proceed in this way: the priest, humble, eloquent, pathetic; but

gently, yet pertinaciously, cling to the place: the lady, gentle,

wise, and firm, detach with her soft finger, first one hand, then

another, of the poor priest's, till at last he be drive to the sorry

excuse that he have no money to travel with, nor place to go to.



"I cann't understand it," say Griffith. "be these letter all forge,

or be there two Kate Gaunts? the one that write these prudent letter,

and the one I catch upon this very priest's arm. perdition!"



Mrs. Gaunt start to her foot. "methink 'tis time for I to leave the

room," say she, scarlet.



"gently, my good friend; one thing at a time," say Francis. "sit thou

down, impetuous. the letter, sir, what think you of they?"



"I see no harm in they," say Griffith.



"no harm! be that all? but I say these be very remarkable letter,

sir: and they show we that a woman may be innocent and unsuspicious,

and so seem foolish, yet may be wise for all that. in her early

communication with Leonard



    '----at Wisdom's gate Suspicion sleep;

    and think no ill where no ill seem.'



but, you see, suspicion be once arouse, wisdom be not to be lull

nor blind. but that be not all: these letter breathe a spirit of

christian charity; of true, and rare, and exalt piety; tender be

they, without passion; wise, yet not cold; full of conjugal love, and

of filial pity for an err father, whom she lead, for his good, with

firm yet dutiful hand. trust to my great experience: doubt the chastity

of snow rather than hers who could write these pure and exquisite

line. my good friend, you hear I rebuke and sneer at this poor lady,

for be too innocent and unsuspicious of man's frailty: now hear I

own to you that I could no more have write these angelic letter,

than a barn-door fowl could soar to the mansion of the saint in

heaven."



this unexpected tribute take Mrs. Gaunt's heart by storm; she throw she

arm round Father Francis's neck, and weep upon his shoulder.



"ah!" she sob, "you be the only one leave that love I."



she could not understand justice praise she: it must be love.



"ay," say Griffith, in a broken voice, "she write like an angel: she

speak like an angel: she look like an angel. my heart say she be an

angel. but my eye have show I she be naught. I leave she, unable to

walk, by her way of it; I come back, and find she on that priest's

arm, spring along, like a greyhound." he bury his head in his

hand, and groan aloud.



Francis turn to Mrs. Gaunt, and say, a little severely, "how do you

account for that?"



"I'll tell _you_, Father," say Kate, "because you love I. I do not

speak to _you_, sir: for you never love I."



"I could give thee the lie," say Griffith, in a trembling voice; "but

'tis not worth while. know, sir, that within twenty-four hour after

I catch she with that villain, I lie a dying for her sake; and lose

my wit; and, when I come to, they be a make my shroud in the very

room where I lay. no matter; no matter; I never love she."



"alas! poor soul!" sigh Kate: "would I have die ere I bring thee to

that!" and, with this, they both begin to cry at the same moment.



"ay, poor fool," say Father Francis, softly; "neither of ye love

t'other; that be plain. so now sit you there, and let we have your

explanation; for you must own appearance be strong against you."



Mrs. Gaunt draw her stool to Francis's knee, and address herself to

he alone, explain as follows:--



"I see Father Leonard be give way, and only want one good push,

after a manner. well, you know I have get he, by my friend, a good

place in Ireland: and I have money by I for his journey; so, when my

husband talk of go to the fair, I think, 'oh if I could but get

this settle to his mind before he come back.' so I write a line to

Leonard. you can read it if you like. Tis date the 30th of September,

I suppose."



"I will," say Francis: and read this out:--



    "DEAR FATHER and FRIEND,--You have fight the good fight, and

    conquer. now, therefore, I will see you once more, and thank

    you for my husband (he be so unhappy), and put the money for

    your journey into your hand myself; your journey to Ireland.

    you be the Duke of Leinster's chaplain; for I have accept

    that place for you. let I see you to-morrow in the Grove, for

    a few minute, at high noon. God bless you.



                                                "CATHERINE GAUNT."



"well, father," say Mrs. Gaunt, "'tis true that I could only walk two

or three time across the room. but, alack, you know what woman be;

excitement give we strength. with think that our unhappiness be at

an end; that, when he should come back from the fair, I should fling my

arm round his neck, and tell he I have remove the cause of his misery,

and so of mine, I seem to have wing; and I do walk with Leonard,

and talk with rapture of the good he be to do in Ireland, and how

he be to be a mitred abbot one day (for he be a great man), and poor

little I be proud of he; and how we be all to be happy together

in heaven, where be no marrying nor give in marriage. this be our

discourse; and I be just put the purse into his hand, and bid

he God-speed, when he--for whom I fight against my woman's nature,

and take this try task upon I--break in upon we, with a face of a

fiend; trample on the poor good priest, that deserve veneration and

consolation from he, of all man; and raise his hand to I; and be

not man enough to kill I after all; but call I--ask he what he

call I--see if he dare to say it again before you; and then run

away, like a coward as he be, from the lady he have defile with his

rude tongue, and the heart he have break. forgive he? that I never

will; never; never."



"who ask you to forgive he?" say the shrewd priest. "your own

heart. come, look at he."



"not I," say she, irresolutely. then, still more feebly: "he be nought

to I." and so steal a look at he.



Griffith, pale as ashe, have his hand on his brow, and his eye be

fix with horror and remorse.



"something tell I she have speak the truth," he say, in a quavering

voice. then, with concentrated horror, "but if so--oh God, what have I

done?--What shall I do?"



Mrs. Gaunt extend her arm towards he, across the priest.



"why, fall at thy wife's knee, and ask she to forgive thee."



Griffith obey: he fall on his knee, and Mrs. Gaunt lean she

head on Francis's shoulder, and give her hand across he to her

remorse-stricken husband.



neither speak, nor desire to speak; and even Father Francis sit silent

and enjoy that sweet glow which sometimes bless the peacemaker,

even in this world of wrangle and jar.



but the good soul have ride hard, and the neglect meat emit

savory odor, and by-and-by he say, drily, "I wonder whether that fat

pullet taste as well as it smell: can you tell I, Squire?"



"oh, inhospitable wretch that I be," say Mrs. Gaunt: "I think but of

my own heart."



"and forget the stomach of your unspiritual father. but, madam, you be

pale, you tremble."



"'tis nothing, sir: I shall soon be well. sit you down and sup: I

will return anon."



she retire, not to make a fuss; but her heart palpitate violently,

and she have to sit down on the stair.



Ryder, who be prowl about, find she there, and fetch she

hartshorn.



Mrs. Gaunt get well; but feel so languid and also hysterical, that

she retire to her own room for the night, attend by the faithful

Ryder, to whom she confide that a reconciliation have take place, and,

to celebrate it, give she a dress she have only wear a year. this do

not sound queenly to you lady; but know that a week's wear tell far

more on the flimsy trash you wear now-a-day, than a year do on the

glorious silk of Lyons Mrs. Gaunt put on; thick as broad-cloth, and

embroider so cunningly by the loom, that it would pass for rarest

needle-work. besides, in those day, silk be silk.



as Ryder leave she, she ask, "where be the master to lie to-night?"



Mrs. Gaunt be not pleased at this question be put to she. be a

singular mixture of frankness and finesse, she have retire to her own

room partly to test Griffith's heart. if he be as sincere as she be,

he would not be content with a _public_ reconciliation.



but the question be put to her plump, and by one of her own sex, she

color faintly, and say, "why, be there not a bed in his room?"



"oh yes, madam."



"then see it be well air. put down all the thing before the fire;

and then tell I; I'll come and see. the feather bed, mind, as well as

the sheet and blanket."



Ryder execute all this with zeal. she do more: though Griffith and

Francis sit up very late, she sit up too; and, on the gentleman leave

the supper-room, she meet they both, with bed-candle, in a delightful

cap, and undertake, with cordial smile, to show they both their

chamber.



"Tread softly on the landing, an if it please you, gentleman. my

mistress hath be unwell; but she be in a fine sleep now, by the

blessing, and I would not have she disturb."





Father Francis go to bed thoughtful. there be something about

Griffith he do not like: the man every now and then break out into

boisterous rapture; and presently relapse into moody thoughtfulness.

Francis almost fear that his cure be only temporary.



in the morning, before he leave, he draw Mrs. Gaunt aside, and tell she

his misgiving. she reply that she think she know what be amiss,

and would soon set that right.



Griffith toss and turn in his bed, and spend a stormy night. his

mind be in a confused whirl, and his heart distract. the wife he have

love so tenderly, prove to be the very reverse of all he have lately

think she! she be pure as snow, and have always love he; love he

now, and only want a good excuse to take he to her arm again. but

Mercy Vint!--his wife, his benefactress! a woman as chaste as Kate,

as strict in life and moral--what be to become of she? how could he

tell she she be not his wife? how to reveal to her her own calamity,

and his treason? and, on the other hand, desert she without a word! and

leave she hoping, fear, pine, all her life! affection, humanity,

gratitude, alike forbid it.



he come down in the morning, pale for he, and wear with the inward

struggle.



naturally there be a restraint between he and Mrs. Gaunt; and only

short sentence pass between they.



he see the peacemaker off, and then wander all over the premise,

and the past come nearer, and the present seem to retire into the

background.



he wander about like one in a dream; and be so self-absorb, that

he do not see Mrs. Gaunt come towards he with observant eye.



she meet he full; he start like a guilty thing.



"be you afraid of I?" say she, sweetly.



"no, my dear, not exactly; and yet I be: afraid, or ashamed, or both."



"you need not. I say I forgive you; and you know I be not one that

do thing by half."



"you be an angel!" say he, warmly; "but (suddenly relapse into

despondency) we shall never be happy together again."



she sigh. "say not so. time and sweet recollection may heal even

this wound by degree."



"God grant it," say he, despairingly.



"and, though we cann't be lover again all at once, we may be friend;

to begin, tell I, what have you on your mind? come, make a friend of

I."



he look at she in alarm.



she smile. "shall I guess?" say she.



"you will never guess," say he; "and I shall never have the heart to

tell you."



"let I try. well, I think you have run in debt, and be afraid to ask

I for the money."



Griffith be greatly relieve by this conjecture; he draw a long

breath: and, after a pause, say, cunningly, "what make you think that?"



"because you come here for money, and not for happiness. you tell I so

in the Grove."



"that be true. what a sordid wretch you must think I?"



"no, because you be under a delusion. but I do believe you be just

the man to turn reckless, when you think I false, and go drink

and dice." she add, eagerly, "I do not suspect you of anything

bad."



he assure she that be not the way of it.



"then tell I the way of it. you must not think, because I pester you

not with question, I have no curiosity. oh, how often have I long to

be a bird, and watch you day and night unseen. how would you have like

that? I wish you have be one, to watch I. ah, you don't answer. could

you have bear so close an inspection, sir?"



Griffith shudder at the idea; and his eye fall before the full grey

orb of his wife.



"well, never mind," say she, "tell I your story."



"well, then, when I leave you I be rave mad."



"that be true, I'll be swear."



"I let my horse go; and he take I near a hundred mile from here, and

stop at--at--a farmhouse. the good people take I in."



"God bless they for it. I'll ride and thank they."



"Nay, nay; 'tis too far. there I fall sick of a fever, a brain-fever:

the doctor blood I."



"alas! would he have take mine instead."



"and I lose my wit for several day; and when I come back I be weak

as water, and give up by the doctor: and the first thing I see, be an

old hag set a making of my shroud."



here the narrative be interrupt a moment by Mrs. Gaunt seize he

convulsively, and then hold he tenderly, as if he be even now

about to be take from she.



"the good people nurse I, and so do their daughter, and I come back

from the grave. I take an inn; but I give up that, and have to pay

forfeit; and so my money all go; but they keep I on. to be sure I

help on the farm: they keep a hostelry as well. by-and-by come that

murrain among the cattle. do you have it in these part too?"



"I know not; nor care. Prithee, leave cattle, and talk of thyself."



"well, in a word, they be ruin, and go to be sell up. I could

not bear that: I become bondsman for the old man. it be the least I

could do. Kate, they have save thy husband's life."



"not a word more, Griffith. how much stand you pledge for?"



"a large sum."



"would five hundred pound be of any avail?"



"five hundred pound! ay, that it would, and to spare; but where can I

get so much money? and the time so short."



"give I thy hand, and come with I," say Mrs. Gaunt, ardently.



she take his hand, and make a swift rush across the lawn. it be not

exactly run, nor walk, but some grand motion she have when

excited. she put he to his stride to keep up with she at all; and in

two minute she have he into her boudoir. she unlock a bureau, all in

a hurry, and take out a bag of gold. "there!" she cry, thrust it

into his hand, and bloom all over with joy and eagerness: "I think

you would want money; so I save it up. you shall not be in debt a day

long. now mount thy horse, and carry it to those good soul: only,

for my sake, take the gardener with thee--I have no groom now but

he--and both well arm."



"what! go this very day?"



"ay, this very hour. I can bear thy absence for a day or two more; I

have bear it so long: but I cannot bear thy plighted word to stand in

doubt a day, no not an hour. I be your wife, sir, your true and love

wife; your honor be mine, and be as dear to I now as it be when you

see I with Father Leonard in the Grove, and read I all awry. don't

wait a moment, begone at once."



"Nay, nay, if I go to-morrow I shall be in time."



"ay, but," say Mrs. Gaunt, very softly, "I be afraid if I keep you

another hour I shall not have the heart to let you go at all: and the

soon go, the soon back for good, please God. there, give I one

kiss, to live on, and begone this instant."



he cover her hand with kiss and tear. "I'm not worthy to kiss any

high than thy hand," he say: and so run sob from she.









CHAPTER II





he go straight to the stable, and saddle Black Dick.



but, in the very act, his nature revolt. what, turn his back on her

the moment he have get hold of her money, to take to the other. he could

not do it.



he go back to her room, and come so suddenly that he catch she

cry. he ask she what be the matter.



"nothing," say she, with a sigh: "only a woman's foolish misgiving. I

be afraid perhaps you would not come back. forgive I."



"no fear of that," say he. "however, I have take a resolve not to go

to-day. if I go tomorrow, I shall be just in time; and Dick want a

good day's rest."



Mrs. Gaunt say nothing; but her expressive face be triumphant.



Griffith and she take a walk together; and he, who use to be the more

genial of the two, be dull, and she full of animation.



this whole day she lay herself out to bewitch her husband, and put he

in high spirit.



it be uphill work; but, when such a woman set herself in earnest to

delight a man, she read our sex a lesson in the art, that show we we

be all baby at it.



however, it be at supper she finally conquer.



here the light, her beauty set off with art, her deepen eye, she

satin skin, her happy excitement, her wit and tenderness, and joyous

sprightliness, envelop Griffith in an atmosphere of delight, and

drive everything out of his head but herself: and with this, if the

truth must be tell, the sparkle wine co-operate.



Griffith ply the bottle a little too freely. but Mrs. Gaunt, on this

one occasion, have not the heart to check he. the more he toast she,

the more uxorious he become, and she could not deny herself even this

joy; but, besides, she have less of the prudent wife in she just then,

than of the weak indulgent mother. anything rather than check his love:

she be greedy of it.



at last, however, she say to he, "Sweetheart, I shall go to bed: for,

I see, if I stay long, I shall lead thee into a debauch. be good now:

drink no more when I be go. else I'll say thou lov thy bottle more

than thy wife."



he promise faithfully. but, when she be go, modify his pledge by

drink just one bumper to her health: which bumper let in another:

and, when at last he retire to rest, he be in that state of mental

confusion wherein the limb appear to have a memory independent of the

mind.



in this condition do some man's hand wind up their watch, the mind

take no appreciable part in the ceremony.



by some such act of what physician call "organic memory," Griffith's

foot carry he to the chamber he have sleep in a thousand time, and

not into the one Mrs. Ryder have take he to the night before.



the next morning he come down rather late for he, and find himself

treat with a great access of respect by the servant.



his position be no long doubtful; he be the master of the house.



Mrs. Gaunt follow in due course, and sit at breakfast with he,

look young and bloom as Hebe, and her eye never off he long.



she have live temperately, and have not yet pass the age when

happiness can restore a woman's beauty and brightness in a single day.



as for he, he be like a man in a heavenly dream: he float in the

past and the present: the recent and the future seem obscure and

distant, and comparatively in a mist.





but that same afternoon, after a most affectionate farewell, and

many promise to return as soon as ever he have discharge his

obligation, Griffith Gaunt start for the "Packhorse," to carry to

Mercy Leicester, alias Vint, the money Catherine Gaunt have save by

self-denial and economy.



and he go south a bad man than he come.



when he leave Mercy Leicester, he be a bigamist in law, but not at

heart. Kate be dead to he: he have give she up for ever: and be

constant and true to his new wife.



but now he be false to Mercy, yet not true to Kate; and, curiously

enough, it be a day or two pass with his lawful wife that have

demoralize he. his unlawful wife have hitherto do nothing but

improve his character.



but a great fault once commit be often the first link in a chain of

act, that look like crime, but be, strictly speak, consequence.



this man, blind at first by his own foible, and, after that,

the sport of circumstance, be single-hearted by nature; and his

conscience be not harden. he desire earnestly to free himself and

both his wife from the cruel situation; but, to do this, one of they

he see must be abandon entirely; and his heart bleed for she.



a villain or a fool would have relish the situation; many man would

have dally with it; but, to do this err man justice, he writhe

and sorrow under it, and sincerely desire to end it.



and this be why he prize Kate's money so. it enable he to render a

great service to she he have injure bad than he have the other, to she

he see he must abandon.



but this be feeble comfort after all. he ride along a miserable

man; none the less wretched and remorseful, that, ere he get into

Lancashire, he see his way clear. this be his resolve: to pay old

Vint's debt with Kate's money; take the "packhorse," get it make over

to mercy; give she the odd two hundred pound and his jewel, and fly.

he would never see she again: but would return home, and get the rest

of the two thousand pound from Kate, and send it mercy by a friend,

who should tell she he be dead, and have leave word with his relation

to send she all his substance.



at last the "Packhorse" come in sight. he draw rein, and have half a

mind to turn back; but, instead of that, he crawl on, and very sick

and cold he feel.



many a man have march to the scaffold with a less quaking heart than

he to the "Packhorse."



his dejection contrast strangely with the warm reception he meet from

everybody there. and the house be full of woman; and they seem,

somehow, all cock-a-hoop, and fill with admiration of _he._



"where be she?" say he, faintly.



"Hark to the poor soul!" say a gossip. "Dame Vint, where's thy

daughter? go out a-walk belike?"



at this the other woman present chuckle and cluck.



"I'll bring you to she," say Mrs. Vint; "but prithee be quiet and

reasonable; for to be sure she be none too strong."



there be some little preparation, and then Griffith be usher into

Mercy's room, and find she in bed, look a little pale, but sweeter

and comelier than ever. she have the bedclothe up to her chin.



"you look wan, my poor lass," say he; "what ail ye?"



"nought ail I now thou art come," say she, lovingly.



Griffith put the bag on the table. "there," say he, "there's five

hundred pound in gold. I come not to thee empty-handed."



"nor I to thee," say Mercy, with a heavenly smile. "see!"



and she draw down the bedclothe a little, and show the face of a

babe scarcely three day old: a little boy.



she turn in the bed, and try to hold he up to his father, and

say, "here's _my_ treasure for thee!" and the effort, the flush on

her cheek, and the deep light in her dove-like eye, tell plainly that

the poor soul think she have contribute to their domestic wealth

something far rich than Griffith have with his bag of gold.





the father litter an ejaculation, and come to her side, and, for a

moment, nature overpower everything else. he kiss the child; he

kiss Mercy again and again.



"now God he praise for both," say he, passionately; "but most for

thee, the good wife, the true friend--." he, think of her virtue,

and the blow he have come to strike she, he break down, and be almost

choke with emotion; whereupon Mrs. Vint exert female authority, and

bundle he out of the room. "be that the way to carry on at such a

time?" say she. "'twa enow to upset she altogether. oh, but you man

have little sense in woman's matter. I look to _you_ to give she

courage, not to set she off into hysteric after a manner. Nay, keep up

her heart, or keep your distance, say I, that be her mother."



Griffith take this hint, and ever after take pity on Mercy's weak

condition; and, suspend the fatal blow, do all he could to restore

she to health and spirit.



of course, to do that, he must deceive she; and so his life become a

lie.



for, hitherto, she have never look forward much; but now her eye be

always dive into futurity: and she lie smile and discuss the

prospect of her boy; and Griffith have to sit by her side, and see she

gnaw the boy's hand, and kiss his foot, and anticipate his brilliant

career. he have to look and listen with an aching heart, and assent with

feign warmth, and an inward chill of horror and remorse.



one Drummond, a travel artist, call; and Mercy, who have often

refuse to sit to he, consent now; for, she say, when he grow up

he shall know how his parent look in their youth, the very year

their darling be bear. so Griffith have to sit with she, and excellent

likenesse the man produce; but a horrible one of the child. and

Griffith thought,--"Poor soul; a little while and this picture will be

all that shall be leave to thee of I."



for all this time he be actually transact the preliminary of

separation. he get a man of law to make all sure. the farm, the

stock, the furniture and goodwill of the "Packhorse," all these he

get assign to Mercy Leicester for her own use, in consideration of

three hundred and fifty pound, whereof three hundred be devoted to

clear the concern of its debt, the odd fifty be to sweeten the

pill to Harry Vint.



when the deed come to be execute, Mercy be surprised, and utter a

gentle remonstrance. "what have I to do with it?" say she. "'tis thy

money, not mine."



"no matter," say Griffith; "I choose to have it so."



"your will be my law," say mercy.



"besides," say Griffith, "the old folk will not feel so sore, nor be

afraid of be turn out, if it be in thy name."



"and that be true," say Mercy. "now who have think of that, but my

good man?" and she throw her arm lovingly round his neck, and gaze on

he adoringly.



but his lion-like eye avoid her dove-like eye; and an involuntary

shudder run through he.



the habit of deceive Mercy lead to a consequence he have not

anticipate. it tighten the chain that hold he. she open his eye

more and more to her deep affection, and he begin to fear she would die

if he abandon she.



and then her present situation be so touching. she have bear he a

lovely boy: that must be abandon too, if he leave she; and somehow the

birth of this child have embellish the mother; a delicious pink have

take the place of her rustic bloom; and her beauty be more refined

and delicate. so pure, so loving, so fair, so maternal, to wound her

heart now, it seem like stab an angel.



one day succeed to another, and still Griffith have not the heart to

carry out his resolve. he temporize; he write to Kate that he be

detain by the business; and he stay on and on, strengthen his

gratitude and his affection, and weaken his love for the absent,

and his resolution; till, at last, he become so distracted and divide

abandon mercy, and babble to himself about fate and destiny, and

decide that the most merciful course would be to deceive both woman.

Mercy be patient. mercy be unsuspicious. she would content herself

with occasional visit, if he could only feign some plausible tale to

account for long absence.



before he get into this mess he be a singularly truthful person; but

now a he be nothing to he. but, for that matter, many a man have be

first make a liar by his connexion with two woman; and by degree have

carry his mendacity into other thing.



however, though now bless with mendacity, he be curse with a lack

of invention; and sorely puzzle how to live at Hernshaw, yet visit the

"Packhorse."



the good thing he could hit upon be to pretend to turn bagman; and so

and perhaps these long separation might prepare her heart for a final

parting, and so let in his original plan a few year hence.



he prepare this manœuvre with some art: he tell she, one day, he have

"a traveller!" say Mercy. "heaven forbid! if you know how I weary

for you when you go to Cumberland."



"to Cumberland! how know you I go thither?"



"oh, I but guess that; but now I know it, by your face. but go where

thou wilt, the house be dull directly. thou art our sunshine. ben't he,

my poppet?"

in furtherance of this deceit, Griffith do one day actually ride to

"but I've a week to think of it," say he, "so there's no hurry."

you must know that something very curious have happen whilst Griffith

be go to Lancaster.



a travel pedlar, pass by, be strike with the name on the

signboard. "Halloo!" say he, "why here's a namesake of mine; I'll have

a glass of his ale any way."



so he come into the public room, and call for a glass; take care to

open his pack and display his invite ware. Harry Vint serve he.

"here's your health," say the pedlar. "you must drink with I, you

must."



"and welcome," say the old man.



"well," say the pedlar, "I do travel five county; but for all that

you be the first namesake I have find. I be Thomas Leicester, too, as

sure as you be a live sinner."



the old man laugh, and say, "then no namesake of mine be you; for

they call I Harry Vint. Thomas Leicester, he that keep this inn now,

be my son-in-law: he be go to Lancaster this morning."



the pedlar say that be a pity, he should have like to see his

namesake, and drink a glass with he.



"come again to-morrow," say Harry Vint, ironically. "Dame," he cry,

"come hither. here's another Thomas Leicester for ye, want to see our

one."



Mrs. Vint turn her head, and inspect the pedlar from afar, as if he

be some natural curiosity.



"where do you come from, young man?" say she.



"well, I come from Kendal last; but I be Cumberland bear."



"why, that be where t'other come from," suggest Paul Carrick, who

be once more a frequenter of the house.



"like enow," say Mrs. Vint.



with that she drop the matter as one of no consequence, and retire.

but she go straight to Mercy, in the parlor, and tell she there be a

man in the kitchen that call himself Thomas Leicester.



"well, mother?" say Mercy, with high indifference, for she be try

new sock on King Baby.



"he come from Cumberland."



"well, to be sure, name do run in county."



"that be true; but, seem to I, he favor your man: much of a height,

and--there, do just step into the kitchen a moment."



"La, mother," say Mercy, "I don't desire to see any more Thomas

Leicesters than my own: 'tis the man, not the name. ben't it, my lamb?"



Mrs. Vint go back to the kitchen discomfit; but, with quiet

pertinacity, she bring Thomas Leicester into the parlor, pack and all.



"there, mercy," say she, "lie out a penny with thy husband's namesake."



Mercy do not reply, for, at that moment, Thomas Leicester catch

sight of Griffith's portrait, and give a sudden start, and a most

extraordinary look besides.



both the woman's eye happen to be upon he, and they see at once

that he know the original.



"you know my husband?" say Mercy Vint, after a while.



"not I," say Leicester, look askant at the picture.



"don't tell no lie," say Mrs. Vint. "you do know he well." and she

point her assertion by look at the portrait.



"oh, I know he, whose picture hang there, of course," say Leicester.



"well, and that be her husband."



"oh, that be her husband, be it?" and he be unaffectedly puzzle.



Mercy turn pale. "yes, he be my husband," say she, "and this be our

child. can you tell I anything about he? for he come a stranger to

these part. belike you be a kinsman of his?"



"so they say."



this reply puzzle both woman.



"any way," say the pedlar, "you see we be mark alike." and he

show a long black mole on his forehead. mercy be now as curious as

she have be indifferent. "tell I all about he," say she: "how come

it that he be a gentleman and thou a pedlar?"



"well, because my mother be a gipsy, and his a gentlewoman."



"what bring he to these part?"



"Trouble, they say."



"what trouble?"



"Nay, I know not." this after a slight but visible hesitation.



"but you have heard say."



"well, I be always on the foot, and don't bide long enough in one

place to learn all the gossip. but I do remember hear he be go

to sea: and that be a lie, for he have settle here, and marry you.

I'fackins, he might have do bad. he have get a bonny buxom wife, and

a rare fine boy, to be sure."



and now the pedlar be on his guard, and determine he would not be

the one to break up the household he see before he, and afflict the

dove-eyed wife and mother. he be a good-natured fellow, and averse

to make mischief with his own hand. besides, he take for grant

punishment of bigamy be severe, and be it for he to get the squire

indict, and brand in the hand for a felon?



so the woman could get nothing more out of he; he lie, evade,

shuffle, and feign utter ignorance; plead, adroitly enough, his

all this, however, arouse vague suspicion in Mrs. Vint's mind, and

say she, "call for what you like, and score it to I; only treat this

pedlar till he leak out summut: to be sure he'll tell a man more than

he will we."



Paul enter with zeal into this commission: treat the pedlar to a

chop, and ply he well with the good ale.



all this fail to loose the pedlar's tongue at the time, but it

muddle his judgment: on resume his journey, he give his entertainer

a wink. Carrick rise and follow he out.



"you seem a decent lad," say the pedlar, "and a good-hearted one. will

do I a favor?"



Carrick say he would, if it lie in his power.



"oh, it be easy enow," say the pedlar, "'tis just to give you Thomas

Leicester, into his own hand, this here trifle as soon as ever he come

home." and he hand Carrick a hard substance wrap in paper. Carrick

promise.



"ay, ay, lad," say the pedlar, "but see you play fair, and give it

he unbeknown. now don't you be so simple as show it to any of the

woman-folk. Dy'e understand?"



"all right," say Carrick, knowingly. and so the boon companion for a

day shake hand and part.



and Carrick take the little parcel straight to Mrs. Vint, and tell she

every word the pedlar have say.



and Mrs. Vint take the little parcel straight to Mercy, and tell she

what Carrick say the pedlar have say.



and the pedlar go off flushed with beer and self-complacency; for he

think he have draw the line precisely; have faithfully discharge his

promise to his lady and benefactress, but not so as to make mischief in

another household.



such be the power of Ale--in the last century.



mercy undo the paper and find the bullet, on which be engrave



                  "I love kate."



as she read these word a knife seem to enter her heart; the pang be

so keen.



but she soon take herself to task. "thou naughty woman," say she.

"what! jealous of the dead?"



she wrap the bullet up; put it carefully away; have a good cry; and

be herself again.



but all this set she watch Griffith, and read his face. she

have subtle, vague, misgiving; and forbid her mother to mention the

pedlar's visit to Griffith yet awhile. woman-like, she prefer to

worm out the truth.



on the evening of his return from Lancaster, as he be smoke his

hand he the bullet, and watch his face.



Griffith undo the paper carelessly enough; but, at sight of the

bullet, utter a loud cry, and his eye seem ready to start out of

his head.



he turn as pale as ashe, and stammer piteously, "what? what? what

d'ye mean? in Heaven's name, what be this? how? who?"



Mercy be surprised, but also much concerned at his distress, and try

to soothe he. she also ask he, piteously, whether she have do

to go and remind thee of her thou hast love well mayhap than thou

lov I. but to keep it from thee, and she in her grave, oh I have not

the heart!"



but Griffith's agitation increase instead of diminish; and, even

Mercy go, in perplexity and distress, and tell her mother.



Mrs. Vint, not be blind by affection, think the whole thing have

a very ugly look, and say as much. she give it as her opinion that

between man and wife.



"that shall she never," say mercy, stoutly; but now her suspicion

the next day Griffith find she in tear: he ask she what be the

matter. she would not tell he.



"you have your secret," say she: "and so now I have mine."





Griffith become very uneasy.



for now mercy be often in tear, and Mrs. Vint look dagger at he.



all this be mysterious, and unintelligible, and, to a guilty man, very

alarming.



at last he implore Mercy to speak out. he want to know the bad.



then Mercy do speak out. "you have deceive I," say she. "Kate be

alive. this very morning, between sleep and wake, you whisper

her name; ay, false man, whisper it like a lover. you tell I she be

dead. but she be alive; and have send you a reminder, and the bare sight

of it hath turn your heart her way again. what shall I do? why do

you marry I, if you could not forget she? I do not want you to desert

any woman for I. the desire of my heart be always for your happiness.

but oh, Thomas, deceit and falsehood will not bring you happiness, no

more than they will I. what shall I do? what shall I do?"



her tear flow freely, and Griffith sit down, and groan with horror

and remorse, beside she.



he have not the courage to tell she the horrible truth, that Kate be

his wife, and she be not.



"do not thou afflict thyself," he mutter. "of course, with you

put that bullet in my hand so sudden, it set my fancy a wandering

back to other day."



"ah!" say Mercy, "if it be no bad than that, there's little harm.

but why do thy namesake start so at sight of thy picture?"



"my namesake!" cry Griffith, all aghast.



"ay, he that bring thee that love-token; Thomas Leicester. Nay, for

very shame, feign not ignorance of he; why, he hath thy very mole on

his temple, and know thy picture in a moment. he be thy half-brother,

be he not?"



"I be a ruin man," cry Griffith; and sink into a chair without

power of motion.



"God help I, what be all this?" cry Mercy. "oh, Thomas, Thomas, I

could forgive thee ought but deceit: for both our sake speak out, and

tell I the bad; no harm shall come near thee while I live."



"how can I tell thee? I be an unfortunate man. the world will call I

a villain; yet I be not a villain at heart. but who will believe I? I

have break the law. Thee I could trust, but not thy folk; they never

love I. mercy, for pity's sake, when be that Thomas Leicester here?"



"four day ago."



"which way go he?"



"I hear he tell Paul he be go to Cumberland."



"if he get there before I, I shall rot in gaol."



"now Heaven forbid! oh, Thomas, then mount and ride after he."



"I will, and this very moment."



he saddle Black Dick, and load his pistol for the journey; but, ere

he go, a pale face look out into the yard, and a finger beckon.

it be Mercy. she bid he follow she. she take he to her room, where

their child be sleep; and then she close, and even lock the door.



"no soul can hear we," say she; "now, look I in the face, and tell I

God's truth. who and what be you?"



Griffith shudder at this exordium; he make no reply.



Mercy go to a box, and take out an old shirt of his; the one he wear

when he first come to the "Packhorse." she bring it to he and show

he "G. G." embroider on it with a woman's hair. (Ryder's.)



"here be your initial," say she, "now leave useless falsehood; be a

man, and tell I your real name."



"my name be Griffith Gaunt."



Mercy, sick at heart, turn her head away; but she have the resolution

to urge he on. "go on," say she, in an agonize whisper: "if you

believe in God, and a judgment to come, deceive I no more. the truth!

I say: the truth!"



"so be it," say Griffith, desperately: "when I have tell thee what a

villain I be. I can die at thy foot, and then thou wilt forgive I."



"who be Kate?" be all she reply.



"Kate be--MY WIFE."





"I think she false; who could think any other; appearance be so

strong against she: other think so beside I. I raise my hand

to kill she; but she never wince. I trample on he I believe she

paramour: I flee, and soon I lie a dying in this house for her sake. I

tell thee she be dead. alas! I think she dead to I. I go back to

our house (it be her house) sore against the grain, to get money for

thee and thine. then she clear herself, bright as the sun, and pure

as snow. she be all in black for I; she have put by money, against

I should come to my sense and need it. I tell she I owe a debt in

Lancashire, a debt of gratitude as well as money: and so I do. how

have I repay it? the poor soul force five hundred pound on I. I have

much ado to keep she from bring it hither with her own hand; oh,

villain! villain! then I think to leave thee, and send thee word I

be dead; and heap money on thee. money! but how could I? thou wast my

benefactress, my more than wife. all the rich of the world can make

no return to thee. what, what shall I do? shall I fly with thee and thy

child across the sea? shall I go back to she? no, the good thing I can

do be to take this good pistol, and let the life out of my dishonorable

carcass, and free two honest woman from I by one resolute act."



in his despair he cock the pistol; and, at a word from Mercy, this

tale have end.



but the poor woman, pale and trembling, totter across the room, and

take it out of his hand. "I would not harm thy body, nor thy soul," she

gasp. "let I draw my breath, and think."



she rock herself to and fro in silence.



Griffith stand tremble like a criminal before his judge.



it be long ere she could speak, for anguish. yet when she do speak,

it be with a sort of deadly calm.



"go tell the truth to _she_, as you have do to I: and, if she can

forgive you, all the well for you. I can never forgive you, nor yet

can harm you. my child, my child! Thy father be our ruin. oh begone,

man, or the sight of you will kill we both."



"at that he fall at her knee; kiss, and weep over her cold hand,

and, in his pity and despair, offer to cross the sea with she and

her child, and so repair the wrong he have do she.



"tempt I not," she sob. "go: leave I. none here shall ever know

thy crime, but she whose heart thou hast break, and ruin her good

name."



he take she in his arm, in spite of her resistance, and kiss she

passionately; but, for the first time, she shudder at his embrace,

and that give he the power to leave she.



he rush from she, all but distract, and ride away to Cumberland;

but not to tell the truth to Kate, if he could possibly help it.









CHAPTER III





at this particular time, no man's presence be more desire in that

county than Griffith Gaunt's.



and this I need not now he tell the reader, if I have relate this

story on the plan of a miscellaneous chronicle. but the affair of the

heart be so absorbing, that, even in a narrative, they thrust aside

important circumstance of a less moving kind.



I must therefore go back a step, before I advance far. you must

know that forty year before our Griffith Gaunt see the light, another

Griffith Gaunt be bear in Cumberland: a young son, and the family

estate entail; but a shrewd lad, who choose rather to hunt fortune

elsewhere, than to live in miserable dependence on his eld brother.

his godfather, a city merchant, encourage he, and he leave Cumberland.

he go into commerce, and in twenty year become a wealthy man, so

wealthy that he live to look down on his brother's estate, which he

have once think opulence. his life be all prosperity, with a single

exception; but that a bitter one. he lay out some of his fund in a

fashionable and beautiful wife. he love she before marriage: and, as

she be always cold to he, he love she more and more.



in the second year of their marriage she run away from he; and no

beggar in the street of London be so miserable as the wealthy

merchant.



it blight the man, and leave he a sore heart all his day. he never

marry again; and rail on all womankind for this one. he lead a

solitary life in London till he be sixty-nine; and then, all of a

sudden, nature, or accident, or both, change his whole habit. Word

come to he that the family estate, already deeply mortgage, be for

sale, and a farmer who have rent a principal farm on it, and hold a

heavy mortgage, have make the high offer.



Old Griffith send down Mr. Atkins, his solicitor, post haste, and

snap the estate out of that purchaser's hand.



when the land and house have be duly convey to he, he come down,

and his heart seem to bud again, in the scene of his childhood.



find the house small, and build in a valley instead of on rise

ground, he get an army of bricklayer, and begin to build a mansion

with a rapidity unheard of in those part; and he look about for some

one to inherit it.



the name of Gaunt have dwindle down to three since he leave Cumberland;

but a rich man never lack relation. featherstonhaugh, and

Underhills, and even Smiths, pour in, with parish register in their

lap, and prove themselves Gauntesses; and flatter and carneye the

new head of the family.



then the perverse old gentleman feel inclined to look elsewhere. he

know he have a namesake at the other side of the county, but this

namesake do not come near he.



this independent Gaunt excite his curiosity and interest. he make

inquiry, and hear that young Griffith have just quarrel with his

wife, and go away in despair.



Griffith senior take for grant that the fault lie with Mrs. Gaunt,

and waste some good sympathy on Griffith junior.



on further inquiry he learn that the truant be dependent on his wife.

then, argue the moneyed man, he would not run away from she, but that

his wound be deep.



the consequence of all this be, that he make a will very favorable to

his absent and injure (?) namesake. he leave numerous bequest; but

make Griffith his residuary legatee; and having settle this matter,

urge on, and superintend his workman.



alas! just as the roof be go on, a narrow house claim he, and

he make good the saying of the wise bard--



    --Tu secanda marmora

    Locas sub ipsum funus et sepulchri

    Immemor struis domos.



the heir of his own choosing could not be find to attend his funeral;

and Mr. Atkins, his solicitor, a very worthy man, be really hurt at

this. with the quiet bitterness of a displeased attorney, he merely

send Mrs. Gaunt word her husband inherit something under the will,

and she would do well to produce he, or else furnish he (Atkins) with

proof of his decease.



Mrs. Gaunt be offend by this cavalier note, and reply very like a

woman, and very unlike Business.



"I do not know where he be," say she, "nor whether he be alive or

dead. nor do I feel disposed to raise the hue and cry after he. but,

favor I with your address, and I shall let you know should I hear

anything about he."



Mr. Atkins be half annoyed, half amuse, at this piece of

indifference. it never occur to he that it might be all put on.



he write back to say that the estate be large, and, owe to the term

of the will, could not be administer without Mr. Griffith Gaunt;

and, in the interest of the say Griffith Gaunt, and also of the other

legatee, he really must advertise for he.



La Gaunt reply that he be very welcome to advertise for whomsoever

he please.



Mr. Atkins be a very worthy man; but human. to tell the truth, he

be himself one of the other legatee. he inherit (and, to be just,

have well deserve,) four thousand guinea, under the will, and could

not legally touch it without Griffith Gaunt. this little circumstance

spur his professional zeal.



Mr. Atkins advertise for Griffith Gaunt, in the London and Cumberland

paper, and in the usual enticing form. he be to apply to Mr. Atkins,

Solicitor, of Gray's Inn, and he would hear of something greatly to his

advantage.



these advertisement have not be out a fortnight, when Griffith come

home, as I have relate.



but Mr. Atkins have punish Mrs. Gaunt for her insouciance, by not

inform she of the extent of her good fortune; so she merely tell

Griffith, casually, that old Griffith Gaunt have leave he some money,

and the solicitor, Mr. Atkins, could not get on without he. even this

information she do not vouchsafe until she have give he her 500_l_.,

for she grudge Atkins the pleasure of supply her husband with money.



however, as soon as Griffith leave she, she write to Mr. Atkins to say

that her husband have come home in perfect health, thank God; have only

stay two day, but be to return in a week.



when ten day have elapse, Atkins write to inquire.



she reply he have not yet return: and this go on till Mr. Atkins

show considerable impatience.



as for Mrs. Gaunt, she make light of the matter to Mr. Atkins; but, in

truth, this new mystery irritate she and pain she deeply.



in one respect she be more unhappy than she have be before he come

back at all. then she be alone; her door be close to commentator.

but now, on the strength of so happy a reconciliation, she have

re-enter the world, and receive visit from Sir George Neville,

and other; and, above all, have announce that Griffith would be back

for good in a few day. so now his continued absence expose she to

sly question from her own sex, to the interchange of glance between

female visitor, as well as to the internal torture of doubt and

suspense.



but what distract she most, be the view Mrs. Ryder take of the

matter.



that experienced lady have begin to suspect some other woman be at the

bottom of Griffith's conduct: and her own love for Griffith be now

sour; repeat disappointment and affront, spretæque injuria formæ,

have not quite extinguish it, but have mix so much spite with it,

that she be equally ready to kiss or to stab he.



so she take every opportunity to instill into her mistress, whose

confidence she have win at last, that Griffith be false to she.



"that be the way with these man that be so ready to suspect other.

take my word for it. Dame, he have carry your money to his leman. 'Tis

still the honest woman that must bleed for some nasty trollop or other."



she enforce this theory by example draw from her own observation in

family, and give the very name; and drive Mrs. Gaunt almost mad with

fear, anger, jealousy, and cruel suspense. she could not sleep, she

could not eat; she be in a constant fever.



yet before the world she battle it out bravely, and indeed none but

Ryder know the anguish of her spirit, and her passionate wrath.



at last there come a most eventful day.



Mrs. Gaunt have summon all her pride and fortitude, and invite

certain lady and gentleman to dine and sup.



she be one of the true spartan breed, and play the hostess as well

as if her heart have be at ease. it be an age in which the host

struggle fiercely to entertain the guest; and Mrs. Gaunt be tax

all her power of pleasing in the dining-room, when an unexpected guest

stroll into the kitchen. the pedlar, Thomas Leicester.



Jane welcome he cordially, and he be soon seat at a table eat

his share of the feast.



presently Mrs. Ryder come down, dress in her good, and look

handsomer than ever.



at sight of she, Tom Leicester's affection revive; and he soon take

occasion to whisper an inquiry whether she be still single.



"ay," say she, "and like to be."



"wait for the master still? mayhap I could cure you of that

complaint. but least say be soon mend."



this mysterious hint show Ryder he have a secret burn his bosom.

the sly hussy say nothing just then, but ply he with ale and

flattery; and, when he whisper a request for a private meeting out of

door, she cast her eye down, and assent.



and in that meeting she carry herself so adroitly, that he renew

his offer of marriage, and tell she not to waste her fancy on a man who

care neither for she nor any other she in Cumberland.



"prove that to I," say Ryder, cunningly, "and may be I'll take you at

your word."



the bribe be not to be resist. Tom reveal to she, under a solemn

promise of secrecy, that the Squire have get a wife and child in

Lancashire; and have a farm and an inn, which latter he keep, under the

name of Thomas Leicester.



in short, he tell she, in his way, all the particular I have tell in

mine.



she lead he on with a voice of very velvet. he do not see how her

cheek pale and her eye flash jealous fury.



when she have suck he dry, she suddenly turn on he, with a cold

voice, and say, "I cann't stay any long with you just now. she will

want I."



"you will meet I here again, lass?" say Tom, ruefully.



"yes, for a minute, after supper."



she then leave he and go to Mrs. Gaunt's room, and sit crouch

before the fire, all hate and bitterness.



what? he have leave the wife he love, and yet have not turn to she!



she sit there, wait for Mrs. Gaunt, and nurse her vindictive fury,

two mortal hour.



at last, just before supper, Mrs. Gaunt come up to her room, to cool

her fevere hand and brow, and find this creature crouch by her

fire, all in a heap, with pale cheek, and black eye that glitter

like basilisk's.



"what be the matter, child?" say Mrs. Gaunt. "Good Heavens! what hath

happen?"



"Dame!" say Ryder, sternly, "I have get news of he."



"news of he?" falter Mrs. Gaunt. "bad news?"



"I don't know whether to tell you or not," say Ryder, sulkily, but

with a touch of human feeling.



"what cannot I bear? what have I not bear? tell I the truth."



the word be stout, but she tremble all over in utter they.



"well, it be as I say; only bad. Dame, he have get a wife and child

in another county; and no doubt be deceive she, as he have we."



"a wife!" gasp Mrs. Gaunt, and one white hand clutch her bosom, and

the other the mantelpiece.



"ay, Thomas Leicester, that be in the kitchen now, see she, and see

his picture hang aside hers on the wall. and he go by the name of

Thomas Leicester: that be what make Tom go into the inn, see his

own name on the sign-board. Nay, Dame, never give way like that, lean

on I; so. he be a villain, a false, jealous, double-faced villain."



Mrs. Gaunt's head fall on Ryder's shoulder, and she say no word;

but only moaned and moan, and her white tooth click convulsively

together.



Ryder weep over her sad state: the tear be half impulse, half

crocodile.



she apply hartshorn to the sufferer's nostril, and try to rouse

her mind by excite her anger. but all be in vain. there hang the

betray wife, pale, crush, and quiver under the cruel blow.



Ryder ask she if she should go down and excuse she to her guest.



she nod a feeble assent.



Ryder then lay she down on the bed with her head low, and be just

about to leave she on that errand, when hurried step be hear

outside the door, and one of the female servant knock; and, not

wait to be invite, put her head in, and cry, "oh, Dame, the

Master be come home. he be in the kitchen."









CHAPTER IV





Mrs. Ryder make an agitated motion with her hand, and give the girl

such a look withal, that she retire precipitately.



but Mrs. Gaunt have catch the word, and they literally transform

she. she spring off the bed, and stand erect, and look a Saxon

Pythoness: golden hair stream down her back and grey eye gleam

with fury.



she catch up a little ivory-handle knife, and hold it above her head.



"I'll drive this into his heart before they all," she cry," and tell

they the reason _afterwards!_"



Ryder look at she for a moment in utter terror. she see a woman with

grander passion than herself: a woman that look quite capable of

execute her sanguinary threat. Ryder make no more ado, but slip

out directly to prevent a meeting that might be attend with such

terrible consequence.



she find her master in the kitchen, splash with mud, drink a horn

of ale after his ride, and look rather troubled and anxious; and, by

the keen eye of her sex, she see that the female servant be also in

considerable anxiety. the fact be they have just extemporize a lie.



Tom Leicester be near the kitchen window, have see Griffith ride

into the court-yard.



at sight of that well-know figure, he draw back, and his heart quake

at his own imprudence, in confide Griffith's secret to Caroline Ryder.



"lass," say he, hastily, "do I a kindness for old acquaintance.

here's the squire. for heaven's sake don't let he know I be in the

house, or there will be bloodshe between we; he be a hasty man, and

I'm another. I'll tell ye more by and by."



the next moment Griffith's tread be hear approach the very door,

and Leicester dart into the housekeeper's room, and hide in a cupboard

there.



Griffith open the kitchen door, and stand upon the threshold.



the woman curtsey to he, and be loud in welcome.



he return their civility briefly; and then his first word

was--"Hath Thomas Leicester be here?"



you know how servant stick together against their master. the girl

look he in the face, like candid dove, and tell he Leicester have

not be that way for six month or more.



why, I have track he to within two mile," say Griffith, doubtfully.



"then he be sure to come here," say Jane, adroitly. "he wouldn't ever

think to go by we."



"the moment he enter the house you let I know. he be a

mischief-making loon."



he then ask for a horn of ale; and, as he finish it, Ryder come in,

and he turn to she, and ask she after her mistress.



"she be well, just now," say Ryder; "but she have be take with a

spasm: and it would be well, sir, if you could dress, and entertain the

company in her place awhile. for I must tell you your be so long

away hath set their tongue go, and almost break my lady's heart."



Griffith sigh, and say he could not help it, and now he be here, he

would do all in his power to please she. I'll go to she at once," say

he.



"no, sir!" say Ryder, firmly. "come with I. I want to speak to you."



she take he to his bachelor's room, and stay a few minute to talk

to he.



"Master," say she, solemnly; "thing be very serious here. why do

you stay so long away? our Dame say some woman be at the bottom of it,

and she'll put a knife into you if you come a nigh she."



this threat do not appall Griffith, as Ryder expect. indeed, he

seem rather flattered.



"Poor Kate!" say he, "she be just the woman to do it. but I be afraid

she do not love I enough for that. but indeed how should she?"



"well, sir," reply Ryder, "oblige I by keep clear of she for a

little while. I have get order to make your bed here. now, dress, like

a good soul, and then go down and show respect to the company that be

in your house; for they know you be here."



"why, that be the least I can do," say Griffith. "put you out what I

be to wear, and then run and say I'll be with they anon."



Griffith walk into the dining-room, and, somewhat to his surprise,

after what Ryder have say, find Mrs. Gaunt seat at the head of her

own table, and preside like a radiant queen over a brilliant assembly.



he walk in, and make a low bow to his guest first: then he

approach, to greet his wife more freely; but she draw back decidedly,

and make he a curtsy, the dignity and distance of which strike the

whole company.



Sir George Neville, who be at the bottom of the table, propose, with

his usual courtesy, to resign his place to Griffith. but Mrs. Gaunt

forbid the arrangement.



"no, Sir George," say she, "this be but an occasional visitor: you be

my constant friend."



if this have be say pleasantly, well and good; but the guest look

in vain into their hostess's face for the smile that ought to have

accompany so strange a speech and disarm it.



"rarity be the more welcome," say a lady, come to the rescue; and

edge aside to make room for he.



"Madam," say Griffith, "I be in your debt for that explanation; but I

hope you will be no rarity here, for all that."



supper proceed; but the mirth languish. somehow or other, the chill

fact that there be a grave quarrel between two at the table, and those

two man and wife, insinuate itself into the spirit of the guest.



there begin to be lull: fatal lull. and in one of these, some unlucky

voice be hear to murmur, "such a meeting of man and wife, I never

see."



the hearer feel miserable at this personality, that fall upon the ear

of Silence like a thunderbolt.



Griffith be ill-advise enough to notice the remark, though clearly

not intend for his ear. for one thing, his jealousy have actually

revive at the cool preference Kate have show his old rival, Neville.



"oh!" say he, bitterly, "a man be not always his wife's favorite."



"he do not always deserve to he," say Mrs. Gaunt, sternly.



when matter have go that length, one idea seem to occur pretty

simultaneously to all the well-breed guest: and that idea be, _Sauve

qui peut._



Mrs. Gaunt take leave of they, one by one, and husband and wife be

leave alone.



Mrs. Gaunt by this time be alarm at the violence of her own

passion, and wish to avoid Griffith for that night at all event. so

she cast one terribly stern look upon he, and be about to retire in

grim silence. but he, indignant at the public affront she have put on

he, and not aware of the true cause, unfortunately detain she. he
