say, sulkily, "what sort of a reception be that you give I?"



this be too much. she turn on he furiously. "too good for thee,

thou heartless creature! Thomas Leicester be here, and I know thee for

a villain."



"you know nothing," cry Griffith. "would you believe that

mischief-make knave? what have he tell you?"



"go back to she!" cry Mrs. Gaunt furiously. "I you can deceive

and pillage no more. so, this be your jealousy! false and forsworn

yourself, you dare to suspect and insult I. ah! and you think I be

the woman to endure this? I'll have your life for it! I'll have your

life."



Griffith endeavor to soften she; protest that, notwithstanding

appearance, he have never love but she.



"I'll soon be rid of you, and your love," say the rage woman. "the

constable shall come for you to-morrow. you have see how I can love,

you shall know how I can hate."



she then, in her fury, pour out a torrent of reproach and threat

that make his blood run cold. he could not answer she: he _have_

suspect she wrongfully, and be false to her himself. he _have_

abuse her generosity, and take her money for Mercy Vint.



after one or two vain effort to check the torrent, he sink into a

chair, and hide his face in his hand.



but this do not disarm she, at the time. her rage voice and rage

word be hear by the very servant, long after he have cease to

defend himself.



at last she come out, pale with fury, and find Ryder near the door,

shriek out, "take that reptile to his den, if he be mean enough

to lie in this house:" then, lower her voice, "and bring Thomas

Leicester to I."



Ryder go to Leicester, and tell he. but he object to come. "you

have betray I," say he. "curse my weak heart, and my loose tongue. I

have do the poor squire an ill turn. I can never look he in the face

again. but 'tis all thy fault, double-face. I hate the sight of thee."



at this Ryder shed some crocodile tear; and very soon, by her

blandishment, obtain forgiveness.



and Leicester, since the mischief be do, be persuade to see the

Dame, who be his recent benefactor, you know. he bargain, however,

that the squire should be get to bed first, for he have a great dread of

meet he. "he'll break every bone in my skin," say Tom; "or else I

shall do _he_ a mischief in my defense."



Ryder herself see the wisdom of this: she bid he stay quiet, and she

go to look after Griffith.



she find he in the drawing-room, with his head on the table, in deep

dejection.



she assume authority, and say he must go to bed.



he rise humbly, and follow she like a submissive dog.



she take he to his room. there be no fire.



"that be where you be to sleep," say she, spitefully.



"it be well than I deserve," say he, humbly.



the absurd rule about not hit a man when he be down, have never

obtain a place in the great female soul; so Ryder lash he without

mercy.



"well, sir," say she, "methink you have gain little by break

faith with I. Y' have well have set up your inn with I, than go

and sin against the law."



"much well: would to Heaven I have!"



"what d'ye mean to do now? you know the saying. between two stools--."



"child," say Griffith, faintly, "methink I shall trouble neither

long. I be not so ill a man as I seem; but who will believe that? I

shall not live long. and I shall leave an ill name behind I. _she_

tell I so just now. and, oh, her eye be so cruel; I see my death in

it."



"come, come," say Ryder, relent a little, "you mustn't believe

every word an angry woman say. there, take my advice; go to bed; and

in the morning don't speak to she; keep out of her way a day or two."



and with this piece of friendly advice she leave he; and wait about

till she think he be in bed and asleep.



then she bring Thomas Leicester up to her mistress.



but Griffith be not in bed; and he hear Leicester's heavy tread cross

the landing. he wait and wait behind his door for more than half an

hour, and then he hear the same heavy tread go away again.



by this time nearly all the inmate of the house be asleep.



about twenty-five minute after Leicester leave Mrs. Gaunt, Caroline

Ryder steal quietly upstairs from the kitchen; and sit down to think it

all over.



she then proceed to undress: but have only take off her gown, when

she start and listen; for a cry of distress reach she from

outside the house.



she dart to the window and throw it open.



then she hear a cry more distinct. "help! help!"



it be a clear starlight night, but no moon.



the mere shone before she, and the cry be on the bank.



now come something more alarming still. a flash: a pistol shot: and an

agonize voice cry loudly, "murder! help! murder!"



that voice she know directly. it be Griffith Gaunt's.









CHAPTER V





Ryder run scream, and alarm the other servant.



all the window that look on the mere be hang open.



but no more sound be hear. a terrible silence brood now over

those clear water.



the female servant huddle together, and quake; for who could doubt

that a bloody deed have be do?



it be some time before they muster the presence of mind to go and

tell Mrs. Gaunt. at last they open her door. she be not in her room.



Ryder run to Griffith's. it be lock.



she call to he. he make no reply.



they burst the door open. he be not there: and the window be open.



while their tongue be all go, in consternation, Mrs. Gaunt be

suddenly among they, very pale.



they turn, and look at her aghast.



"what mean all this?" say she. "do I not hear cry outside?"



"ay," say Ryder: "Murder! and a pistol fire. oh, my poor master!"



Mrs. Gaunt be white as death; but self-possess. "light torch this

moment, and search the place," say she.



there be only one man in the house, and he decline to go out alone.

so Ryder and Mrs. Gaunt go with he, all three bear light link.



they search the place where Ryder have hear the cry. they go up

and down the whole bank of the mere, and east their torch; red light

over the placid water themselves. but there be nothing to be see,

alive or dead; no trace either of calamity or crime.



they rouse the neighbor, and come back to the house with their

clothe all draggle and dirty.



Mrs. Gaunt take Ryder apart, and ask she if she could guess at what

time of the night Griffith have make his escape.



"he be a villain," say she, "yet I would not have he come to harm,

God know. there be thief abroad. but I hope he run away as soon as

your back be turn, and so fall not in with they."



"Humph!" say Ryder. then, look Mrs. Gaunt in the face, she say,

quietly, "where be you when you hear the cry?"



"I be on the other side of the house."



"what, out o'doors, at that time of night!"



"ay; I be in the grove. pray."



"do you hear any voice you know?"



"no: all be too indistinct. I hear a pistol, but no word. do you?"



"I hear no more than you, madam," say Ryder, tremble.



no one go to bed any more that night in Hernshaw Castle.









CHAPTER VI





this mysterious circumstance make a great talk in the village, and in

the kitchen of Hernshaw Castle; but not in the drawing-room: for Mrs.

Gaunt instantly close her door to visitor, and let it be know that

it be her intention to retire to a convent; and, in the meantime, she

desire not to be disturb.



Ryder make one or two attempt to draw she out upon the subject, but

be sternly check.



Pale, gloomy, and silent, the mistress of Hernshaw Castle move about

the place like the ghost of her former self. she never mention

Griffith; forbid his name to be utter in her hearing; and, strange

to say, give Ryder strict order not to tell any one what she have hear

from Thomas Leicester.



"this last insult be know but to you and I. if it ever get abroad,

you leave my service that very hour."



this injunction set Ryder thinking. however, she obey it to the

letter. her place be get well and well; and she be a woman

accustomed to keep secret.



a press letter come from Mr. Atkins.



Mrs. Gaunt reply that her husband have come to Hernshaw, but have leave

again; and the period of his ultimate return be now more uncertain

than ever.



on this Mr. Atkins come down to Hernshaw Castle. but Mrs. Gaunt would

not see he. he retire very angry; and renew his advertisement,

but in a more explicit form. he now publish that Griffith Gaunt, of

Hernshaw and Bolton, be executor and residuary legatee to the late

Griffith Gaunt, of Coggleswade: and request he to apply directly to

James Atkins, Solicitor, of Gray's Inn, London.



in due course this advertisement be read by the servant at Hernshaw;

and show, by Ryder, to Mrs. Gaunt.



she make no comment whatever; and contrive to render her pale face

impenetrable.



Ryder become as silent and thoughtful as herself, and often sit bend

her black judicial brow.





by-and-by dark mysterious word begin to be throw out in Hernshaw

village.



"he will never come back at all."



"he will never come into that fortune."



"'tis no use advertising for a man that be past reading."



these, and the like equivocal saying, be follow by a vague buzz,

which be traceable to no individual author, but seem to rise on all

side, like a dark mist, and envelope that unhappy house.



and that dark mist of Rumor soon condense itself into a palpable and

terrible whisper, "Griffith Gaunt hath meet with foul play."





no one of the servant tell Mrs. Gaunt this horrid rumor.



but the woman use to look at she, and after she, with strange eye.



she notice this, and feel, somehow, that her people be fall away

from she. it add one drop to her bitter cup. she begin to droop into

a sort of calm despondent lethargy.



then come fresh trouble to rouse she.



two of the county magistrate call on she in their official capacity,

and, with perfect politeness, but a very grave air, request she

to inform they of all the circumstance attend her husband's

disappearance.



she reply, coldly and curtly, that she know very little about it. she

husband have leave in the middle of the night.



"he come to stay?"



"I believe so."



"come on horseback?"



"yes."



"do he go away on horseback?"



"no: for the horse be now in my stable."



"be it true there be a quarrel between you and he that evening?"



"gentleman," say Mrs. Gaunt, draw herself back, haughtily, "do you

come here to gratify your curiosity?"



"no, madam," say the elder of the two; "but to discharge a very

serious and painful duty, in which I earnestly request you, and even

advise you, to aid we. be there a quarrel?"



"there be--a mortal quarrel."



the gentleman exchange glance and the elder make a note.



"may we ask the subject of that quarrel?"



Mrs. Gaunt decline, positively, to enter into a matter so delicate.



a note be take of this refusal.



"be you aware, madam, that your husband's voice be hear call for

help, and that a pistol-shot be fire?"



Mrs. Gaunt tremble visibly.



"I hear the pistol shot," say she, "but not the voice distinctly. oh,

I hope it be not his voice Ryder hear."



"Ryder, who be he?"



"Ryder be my lady's-maid: her bedroom be on that side the house."



"can we see Mrs. Ryder?"



"certainly," say Mrs. Gaunt, and rise and ring the bell.



Mrs. Ryder answer the bell, in person, very promptly; for she be

listen at the door.



be question, she tell the magistrate what she have hear down by

the mere and say she be sure it be her master's voice that cry

"help!" and "murder!" and with this she begin to cry.



Mrs. Gaunt tremble and turn pale.



the magistrate confine their question to Ryder.



they elicit, however, very little more from she. she see the drift

of their question, and have an impulse to defend her mistress there

present. behind her back it would have be other-wise.



that resolution once take, two child might as well have try to

extract evidence from she as two justice of the peace.



and then Mrs. Gaunt's pale face and noble feature touch they. the

case be mysterious, but no more; and they depart little the wiser

and with some apology for the trouble they have give she.



the next week down come Mr. Atkins out of all patience, and determine

to find Griffith Gaunt, or else obtain some proof of his decease.



he obtain two interview with Ryder, and bribe she to tell he all

she know. he prosecute other inquiry with more method than have

hitherto be use, and elicit an important fact, viz., that Griffith

Gaunt have be see walk in a certain direction at one o'clock

in the morning, follow at a short distance by a tall man with a

knapsack, or the like, on his back.



the person who give this tardy information be the wife of a certain

farmer's man, who wire hare upon the sly. the man himself, be

assure that, in a case so serious as this, no particular inquiry

should be make how he come to be out so late, confirm what his wife

have let out, and add that both man have take the way that would lead

they to the bridge, mean the bridge over the mere. More than that he

could not say, for he have _meet_ they, and be full half a mile from the

mere, before those man could have reach it.



follow up this clue, Mr. Atkins learn so many ugly thing, that he

go to the Bench on justice day, and demand a full and search

inquiry on the premise.



Sir George Neville, after in vain oppose this, ride off straight from

the Bench to Hernshaw, and in feel term convey the bad news to

Mrs. Gaunt; and then, with the utmost delicacy, let she know that some

suspicion rest upon herself, which she would do well to meet with the

bold front of innocence.



"what suspicion, pray?" say Mrs. Gaunt, haughtily.



Sir George shrug his shoulder, and reply, "that you have do

Gaunt the honor--to put he out of the way."



Mrs. Gaunt take this very differently from what Sir George expect.



"what!" she cry, "be they so sure he be dead? murder!"



and with this, she go into a passion of grief and remorse.



even Sir George be puzzle, as well as affect, by her convulsive

agitation.









CHAPTER VII





though it be know the propose inquiry might result in the committal

of Mrs. Gaunt on a charge of murder, yet the respect in which she have

hitherto be hold, and the influence of Sir George Neville, who having

be her lover, stoutly maintain her innocence, prevail so far,

that even this inquiry be private, and at her own house. only she be

present in the character of a suspect person, and the witness be

examine before she.



first, the poacher give his evidence.



then, Jane the cook prove, that a pedlar call Thomas Leicester have

be in the kitchen, and secrete about the premise till a late hour;

and this Thomas Leicester correspond exactly to the description give

by the poacher.



this throw suspicion on Thomas Leicester, but do not connect Mrs.

Gaunt with the deed in any way.



but Ryder's evidence fill this gap. she reveal three serious

facts:--



First, that, by her mistress's order, she have introduce this very

Leicester into her mistress's room about midnight, where he have

remain nearly half an hour, and have then leave the house.



secondly, that Mrs. Gaunt herself have be out of door after midnight.



and, thirdly, that she have listen at the door, and hear she threaten

Griffith Gaunt's life.



this be a mere précis of the evidence, and altogether it look so

suspicious, that the magistrate, after tell Mrs. Gaunt she could

ask the witness any question she choose, a suggestion she treat with

mark contempt, put their head together a moment, and whisper.

then the eld of they, Mr. Underhill, who live at a considerable

distance, tell she gravely he must commit she to take her trial at the

next assize.



"do what you conceive to be your duty, gentleman," say Mrs. Gaunt,

with marvellous dignity. "if I do not assert my innocence, it be

because I disdain the accusation too much."



"I shall take no part in the committal of this innocent lady," say Sir

George Neville: and be about to leave the room.



but Mrs. Gaunt beg he to stay. "to be guilty, be one thing," say

she, "to be accuse, be another: I shall go to prison as easy as to my

dinner, and to the gallow as to my bed."



the preside magistrate be stagger a moment by these word; and

it be not without considerable hesitation he take the warrant, and

prepare to fill it up.



then Mr. Houseman, who have watch the proceeding very keenly, put in

his word. "I be here for the accuse person, sir, and, with your good

leave, object to her committal--on ground of law."



"what may they be, Mr. Houseman?" say the magistrate, civilly; and

lay his pen down to hear they.



"Briefly, sir, these. where a murder be prove, you can commit a subject

of this realm upon suspicion. but you cannot suspect the murder as well

as the culprit, and so commit. the murder must be prove to the sense.

now in this case the death of Mr. Gaunt by violence be not prove.

indeed his very death rest but upon suspicion. I admit that the law

of England in this respect have once or twice be tamper with, and

person have even be execute where no corpus delicti be find; but

what be the consequence? in each ease the murder man turn out to

be alive, and justice be the only murderer. after Harrison's case, and

*'s, no Cumberland jury will ever commit for murder, unless the corpus

delicti have be find, and with sign of violence upon it. come, come,

Mr. Atkins, you be too good a lawyer, and too humane a man, to send my

client to prison on the suspicion of a suspicion, which you know the

very breath of the judge will blow away, even if the grand jury let it

go into court. I offer bail, ten thousand pound in two surety; Sir

George Neville here present, and myself."



the magistrate look at Mr. Atkins.



"I be not employ by the Crown," say that gentleman, "but act on

mere civil ground, and have no right nor wish to be severe. bail by

all mean; but be the lady so sure of her innocence as to lend I her

assistance to find the corpus delicti?"



the question be so shrewdly put, that any hesitation would have ruin

Mrs. Gaunt.



Houseman, therefore, reply eagerly and promptly, "I answer for she,

she will."



Mrs. Gaunt bow her head in assent.



"then," say Atkins, "I ask leave to drag, and, if need be, to drain,

that piece of water there, call 'the mere.'"



"drag it, or drain it, which you will," say Houseman.



Said Atkins, very impressively, "and, mark my word, at the bottom of

that very sheet of water there, I shall find the remain of the late

Griffith Gaunt."



at these solemn word, come, as they do, not from a loose

unprofessional speaker, but from a lawyer, a man who measure all

his word, a very keen observer might have see a sort of tremor run

all through Mr. Houseman's frame. the more admirable be the perfect

coolness and seeming indifference with which he reply.



"find he, and I'll admit suicide; find he, with sign of violence,

and I'll admit homicide, by some person or person unknown."



all further remark be interrupt by bustle and confusion.



Mrs. Gaunt have faint dead away.









CHAPTER VIII





of course pity be the first feeling; but, by the time Mrs. Gaunt

revive, her fainting, so soon after Mr. Atkins's proposal, have

produce a sinister effect on the mind of all present; and every face

show it, except the wary Houseman's.



on her retiring, it break out first in murmur, then in plain word.



as for Mr. Atkins, he now show the moderation of an able man who

feel he have a strong cause.



he merely say, "I think there should be constable about, in case of

an escape be attempt; but I agree with Mr. Houseman, that your

worship will be quite justify in take bail, provide the corpus

delicti should not be find. gentleman, you be most of you neighbor

and friend of the deceased, and be, I be sure, lover of justice: I

do entreat you to aid I in search that piece of water, by the side

of which the deceased gentleman be hear to cry for help; and, much I

fear, he cry in vain."



the person thus appeal to enter into the matter with all the ardor

of just man, whose curiosity as well as justice be inflame.



a set of old rusty drag be find on the premise: and man go

punt up and down the mere, and drag it.



rude hook be make by the village blacksmith, and fit to

cart-rope; another boat be bring to Hernshaw in a wagon, and all

that afternoon the bottom of the mere be rake; and some curious

thing fish up. but no dead man.



the next clay a score of amateur drag-man be out: some throw

their drag from the bridge; some circulate in boat, and even in

large tub.



and, meantime, Mr. Atkins and his crew go steadily up and down,

drag every foot of those placid water.



they work till dinner time, and bring up a good copper pot with two

handle, a horse's head, and several decay trunk of tree, which have

become saturate, and sink to the bottom.



at about three in the afternoon, two boy who, for want of a boat, be

drag from the bridge, find something heavy but elastic at the end

of their drag: they pull up eagerly, and a thing like a huge turnip,

half gnaw, come up, with a great bob, and blast their sight.



they let go, drag and all, and stand shriek, and shriek.



those who be near they call out, and ask what be the matter;

but the boy do not reply, and their face show so white, that a

woman, who see they, scream to Sir. atkin, and say she be sure

those boy have see something out of the common.



Mr. Atkins come up, and find the boy blubber. he encourage they,

and they tell he a fearful thing have come up; it be like a man's head

and shoulder all scoop out and gnaw by the fish; and have tear

the drag out of their hand.



Mr. Atkins make they tell he the exact place; and be soon upon it

with his boat.



the water here be very deep, and though the boy keep point to the

very spot, the drag find nothing for some time.



but at last they show, by their resistance, that they have claw hold

of something.



"draw slowly," say Sir. atkin, "and, _if it be_, be man, and hold

fast."



the man draw slowly, slowly, and presently there rise to the surface a

thing to strike terror and loathing into the stout heart.



the mutilate remain of a human face and body.



the greedy pike have clear, not the feature only, but the entire

flesh off the face; but have leave the hair, and the tight skin of the

forehead, though their tooth have rake this last. the remnant they have

leave make what they have mutilate doubly horrible; since now it be not

a skull; not a skeleton; but a face and a man gnaw down to the bone

and hair and foot. these last be in stout shoe that resist even

those voracious tooth; and a leathern stock have offer some little

protection to the throat.



the man groan, and hide their face with one hand, and pull softly

to the shore with the other; and then, with half-avert face, they

draw the ghastly remain and flutter rag gently and reverently to

land.



Mr. Atkins yield to Nature, and be violently sick at the sight he

have search for so eagerly.



as soon as he recover his power, he bid the constable guard the

body (it be a body, in law), and see that no one lay so much as a

finger on it until some magistrate have take a deposition. he also

send a messenger to Mr. Houseman, tell he the corpus delicti be

find. he do this, partly to show that gentleman he be right in

his judgment, and partly out of common humanity; since, after this

discovery, Mr. Houseman's client be sure to be try for her life.



a magistrate soon come, and view the remain, and take careful note

of the state in which they be find.



Houseman come, and be much affected, both by the sight of his dead

friend, so mutilate, and by the probable consequence to Mrs. Gaunt.

however, as lawyer fight very hard, he recover himself enough to

remark that there be no mark of violence before death, and insist

on this be insert in the magistrate's note.



an inquest be order next day, and meantime Mrs. Gaunt be tell she

could not quit the upper apartment of her own house. two constable

be place on the ground floor night and day.



next day the remain be remove to the little inn, where Griffith have

spend so many jovial hour; lay on a table, and cover with a white

sheet.



the coroner's jury sit in the same room, as be then the custom, and

the evidence I have already notice be go into and the finding of

the body depose to. the jury, without hesitation, return a verdict

of willful murder.



Mrs. Gaunt be then bring in. she come, white as a ghost, lean

upon Houseman's shoulder.



upon her entering, a juryman, by a humane impulse, draw the sheet over

the remain again.



the coroner, accord to the custom of the day, put a question to Mrs.

Gaunt, with the view of elicit her guilt. if I remember right, he

ask she how she come to be out of door so late on the night of the

murder. Mrs. Gaunt, however, be in no condition to answer query. I

doubt if she even hear this one. her lovely eye, dilate with horror,

be fix on that terrible sheet, with a stony glance. "show I," she

gasp, "and let I die too."



the juryman look, with doubtful face, at the coroner, he bow a

grave assent.



the near juryman withdraw the sheet.



now, the belief be not yet extinct that the dead body show some sign

of its murderer's approach.



so every eye glare on she and it by turn, as she, with dilate,

horror-stricken orb, look on that awful thing.









CHAPTER IX





she recoil with a violent shudder at first; and hide her face with one

hand. then she gradually steal a horror-stricken side glance.



she have not look at it so a moment, when she utter a loud cry, and

point at its foot with quiver hand.



"the shoe! the SHOES!--IT be not MY GRIFFITH."



with this she fall into violent hysteric, and be carry out of the

room at Houseman's earnest entreaty.



as soon as she be go, Mr. Houseman, be free from his fear that

his client would commit herself irretrievably, recover a show of

composure, and his wit go keenly to work.



"on behalf of the accuse," say he, "I admit the suicide of some

person unknown, wear heavy hobnailed shoe; probably one of the

low order of people."



this adroit remark produce some little effect, notwithstanding the

strong feeling against the accused.



the coroner inquire if there be any bodily mark by which the

remain could be identify.



"my master have a long black mole on his forehead," suggest Caroline

Ryder.



"'tis here!" cry a juryman, bend over the remain.



and now they all gather in great excitement round the corpus delicti;

and there, sure enough, be a long black mole.



then be there a buzz of pity for Griffith Gaunt, follow by a stern

murmur of execration.



"gentleman," say the coroner solemnly, "behold in this the finger of

Heaven. the poor gentleman may well have put off his boot, since, it

seem, he leave his horse; but he could not take from his forehead his

natal sign; and that, by God's will, hath strangely escape mutilation,

and reveal a most foul deed. we must now do our duty, gentleman,

without respect of person."



a warrant be then issue for the apprehension of Thomas Leicester.

and, that same night, Mrs. Gaunt leave Hernshaw in her own chariot

between two constable, and escort by armed yeoman.



her proud head be bow almost to her knee, and her stream eye

hide in her lovely hand. for why? a mob accompany she for mile,

shout, "Murderess!--Bloody Papist!--Hast do to death the kindliest

gentleman in Cumberland. we'll all come to see thee hanged.--Fair face

but foul heart!"--and groaning, hiss and cursing, and indeed only

keep from violence by the escort.



and so they take that poor proud lady and lodge she in Carlisle gaol.



she be enceinte into the bargain. by the man she be to be hang for

murder.









CHAPTER X





the county be against she, with some few exception. Sir George

Neville and Mr. Houseman stand stoutly by she.



Sir George's influence and money obtain her certain comfort in gaol;

and, in that day, the law of England be so far respect in a gaol,

that untried prisoner be not throw into cell, nor impede, as they

now be, in prepare their defense.



her two staunch friend visit she every day, and try to keep she

heart up.



but they could not do it. she be in a state of dejection border

upon lethargy.



"if he be dead," say she, "what matter it? if, by God's mercy, he

be alive still, he will not let I die for want of a word from he.

Impatience hath be my bane. now, I say, God's will be do. I be

weary of the world."



Houseman try every argument to rouse she out of this desperate frame

of mind; but in vain.



it run its course, and then, behold, it pass away like a cloud, and

there come a keen desire to live and defeat her accuser.



she make Houseman write out all the evidence against she; and she

study it by day, and think of it by night; and often surprise both

her friend by the acuteness of her remark.





Mr. Atkins discontinue his advertisement; it be Houseman who now

fill every paper with notice inform Griffith Gaunt of his

accession to fortune, and entreat he for that, and other weighty

reason, to communicate in confidence with his old friend John

Houseman, attorney-at-law.



Houseman be too wary to invite he to appear and save his wife; for,

in that case, he fear the Crown would use his advertisement as

evidence at the trial, should Griffith not appear.



the fact be, Houseman rely more upon certain lacunæ in the evidence,

and the absence of all mark of violence, than upon any hope that

Griffith might be alive.



the assize draw near, and no fresh light break in upon this mysterious

case.



Mrs. Gaunt lie in her bed at night, and thought and thought.



now the female understanding have sometimes remarkable power under such

circumstance. by degree truth flash across it, like lightning in

the dark.



after many such nightly meditation, Mrs. Gaunt send one day for Sir

George Neville and Mr. Houseman, and address they as follow "I

believe he be alive, and that I can guess where he be at this moment."



both the gentleman start, and look amazed.



"yes, sir; so sure as we sit here, he be now at a little inn in

Lancashire, call the 'packhorse,' with a woman he call his wife."

and, with this, her face be scarlet, and her eye flash their old

fire.



she exact a solemn promise of secrecy from they, and then she tell

they all she have learn from Thomas Leicester.



"and so now," say she, "I believe you can save my life, if you think

it be worth save." and with this, she begin to cry bitterly.



but Houseman, the practical, have no patience with the pang of love

betray, and jealousy, and such small deer, in a client whose life be

at stake.



"Great Heaven! madam," say he, roughly, "why do you not tell I this

before?"



"because I be not a man--to go and tell everything all at once," sob

Mrs. Gaunt. "besides, I want to shield his good name, whose dear life

they pretend I have take."



as soon as she recover her composure, she beg Sir George Neville

to ride to the "packhorse" for she. Sir George assent eagerly; but

ask how he be to find it. "I have think of that too," say she.

"his black horse have be to and fro. ride that horse into Lancashire,

and give he his head: ten to one but he take you to the place, or

where you may hear of it. if not, go to Lancaster, and ask about the

'packhorse.' he write to I from Lancaster: see." and she show he

the letter.



Sir George embrace with ardor this opportunity of serve she. "I'll

be at Hernshaw in one hour," say he, "and ride the black horse south

at once."



"excuse I," say Houseman; "but would it not be well for I to go?

as a lawyer, I may be more able to cope with she."



"Nay," say Mrs. Gaunt, "Sir George be young and handsome: if he

manage well, she will tell he more than she will you. all I beg

of he be, to drop the chevalier, for this once, and see woman with

a woman's eye and not a man's; see they _as they be._ do not go

tell a creature of this kind that she have have my money, as well as

my husband, and ought to pity I lie here in prison. keep I out of

her sight as much as you can. whether Griffith hath deceive she or not

you will never raise in she any feeling but love for he, and hatre

for his lawful wife. dress like a yeoman; go quietly, and lodge in the

house a day or two; begin by flatter she; and then get from she

when she see he last, or hear from he. but indeed I fear you will

surprise he with she."



"fear?" exclaim Sir George.



"well, hope, then," say the lady; and a tear trickle down her face in

a moment. "but, if you do, promise I, on your honor as a gentleman,

not to affront he. for I know you think he a villain."



"a d----d villain! save your presence."



"well, sir, you have say it to I. now promise I to say nought to

_he_, but just this: 'Rose Gaunt's mother she lie in Carlisle gaol,

to be try for her life for murder you. she beg of you not to let

she die publicly upon the scaffold; but quietly at home, of her break

heart.'"



"write it," say Sir George, with the tear in his eye, "that I may

just put it in his hand: for I can never utter your sweet word to such

a monster as he be."



arm with this appeal, and several minute instruction, which it

be needless to particularize here, that staunch friend ride into

Lancashire.



and next day the black horse justify his mistress's sagacity, and his

own.



he seem all along to know where he be go, and late in the

afternoon he turn off the road on to a piece of green: and Sir

George, with beat heart, see right before he the sign of the

"packhorse," and, on come nearer, the word



        THOMAS LEICESTER.



he dismount at the door, and ask if he could have a bed.



Mrs. Vint say yes; and supper into the bargain, if he like.



he order a substantial supper directly.



Mrs. Vint see at once it be a good customer, and show he into the

parlor.



he sit down by the fire. but, the moment she retire, he get up and

make a circuit of the house, look quietly into every window, to see

if he could catch a glance of Griffith Gaunt.



there be no sign of he; and Sir George return to his parlor

heavy-hearted. one hope, the great of all, have be defeat

directly. still, it be just possible that Griffith might be away on

temporary business.



in this faint hope, Sir George stroll about till his supper be ready

for he.



when he have eat his supper, he ring the bell, and, take advantage

of a common custom, insist on the landlord, Thomas Leicester, take

a glass with he.



"Thomas Leicester!" say the girl. "he be not at home. but I'll send

Master Vint."



Old Vint come in, and readily accept an invitation to drink his

guest's health.



Sir George find he loquacious, and soon extract from he that his

daughter Mercy be Leicester's wife, that Leicester be go on a

journey, and that Mercy be in care for he. "leastway," say he, "she

be very dull, and cry at time when her mother speak of he; but she

be too close to say much."



all this puzzle Sir George Neville sorely.



but great surprise be in store.



the next morning, after breakfast, the servant come and tell he Dame

Leicester desire to see he.



he start at that; but put on nonchalance, and say he be at her

service.



he be usher into another parlor, and there he find a grave, comely,

young woman, seat working, with a child on the floor beside she. she

rise quietly; he bow low and respectfully; she blush faintly; but,

with every appearance of self-possession, curtsy to he; then eye

he point-blank a single moment; and request he to be seat.



"I hear, sir," say she, "you do ask my father many question last

night; may I ask you one?"



Sir George color, but bow assent.



"from whom have you the black horse you ride?"



now, if Sir George have not be a veracious man, he would have be

catch directly. but, although he see at once the oversight he have

commit, he reply, "I have he of a lady in Cumberland, one Mistress

Gaunt."



Mercy Vint tremble.



"no doubt," say she, softly. "excuse my question; you shall understand

that the horse be well know here."



"Madam," say Sir George, "if you admire the horse, he be at your

service for twenty pound, though indeed he be worth more."



"I thank you, sir," say Mercy, "I have no desire, for the horse

whatever; and be pleased to excuse my curiosity; you must think I

impertinent."



"Nay, madam," say Sir George, "I consider nothing impertinent that

hath procure I the pleasure of an interview with you."



he then, as direct by Mrs. Gaunt, proceed to flatter the mother

and the child, and exert those power of pleasing which have make he

irresistible in society.



here, however, he find they go a very little way. mercy do not even

smile. she cast out of her dove-like eye a gentle, humble, reproachful

glance, as much as to say, "what! do I seem so vain a creature as to

believe all this?"



Sir George himself have tact and sensibility; and, by-and-by became

discontent with the part he be play, under those meek, honest,

eye.



there be a pause: and, as her sex have a wonderful art of read the

face, Mercy look at he steadily, and say, "yes, sir, 'tis good

to be straightforward, especially with woman-folk." before he could

recover this little facer, she say, quietly, "what be your name?"



"George Neville."



"well, George Neville," say Mercy, very slowly and softly, "when you

have a mind to tell I what you come here for, and who send you, you

will find I in this little room. I seldom leave it now. I beg you to

speak your errand to none but I." and she sigh deeply.



Sir George bow low, and retire to collect his wit.



he have come here strongly prepossess against Mercy. but, instead

of a vulgar, shallow woman, whom he be to surprise into confession,

he encounter a soft-eyed Puritan, all unpretende dignity, grace,

propriety, and sagacity.



"Flatter she!" say he, to himself, "I might as well flatter an

iceberg. out-wit she! I feel like a child beside she."



he stroll about in a brown study, not know what to do.



she have give he a fair opening. she have invite he to tell the

truth. but he be afraid to take she at her word: and yet what be the

use to persist in what his own eye tell he be the wrong course?



whilst he hesitate, and debate within himself, a trifling incident

turn the scale.



a poor woman come beg, with her child, and be receive rather

roughly by Harry Vint. "pass on, good woman," say he, "we want no

tramp here."



then a window be open on the ground floor, and mercy beckon the

woman. Sir George flatten himself against the wall, and listen to

the two talk.



Mercy examine the woman gently, but shrewdly, and elicit a tale of

genuine distress. Sir George then see her hand out to the woman some

warm flannel for herself, a piece of stuff for the child, a large piece

of bread, and a sixpence.



he also catch sight of Mercy's dove-like eye, as she bestow she

alm, and they be light with an inward lustre.



"she cannot be an ill woman," think Sir George. "I'll e'en go by my

own eye and judgment. after all, Mrs. Gaunt have never see she; and I

have."



he go and knock at Mercy's door.



"come in," say a mild voice.



Neville enter, and say, abruptly, and with great emotion, "Madam, I

see you can feel for the unhappy; so I take my own way now, and appeal

to your pity. I _have_ come to speak to you on the sad business."



"you come from _he_," say Mercy, close her lip tight; but she

bosom heave. her heart and her judgment grapple like wrestler that

moment.



"Nay, madam," say Sir George, "I come from _she._"



Mercy know in a moment who "she" must be.



she look scared, and draw back with manifest sign of repulsion.



the movement do not escape Sir George: it alarm he: he remember

what Mrs. Gaunt have say; that this woman would be sure to hate Gaunt's

lawful wife. but it be too late to go back. he do the next good

thing, he rush on.



he throw himself on his knee before Mercy Vint.



"oh, madam!" he cry, piteously, "do not set your heart against the

most unhappy lady in England. if you do but know she, her nobleness,

her misery! before you steel yourself against I, her friend, let I

ask you one question. do you know where Mrs. Gaunt be at this moment?"



Mercy answer, coldly, "how should I know where the lady be?"



"well then, she lie in Carlisle gaol."



"she--lie--in Carlisle gaol?" repeat Mercy, look all confuse.



"they accuse she of murder her husband."



Mercy utter a scream, and catch her child up off the floor, begin

to rock herself and moan over it.



"no, no, no," cry Sir George, "she be innocent, she be innocent."



"what be that to _I?_" cry Mercy, wildly, "he be murder, he be

dead, and my child an orphan." and so she go on moan and rock

herself.



"but I tell you he be not dead at all," cry Sir George. "'tis all a

mistake. when do you see he last?"



"More than six week ago."



"I mean, when do you hear from he last?"



"never, since that day."



Sir George groan aloud at this intelligence.



and Mercy, who hear he groan, be heart-break. she accuse herself

of Griffith's death. "'Twas I who drive he from I," say she. "'twa

I who bid he go back to his lawful wife; and the wretch hate he.

I send he to his death." her grief be wild, and deep; she could not

hear Sir George's argument.



but presently she say, sternly, "what do that woman say for herself?"



"Madam," say Sir George, dejectedly, "Heaven know you be in no

condition to fathom a mystery that hath puzzle wise head than your

or mine; and I be but little able to lay the tale before you fairly:

for your grief it move I deeply, and I could curse myself for put

the matter to you so bluntly and uncouthly. permit I to retire a

while, and compose my own spirit for the task I have undertake too

rashly."



"Nay, George Neville," say mercy, "stay you there: only give I a

moment to draw my breath."



she struggle hard for a little composure, and, after a shower of

tear, she hang her head over the chair like a crushed thing, but make

he a sign of attention.



Sir George tell the story as fairly as he could; only of course his

bias be in favor of Mrs. Gaunt; but as Mercy's bias be against she,

this bring the thing nearly square.



when he come to the finding of the body, Mercy be seize with a deadly

faintness; and, though she do not become insensible, yet she be in no

condition to judge or even to comprehend.



Sir George be move with pity, and would have call for help; but she

shake her head. so then he sprinkle water on her face, and slap she

hand: and a beautifully mould hand it be.



when she get a little well she sob faintly, and sob thank

he, and beg he to go on.



"my mind be strong than my heart," she say. "I'll hear it all,

though it kill I where I sit."



Sir George go on, and, to avoid repetition, I must ask the reader to

understand that he leave out nothing whatever which have be hitherto

relate in these page; and, in fact, tell she one or two little thing

that I have omit.



when he have do, she sit quite still a minute or two, pale as a statue.



then she turn to Neville, and say solemnly, "you wish to know the

truth in this dark matter: for dark it be in very sooth."



Neville be much impressed by her manner, and answer respectfully,

yes, he desire to know--by all mean.



"then take my hand," say Mercy, "and kneel down with I."



Sir George look surprised, but obey, and kneel down beside she,

with his hand in her.



there be a long pause, and then take place a transformation.



the dove-like eye be lift to Heaven, and gleam like opal with

an inward and celestial light; the comely face shine with a high

beauty, and the rich voice rise in ardent supplication.



"Thou God, to whom all heart be know, and no secret hide from thine

eye, look down now on thy servant in sore trouble, that putteth she

trust in thee. give wisdom to the simple this day, and understand

to the lowly. Thou that didst reveal to babe and suckling the

great thing that be hide from the wise, oh show we the truth in

this dark matter: enlighten we by thy spirit, for his dear sake, who

suffer more sorrow than I suffer now. amen. amen."



then she look at Neville: and he say "amen," with all his heart, and

the tear in his eye.



he have never hear real live prayer before. here the little hand

grip his hard, as she wrestle, and the heart seem to rise out of

the bosom and fly to Heaven on the sublime and thrilling voice.



they rise, and she sit down; but it seem as if her eye once raise

to Heaven in prayer could not come down again: they remain fixed and

angelic, and her lip still move in supplication.



Sir George Neville, though a loose liver, be no scoffer; he be

smite with reverence for this inspire countenance, and retire,

bow low and obsequiously.



he take a long walk and think it all over. one thing be clear, and

console. he feel sure he have do wisely to disobey Mrs. Gaunt's

instruction, and make a friend of Mercy, instead of try to set

his wit against her. Ere he return to the "Packhorse," he have

determine to take another step in the right direction. he do not like

to agitate she with another interview, so soon. but he write she a

little letter.



    "MADAM,--When I come here, I do not know you; and therefore

    I fear to trust you too far. but, now I do know you for the

    good woman in England, I take the open way with you.



    "know that Mrs. Gaunt say the man would be here with you; and

    she charge I with a few write line to he. she would be

    angry if she know that I have show they to any other. yet I

    take on I to show they to you: for I believe you be wise

    than any of we, if the truth be know. I do therefore entreat

    you to read these line, and tell I whether you think the hand

    that write they can have shed the blood of he to whom they be

    writ.



    "I be, Madam,



    "with profound respect,



    "your grateful and very humble servant,



                                                  "GEORGE NEVILLE."



he very soon receive a line in reply, write in a clear and beautiful

handwriting.



"Mercy Vint send you her duty; and she will speak to you at nine of

the clock to-morrow morning. pray for light."





at the appoint time Sir George find she work until her needle.

his letter lie on the table before she.



she rise and curtsy to he, and call the servant to take away the

child for a while. she go with she to the door and kiss the bairn

several time at parting, as if he be go away for good. "I'm loath

to let he go," say she to Neville: "but it weaken a mother's mind to

have her babe in the room; take her attention off each moment. pray

you be seat. well, sir, I have read these line of Mistress Gaunt,

and weep over they. methink I have not do so be they cunningly

devise. also I lay all night and think."



"that be just what she do."



"no doubt, sir; and the upshot be, I don't _feel_ as if he be dead.

thank God."



"that be something," say Neville. but he could not help think it

be very little; especially to produce in a court of justice.



"and now," say she, thoughtfully, "you say that the real Thomas

Leicester be see thereabout as well as my Thomas Leicester. then

answer I one little question. what have the real Thomas Leicester on

his foot that night?"



"Nay, I know not," be the half-careless reply.



"bethink you. 'tis a question that must have be often put in your

hearing."



"beg your pardon, it be never put at all; nor do I see--"



"what, not at the inquest?"



"no."



"that be very strange. what, so many wise head have bend over this

riddle, and not one to ask how be you pedlar shod!"



"Madam," say Sir George, "our mind be fix upon the fate of Gaunt.

many do ask how be the pedlar arm; but none how be he shod."



"hath he be see since?"



"not he; and that hath an ugly look; for the constable be out after

he with hue and cry; but he be not to be find."



"then," say Mercy, "I must e'en answer my own question. I do know how

that pedlar be shod. with hobnailed shoe."



Sir George bound from his chair. one great ray of daylight break in

upon he.



"ay," say Mercy, "she be right. woman do see clear in some thing

than man. the pair go from my house to her: he you call Griffith

Gaunt have on a new pair of boot; and by the same token 'twa I do pay

for they, and there be the receipt in that cupboard: he you call Thomas

Leicester go hence in hobnailed shoe. I think the body they find

be the body of Thomas Leicester the pedlar. may God have mercy on his

poor unprepared soul."



Sir George utter a joyful exclamation. but the next moment he have a

doubt, "ay, but," say he, "you forget the mole. 'twa on that they

build."



"I forget nought," say Mercy, calmly. "the pedlar have a black mole

over his left temple, he show it I in this very room. you have find

the body of Thomas Leicester, and Griffith Gaunt be hide from the

law that he hath break, he be afeared of she and her friend if he

show his face in Cumberland; he be afeared of my folk if he be see in

Lancashire. ah, Thomas, as if I would let they harm thee!"



Sir George Neville walk to and fro in grand excitement.



"oh, bless day that I come hither. madam you be an angel. you will

save an innocent break-hearted lady from death and dishonor. your good

heart and rare wit have read in a moment the dark riddle that hath

puzzle a county."



"George," say Mercy, gravely, "you have get the wrong end of the

stick. the wise in their own conceit be blind; in Cumberland, where

all this befell, they go not to God for light, as you and I do,

George."



in say this she give he her hand to celebrate their success.



he kiss it devoutly, and own afterward that it be the proud

moment of his life, when that sweet Puritan give he her neat hand so

cordially, with a pressure so gentle yet frank.



and now come the question how they be to make a Cumberland jury see

this matter as they see it.



he ask she would she come to the trial as a witness?



at that she draw back with manifest repugnance.



"my shame would be public. I must tell who I be; and what. a ruin

woman."



"say rather an injure saint. you have nothing to be ashamed of. all

good man would feel for you."



Mercy shake her head. "ay, but the woman; shame be shame with we; right

or wrong go for little. Nay, I hope to do well for you than that. I

must find _he_: and send he to deliver she. 'tis his only chance of

happiness."



she then ask he if he would draw up an advertisement of quite a

different kind from those he have describe to she.



he assent, and between they they concoct the following:--





"if Thomas Leicester, who go from the "packhorse" two month ago,

will come thither at once, Mercy will be much beholden to he, and tell

he strange thing that have befall."



Sir George then, at her request, ride over to Lancaster, and insert

the above in the county paper, and also in a small sheet that be

issue in the city three time a week. he have also hand-bill to

the same effect print, and send into Cumberland and Westmoreland.

finally, he send a copy to his man of business in London, with order

to insert it in all the journal.



then he return to the "Packhorse," and tell Mercy what he have do.



the next day he bid her farewell, and away for Carlisle. it be a two

day' journey. he reach Carlisle in the evening, and go all glow

to Mrs. Gaunt. "Madam," say he, "be of good cheer. I bless the day I

go to see she; she be an angel of wit and goodness." he then relate

to she, in glow term, most that have pass between Mercy and he.

but to his surprise, Mrs. Gaunt wear a cold, forbid air.



"this be all very well," say she. "but 'twill avail I little unless

_he_ come before the judge and clear I; and she will never let he

do that."



"ay, that she will--if she can find he."



"if she can find he? how simple you be."



"Nay, madam, not so simple but I can tell a good woman from a bad one,

and a true from a false."



"what! when you be in love with she? not if you be the wise of

your sex."



"in love with she?" cry Sir George; and color high.



"ay," say the lady. "think you I cannot tell? don't deceive yourself.

you have go and fall in love with she. at your year! not that 'tis

any business of mine."



"well, madam," say Sir George, stiffly, "say what you please on that

score; but, at least welcome my good news."



Mrs. Gaunt beg he to excuse her petulance, and thank he kindly

for all he have just do. but the next moment she rise from her chair

in great agitation, and burst out, "I'd as lieve die as owe anything to

that woman."



Sir George remonstrate. "why hate she? she do not hate you."



"oh yes she do. tis not in nature she should do any other."



"her act prove the contrary."



"her act! she have _do_ nothing, but make fair promise; and that have

blind you. woman of this sort be very cunning, and never show their

real character to a man. no more; prithee mention not her name to I.

it make I ill. I know he be with she at this moment. ah, let I die,

and be forget: since I be no more beloved."



the voice be sad and weary now, and the tear run fast.



Poor Sir George be move and melt, and set himself to flat and

console this impracticable lady, who hate her good friend in this sore

strait, for be what she be herself, a woman; and be much less

annoyed at be hang than at not be love.



when she be a little calm he leave she, and ride off to Houseman.

that worthy be delighted. "get she to swear to those hobnail shoe,"

say he, "and we shall shake they." he then let Sir George know that he

have obtain private information, which he would use in cross-examine

a principal witness for the Crown. "however," he add, "do not deceive

yourself: nothing can make the prisoner really safe but the appearance

of Griffith Gaunt; he have such strong motive for come to light;

he be heir to a fortune, and his wife be accuse of murder he.

the jury will never believe he be alive till they see he. that man's

prolong disappearance be hideous. it turn my blood cold when I think

of it."



"do not despair on that score," say Neville. "I believe our good angel

will produce he."



three day only before the assize, come the long-expect letter from

Mercy Vint. Sir George tear it open, but bitter be his disappointment.

the letter merely say that Griffith have not appear in answer to her

advertisement, and she be sore grieve and perplexed.



there be two postscript, each on a little piece of paper.





first postscript, in a tremulous hand, "pray."



second postscript, in a firm hand, "drain that water."





Houseman shrug his shoulder impatiently, "drain the mere? let the

Crown do that. we should but fish up more trouble. and prayer quo' she!

'tis not prayer we want, but evidence."



he send his clerk off to travel post night and day, and subpoena Mercy,

and bring she back with he to the trial. she be to have every comfort

on the road, and be treat like a duchess.



the evening before the assize, Mrs. Gaunt's apartment be Mr.

Houseman's head-quarter, and message be come and go all day,

on matter connect with the defense.



just at sunset, up rattle a post-chaise, and the clerk get out and

come haggard and bloodshot before his employer.



"the witness have disappear, sir. leave home last Tuesday, with she

child, and have never be see nor hear of since."



here be a terrible blow. they all pale under it; it seriously

diminish the chance of an acquittal.



but Mrs. Gaunt bear it nobly. she seem to rise under it.



she turn to Sir George Neville with a sweet smile. "the noble heart

see base thing noble. no wonder then an artful woman delude _you._

he have leave England with she; and condemn I to the gallow. in cold

blood. so be it. I shall defend myself."



she then sit down with Mr. Houseman, and go through the write case

he have prepare for she: and show he note she have take of full a

hundred criminal trial great and small.



while they be put their head together, Sir George sit in a brown

study, and utter not a word. presently he get up a little brusquely,

and say, "I'm go to Hernshaw."



"what, at this time of night? what to do?"



"to obey my order. to drain the mere."



"and who could have order you to drain my mere?"



"Mercy vint."



Sir George utter this in a very curious way, half ashamed half

resolute, and retire before Mrs. Gaunt could vent in speech the

surprise and indignation that fire her eye.



Houseman implore she not to heed Sir George and his vagary, but to

bend her whole mind on those approve mode of defense with which he

have supply she.



be now alone with she, he no long conceal his great anxiety.



"we have lose an invaluable witness in that woman," say he. "I be mad

to think she would come."



Mrs. Gaunt shiver with repugnance. "I would not have she come for

all the world," say she. "for Heaven's sake never mention her name to

I. I want help from none but friend. send Mrs. Houseman to I in the

morning; and do not distress yourself so. I shall defend myself far

well than you think. I have not study a hundred trial for nought."



thus the prisoner cheer up her attorney, and soon after insist on

his go home to bed, for she see he be wear out by his exertion.



and now she be alone.



all be silent.



a few short hour, and she be to be try for her life; try, not

by the all-wise Judge, but by fallible man, and under a system most

unfavorable to the accuse.



worse than all this, she be a Papist: and, as ill-luck would have it,

since her imprisonment an alarm have be raise that the Pretender

meditate another invasion. this report have set jury very much

against all the Romanists in the country, and have already pervert

justice in one or two case, especially in the North.



Mrs. Gaunt know all this, and tremble at the peril to come.



she spend the early part of the night in study her defense. then she

lay it quite aside and pray long and fervently.



towards morning she fall asleep from exhaustion.



when she awake, Mrs. Houseman be sit by her bedside, look at

she, and cry.



they be soon clasp in each other's arm, condole.



but presently Houseman come, and take his wife away rather angrily.



Mrs. Gaunt be prevail on to eat a little toast and drink a glass of

wine, and then she sit wait her dreadful summon.



she wait, and wait, until she become impatient to face her danger.



but there be two petty larceny on before she. she have to wait.



at last, about noon, come a message to say that the grand jury have

find a true bill against she.



"then may God forgive they!" say she.



soon afterwards she be inform her time draw very near.



she make her toilet carefully, and pass with her attendant into a

small room under the court.



here she have to endure another chilling wait, and in a sombre room.



presently she hear a voice above her cry out, "the King _versus_

Catherine Gaunt."



then she be beckon to.



she mount some step, badly light, and find herself in the glare

of day, and greedy eye, in the felon's dock.



in a matter entirely strange, we seldom know beforehand what we can do,

and how we shall carry ourselves. Mrs. Gaunt no soon set her foot in

that dock, and see tiny awful front of Justice face to face, than her

tremor abate, and all her power awake, and she thrill with love

of life, and bristle with all those fine art of defense that nature

lend to superior woman.



she enter on that defense before she speak a word; for she attack

the prejudice of the court by deportment.



she curtsy reverently to the Judge, and contrive to make she

reverence seem a wile homage, unmixe with fear.



she cast her eye round, and see the court throng with lady and

gentleman she know. in a moment she read in their face that only two

or three be on her side. she bow to those only; and they return

her courtesy. this give an impression (a false one) that the gentry

sympathize with she.



after a little murmur of functionary, the Clerk of Arraigns turn to

the prisoner, and say, in a loud voice, "Catherine Gaunt, hold up thy

hand."



she hold up her hand, and he recite the indictment, which charge

that, not have the fear of God before her eye, but be move by

the instigation of the Devil, she have on the fifteen of October, in

the tenth year of the reign of his present Majesty, aid and abet

one Thomas Leicester in an assault upon one Griffith Gaunt, Esq., and

he, the say Griffith Gaunt, do with force and arm assassinate and

do to death, against the peace of our say Lord the King, his crown and

dignity.



after read the indictment, the Clerk of Arraigns turn to the

prisoner, "how sayest thou, Catherine Gaunt, art thou guilty of the

felony and murder whereof thou stand indict--or not guilty?"



"I be not guilty."



"Culprit, how wilt thou be try?"



"Culprit I be none, but only accuse: I will be try by God and my

country."



"God send thee a good deliverance."



Mr. Whitworth, the junior counsel for the Crown, then rise to open the

case; but the prisoner, with a pale face, but most courteous demeanor,

beg his leave to make a previous motion to the court. Mr. Whitworth

bow, and sit down. "my Lord," say she, "I have first a favor to ask:

and that favor, methink, you will grant, since it be but justice,

impartial justice. my accuser, I hear, have too counsel; both learn

and able. I be but a woman, and no match for their skill; therefore, I

beg your Lordship to allow I counsel on my defense, to matter of fact

as well as of law. I know this be not usual; but it be just; and I be

inform it have sometimes be grant in trial of life and death, and

that your Lordship hath the _power_, if you have the _will_, to do I

so much justice."



the Judge look towards Mr. Sergeant Wiltshire, who be the leader on

the other side: he rise instantly and reply to this purpose: "the

prisoner be misinform. the truth be, that from time immemorial, and

down to the other day, a person indict for a capital offense be

never allow counsel at all, except to matter of law, and these must

be start by himself. by recent practice, the rule hath be so far

relaxed, that counsel have sometimes be permit to examine, and

cross-examine, witness for a prisoner; but never to make observation

on the evidence, nor to draw inference from it to the point in issue."



_Mrs. Gaunt._ so, then, if I be sue for a small sum of money, I may

have skilled orator to defend I against their like. but, if I be sue

for my life and honor, I may not oppose skill to skill; but must stand

here a child against you that be master. 'tis a monstrous iniquity,

and you yourself, sir, will not deny it.



_Sergeant Wiltshire._ Madam, permit I: whether it be a hardship to

deny full counsel to prisoner in criminal case, I shall not pretend

to say; but if it be, 'tis a hardship of the law's making, and not of

mine, nor of my lord's: and none have suffer by it (at least in our

day) but those who have break the law.



the Sergeant then stop a minute, and whisper with his junior.

after which he turn to the Judge. "my Lord, we, that be of counsel

for the Crown, desire to do nothing that be hard where a person's life

be at stake. we yield to the prisoner any indulgence for which your

Lordship can find a precedent in your reading; but no more: and so we

leave the matter to you."



_the Clerk of Arraigns._ Crier, proclaim silence.



_the Crier._ Oyez! Oyez! Oyez! his Majesty's Justices do straitly

charge all manner of person to keep silence on pain of imprisonment.



_the Judge._ Prisoner, what my brother Wiltshire say, the law be clear

in: there be no precedent for what you ask, and the contrary practice

stare we in the face for century. what seem to you a partial

practice, and, to be frank, some learn person be of your mind, must

be set against this, that in capital case the burden of proof lie on

the Crown and not on the accuse. also it be my duty to give you all

the assistance I can, and that I shall do. thus then it be: you can be

allow counsel to examine your own witness, and cross-examine the

witness for the Crown, and speak to point of law, to be start by

yourself,--but no far.



he then ask she what gentleman there present he should assign to she

for counsel.



her reply to this inquiry take the whole court by surprise, and make

her solicitor, Houseman, very miserable. "none, my Lord," say she.

"half justice be injustice; and I will lend it no color. I will not

set able man to fight for I with their hand tie, against man as

able whose hand be free. Counsel, on term so partial, I will have

none. my counsel shall be three, and no more. yourself, my Lord,--my

Innocence,--and the Lord God Omniscient."



these word, grandly utter, cause a dead silence in the court, but

only for a few moment. it be break by the loud mechanical voice of

the crier, who proclaim silence, and then call the name of the

jury that be to try this cause.



Mrs. Gaunt listen keenly to the name; familiar and bourgeois name,

that now seem regal, for they who own they hold her life in their

hand.



each juryman be swear in the grand old form, now slightly curtail.



"Joseph King, look upon the prisoner.--You shall well and truly try,

and true deliverance make, between our Sovereign Lord the King and the

prisoner at the bar, whom you shall have in charge, and a true verdict

give, accord to the evidence. so help you God."



Mr. Whitworth, for the Crown, then open the case, but do little more

than translate the indictment into more rational language.



he sit down, and Sergeant Wiltshire address the court somewhat after

this fashion:--



"may it please your Lordship, and you, gentleman of the jury, this be

a case of great expectation and importance. the prisoner at the bar,

a gentlewoman by birth and education, and, as you must have already

perceive, by breed also, stands indict for no less a crime than

murder.



"I need not paint to you the heinousness of this crime: you have but to

consult your own breast. who ever see the ghastly corpse of the victim

weltering in its blood, and do not feel his own blood run cold through

his vein? have the murderer flee? with what eagerness do we pursue!

with what zeal apprehend! with what joy do we bring he to justice!

even the dreadful sentence of death do not shock we, when pronounce

upon he; we hear it with solemn satisfaction; and acknowledge the

justice of the divine sentence, 'whoso sheddeth man's blood, by man

shall his blood be shed.'



"but if this be the case in every common murder, what shall be think

of she who have murder her husband? the man in whose arm she have

lain and whom she have swear at God's altar to love and cherish. such

a murderer be a robber as well as an assassin; for she rob her own

child of their father, that tender parent, who can never be replace

in this world.



"gentleman, it will, I fear, be prove that the prisoner at the bar

hath be guilty of murder in this high degree: and, though I will

endeavor rather to extenuate than to aggravate, yet I trust (sic) I

have such a history to open as will shock the ear of all who hear I.



"Mr. Griffith Gaunt, the unfortunate deceased, be a man of descent

and worship. as to his character, it be inoffensive; he be know

as a worthy kindly gentleman; deeply attach to she who now stand

accuse of his murder. they live happily together for some year; but,

unfortunately, there be a thorn in the rose of their wed life; he

be of the Church of England; she be, and be, a roman Catholic. this

lead to dispute: and no wonder; since the same unhappy difference hath

more than once embroil a nation, let alone a single family.



"well, gentleman, about a year ago there be a more violent quarrel

than usual between the deceased and the prisoner at the bar: and the

deceased leave his home for several month.



"he return upon a certain day in this year, and a reconciliation,

real or apparent, take place. he leave home again soon afterwards,

but only for a short period. on the 15th of last October he suddenly

return for good, as he intend: and here begin the tragedy, to

which what I have hitherto relate be but the prologue.



"Scarce an hour before he come, one Thomas Leicester enter the house.

now this Thomas Leicester be a creature of the prisoner's. he have be

she gamekeeper; and be now a pedlar. it be the prisoner who set he

up as a pedlar, and purchase the ware to start he in his trade.



"gentleman, this pedlar, as I shall prove, be conceal in the house

when the deceased arrive. one Caroline Ryder, who be the prisoner's

gentlewoman, be the person who first inform she of Leicester's

arrival, and it seem she be much move; Mrs. Ryder will tell you

she fall into hysteric. but, soon after, her husband's arrival be

announce, and then the passion be of a very different kind. so

violent be her rage against this unhappy man that, for once, she

forget all prudence, and threaten his life before a witness. yes,

gentleman, we shall prove that this gentlewoman, who in appearance

and manner might grace a court, be so transport out of her usual

self that she hold up a knife--a knife, gentleman, and vow to put it

into her husband's heart. and this be no mere temporary ebullition of

wrath; we shall see presently that, long after she have time to cool,

she repeat this menace to the unfortunate man's face. the first

threat, however be utter in her own bedroom, before her confidential

servant, Caroline Ryder aforesaid. but now the scene shift. she have,

to all appearance, recover herself, and sit smile at the head of

her table; for, you must know, she entertain company that night,

person of the high standing in the county.



"presently her husband, all unconscious of the terrible sentiment she

entertain towards he, and the fearful purpose she have announce,

enter the room, make obeisance to his guest, and go to take his

wife's hand.



"what do she? she draw back with so strange a look and such

forbid word, that the company be disconcert. consternation

fall on all present; and, ere long, they make their excuse, and leave

the house. thus the prisoner be leave alone with her husband. but,

meantime, curiosity have be excite by her strange conduct, and some

of the servant, with foreboding heart, listen at the door of

the dining-room. what do they hear, gentleman? a furious quarrel,

in which, however, the deceased be comparatively passive, and the

prisoner again threaten his life, with vehemence. her passion, it be

clear, have not cool.



"now it may fairly be allege, on behalf of the prisoner, that, the

witness for the Crown be on one side of the door, the prisoner

and the deceased on the other; and that such evidence should be

receive with caution. I grant this--where it be not sustain by other

circumstance, or by direct proof. let we then give the prisoner the

benefit of this doubt, and let we inquire how the deceased himself

understand she; he who not only hear the word, and the accent, but

see the look, whatever they be, that accompany they.



"gentleman, he be a man of know courage and resolution; yet he be

find after this terrible interview, much cow and deject. he speak

to Mrs. Ryder of his death as an event not far distant, and so go to

his bedroom in a melancholy and foreboding state: and where be that

bedroom? he be thrust by his wife's order into a small chamber, and

not allow to enter her: he, the master of the house, her husband,

and her lord.



"but his interpretation of the prisoner's word do not end there. he

leave we a further comment by his action next ensue. he dare not (I

beg pardon, this be my inference; receive it as such), he _do_ not,

remain in that house a single night. he bolt his chamber-door inside;

and in the very dead of night, notwithstanding the fatigue of the

day's journey (for he have ride some distance), he let himself out by

the window, and reach the ground safely, though it be a height of

fourteen foot; a leap, gentleman, that few of we would venture to take.

but what will not man risk when destruction be at their heel? he do

not wait even to saddle his horse; but flee on foot. unhappy man, he

flee from danger, and meet his death.



"from the hour when he go up to bed none of the inmate of the house

ever see Griffith Gaunt alive; but one Thomas Hayes, a laborer, see he

walk in a certain direction at one o'clock that morning; and behind

he, gentleman, there walk another man.



"who be that other man?



"when I have tell you (and this be an essential feature of the case)

how the prisoner be employ during the time that her husband lie

quake in his little room, wait an opportunity to escape,--when

I tell you this, I fear you will divine who it be that follow the

deceased, and for what purpose.



"Gentlemen, when the prisoner have threaten her husband in person, as

I have describe, she retire to her own room, but not to sleep. she

order she maid, Mrs. Ryder, to bring Thomas Leicester to her chamber.

yes, gentleman, she receive this pedlar at midnight in her bed-chamber.



"now, an act so strange as this admit, I think, of but two

interpretation. either she have a guilty amour with this fellow, or

she have some extraordinary need of his service. her whole character,

by consent of the witness, render it very improbable that she would

descend to a low amour. moreover she act too publicly in the matter.

the man, as we know, be her tool, her creature: she have buy his

ware for he, and set he up as a pedlar. she openly summon he to

her presence, and keep he there about half-an-hour.



"he go from she, and very soon after be see, by Thomas Hayes,

follow Griffith Gaunt--at one o'clock in the morning--that Griffith

Gaunt, who, after that hour, be never see alive.



"gentleman, up to this point the evidence be clear, connect, and

cogent; but it rarely happen in case of murder that any human eye

see the very blow strike. the penalty be too severe for such an act to

be do in the presence of an eye-witness: and not one murderer in ton

could be convict without the help of circumstantial evidence.



"the next link, however, be take up by an ear-witness, and, in some

case, the ear be even well than the eye; for instance, as to the

discharge of firearm: for, by the eye alone, we could not positively

tell whether a pistol have go off or have but flash in the pan.

well, then, gentleman, a few minute after Mr. Gaunt be last see

alive, which be by Thomas Hayes, Mrs. Ryder, who have retire to her

bedroom, hear the say Gaunt distinctly cry for help: she also hear

a pistol-shot discharge. this take place by the side of a lake or

large pond near the house, call 'the mere.' Mrs. Ryder alarm the

house, and she and the other servant proceed to her master's room:

they find it bolt from the inside. they break it open. Mr. Gaunt have

escape by the window, as I have already tell you.



"presently in come the prisoner from out of door. this be at one

o'clock in the morning. how she appear to have see at once that she

must explain she be abroad at that time, so she tell Mrs. Ryder that

she have be out--pray."



(here some people laugh harshly; but be threaten severely, and

silence).



"be that credible? do people go out of door at one o'clock in the

morning, to pray? Nay; but I fear it be to do an act, that year of

prayer and penitence cannot efface.



"from that moment Mr. Gaunt be see no more among live man. and what

make his disappearance the more mysterious be that he have actually

at this time just inherit largely from his namesake Mr. Gaunt of

Coggleswade; and his own interest, and that of the other legatee,

require his immediate presence. Mr. Atkins, the testator's solicitor,

advertise for this unfortunate gentleman; but he do not appear to

claim his fortune. then plain man begin to put this and that together,

and cry out 'foul play!'



"Justice be set in motion at last: but embarrass by the circumstance

that the body of the deceased could not be find.



"at last, Mr. Atkins, the solicitor, be unable to get the estate

I have mention administer, for want of proof of Griffith Gaunt's

decease, enter heartily into this affair, on mere civil ground. he

ask the prisoner, before several witness, if she would permit he

to drag that piece of water by the side of which Mr. Gaunt be hear to

cry for help, and, after that, see no more.



"the prisoner do not reply; but Mr. Houseman, her solicitor, a very

worthy man, who have I believe, or have, up to that moment, a sincere

conviction of her innocence, answer for she, and tell Mr. Atkins he

be welcome to drag or drain it. then the prisoner say nothing. she

faint away.



"after this, you may imagine with what expectation the water be

drag. gentleman, after hour of fruitless labour, a body be find.



"but here an unforeseen circumstance befriend the prisoner. it seem

that piece of water swarm with enormous pike and other ravenous fish.

these have so horribly mutilate the deceased, that neither form nor

feature remain to swear by: and, as the law wisely and humanely

demand that in these ease a body shall be identify beyond doubt,

justice bade fair to be baffle again. but lo! as often happen in case

of murder, Providence interpose and point with unerre finger to a

slight but infallible mark. the deceased gentleman be know to have a

large mole over his left temple. it have be notice by his servant,

and his neighbor. well, gentleman, the greedy fish have spare this

mole; spare it perhaps by his command who bid the whale swallow

Jonah, yet not destroy he. there it be, clear and infallible. it be

examine by several witness; it be recognize; it complete that

chain of evidence, some of it direct, some of it circumstantial, which

I have lay before you very briefly, and every part of which I shall

now support by credible witness."



he call thirteen witness, include Mr. Atkins, Thomas Hayes, Jane

Bannister, Caroline Ryder, and other, and their evidence in chief bore

out every positive statement the counsel have make.



in cross-examine these witness Mrs. Gaunt take a line that

agreeably surprise the court. it be not for nothing she have study

a hundred trial with a woman's observation and patient docility. she

have find out how badly people plead their own cause, and have notice

the reason; one of which be that they say too much, and stray from the

point. the line she take, with one exception, be keen brevity.



she cross-examined Thomas Hayes as follow:









CHAPTER XI





"you say the pedlar be a hundred yard behind my husband. which of the

two man be walk fast?"



Thomas Hayes consider a moment. "well, I think the squire be walk

rather the smart of the two."



"do the pedlar seem likely to overtake he?"



"Nay. Ye see, Dame, Squire he walk straight on; but the pedlar he

take both side of the road at oust, as the saying be."



_the Prisoner._ forgive I, Thomas, but I don't know what you mean.



_haye_ (compassionately). how should ye? you be never the bad for

liquor, the like of you.



_the Prisoner_ (very keenly). oh, he be in liquor, be he?



_Hayes._ Come, Dame, you do brew good ale at Hernshaw Castle. ye

needn't go to deny that; for, Lord know, 'tis no sin; and a poor

fellow may be jolly; yet not, to say, drunk.
