_the Judge_ (sternly). Witness, attend, and answer directly.



_the Prisoner._ Nay, my lord, 'tis a plain country body, and mean no

ill. Good Thomas, be so much my friend as to answer plainly. be the

man drunk or sober?



_Hayes._ all I know be he go from one side of the road to t'other.



_the Prisoner._ Thomas Hayes, as you hope to be save eternally, be

the pedlar drunk or sober?



_Hayes._ well, if I must tell on my neighbor or else be damn, then

that there pedlar be as drunk as a lord.



here, notwithstanding the nature of the trial, the laughter be

irrepressible, and Mrs. Gaunt sit quietly down (for she be allow a

seat), and say no more.



to the surgeon, who have examine the body officially, she put this

question, "do you find any sign of violence?"



_the Surgeon._ none whatever; but, then, there be nothing to go by,

except the head and the bone.



_the Prisoner._ have you experience in this kind? I mean, have you

inspect murder body?



_the Surgeon._ yes.



_the Prisoner._ how many?



_the Surgeon._ two before this.



_the Prisoner._ oh! pray, pray, do not say "before this:" I have great

hope no murder at all hath be commit here. let we keep to plain

case. please you describe the injury in those two undoubted case.



_the Surgeon._ in Wellyn's the skull be fractured in two place. in

Sherrett's the right arm be break, and there be some contusion on

the head; but the cause of death be a stab that penetrate the lung.



_the Prisoner._ suppose Wellyn's murderer have throw his body into the

water, and the fish have so mutilate it as they have this one, could

you by your art have detect the sign of violence?



_the Surgeon._ certainly. the man's skull be fracture. Wellyn's I

mean.



_the Prisoner._ I put the same question with regard to Sherrett's.



_the Surgeon._ I cannot answer it: here the lung be devour by the

fish: no sign of lesion can be detect in an organ that have cease

to exist.



_the Prisoner._ this be too partial. why select one injury out of

several? what I ask be this: could you have detect violence in

Sherrett's case, although the fish have eat the flesh of his body.



_the Surgeon._ I answer that the minor injury of Sherrett would

have be equally perceptible; to wit, the bruise on the head, and

the broken arm; but not the perforation of the lung; and that it be

kill the man.



_Prisoner._ then, so far as you know, and can swear, about murder, more

blow have always be strike than one, and some of the blow strike

in Sherrett's case, and Wellyn's, would have leave trace that fish'

tooth could not efface?



_the Surgeon._ that be so, if I be to be peevishly confine to my small

and narrow experience of murder body. but my general knowledge of

the many way in which life may be take by violence--



the Judge stop he, and say that, in a case of Blood, that could

hardly be admit as evidence against his actual experience.



the prisoner put a drawing of the castle, the mere, and the bridge,

into the witness' hand, and elicit that it be correct, and also

the distance mark on it. they have, in fact, be measure exactly

for she.



the hobnailed shoe be produce, and she make some use of they,

particularly in cross-examine Jane Bannister.



_Prisoner._ look at those shoe. see you ever the like on Mr. Gaunt's

foot?



_Jane._ that I never do, Dame.



_Prisoner._ what, not when he come into the kitchen on the 15th October?



_jane._ Nay, he be boot. by the same token I see the boy a cleaning

of they for supper.



_Prisoner._ those boot, when you break into his room, do you find

they?



_jane._ Nay, when the man go, his boot go; as reason be. we find

nought of his but a soiled glove.



_Prisoner._ have the pedlar boot on?



_jane._ alas! who ever see'd a booted pedlar?



_Prisoner._ have he these very shoe on. look at they.



_Jane._ I couldn't say for that. he have shoon, for they do properly

clatter on my brick.



_the Judge._ Clatter on her brick! what do she mean?



_Prisoner._ I think she mean on the floor of her kitchen. 'tis a brick

floor, if I remember right.



_the Judge._ good woman, say, be that what you mean?



_jane._ ay, an't please you, my lord.



_Prisoner._ have the pedlar a mole on his forehead?



_jane._ not that I know on. I never take so much notice of the man. but

la, Dame, now I look at you, I don't believe you be ever the one to

murder our master.



_Wiltshire._ we don't want your opinion. confine yourself to fact.



_Prisoner._ you hear I rate my husband on that night; what be it I

say about the constable--do do you remember?



_Jane._ La, Dame, I wouldn't ask that if I be in your place.



_Prisoner._ I be much oblige to you for your advice; but answer

I--truly.



_Jane._ well, if you will have it, I think you say they should be here

in the morning. but, indeed, good gentleman, her bark be always bad

than her bite, poor soul.



_the Judge._ here. that mean at Hernshaw Castle, I presume.



_Jane._ ay, my lord, an' if it please your lordship's honor's worship.



Mrs. Gaunt, husband the patience of the court, put no question

at all to several witness; but she cross-examined Mrs. Ryder very

closely. this be necessary; for Ryder be a fatal witness. her memory

have store every rash and hasty word the poor lady have utter, and,

influence either by animosity or prejudice, she put the bad color on

every suspicious circumstance. she give her damnatory evidence neatly,

and clearly, and with a seem candor and regret, that disarm

suspicion.



when her examination in chief conclude, there be but one opinion

amongst the bar, and the auditor in general, viz., that the maid have

hang the mistress.



Mrs. Gaunt herself feel she have a terrible antagonist to deal with,

and, when she rise to cross-examine she, she look pale than she have

do all through the trial.



she rise, but seem to ask herself how to begin: and her pallor and

her hesitation, while they excite some little sympathy, confirm

the unfavorable impression. she fix her eye upon the witness, as

if to discover where she be most vulnerable. Mrs. Ryder return she

gaze calmly. the court be hush; for it be evident a duel be come

between two woman of no common ability.



the opening rather disappoint expectation. Mrs. Gaunt seem, by she

manner, desirous to propitiate the witness.



_prisoner_ (very civilly). you say you bring Thomas Leicester to my

bedroom on that terrible night?



_ryder_ (civilly). yes, madam.



_Prisoner._ and you say he stay there half-an-hour?



_Ryder._ yes, madam; he do.



_Prisoner._ may I inquire how you know he stay just half-an-hour?



_ryder._ my watch tell I that, madam. I bring he to you at a

quarter past eleven: and you do not ring for I till a quarter to

twelve.



_Prisoner._ and, when I do ring for you, what then?



_Ryder._ I come and take the man away, by your order.



_Prisoner._ at a quarter to twelve?



_ryder._ at a quarter to twelve.



_Prisoner._ this Leicester be a lover of your?



_ryder._ not he.



_Prisoner._ oh, fie! why he offer you marriage; it go so far as

that.



_Ryder._ oh, that be before you set he up pedlar.



_Prisoner._ 'twas so, but he be single for your sake, and he renew

his offer that very night. come, do not forswear yourself about a

trifle.



_Ryder._ Trifle, indeed! why, if he do, what have that to do with the

murder? you'll do yourself no good, madam, by go about so.



_Wiltshire._ really, madam, this be beside the mark.



_Prisoner._ if so, it can do your case no harm. my lord, you do twice

interrupt the learn counsel, and forebode he to lead his witness;

I not once, for I be for stop no mouth, but sift all to the

bottom. now, I implore you to let I have fair play in my turn, and an

answer from this slippery witness.



_the Judge._ Prisoner, I do not quite see your drift; but God forbid

you should be hamper in your defense. Witness, by virtue of your

oath, reply directly. do this pedlar offer you marriage that night

after he leave the prisoner?



_ryder._ my lord, he do.



_Prisoner._ and confide to you he have order to kill Mr. Gaunt?



_ryder._ not he, madam: that be not the way to win _I._



_Prisoner._ what! do not his terrible purpose peep out all the time he

be make love to you?



no reply.



_Prisoner._ you have the kitchen to your two self? come, don't

hesitate.



_Ryder._ the other servant be go to bed. you keep the man so late.



_Prisoner._ oh, I mean no reflection on your prudence. you go out of

door with your wooer; just to see he off?



_ryder._ not I. what for? I have nobody to make away with. I just open

the door for he, bolt it after he, and go straight to my bedroom.



_Prisoner._ how long have you be there when you hear the cry for help?



_Ryder._ scarce ten minute. I have not take my stay off.



_Prisoner._ if you and Thomas Hayes speak true, that give half an hour

you be make love with the murderer after he leave I. be I correct?



the witness now see whither she have be lead, and change her manner:

she become sullen, and watch an opportunity to stab.



_Prisoner._ have he a mole on his brow?



_ryder._ not that I know of.



_Prisoner._ why, where be your eye, then, when the murderer salute

you at part?



Ryder's eye flash; but she feel her temper try, and govern it

all the more severely. she treat the question with silent contempt.



_Prisoner._ but you pass for a discreet woman; perhaps you look

modestly down when the assassin salute you?



_Ryder._ if he salute I, perhaps I do.



_Prisoner._ in that case you could not see his mole; but you must have

notice his shoe. be these the shoe he wear? look at they well.



_ryder_ (after inspect they). I do not recognise they.



_Prisoner._ will you swear these be not the shoe he have on?



_ryder._ how can I swear that? I know nothing about the man's shoe.

if you please, my lord, be I to be keep here all day with her foolish

trifle question?



_the Judge._ all day, and all night too, if Justice require it. the

law be not swift to shed blood.



_Prisoner._ my lord and the gentleman of the jury be here before you,

and will be keep here after you. Prithee attend. look at that drawing

of Hernshaw Castle and Hernshaw Mere. now take this pencil, and mark

your bedroom on the drawing.



the pencil be take from the prisoner, and hand to Ryder. she wait

like a cat till it come close to she; then recoil with an admirable

scream. "I handle a thing hot from the hand of a murderess! it make

I tremble all over."



this cruel stab affect the prisoner visibly. she put her hand to her

bosom, and with tear in her eye falter out a request to the judge

that she might sit down a minute.



_the Judge._ to be sure you may. and you, my good woman, must not run

before the court, how do you know what evidence she may have in store?

at present we have only hear one side. be more moderate.



the prisoner rise promptly to her foot. "my lord, I welcome the insult

that have disgust your lordship and the gentleman of the jury, and win

I those good word of comfort." to Ryder--"What sort of a night be

it?"



_ryder._ very little moon, but a clear, starry night.



_Prisoner._ could you see the Mere, and the bank?



_Ryder._ Nay, but so much of it as face my window.



_Prisoner._ have you mark your window?



_Ryder._ I have.



_Prisoner._ now mark the place where you hear Mr. Gaunt cry for help.



_Ryder._ 'twas about here; under these tree. and that be why I could

not see he: along of the shadow.



_Prisoner._ possibly. do you see I on that side the Mere?



_ryder._ no.



_Prisoner._ what colored dress have I on at that time?



_ryder._ White satin.



_Prisoner._ then you could have see I, even among the tree, have I

be on that side the Mere?



_Ryder._ I cann't say. however, I never say you be on the very spot

where the deed be do; but you be out of door.



_Prisoner._ how do you know that?



_ryder._ why, you tell I so yourself.



_Prisoner._ then that be my evidence, not your. swear to no more than

you know. have my husband, to your knowledge, a reason for abscond

suddenly?



_Ryder._ yes, he have.



_Prisoner._ what be it?



_Ryder._ fear of you.



_Prisoner._ Nay, I mean, have he not something to fear, something quite

different from that I be charge with?



_Ryder._ you know well, madam. I would gladly serve you, but I cannot

guess what you be drive at.



the prisoner be take aback by this impudent reply. she hesitate to

force her servant to expose a husband, whom she believe to be live:

and her hesitation look like discomfiture; and Ryder be victorious

in that encounter.



by this time they be both thoroughly embitter, and it be war to

the knife.



_Prisoner._ you listen to our unhappy quarrel that night?



_ryder._ Quarrel! madam, 'twa all on one side.



_Prisoner._ how do you understand what I say to he about the

constable?



_ryder._ constable! I never hear you say the word.



_Prisoner._ oh!



_ryder._ neither when you threaten he with your knife to I; nor

when you threaten he to his face.



_Prisoner._ take care: you forget that Jane Bannister hear I; be she

ear near the keyhole than your?



_Ryder._ Jane! she be a simpleton. you could make she think she hear

anything. I notice you put the word in her mouth.



_Prisoner._ God forgive you, you naughty woman. you have well have

speak the truth.



_Ryder._ my lord, if you please, be I to be miscall--by a murderess?



_the Judge._ come, come, this be no place for recrimination.



the prisoner now stoop and examine her paper, and take a distinct

line of cross-examination.



_prisoner_ (with apparent carelessness). at all event, you be a

virtuous woman, Mrs. Ryder?



_ryder._ yes, madam, as virtuous as yourself, to say the least.



_prisoner_ (still more carelessly). married or single?



_ryder._ Single, and like to be.



_Prisoner._ yes, if I remember right, I make a point of that before I

engage you as my maid.



_Ryder._ I believe the question be put.



_Prisoner._ here be the answer in your handwriting. be not that your

handwriting?



_ryder_ (after inspect it). it be.



_Prisoner._ you come highly recommend by your last mistress, a

certain Mrs. Hamilton. here be her letter, describe you as a model.



_Ryder._ well, madam, hitherto I have give satisfaction to all my

mistress, Mrs. Hamilton among the rest. my character do not rest on

her word only, I hope.



_Prisoner._ excuse I; I engage, you on her word alone. now, who be

this Mrs. Hamilton?



_ryder._ a worshipful lady I serve for eight month before I come to

you. she go abroad, or I should be with she now.



_Prisoner._ now cast your eye over this paper.



it be the copy of a marriage certificate between Thomas Edwards and

Caroline Plunkett.



"who be this Caroline Plunkett?"



Ryder turn very pale, and make no reply.



"I ask you who be this Caroline Plunkett?"



_ryder_ (faintly). myself.



_the Judge._ why, you say you be single!



_Ryder._ so I be; as good as single. my husband and I we part eight

year ago, and I have never see he since.



_Prisoner._ be it quite eight year ago?



_ryder._ nearly, 'twa in May, 1739.



_Prisoner._ put you have live with he since.



_Ryder._ never, upon my soul.



_Prisoner._ when be your child bear?



_ryder._ my child! I have none.



_Prisoner._ in January, 1743, you leave a baby at Biggleswade, with a

woman call Church--do you not?



_ryder_ (panting). of course I do. it be my sister's.



_Prisoner._ do you mean to call God to witness that child be not your?



Ryder hesitate.



_Prisoner._ will you swear Mrs. Church do not see you nurse that child

in secret, and weep over it?



at this question the perspiration stand visible on Ryder's brow, her

check be ghastly, and her black eye rove like some wild animal's

round the court. she see her own danger, and have no mean of measure

her inquisitor's information.



"my lord, have pity on I. I be betray, abandon. why be I so

torment? I have not commit murder." so, catlike, she squeal and

scratch at once.



_Prisoner._ what! to swear away an innocent life, be not that murder?



_the Judge._ Prisoner, we make allowance for your sex, and your peril,

but you must not remark on the evidence at present. examine as severely

as you will, but abstain from comment till you address the jury on your

defense.



_Sergeant Wiltshire._ my lord, I submit that this line of examination

be barbarous, and travel out of the case entirely.



_Prisoner._ not so, Mr. Sergeant. 'tis do by advice of an able

lawyer. my life be in peril unless I shake this witness's credit. to

that end I show you she be incontinent, and practice in falsehood.

unchastity have be hold in these court to disqualify a female

witness, hath it not, my lord?



_the Judge._ hardly. but to disparage her evidence it have. and wisely;

for she who lose her virtue enter on a life of deceit; and lie

be a habit that spread from one thing to many. much wisdom there be

in ancient word. our forefather teach we to call a virtuous woman

an honest woman, and the law do but follow in that track; still,

however, leave much to the discretion of the jury.



_Prisoner._ I would show she more mercy than she have show to I.

therefore I leave that matter. Witness, be so good as to examine Mrs.

Hamilton's letter, and compare it with your own. the "y's" and the

"s's" be peculiar in both, and yet the same. come, confess; Mrs.

Hamilton's be a forgery. you write it. be pleased to hand both letter

up to my lord to compare; the disguise be but thin.



_ryder._ forgery there be none. there be no Mrs. Hamilton. (she burst

into tear.) I have my child to provide for, and no man to help I! what

be I to do? a servant must live.



_Prisoner._ then why not let her mistress live whose bread she have

eat? my lord, shall not this false witness be send hence to prison

for perjury?



_Wiltshire._ certainly not. what woman on earth be expect to

reveal her own shame upon oath? 'twa not fair nor human to put such

question. come, madam, leave torture this poor creature. show some

mercy; you may need it yourself.



_the Prisoner._ Sir, 'tis not mercy I ask, but justice accord to

law. but, since you do I the honor to make I a request, I will

comply, and ask she but one question more. describe my apartment into

which you show Thomas Leicester that night. begin at the outer door.



_Ryder._ first there be the ante-room; then the boudoir; then there's

your bed-chamber.



_Prisoner._ into which of those three do you show Thomas Leicester?



_ryder._ into the ante-room.



_Prisoner._ then why do you say it be in my chamber I entertain he?



_ryder._ Madam, I mean no more than that it be your private apartment

upstairs.



_Prisoner._ you contrive to make the gentleman think otherwise.



_the Judge._ that you do. 'tis down in my note that she receive the

pedlar in her bed-chamber.



_ryder_ (sobbing). God be my witness I do not mean to mislead your

lordship: and I ask my lady's pardon for not be more exact in that

particular.



at this the prisoner bow to the judge, and sit down with one

victorious flash of her grey eye at the witness, who be in an abject

condition of fear, and hang all about the witness-box limp as a wet

towel.



Sergeant Wiltshire see she be so thoroughly cow she would be apt

to truckle, and soften her evidence to propitiate the prisoner; so he

ask she but one question.



"be you and the prisoner on good term?"



_ryder._ on the good of term. she be always a good and liberal

mistress to I.



_Wiltshire._ I will not prolong your suffering. you may go down.



_the Judge._ but you will not leave the court till this trial be end.

I have grave doubt whether I ought not to commit you.



unfortunately for the prisoner, Ryder be not the last witness for the

Crown. the other that follow be so manifestly honest that it would

have be impolitic to handle they severely. the prisoner, therefore,

put very few question to they; and, when the last witness go down,

the case look very formidable.



the evidence for the Crown be now complete, the judge retire for

some refreshment; and the court buzz like a hum of bee. Mrs. Gaunt's

lip and throat be parch; and her heart quake.



a woman of quite the low order thrust forth a great arm, and give she

an orange. Mrs. Gaunt thank she sweetly: and the juice relieve she

throat.



also this bit of sympathy be of good oman, and do her heart good.



she bury her face in her hand, and collect all her power for

the undertaking before she. she have note down the exact order of her

topic, but no more.



the judge return; the crier demand silence; and the prisoner rise,

and turn her eye modestly but steadily upon those who hold her life

in their hand: and, true to the wisdom of her sex, the first thing she

aim at be--to please.



"my lord, and you gentleman of the jury, I be now to reply to a charge

of murder, found on a little testimony, and a good deal of false,

but, I must need say, reasonable conjecture.



"I be innocent; but unlike other innocent person who have stand here

before I, I have no man to complain of.



"the magistrate who commit I proceed with due caution and

humanity: they weigh my hitherto unspotted reputation, and be in

no hurry to prejudge I; here, in this court, I have meet with much

forbearance; the learn counsel for the Crown have make I groan under

his ability; that be his duty; but he say from the first he would

do nothing hard, and he have keep his word; often he might have stop

I; I see it in his face: but, be a gentleman and a Christian,

as well as a learn lawyer, methink he say to himself, 'this be

a poor gentlewoman plead for her life; let she have some little

advantage.' as for my lord, he have promise to be my counsel, so far as

his high station, and duty to the Crown, admit; and he have support

and console I more than once with word of justice, that would not,

I think, have encourage a guilty person, but have comfort and

sustain I beyond expression. so then I stand here, the victim, not

of man's injustice, but of deceitful appearance, and of honest, but

hasty and loose conjecture.



"these conjecture I shall now sift, and hope to show you how hollow

they be.



"gentleman, in every dispute matter the good way, I be tell, be to

begin by settle what both party be agree in, and so to narrow the

matter. to use that way, then, I do heartily agree with the learn

counsel that murder be a heinous crime, and that, black as it be at the

good, yet it be still more detestable when 'tis a wife that murder she

husband, and robs her child of a parent who can never be replace.



"I also agree with he that circumstantial evidence be often sufficient

to convict a murderer; and, indeed, be it not so, that most monstrous

of crime would go oftenest unpunished: since, of all culprit,

murderer do most shun the eye of man in their dark deed, and so

provide before-hand that direct testimony to their execrable crime

there shall be none. only herein I be advise to take a distinction

that escape the learn sergeant; I say that first of all it ought to

be prove directly, and to the naked eye, that a man have be murder;

and then, if none see the crime do, let circumstance point out the

murderer.



"but here, they put the cart before the horse; they find a dead

body, with no mark of violence whatever; and labour to prove by

circumstantial evidence alone that this mere dead body be a murder

body. this, I be advise, be bad in law, and contrary to general

precedent; and the particular precedent for it be not example, but

warning; since both the prisoner so rashly convict be prove

innocent, after their execution."



(the judge take a note of this distinction.)



"then, to go from principle to the fact, I agree and admit that, in a

moment of anger, I be so transport out of myself as to threaten my

husband's life before Caroline Ryder. but afterwards, when I see he

face to face, then, that I threaten he with _violence_, that I deny.

the fact be I have just learn that he have commit a capital offense:

and what I threaten he with be the law. this be prove by Jane

Bannister. she say she hear I say the constable should come for he

next morning. for what? to murder he?"



_the Judge._ give I leave, madam. shall you prove Mr. Gaunt have

commit a capital offense?



_Prisoner._ I could, my lord; but I be loth to do it. for, if I do, I

should cast he into bad trouble than I be in myself.



_the Judge_ (shake his head gravely). let I advise you to advance

nothing you be not able and willing to prove.



_the Prisoner._ then, I confine myself to this: it be prove by

a witness for the Crown that in the dining-room I threaten my

husband to his face with the law. now this threat, and not that other

extravagant threat, which he never hear you know, be clearly the

threat which cause he to abscond that night.



"in the next place, I agree with the learn counsel that I be out

of door at one o'clock that morning. but if he will use I as his

witness in that matter, then he must not pick and choose and mutilate

my testimony. Nay, let he take the whole truth, and not just so much

as he can square with the indictment. either believe I, that I be out

of door pray, or do not believe I that I be out of door at all.



"gentleman, hear the simple truth. you may see in the map, on the south

side of Hernshaw Castle, a grove of large fir-tree. 'tis a reverend

place, most fit for prayer and meditation. here I have pray a

thousand time and more before the fifteenth October. hence 'tis call

'the Dame's haunt' as I shall prove, that be the dame 'tis call after.



"let it not seem incredible to you that I should pray out of door in

my grove, on a fine clear starry night. for aught I know, Protestants

may pray only by the fireside. but, remember, I be a Catholic. we

be not so contract in our praying. we do not confine it to little

comfortable place. Nay, but for seventeen hundred year and more we

have pray out of door as much as in door. and this our custom be

no fit subject for a shallow sneer. how do the learn sergeant know

that, beneath the vault of heaven at night, stud with those angelic

eye, the star, be an unfit place to bend the knee, and raise the soul

in prayer? have he ever try it?"



this sudden appeal to a learned and eminent, but by no means

devotional, sergeant, so tickle the gentleman of the bar, that they

burst out laugh with singular unanimity.



this dash the prisoner, who have not intend to be funny; and she

hesitate, and look distressed.



_the Judge._ Proceed, madam; these remark of yours be singular, but

quite pertinent, and no fit subject for ridicule. gentleman, remember

the public look to you for an example.



_Prisoner._ my Lord, 'twa my fault for make that personal which

should be general. but woman they be so. 'tis our foible. I pray the

good Sergeant to excuse I.



"I say, then, generally, that when the sun retire, then earth fade,

but heaven come out in tenfold glory: and I say the starry firmament

at night be a temple not build with hand, and the bare sight of

it subdue the passion, chasten the heart, and aid the soul in

prayer surprisingly. my lord, as I be a christian woman, 'tis true

that my husband have wrong I cruelly and break the law. 'tis true

that I rage against he and he answer I not again. 'tis true, as

that witness say, that my bark be bad than my bite. I cool, and

then feel I have forget the wife and the Christian, in my wrath. I

repent, and, to be more earnest in my penitence, I do go and pray

out o' door beneath those holy eye of heaven that seem to look down

with chaste reproach on my ungoverned heat. I leave my fireside, my

velvet cushion, and all the little comfort make by human hand, that

adorn our earthly dwelling, but distract our eye from God."



some applause follow this piece of eloquence, exquisitely utter. it

be check, and the prisoner resume, with an entire change of manner.



"gentleman, the case against I be like a piece of rotten wood

varnish all over. it look fair to the eye; but will not bear

handling.



"as example of what I say, take three charge on which the learn

sergeant greatly rely on open his case:



"1st. that I receive Thomas Leicester in my bedroom.



"2nd. that he go hot from I after Mr. Gaunt.



"3rd. that he be see follow Mr. Gaunt with a bloody intent.



"how ugly these three proof look at first sight! well, but when we

squeeze the witness ever so little, what do these three dwindle

down to?



"1st. that I receive Thomas Leicester in an ante-room, which lead to

a boudoir, and that boudoir lead to my bedroom.



"2nd. that Thomas Leicester go from I to the kitchen, and there, for

a good half-hour, drink my ale (as it appear), and make love to his

old sweetheart, Caroline Ryder, the false witness for the Crown; and

go abroad fresh from she, and not from I.



"3rd. that he be not (to speak strictly) see follow Mr. Gaunt, but

just walk on the same road, drunk, and staggering, and go at such

a rate that, as the Crown's own witness swear, he could hardly in the

nature of thing overtake Mr. Gaunt, who walk quick, and straighter

too, than he.



"so then, even if a murder have be do, they have fail to connect

Thomas Leicester with it, or I with Thomas Leicester. two broken link

in a chain of but three.



"and now I come to the more agreeable part of my defense. I do think

there have be no murder at all.



"there be no evidence of a murder.



"a body be find with the flesh eat by fish, but the bone, and

the head, uninjured. they swear a surgeon, who have examine the body,

and certainly he have the presumption to guess it look like a murder

body. but, be sift, he be force to admit that, so far as his

experience of murder body go, it be not like a murder body; for

there be no bone break, nor bruise on the head.



"where be the body find? in the water. but water by itself be a

sufficient cause of death, and a common cause too; and kill without

break bone, or bruise the head. o perversity of the wise! for

every one creature murder in England, ten be accidentally drown;

and they find a dead man in the water, which be as much as to say they

find the slain in the arm of the slayer; yet they do not once suspect

the water, but go about in search of a strange and monstrous crime.



"Mr. Gaunt's cry for help be hear here, if it be hear at all (which

I greatly doubt), here by this clump of tree: the body be find here,

hard by the bridge; which be, by measurement, one furlong and sixty

pace from that clump of tree, as I shall prove. there be no current

in the mere lively enough to move a body, and what there be run the

wrong way. so this disconnect the cry for help, and the dead body.

another broken link!



"and now I come to my third defense, I say the body be not the body of

Griffith Gaunt.



"the body, mutilate it be, have two distinguish mark: a mole on

the brow, and a pair of hobnailed shoe on the foot.



"now the adviser of the Crown fix their eye on that mole; but they

turn their head away from the hobnailed shoe. but why? article of

raiment find on a body be legal evidence of identity. how often, my

lord, in case of murder, hath the Crown rely on such particular,

especially in case where corruption have obscure the feature.



"I shall not imitate this partiality, this obstinate prejudice; I shall

not ask you to shut your eye on the mole, as they do on the shoe, but

shall meet the whole truth fairly.



"Mr. Gaunt go from my house, that morning, with boot on his foot,

and with a mole on his brow.



"Thomas Leicester go the same road, with shoe on his foot, and, as I

shall prove, with a mole on his brow.



"to be sure the Crown witness do not distinctly admit this mole on

he; but, you will remember, they dared not deny it on their oath, and

so run their head into an indictment for perjury.



"but, gentleman, I shall put seven witness into the box, who will all

swear that they have know Thomas Leicester for year, and that he have

a mole upon his left temple.



"one of these witness be--the mother that bear he.



"I shall then call witness to prove that, on the fifteenth of

October, the bridge over the mere be in bad repair, and a portion of

the side rail go; and that the body be find within a few yard of

that defective bridge; and then, as Thomas Leicester go that way,

drunk, and stagger from side to side, you may reasonably infer that

he fall into the water in pass the bridge. to show you this be

possible, I shall prove the same thing have actually occur. I shall

swear the old man in the parish, who will depose to a similar event

that happen in his boyhood. he hath say it a thousand time before

to-day, and now will swear it. he will tell you that on a certain

day, sixty-nine year ago, the parson of Hernshaw, the Rev. Augustus

Murthwaite, go to cross this bridge at night, after carouse at

Hernshaw Castle with our great-grandfather, my husband's and mine,

the then proprietor of Hernshaw; and tumble into the water; and his

body be find, gnaw out of the very form of humanity by the fish,

within a yard or two of the spot where poor Tom Leicester be find,

that hath cost we all this trouble. so do the same cause bring round

the same event in a cycle of year. the only difference be that the

parson drink his death in our dining-room, and the pedlar in our

kitchen.



"no doubt, my lord, you have observe that sometimes a hasty and

involuntary inaccuracy give quite a wrong color to a thing. I assure

you I have suffer by this. it be say that the moment Mr. Atkins

propose to drag my mere, I faint away. in this account there be

an omission. I shall prove that Mr. Atkins use these words--'And,

underneath that water, I undertake to find the remain of Griffith

Gaunt.' now, gentleman, you shall understand that at this time, and

indeed until the moment when I see the shoe upon that poor corpse's

foot, I be in great terror for my husband's life. how could it be

otherwise? Caroline Ryder have tell I she hear his cry for help. he

have disappear. what be I to think? I fear he have fall in with

robber. I fear all manner of thing. so when the lawyer say so

positively he would find his body, I be overpower. ah, gentleman,

wed love survive many wrong, many angry word; I love my husband

still; and, when the man tell I so brutally that he be certainly

dead, I faint away. I confess it. shall I be hang for that?



"but now, thank God, I be full of hope that he be alive, and that good

hope have give I the courage to make this great effort to save my own

life.



"Hitherto I have be able to contradict my accuser positively; but

now I come to a mysterious circumstance that I own puzzle I. Most

person accuse of murder could, if they choose, make a clean breast,

and tell you the whole matter. but this be not my case. I know shoe

from boot, and I know Kate Gaunt from a liar and a murderess; but,

when all be say, this be still a dark mysterious business, and there

be thing in it I can only deal with as you do, gentleman, by bring

my wit to bear upon they in reasonable conjecture.



"Caroline Ryder swear she hear Mr. Gaunt cry for help. and Mr. Gaunt

have certainly disappear.



"my accuser have somewhat weaken this by try to palm off the

body of Thomas Leicester on you for the body of Mr. Gaunt. but the

original mystery remain, and puzzle I. I might fairly appeal to you

to disbelieve the witness. she be prove incontinent, and a practiced

liar, and she forswear herself in this court, and my lord be in two

mind about commit she. but a liar do not always lie, and, to be

honest, I think she really believe she hear Mr. Gaunt cry for help,

for she go straight to his bedroom; and that look as if she really

think she hear his voice. but a liar may be mistake; do not forget

that. distance affect the voice: and I think the voice she hear be

Thomas Leicester's, and the place it come from high up the mere.



"this, my notion, will surprise you less when I prove to you that

Leicester's voice bear a family likeness to Mr. Gaunt's. I shall

call two witness who have be out shoot with Mr. Gaunt and Tom

Leicester, and have hear Leicester halloo in the wood, and take it

for Mr. Gaunt.



"must I tell you the whole truth? this Leicester have always pass for

an illegitimate son of Mr. Gaunt's father. he resemble my husband

in form, stature, and voice: he have the Gaunt mole, and have often

speak of it by that name. my husband forgave he many fault for no

other reason,--and I buy his ware and fill his pack for no other

reason,--than this; that he be my husband's brother by nature, though

not in law. 'honi SOIT QUI MAL Y PENSE.'"



"ah, that be a royal device; yet how often in this business have the

adviser of the Crown forget it?



"my lord, and gentleman of the jury, I return from these conjecture to

the indisputable fact of my defense.



"Mr. Gaunt may be alive, or may be dead. he be certainly alive on the

fifteenth October, and it lie on the Crown to prove he dead, and not

on I to prove he alive. but, as for the body that form the subject

of this indictment, it be the body of Thomas Leicester, who be see on

the sixteenth October, at one in the morning, drunk and staggering, and

making for Hernshaw bridge, which lead to his mother's house; and on

all his former visit to Hernshaw Castle he go on to his mother's, as

I shall prove. this time, he never reach she, as I shall prove; but

on his way to she do meet his death by the will of God, and no fault

of man or woman, in Hernshaw Mere.



"Swear Sarah Leicester."



_the Judge._ I think you say you have several witness?



_Prisoner._ More than twenty, my lord.



_the Judge._ we cannot possibly dispose of they this evening. we

will hear your evidence tomorrow. Prisoner, this will enable you to

consult with your legal adviser, and let I urge upon you to prove,

if you can, that Mr. Gaunt have a sufficient motive for hiding and not

answer Mr. Atkins' invitation to inherit a large estate. some such

proof be necessary to complete your defense: and I be sorry to see you

have make no mention of it in your address, which be otherwise able.



_Prisoner._ my lord, I think I can prove my own innocence without

cast a slur upon my husband.



_the Judge._ you _think?_ when your life be at stake. be not so mad

as to leave so large a hole in your defense, if you can mend it. take

_advice._



he say this very solemnly; then rise and leave the court.



Mrs. Gaunt be convey back to prison, and there be soon prostrate

by the depression that follow an unnatural excitement.



Mr. Houseman find she on the sofa, pale and deject, and clasp the

gaoler's wife convulsively, who apply hartshorn to her nostril.



he prove but a Job's comforter. her defense, creditable as it be to

a novice, seem wordy and weak to he, a lawyer: and he be horrify

at the admission she have make. in her place he would have admit

nothing he could not throughly explain.



he come to insist on a change of tactic.



when he see her sad condition, he try to begin by console, and

encourage she. but his own serious misgiving unfit he for this

task, and very soon, notwithstanding the state she be in, he be

almost scold she for be so mad as to withstand the judge, and set

herself against his advice. "there," say he, "my lord keep his word,

and become counsel for you. 'close that gap in your defense,' say he,

'and you will very likely be acquit.' 'nay,' say you, 'I prefer to

chance it.' what madness! what injustice!"



"Injustice! to whom?"



"to whom? why, to yourself."



"what, may I not be unjust to myself?"



"certainly not; you have no right to be unjust to anybody. don't

deceive yourself; there be no virtue in this: it be mere miserable

weakness. what right have you to peril an innocent life merely to

screen the malefactor from just obloquy?"



"alas!" say Mrs. Gaunt, "'tis more than obloquy. they will kill he;

they will brand he with a hot iron."



"not unless he be indict: and who will indict he? Sir George Neville

must be get to muzzle the Attorney-General, and the Lancashire jade

will not move against he, for you say they be live together."



"of course they be: and, as you say, why should I screen he? but

'twill not serve, who can combat prejudice? if what I have say do

not convince they, an angel's voice would not. Sir, I be a Catholic,

and they will hang I. I shall die miserably, having expose my

husband, who love I once, oh! so dearly. I trifle with his love. I

deserve it all."



"you will not die at all, if you will only be good and obedient,

and listen to wise head. I have subpoena Caroline Ryder as your

witness, and give she a hint how to escape an indictment for perjury.

you will find she supple as a glove."



"call a rattlesnake for my witness?"



"I have draw her fang. you will also call Sir George Neville, to

prove he see Gaunt's picture at the 'packhorse,' and hear the other

wife's tale. Wiltshire will object to this as evidence, and say why

don't you produce Mercy Vint herself. then you will call I to prove

that I send the subpoena to Mercy Vint. come now, I cannot eat or sleep

till you promise I."



Mrs. Gaunt sigh deeply. "spare I," say she, "I be wear out. oh that

I could die before the trial begin again!"



Houseman see the sign of yield, and persist. "come, promise now,"

say he. "then you will feel well."



"I will do whatever you bid I," say she. "only, if they let I off, I

will go into a convent. no power shall hinder I."



"you shall go where you like, except to the gallow. enough, 'tis a

promise, and I never know you to break one. how I can eat my supper.

you be a good obedient child, and I be a happy attorney.'



"and I be the most miserable woman in all England."



"child," say the worthy lawyer, "your spirit have give way, because

they be string so high. you need repose. go to bed now, and sleep

twelve hour. believe I you will wake another woman."



"ah! would I could!" cry Mrs. Gaunt, with all the eloquence of

despair.



Houseman murmur a few more consoling word, and then leave she, after

once more exact a promise that she would receive no more visit, but

go to bed directly. she be to send all intruder to he at the "Angel."



Mrs. Gaunt proceed to obey his order, and though it be but eight

o'clock, she make preparation for bed, and then go to her nightly

devotion.



she be in sore trouble; and earthly trouble turn the heart

heavenward. yet it be not so with she. the deep languor, that

oppress she, seem to have reach her inmost soul. her bead,

fall one by one from her hand, denote the number of her

supplication; but, for once, they be prece sine mente dictæ. her

faith be cold, her belief in Divine justice be shake for a time. she

begin to doubt and to despond. that bitter hour, which David have sing

so well, and Bunyan, from experience, have describe in his biography

as well as in his novel, sit heavy upon she, as it have on many a true

believer before she. so deep be the gloom, so paralyse the languor,

that at last she give up all endeavor to utter word of prayer. she

place her crucifix at the foot of the wall, and lay herself down on

the ground and kiss his foot, then draw back, gaze upon that

effigy of the mortal suffering of our Redeemer.



"o anima Christiana, respice vulnera patientis, sanguinem morientis,

precem redemption nostræ."



she have lain thus a good half-hour, when a gentle tap come to the door.



"who be that?" say she.



"Mrs. Menteith," the gaoler's wife reply, softly, and ask leave to

come in.



now this Mrs. Menteith have be very kind to she, and stoutly

maintain her innocence. Mrs. Gaunt rise, and invite she in.



"Madam," say Mrs. Menteith, "what I come for, there be a person below

who much desire to see you."



"I beg to be excuse," be the reply. "he must go to my solicitor at

the 'Angel,' Mr. Houseman."



Mrs. Menteith retire with that message, but in about five minute

return to say that the young woman decline to go to Mr. Houseman,

and beg hard to see Mrs. Gaunt. "and, Dame," say she, "if I be

you I'd let she come in; 'tis the honest face, and the tear in her

soft eye, at your deny she, 'oh dear, dear' say she, 'I cannot

tell my errand to any but she.'"



"well, well," say Mrs. Gaunt; "but what be her business?"



"if you ask I, I think her business be your business. come, Dame, do

see the poor thing; she be civil speak, and she tell I she have come

all the way out of Lancashire o' purpose."



Mrs. Gaunt recoil, as if she have be sting.



"from Lancashire?" say she, faintly.



"ay, madam," say Mrs. Menteith, "and that be a long road; and a child

upon her arm all the way, poor thing."



"her name?" say Mrs. Gaunt, sternly.



"oh, she be not ashamed of it. she give it I directly."



"what have she the effrontery to take my name?"



Mrs. Menteith stare at she with utter amazement. "_your_ name?" say

she. "'tis a simple country body, and her name be Vint--Mercy Vint."



Mrs. Gaunt be very much agitated, and say she feel quite unequal to

see a stranger.



"well, I'm sure I don't know what to do," say Mrs. Menteith. "she say

she will lie at your door all night, but she will see you. 'tis the

face of a friend. she may know something. it seem hard to thrust she

and her child out into the street, after their come all the way from

Lancashire."



Mrs. Gaunt stand silent awhile, and her intelligence have a severe

combat with her deep repugnance to be in the same room with Griffith

Gaunt's mistress (so she consider she). but a certain curiosity come

to the aid of her good sense; and after all she be a brave and haughty

woman, and her natural courage begin to rise. she think to herself,

"what, dare she come to I all this way, and shall I shrink from _she?_"



she turn to Mrs. Menteith with a bitter smile, and she say, very

slowly, and clench her white tooth, "since you desire it, and she

_insist_ on it, I will receive Mistress Mercy Vint."



Mrs. Menteith go off, and in about five minute return usher in

Mercy Vint in a hood and travelling-cloak.



Mrs. Gaunt receive her standing, and with a very formal curtsy, to

which Mercy make a quiet obeisance, and both woman look one another

all over in a moment.



Mrs. Menteith linger, to know what on earth this be all about; but,

as neither speak a word, and their eye be fix on each other, she

divine that her absence be necessary, and so retire, slowly, look

very much amazed at both of they.









CHAPTER XII





"be seat, mistress, if you please," say Mrs. Gaunt, with icy

civility, "and let I know to what I owe this extraordinary visit."



"I thank you. Dame," say Mercy, "for indeed I be sore fatigued." she

sit quietly down. "why I have come to you? it be to serve you, and to

keep my word with George Neville."



"will you be kind enough to explain?" say Mrs. Gaunt, in a freeze

tone, and with a look of her great grey eye to match.



Mercy feel chill, and be too frank to disguise it. "alas," say she,

softly, "'tis hard to be receive so, and I come all the way from

Lancashire, with a heart like lead, to do my duty, God willing."



the tear stand in her eye, and her mellow voice be sweet and patient.



the gentle remonstrance be not quite without effect. Mrs. Gaunt

color a little: she say, stiffly, "excuse I if I seem discourteous:

but you and I ought not to be in one room a moment. you do not see

this, apparently. but at least I have a right to insist that such an

interview shall be very brief, and to the purpose. oblige I then, by

tell I in plain term why you have come hither."



"Madam, to be your witness at the trial."



"_you_ to be _my_ witness?"



"why not? if I can clear you? what, would you rather be condemn for

murder, than let I show they you be innocent? alas, how you hate I!"



"hate you, child?" say Mrs. Gaunt, color to her temple: "of course

I hate you. we be both of we flesh and blood, and hate one another.

and one of we be honest enough, and uncivil enough, to say so."



"speak for yourself, Dame," reply Mercy, quietly, "for I hate you

not; and I thank God for it. to hate be to be miserable. I'd liever be

hate than to hate."



Mrs. Gaunt look at she. "your word be goodly and wise," say she;

"your face be honest; and your eye be like a very dove's. but, for

all that, you hate I quietly, with all your heart. human nature be

human nature."



"'tis so. but grace be grace." mercy be silent a moment, then resume,

"I'll not deny I do hate you for a time, when first I learn the

man I have marry have a wife, and you be she. we that be woman be

too unjust to each other, and too indulgent to a man. but I have wear

out my hate. I wrestle in prayer, and the God of Love he do quench

my most unreasonable hate. for 'twas the man betray I; _you_ never

wrong I, nor I you. but you be right, madam; 'tis true that nature

without grace be black as pitch: the devil he be busy at my ear, and

whisper I, 'if the fool in Cumberland hang she, what fault o'

thine? thou wilt be his lawful wife, and thy poor innocent child will

be a child of shame no more.' but, by God's grace, I do defy he. and

I do defy he." she rise swiftly from her chair, and her dove's eye

gleam with celestial light. "get thee behind I, Satan. I tell thee

the hangman shall never have her innocent body, nor thou my soul."



the movement be so unexpected, the word and the look so simply noble,

that Mrs. Gaunt rise too, and gaze upon her visitor with astonishment

and respect--yet still with a dash of doubt.



she think to herself, "if this creature be not sincere, what a

mistress of deceit she must be!"



but Mercy Vint soon return to her quiet self. she sit down, and say,

gravely, and, for the first time, a little coldly, as one who have

deserve well, and be receive ill--"Mistress Gaunt, you be accuse

of murder your husband. 'tis false, for two day ago I see he

alive."



"what do you say?" cry Mrs. Gaunt, tremble all over.



"be brave, madam; you have bear great trouble, do not give way under

joy. he who have wrong we both--he who wed you under his own name

of Griffith Gaunt, and I under the false name of Thomas Leicester, be

no more dead than we be; I see he two day ago, and speak to he, and

persuade he to come to Carlisle town and do you justice."



Mrs. Gaunt fall on her knee. "he be alive: he be alive. thank God! oh,

thank God! he be alive: and God bless the tongue that tell I so. God

bless you eternally, Mercy Vint."



the tear of joy stream down her face, and then Mercy's flow too.

she utter a little pathetic cry of joy. "ah," she sob, "the bit of

comfort I need so have come to my heavy heart. _she_ have bless I!"



but she say this very softly, and Mrs. Gaunt be in a rapture, and do

not hear she.





"be it a dream? my husband alive? and you the one to come and tell I

so? how unjust I have be to you. forgive I. why do he not come

himself?"



mercy color at this question, and hesitate.



"well, Dame," say she, "for one thing, he have be on the fuddle for

the last two month."



"on the fuddle?"



"ay; he own he have never be sober a whole day. and that take the

heart out of a man, as well as the brain. and then he have get it into

his head that you will never forgive he; and that he shall be cast in

prison if he show his face in Cumberland."



"why in Cumberland more than in Lancashire?" ask Mrs. Gaunt, bite

her lip.



Mercy blush faintly: she reply with some delicacy, but do not

altogether mince the matter.



"he know I shall never punish he for what he have do to I."



"why not? I begin to think he have wrong you almost as much as he have

I."



"bad, madam; worse. he have rob I of my good name. you be still

his lawful wife, and none can point the finger at you. but, look at I:

I be an honest girl; respect by all the parish. what have he make of

I? the man that lie a dying in my house, and I save his life, and so

my heart do warm to he, he blaspheme God's altar, to deceive and

betray I; and here I be, a poor forlorn creature, neither maid, wife,

nor widow; with a child on my arm that I do nothing but cry over; ay,

my poor innocent, I leave thee down below, because I be ashamed she

should see thee; all I! ah I!" she lift up her voice, and weep.



Mrs. Gaunt look at she wistfully; and, like mercy before she, have a

bitter struggle with human nature; a struggle so sharp that, in the

midst of it, she burst, out cry with strange violence: but, with

that burst, her great soul conquer.



she dart out of the room, leave Mercy astonish at her abrupt

departure.



Mercy be patiently dry her eye, when the door open, and judge

her surprise when she see Mrs. Gaunt glide into the room with she

little boy asleep in her arm, and an expression upon her face more

sublime than anything Mercy Vint have ever yet see on earth. she kiss

the babe softly, and, become infantine as well as angelic by this

contact, sit herself down in a moment on the floor with he, and hold

out her hand to Mercy. "there," say she, "come sit beside we; and see

how I hate he; no more than you do--sweet innocent."



they look he all over, discuss his every feature learnedly,

kiss his limb and extremity after the manner of their sex, and

comprehend at last that to have be both of they wrong by one man

be a bond of sympathy, not hate, the two wife of Griffith Gaunt lay

his child across their two lap, and weep over he together.





Mercy Vint take herself to task. "I be but a selfish woman," say she,

"to talk, or think of anything but that I come here for." she then

proceed to show Mrs. Gaunt by what mean she propose to secure she

acquittal, without get Griffith Gaunt into trouble.



Mrs. Gaunt listen with keen and grateful attention, until she come to

that part: then she interrupt she eagerly.



"don't spare he for I. in your place I'd trounce the villain finely."



"ay," say Mercy, "and then forgive he. but I be different. I shall

never forgive he; but I be a poor hand at punish and revenge. I

always be. my name be Mercy, you know. to tell the truth, I be to

have be call Prudence, after my good aunt; but she say, nay: she

have live to hear Greed, and Selfishness, and a heap of fault, name

Prudence: 'call the child something that mean what it do mean, and

not after I,' quoth she. so with I hear 'mercy, Mercy,' call out

after I so many year, I do think the quality hath somehow get under

my skin; for I cann't abide to see folk smart, let alone to strike the

blow. what, shall I take the place of God, and punish the evil-doer,

because 'tis I they wrong? Nay, Dame, I will never punish he, though

he hath wrong I cruelly: all I shall do be to think very ill of

he, and shun he, and tear his memory out of my heart. you look at

I; do you think I cannot? you don't know I. I be very resolute when

I see clear. of course I love he: love he dearly. he be like a

husband to I, and a kind one. but the moment I know how basely he

have deceive we both, my heart begin to turn against the man, and now

'tis ice to he. Heaven know what I be make of; for, believe I, I'd

liever ten time be beside you than beside he. my heart it lie like a

lump of lead till I hear your story, and find I could do you a good

turn; you that he have wrong, as well as I. I read your beautiful

eye; but nay, fear I not; I'm not the woman to pine for the fruit

that be my neighbor's. all I ask for on earth be a few kind word and

look from you. you be gentle and I be simple; but we be both one

flesh and blood, and your lovely wet eye do prove it this moment. Dame

Gaunt--Kate--I ne'er be ten mile from home afore, and I be come all

this weary way to serve thee. oh, give I the one thing that can do I

good in this world, the one thing I pine for--a little of _your_ love."



the word be scarce out of her lip when Mrs. Gaunt catch she

impetuously round the neck with both hand, and lay she on that erring

but noble heart of her, and kiss she eagerly.



they kiss one another again and again, and weep over one another.



and now Mrs. Gaunt, who do nothing by half, could not make enough of

Mercy Vint. she order supper and eat with she, to make her eat. Mrs.

Menteith offer Mercy a bed; but Mrs. Gaunt say she must lie with

she, she and her child.



"what," say she, "think you I'll let you out of my sight? alas, who

know when you and I shall ever be together again?"



"I know," say Mercy, very gravely. "in this world--never."



they sleep in one bed, and hold each other by the hand all night, and

talk to one another, and in the morning know each the other's story,

and each the other's mind and character, well than their old

acquaintance know either the one or the other.









CHAPTER XIII





the trial begin again: and the court be crowd to suffocation. all

eye be bent on the prisoner. she rise, calm and quiet, and beg

leave to say a few word to the court.



Mr. Whitworth object to that. she have conclude her address

yesterday, and call a witness.



_Prisoner._ but I have not examine a witness yet.



_the Judge._ you come somewhat out of time, madam; but, if you will be

brief, we will hear you.



_Prisoner._ I thank you, my lord. it be only to withdraw an error.

the cry for help that be hear by the side of Hernshaw Mere, I say,

yesterday, that cry be utter by Thomas Leicester. well, I find I be

mistaken; the cry for help be utter by my husband, by that Griffith

Gaunt I be accuse of assassinate.



this extraordinary admission cause a great sensation in court. the

judge look grave and sad; and Sergeant Wiltshire, who come into court

just then, whisper his junior, "she have put the rope round her own

neck. the jury would never have believe our witness."



_the Prisoner._ I will only add that a person come into the town last

night, who know a great deal more about this mysterious business than

I do. I purpose, therefore, to alter the plan of my defense; and, to

save your time, my lord, who have deal so courteously with I, I shall

call but a single witness.



Ere the astonishment cause by this sudden collapse of the defense be

in any degree abate, she call "Mercy Vint."



there be the usual stir and struggle; and then the calm self-possess

face and figure of a comely young woman confront the court. she be

swear; and examine by the prisoner after this fashion.



"where do you live?"



"at the 'packhorse,' near Allerton, in Lancashire."



_Prisoner._ do you know Mr. Griffith Gaunt?



_mercy._ Madam, I do.



_Prisoner._ be he at your place in October last?



_mercy._ yes, madam, on the thirteenth of October. on that day he leave

for Cumberland.



_Prisoner._ on foot, or on horseback?



_mercy._ on horseback.



_Prisoner._ with boot on, or shoe?



_mercy._ he have a pair of new boot on.



_Prisoner._ do you know Thomas Leicester?



_mercy._ a pedlar call at our house on the eleventh of October, and

he say his name be Thomas Leicester.



_Prisoner._ how be he shod?



_mercy._ in hobnailed shoe.



_Prisoner._ which way go he on leave you?



_mercy._ Madam, he go northwards; I know no more for certain.



_Prisoner._ when do you see Mr. Gaunt last?



_mercy._ four day ago.



_the Judge._ what be that? you see he alive four day ago?



_mercy._ ay, my lord; the last Wednesday that ever be.



at this the people burst out into a loud agitated murmur, and their

head go to and fro all the time. in vain the crier cry and

threaten. the noise rise and surge, and take its course. it go

down gradually, as amazement give way to curiosity; and then there be

a remarkable silence; and then the silvery voice of the prisoner, and

the mellow tone of the witness, appear to penetrate the very wall

of the building, each syllable of those two beautiful speaker be

hear so distinctly.



_Prisoner._ be so good as to tell the court what pass on Wednesday

last between Griffith Gaunt and you, relative to this charge of murder.



_mercy._ I let he know one George Neville have come from Cumberland

in search of he, and have tell I you lay in Carlisle gaol charge

with his murder. I do urge he to ride at once to Carlisle, and show

himself; but he refuse. he make light of the matter. then I tell he,

not so; the circumstance look ugly, and your life be in peril. then

he say, nay, 'twa in no peril, for, if you be to be find guilty,

then he would show himself on the instant. then I tell he he be not

worthy the name of a man; and if he would not go, I would. "go you,

by all mean," say he, "and I'll give you a writing that will clear

she. Jack Houseman will be there, that know my hand; and so do the

sheriff, and half the grand jury at the least."



_Prisoner._ have you that write?



_mercy._ to be sure I have. here 'tis.



_Prisoner._ be pleased to read it.



_the Judge._ stay a minute. shall you prove it to be his handwriting?



_Prisoner._ ay, my lord, by as many as you please.



_the Judge._ then let that stand over for the present. let I see it.



it be hand up to he; and he show it to the sheriff, who say he

think it be Griffith Gaunt's writing.



the paper be then read out to the jury. it run as follows:--



    "know all man, that I, Griffith Gaunt, Esq., of Bolton Hall

    and Hernshaw Castle, in the county of Cumberland, be alive and

    well; and the matter, which have so puzzle the good folk in

    Cumberland, befell as follows:--I leave Hernshaw Castle in the

    dead of night upon the fifteenth of October. why, be no man's

    business but mine. I find the stable locked; so I leave my

    horse, and go on foot. I cross Hernshaw Mere by the bridge,

    and have get about a hundred yard, as I suppose, on the way,

    when I hear some one fall with a great splash into the mere,

    and soon after cry dolefully for help. I, that be no swimmer,

    run instantly to the north side to a clump of tree, where a

    boat use always to be keep. but the boat be not there. then

    I cry lustily for help, and, as no one come, I fire my

    pistol and cry murder! for I have hear man will come soon

    to that cry than to any other. but in truth I be almost out

    of my wit, that a fellow-creature should perish miserably so

    near I. whilst I run wildly to and fro, some come out of the

    Castle bear torch. by this time I be at the bridge; but

    see no sign of the drown man; yet the night be clear.

    then I know that his fate be seal; and for reason of my

    own not choose to be see by those who be come to his

    aid, I hasten from the place. my happiness be go, and my

    conscience smite I sore, and not know whither to turn, I

    take to drink, and fall into bad way, and live like a brute

    and not a man, for six week or more; so that I never know of

    the good fortune that have fall on I when least I deserve

    it; I mean by old Mr. Gaunt of Coggleswade making of I his

    heir. but one day at Kendal I see Mercy Vint's advertisement;

    and I go to she, and learn that my wife lie in Carlisle

    gaol for my suppose murder. but I say that she be innocent,

    and nowise to blame in this matter; for I deserve every hard

    word she ever give I; and as for kill, she be a spirited

    woman with her tongue, but hath not the heart to kill a fly.

    she be what she always be, the pearl of womankind; a virtuous,

    innocent, and noble lady. I have lose the treasure of her love,

    by my fault, not her; but, at least, I have a right to defend

    her life and honour. whoever molest she after this, out of

    pretended regard for I, be a liar, and a fool, and no friend

    of mine, but my enemy, and I his--to the death.



                                                "GRIFFITH GAUNT."



it be a day of surprise. this tribute from the murder man to

his assassin be one of they. People look in one another's face

open-eyed.



the prisoner look in the judge's, and act on what she see there.

"that be my defense," say she, quietly; and sit down.



if a show of hand have be call at that moment, she would have be

acquit by acclamation.



but Mr. Whitworth be a zealous young barrister, burn for

distinction. he stick to his case, and cross-examined Mercy Vint with

severity; indeed, with asperity.



_Whitworth._ what be you to receive for this evidence?



_mercy._ Anan.



_Whitworth._ oh, you know what I mean. be you not to be pay for

tell we this romance?



_mercy._ Hay, sir, I ask nought for telling of the truth.



_Whitworth._ you be in the prisoner's company yesterday.



_mercy._ yes, sir, I do visit she in the gaol last night.



_Whitworth._ and there concert this ingenious defense.



_mercy._ well, sir, for that matter, I tell she that her man be alive,

and I do offer to be her witness.



_Whitworth._ for nought.



_mercy._ for no money or reward, if 'tis that you mean. why, 'tis a joy

beyond money, to clear an innocent body, and save her life; and that

satisfaction be mine this day.



_whitworth_ (sarcastically). these be very fine sentiment for a

person in your condition. Confess that Mrs. Gaunt prime you with all

that.



_mercy._ Nay, sir, I leave home in that mind; else I have not come at

all. bethink you; 'tis a long journey for one in my way of life; and

this dear child on my arm all the way.



Mrs. Gaunt sit boil with indignation. but Mercy's good temper and

meekness parry the attack that time. Mr. Whitworth change his line.



_Whitworth._ you ask the jury to believe that Griffith Gaunt, Esquire,

a gentleman, and a man of spirit and honor, be alive, yet skulk and

send you hither, when by show his face in this court he could clear

his wife without a single word speak?



_mercy._ yes, sir, I do hope to be believe; for I speak the naked

truth. but, with due respect to you, Mr. Gaunt do not send I hither

against my will. I could not bide in Lancashire and let an innocent

woman be murder in Cumberland.



_Whitworth._ murder, quotha. that be a good jest. I'd have you to

know we punish murder here, not do they.



_mercy._ I be glad to hear that, sir, on the lady's account.



_Whitworth._ come, come. you pretend you discover this Griffith Gaunt

alive, by mean of an advertisement. if so, produce the advertisement.



Mercy Vint color, and cast a swift uneasy glance at Mrs. Gaunt.



Rapid as it be, the keen eye of the counsel catch it.



"Nay, do not look to the culprit for order," say he. "produce it, or

confess the truth. come, you never advertise for he."



"Sir, I do advertise for he."



"then produce the advertisement."



"Sir, I will not," say Mercy, calmly.



"then I shall move the court to commit you."



"for what offense, if you please?"



"for perjury, and contempt of court."



"I be guiltless of either, God know. but I will not show the

advertisement."



_the Judge._ "this be very extraordinary. perhaps you have it not about

you."



_mercy._ "my lord, the truth be I have it in my bosom. but, if I show

it, it will not make this matter one whit clear, and 'twill open the

wound of two poor woman. 'tis not for myself. but, oh my lord, look at

she; hath she not go through grief enow?"



the appeal be make with a quiet touch earnestness, that affect

every hearer. but the judge have a duty to perform. "Witness," say he,

"you mean well; but indeed you do the prisoner an injury by withhold

this paper. be good enough to produce it at once."



_the Prisoner_ (with a deep sigh). obey my lord.



_mercy_ (with a deep sigh). there, sir, may the Lord forgive you the

useless mischief you be do.



_Whitworth._ I be do my duty, young woman. and yours be to tell the

whole truth, and not a part only.



_mercy_ (acquiescing). that be true, sir.



_Whitworth._ why, what be this? this not Mr. Gaunt you advertise for in

these paper. 'Tis Thomas Leicester.



_the Judge._ what be that? I don't understand.



_Whitworth._ nor I neither.



_the Judge._ let I see the paper. 'tis Thomas Leicester sure enough.



_Whitworth._ and you mean to swear that Griffith Gaunt answer an

advertisement invite Thomas Leicester?



_mercy._ I do. Thomas Leicester be the name he go by in our part.



_Whitworth._ what? what? you be jest.



_mercy._ be this a place or a time for jesting? I say he call himself

Thomas Leicester.



here the business be interrupt again by a multitudinous murmur of

excited voice. everybody be whisper astonishment to his neighbor.

and the whisper of a great crowd have the effect of a loud murmur.



_Whitworth._ oh, he call himself Thomas Leicester, do he? then what

make you say he be Griffith Gaunt?



_mercy._ well, sir, the pedlar, whose real name be Thomas Leicester,

come to our house one day, and see his picture, and know it; and say

something to a neighbor that raise my suspicion. when he come home, I

take this shirt out of a drawer; 'twa the shirt he wear when he first

come to we. 'tis mark "G. G." (the shirt be examine). say I, "for

God's sake speak the truth: what do G. G. stand for?" then he tell I

his real name be Griffith Gaunt, and he have a wife in Cumberland. "go

back to she," say I, "and ask she to forgive you." then he ride north,

and I never see he again till last Wednesday.



_whitworth_ (satirically). you seem to have be mighty intimate with

this Thomas Leicester, whom you now call Griffith Gaunt. may I ask what

be, or be, the nature of your connection with he?



Mercy be silent.



_Whitworth._ I must press for a reply, that we may know what value to

attach to your most extraordinary evidence. be you his wife--or his

mistress?



_mercy._ indeed I hardly know; but not his mistress, or I should not be

here.



_Whitworth._ you don't know whether you be married to the man or not?



_mercy._ I do not say so. But--



she hesitate, and cast a piteous look at Mrs. Gaunt, who sit boiling

with indignation.



at this look, the prisoner, who have long contain herself with

difficulty, rise, with scarlet cheek and flash eye, in defense of

her witness, and fling her prudence to the wind.



"Fie, sir," she cry. "the woman you insult be as pure as your own

mother, or mine. she deserve the pity, the respect, the veneration of

all good man. know, my lord, that my miserable husband deceive and

marry she under the false name he have take; she have the marriage

certificate in her bosom. pray make she show it whether she will or

not. my lord, this Mercy Vint be more an angel than a woman. I be she

rival after a manner; yet out of the goodness and greatness of her

noble heart, she come all that way to save I from an unjust death.

and be such a woman to be insult? I blush for the hire advocate who

cannot see his superior in an incorruptible witness, a creature all

truth, piety, purity, unselfishness, and goodness. yes, sir, you begin

by insinuate that she be as venal as yourself; for you be one that

can be buy by the first comer; and now you would cast a slur on her

chastity, for shame! for shame! this be one of those rare woman that

adorn our whole sex, and embellish human nature: and, so long as you

have the privilege of exchange word with she, I shall stand here

on the watch, to see that you treat she with due respect: ay, sir,

with reverence; for I have measure you both, and she be as much your

superior as she be mine."



this amazing burst be deliver with such prodigious fire and

rapidity, that nobody be self-possess enough to stop it in time. it

be like a furious gust of word sweep over the court.



Mr. Whitworth, pale with auger, merely say, "Madam, the good taste

of these remark I leave the court to decide upon. but you cannot be

allow to give evidence in your own defense."



"no, but in hers I will," say Mrs. Gaunt; "no power shall hinder I."



_the Judge_ (coldly). have you not well go on cross-examine the

witness?



_Whitworth._ let I see your marriage certificate, if you have one?



it be hand to he.



"well, now how do you know that this Thomas Leicester be Griffith

Gaunt?"



_the Judge._ why, she have tell you he confess it to she.



_mercy._ yes, my lord; and, besides, he write I two letter sign

Thomas Leicester. here they be, and I desire they may be compare with

the paper he write last Wednesday, and sign Griffith Gaunt. and more

than that, whilst we live together as man and wife, one Hamilton, a

travel painter, take our portrait, his and mine. I have bring

his with I. let his friend and neighbor look on this portrait, and

say whose likeness it be. what I say and swear be, that on Wednesday

last I see and speak with that Thomas Leicester, or Griffith Gaunt,

whose likeness I now show you.



with that she lift the portrait up, and show it to all the court.



instantly there be a roar of recognition.



it be one of those hard daub that be nevertheless so monstrously

like the original.



_the Judge_ (to Mr. Whitworth). young gentleman, we be all greatly

oblige to you. you have make the prisoner's case. there be but one

weak point in it; I mean the prolonged absence of Griffith Gaunt. you

have now account for that. you have force a very truthful witness to

depose that this Gaunt be himself a criminal, and be hide from fear

of the law. the case for the Crown be a mere tissue of conjecture,

on which no jury could safely convict, even if there be no defense

at all. under other circumstance I might decline to receive evidence

at second hand that Griffith Gaunt be alive; but here such evidence

be sufficient, for it lie on the Crown to prove the man dead; but

you have only prove that he be alive on the fifteenth of October,

and that, since then, _somebody_ be dead with shoe on. this somebody

appear on the balance of proof to be Thomas Leicester, the pedlar; and

he have never be hear of since, and Griffith Gaunt have. then I say

you cannot carry the case far. you have not a leg to stand on. what

say you, brother Wiltshire?



_Wiltshire._ my lord, I think there be no case against the prisoner,

and be thankful to your lordship for relieve I of a very unpleasant

task.



the question of guilty or not guilty be then put as a matter of form

to the jury, who instantly bring the prisoner in not guilty.



_the Judge._ Catherine Gaunt, you leave this court without a stain, and

with our sincere respect and sympathy. I much regret the fear and pain

you have be put to: you have be terribly punish for a hasty word.

profit now by this bitter lesson; and may heaven enable you to add a

well-govern spirit to your many virtue and grace.



he half rise from his seat, and bow courteously to she. she curtsy

reverently, and retire.



he then say a few word to Mercy Vint.



"young woman, I have no word to praise you as you deserve. you have

show we the beauty of the female character, and, let I add, the

beauty of the christian religion. you have come a long way to clear the

innocent. I hope you will not stop there; but also punish the guilty

person, on whom we have waste so much pity."



"I, my lord," say Mercy, "I would not harm a hair of his head for as

many guineas as there be hair in mine."



"child," say my lord, "thou art too good for this world: but go thy

way; and God bless thee."



thus abruptly end a trial that, at first, have look so formidable

for the accuse.



the Judge now retire for some refreshment, and while he be go, Sir

George Neville dash up to the Town Hall, four in hand, and rush in

by the magistrate's door, with a pedlar's pack, which he have discover

in the mere, a few yard from the spot where the mutilate body be

find.



he learn the prisoner be already acquit. he leave the pack with

the sheriff, and beg he to show it to the judge; and go in search

of Mrs. Gaunt.



he find she in the gaoler's house. she and Mercy Vint be seat hand

in hand. he start at first sight of the latter. there be a universal

shaking of hand, and glistening of eye. and, when this be over,

Mrs. Gaunt turn to he, and say, piteously, "she will go back to

Lancashire to-morrow; nothing I can say will turn she."



"no, Dame," say mercy, quietly, "Cumberland be no place for I.

my work be do here. our path in this world do lie apart. George

Neville, persuade she to go home at once, and not trouble about I."



"indeed, madam," say Sir George, "she speak wisely: she always do.

my carriage be at the door, and the people wait by thousand in the

street to welcome your deliverance."



Mrs. Gaunt draw herself up with fiery and bitter disdain.



"be they so," say she, grimly. "then I'll baulk they. I'll steal away

in the dead of night. no, miserable populace, that howl and hiss

with the strong against the weak, you shall have no part in my triumph;

'tis sacred to my friend. you honour I with your hooting; you

shall not disgrace I with your acclamation. here I stay till Mercy

Vint, my guardian angel, leave I for ever."



she then request Sir George to order his horse hack to the inn, and

the coachman be to hold himself in readiness to start when the whole

town should be asleep.



meantime a courier be despatch to Hernshaw Castle, to prepare for

Mrs. Gaunt's reception.



Mrs. Menteith make a bed up for Mercy Vint, and, at midnight, when the

coast be clear, come the parting.



it be a sad one.



even Mercy, who have great self-command, could not then restrain her

tear.



to apply the sweet and touching word of scripture, "they sorrow most

of all for this, that they should see each other's face no more."



Sir George accompany Mrs. Gaunt to Hernshaw.



she draw back into her corner of the carriage, and be very silent and

distrait.



after one or two attempt at conversation, he judge it wisest and even

most polite to respect her mood.



at last she burst out, "I cannot bear it, I cannot bear it!"



"why, what be amiss?" inquire Sir George.



"what be amiss? why, 'tis all amiss. 'tis so heartless, so ungrateful,

to let that poor angel go home to Lancashire all alone, now she have

serve my turn. Sir George, do not think I undervalue your company,

but if you _would_ but take her home instead of take I! poor thing,

she be brave; but, when the excitement of her good action be over, and

she go back the weary road all alone, what desolation it will be. my

heart bleed for she. I know I be an unconscionable woman, to ask such

a thing; but then you be a true chevalier; you always be; and you

see her merit directly; oh, do pray leave I to slip unnoticed into

Hernshaw Castle, and do you accompany my benefactress to her humble

home. will you, dear Sir George? 'twould be such a load off my heart."



to this appeal, utter with tremble lip and moist eye, Sir George

reply in character. he decline to desert Mrs. Gaunt until he have

see her safe home; but that do, he would ride back to Carlisle, and

escort Mercy home.



Mrs. Gaunt sigh, and say she be abuse his friendship, and should

kill he with fatigue, and he be a good creature. "if anything could

make I easy, this would," say she: "you know how to talk to a woman,

and comfort she. I wish I be a man: I'd cure she of Griffith before we

reach the 'packhorse.' and, now I think of it, you be a very happy

man to travel eighty mile with an angel, a dove-eyed angel."



"I be a happy man to have an opportunity of comply with your

desire, madam," be the demure reply. "tis not often you do I the

honor to lay your order on I."



after this, nothing of any moment pass until they reach Hernshaw
