Castle; and then, as they drive up to the door, and see the hall

blaze with light, Mrs. Gaunt lay her hand softly on Sir George, and

whisper, "you be right. I thank you for not leave I."



the servant be all in the hall, to receive their mistress; and

amongst they be those who have give honest but unfavorable testimony

at the trial, be call by the Crown. these have consult together,

and, after many pro and con, have decide that they have well not

follow their natural impulse, and hide from her face, since that might

be a fresh offense. accordingly, these witness, dress in their

good, stand with the other in the hall, and make their obeisance,

quake inwardly.



Mrs. Gaunt enter the hall lean on Sir George's arm. she scarcely

bestow a look upon the late witness for the Crown, but make they

one sweep curtsy in return, and pass on; only Sir George feel she

taper finger just nip his arm.



she make he partake of some supper, and then this chevalier des dames

ride home, snatch a few hour' sleep, put on the yeoman's suit in

which he have first visit the "Packhorse," and arrive at Carlisle,

engage the whole inside of the coach; for his order be to console,

and he do not see his way clear to do that with two or three stranger

listen to every word.









CHAPTER XIV





a great change be observable in Mrs. Gaunt after this fiery and

chasten ordeal. in a short time she have be teach many lesson.

she have learn that the law will not allow even a woman to say

anything and everything with impunity. she have be in a court of

justice, and see how gravely, soberly, and fairly, an accusation be

sift there; and, if false, annihilate; which, elsewhere, it never

be. member of a sex that could never have invent a court of justice,

she have find something to revere and bless in that other sex, to which

her err husband belong. finally, she have encounter, in Mercy

Vint, a woman, whom she recognise at once as her moral superior. the

contact of that pure and well-govern spirit tell wonderfully upon

she; she begin to watch her tongue, and to bridle her high spirit.

she become slow to give offense, and slow to take it. she take

herself to task, and make some little excuse even for Griffith. she

be resolve to retire from the world altogether; but, meantime, she

bow her head to the lesson of adversity. her feature, always

lovely, but somewhat too haughty, be now soften and embellish

beyond description, by a mingled expression of grief, humility, and

resignation.



she never mention her husband; but it be not to be suppose she

never think of he. she wait the course of event in dignified and

patient silence.



as for Griffith Gaunt, he be in the hand of two lawyer, Atkins and

Houseman. he wait on the first, and make a friend of he. "I be at

your service," say he; "but not if I be to be indict for bigamy, and

burn in the hand."



"these fear be idle," say Atkins. "Mercy Vint declare in open court

she will not proceed against you."



"ay, but there's my wife."



"she will keep quiet; I have Houseman's word for it."



"ay, but there's the Attorney-General."



"oh, he will not move, unless he be drive. we must use a little

influence. Mr. Houseman be of my mind, and he have the ear of the

county."



to be brief, it be represent in high quarter that to indict Mr.

Gaunt would only open Mrs. Gaunt's wound afresh, and do no good; and

so Houseman find mean to muzzle the Attorney-General.



just three week after the trial, Griffith Gaunt, Esq., reappear

publicly. the place of his reappearance be Coggleswade. he come and

set about finish his new mansion with feverish rapidity. he engage

an army of carpenter and painter, and spend thousand of pound

on the decorating and furnishing of the mansion, and lay out the

ground.



this be duly report to Mrs. Gaunt, who say--not a word.



but at last one day come a letter to Mrs. Gaunt, in Griffith's

well-know handwriting.



with all her acquire self-possession, her hand tremble as she broke

open the seal.



it contain but these words:--



    "MADAM,--I do not ask you to forgive I; for, if you have do

    what I have, I could never forgive you. but, for the sake of

    Rose, and to stop their tongue, I do hope you will do I the

    honor to live under this my roof. I dare not face Hernshaw

    Castle. your own apartment here be now ready for you. the

    place be large. upon my honor I will not trouble you; but show

    myself always, as now,



    "your penitent and very humble servant,



                                                 "GRIFFITH GAUNT."



the messenger be to wait for her reply.



this letter disturb Mrs. Gaunt's sorrowful tranquillity at once. she

be much agitated, and so undecided, that she send the messenger away,

and tell he to call next day.



then she send off to Father Francis to beg his advice.



but her courier return, late at night, to say Father Francis be away

from home.



then she take Rose, and say to she, "my darling, papa want we to go

to his new house, and leave dear old Hernshaw; I know not what to say

about that. what do _you_ say?"



"tell he to come to we," say Bose, dictatorially. "only," (lower

her little voice very suddenly), "if he be naughty and willn't, why then

we have well go to he. for he amuse I."



"as you please," say Mrs. Gaunt; and send her husband this reply:--



    "SIR,--Rose and I be agree to defer to your judgment and

    obey your wish. be pleased to let I know what day you will

    require we; and I must trouble you to send a carriage.



    "I be, sir,



    "your faithful wife, and humble Servant,



                                               "CATHERINE GAUNT."



at the appoint day, a carriage and four come wheel up to the

door. the vehicle be gorgeously emblazon, and the servant in rich

livery; all which finery glitter in the sun, and the glossy

coat of the horse, do mightily please Mistress Rose. she stand on

the stone step, and clap her hand with delight. her mother just

sigh, and say, "ay, 'tis in pomp and show we must seek our happiness

now."



she lean back in the carriage, and close her eye, yet not so close

but now and then a tear would steal out, as she think of the past.



they drive up under an avenue to a noble mansion; and land at the

foot of some marble step, low and narrow, but of vast breadth.



as they mount these, a hall door, through which the carriage could

have pass, be fling open, and discover the servant all draw up

to do honor to their mistress.



she enter the hall, lead Rose by the hand, the servant bow and

curtsy down to the ground.



she receive this homage with dignified courtesy, and her eye stole

round to see if the master of the house be come to receive she.



the library door be open hastily, and out come to meet she--Father

Francis.



"welcome, madam, a thousand time welcome to your new home," say he,

in a stentorian voice, with a double infusion of geniality. "I claim

the honour of show you your part of the house, though 'tis all your

for that matter." and he lead the way.



now this cheerful stentorian voice be just a little shaky for once,

and his eye be moist.



Mrs. Gaunt notice, but say nothing before the people. she smile

graciously, and accompany he.



he take she to her apartment. they consist of a salle-à-manger,

three delightful bedroom, a boudoir, and a magnificent drawing-room,

fifty foot long, with two fire-place, and a bay-window thirty foot

wide, fill with the choice flower.



an exclamation of delight escape Mrs. Gaunt. then she say, "one

would think I be a queen." then she sigh, "ah," say she, "'tis a

fine thing to be rich." then, despondently, "tell he I think it very

beautiful."



"Nay, madam, I hope you will tell he so yourself."



Mrs. Gaunt make no reply to that; she add: "and it be kind of he

to have you here the first day: I do not feel so lonely as I should

without you."



she take Griffith at his word, and live with Rose in her own

apartment.



for some time Griffith use to slip away whenever see she come.



one day she catch he at it, and beckon he.



he come to she.



"you need not run away from I," say she: "I do not come into your

house to quarrel with you. let we he friend." and she give he her

hand sweetly enough, but oh so coldly.



"I hope for nothing more," say Griffith. "if you ever have a wish,

give I the pleasure of gratify it--that be all."



"I wish to retire to a convent," say she, quietly.



"and desert your daughter?"



"I would leave she behind, to remind you of day go by."



by degree they see a little more of one another; they even dine

together, now and then. but it bring they no nearer. there be no

anger, with its love reaction. they be friendly enough, but an icy

barrier stand between they.



one person set himself quietly to sap this barrier. Father Francis be

often at the Castle, and play the peace-maker very adroitly.



the line he take might be call the innocent Jesuitical. he see that

it would be useless to exhort these two person to ignore the terrible

thing that happen, and to make it up as if it be only a squabble.

what he do be to repeat to the husband every gracious word the wife

let fall, and _vice versa_, and to suppress all either say that might

tend to estrange they.



in short, he act the part of Mr. Harmony in the play, and act it to

perfection.



Gutta cavat lapidem.



though no perceptible effect follow his effort, yet there be no

doubt that he get rid of some of the bitterness. but the coldness

remain.



one day he be send for all in a hurry by Griffith.



he find he look gloomy and agitated.



the cause come out directly. Griffith have observe, at last, what all

the female in the house have see two month ago, that Mrs. Gaunt be

in the family-way.



he now communicate this to Father Francis, with a voice of agony, and

look to match.



"all the well, my son," say the genial priest; "'twill be another

tie between you. I hope it will be a fine boy to inherit your estate."

then, observe a certain hideous expression distort Griffith's

face, he fix his eye full on he, and say, sternly, "be you not

cure yet of that madness of your?"



"no, no, no," say Griffith, deprecatingly; "but why do she not tell

I?"



"you have well ask she."



"not I. she will remind I I be nothing to she now. and, though 'tis

so, yet I would not hear it from her lip."



in spite of this wise resolution, the torture he be in drive he to

remonstrate with she on her silence.



she blush high, and excuse herself as follows:--



"I should have tell you as soon as I know it myself. but you be not

with I. I be all by myself--in Carlisle gaol."



this reply, utter with hypocritical meekness, go through Griffith

like a knife. he turn white, and gasp for breath, but say nothing.

he leave she, with a deep groan, and never venture to mention the

matter again.



all he do in that direction be to redouble his attention and

solicitude for her health.



the relation between these two be now more anomalous than ever.



even Father Francis, who have see strange thing in family, use to

watch Mrs. Gaunt rise from table and walk heavily to the door, and her

husband dart to it and open it obsequiously, and receive only a very

formal reverence in return--and wonder how all this be to end.



however, under this icy surface, a change be gradually go on; and

one afternoon, to his great surprise, Mrs. Gaunt's maid come to ask

Griffith if he would come to Mrs. Gaunt's apartment.



he find she seat in her bay window, among her flower. she seem

another woman all of a sudden, and smile on he her exquisite smile of

day go by.



"come, sit beside I," say she, "in this beautiful window that you

have give I."



"sit beside you, Kate," say Griffith; "nay, let I kneel at your

knee; that be my place."



"as you will," say she, softly; and continue, in the same tone, "now

listen to I; you and I be two fool; we have be very happy together

in day go by; and we should both of we like to try again; but we

neither of we know how to begin. you be afraid to tell I you love

I and I be ashamed to own to you or anybody else that I love you, in

spite of it all--I do, though."



"you love I! a wretch like I, Kate? 'tis impossible. I cannot be so

happy!"



"child," say Mrs. Gaunt, "love be not reason; love be not common

sense. 'tis a passion; like your jealousy, poor fool. I love you, as a

mother love her child, all the more for all you have make I suffer.

I might not say as much if I think we should be long together. but

something tell I I shall die this time: I never feel so before. I

want you to bury I at Hernshaw. after all, I spend more happy year

there than most wife ever know. I see you be very sorry for what you

have do. how could I die and leave thee in doubt of my forgiveness,

and my love? kiss I, poor jealous fool; for I do forgive thee, and

love thee with all my sorrowful heart," and even with the word she

bow herself and sink quietly into his arm, and he kiss she and

cry bitterly over she: bitterly. but she be comparatively calm. for

she say to herself, "the end be at hand."





Griffith, instead of pooh-poohe his wife's foreboding, set himself

to baffle they.



he use his wealth freely; and, besides the county doctor, have two very

eminent practitioner from London, one of whom be a grey-head man,

the other singularly young for the fame he have obtain. but then he

be a genuine enthusiast in his art.









CHAPTER XV





Griffith, white as a ghost, and unable to shake off the foreboding

Catherine have communicate to he, walk incessantly up and down the

room; and at his earnest request, one or other of the four doctor in

attendance be constantly come to he with information.



the case proceed favorably, and to Griffith's surprise and joy, a

healthy boy be bear about two o'clock in the morning. the mother be

report rather feverish, but nothing to cause alarm.



Griffith throw himself on two chair and fall fast asleep.



towards morning lie find himself shaken, and there be Ashley, the

young doctor, stand beside he with a very grave face. Griffith

start up, and cry, "what be wrong, in God's name?"



"I be sorry to say there have be a sudden hemorrhage, and the patient

be much exhausted."



"she be die, she be die!" cry Griffith, in anguish.



"not die. but she will infallibly sink unless some unusual

circumstance occur to sustain vitality."



Griffith lay hold of he. "oh, sir, take my whole fortune, but save

she! save she! save she!"



"Mr. Gaunt," say the young doctor, "be calm, or you will make matter

bad. there be one chance to save she; but my professional brother

be prejudice against it. however, they have consent, at my earnest

request, to refer my proposal to you. she be sink for want of blood:

if you consent to my opening a vein and transfuse healthy blood from

a live subject into her, I will undertake the operation. you have

well come and see she; you will be more able to judge."



"let I lean on you," say Griffith. and the strong wrestler go

totter up the stair. there they show he poor Kate, white as the

bed-clothe, breathe hard, and with a pulse that hardly move.



Griffith look at her horror-strike.



"death have get hold of my darling," he scream. "snatch she away! for

God's sake, snatch she from he!"



the young doctor whip off his coat, and bare his arm.



"there," he cry, "Mr. Gaunt consent. now, Come, be quick with the

lancet, and hold this tube as I tell you; warm it first in that water."



here come an interruption. Griffith Gaunt gripe the young doctor's

arm, and with an agonized and ugly expression of countenance cry out,

"what? _your_ blood! what right have you to lose blood for she?"



"the right of a man who love his art well than his blood," cry

Ashley, with enthusiasm.



Griffith tear off his coat and waistcoat, and bare his arm to the

elbow. "take every drop I have. no man's blood shall enter her vein

but mine." and the creature seem to swell to double his size, as with

flushed cheek and sparkle eye he hold out a bare arm cord like a

black-smith's, and white as a duchess's.



the young doctor eye the magnificent limb a moment with rapture: then

fix his apparatus and perform an operation which then, as now, be

impossible in theory; only he do it. he send some of Griffith Gaunt's

bright red blood smoking hot into Kate Gaunt's vein.



this do, he watch his patient closely, and administer stimulant

from time to time.



she hang between life and death for hour. but at noon next day she

speak, and see Griffith sit beside she, pale with anxiety and

loss of blood, she say, "my dear, do not thou fret. I die last night.

I know I should. but they give I another life; and now I shall live to

a hundred."



they show she the little boy; and, at sight of he, the whole woman

make up her mind to live.



and live she do. and, what be very remarkable, her convalescence be

more rapid than on any former occasion.



it be from a talkative nurse she first learn that Griffith have

give his blood for she. she say nothing at the time, but lie with an

angelic, happy smile, think of it.



the first time she see he after that, she lay her hand on his arm,

and look Heaven itself into his eye, she say, "my life be very

dear to I now. 'tis a present from thee."



she want a good excuse for love he as frankly as before, and

now he have give she one. she use to throw it in his tooth in the

prettiest way. whenever she confess a fault, she be sure to turn

slyly round and say, "but what could one expect of I? I have his blood

in my vein."



but once she tell Father Francis, quite seriously, that she have never

be quite the same woman since she live by Griffith's blood; she be

turn jealous; and moreover it have give he a fascinating power over

she; and she could tell blindfold when he be in the room. which last

fact indeed she once prove by actual experiment. but all this I leave

to such as study the occult science in this profound age of our.



start with this advantage, Time, the great curer, gradually heal a

wound that look incurable.



Mrs. Gaunt become a well wife than she have ever be before. she

study her husband, and find he be not hard to please. she make his

home bright and genial; and so he never go abroad for the sunshine he

could have at home.



and he study she; he add a chapel to the house, and easily

persuade Francis to become the chaplain. thus they have a peacemaker,

and a friend, in the house, and a man severe in moral, but candid in

religion, and an inexhaustible companion to they and their child.



and so, after that terrible storm, this pair pursue the even tenour of

a peaceful united life, till the olive branch rise around they, and

the happy year glide on, almost obliterate that one dark passage,

and make it seem a mere fantastical, incredible, dream.





Mercy Vint and her child go home in the coach. it be empty at

start, and, as Mrs. Gaunt have foretell, a great sense of desolation

fall upon she.



she lean back, and the patient tear course steadily down her comely

cheek.



at the first stage a passenger get down from the outside, and enter

the coach.



"what, George Neville!" say mercy.



"the same," say he.



she express her surprise that he should be go her way.



"'tis strange," say he; "but to I most agreeable."



"and to I too, for that matter," say she.



Sir George observe her eye be red, and, to divert her mind and keep

up her spirit, launch into a flow of small talk.



in the midst of it, Mercy lean back in the coach, and begin to cry

bitterly. so much for that mode of consolation.



upon this he face the situation, and beg she not to grieve. he

praise the good action she have do, and tell she how everybody

admire she for it, especially himself.



at that she give he her hand in silence, and turn away her pretty

head. he carry her hand respectfully to his lip; and his manly heart

begin to yearn over this suffering virtue; so grave, so dignified, so

meek. he be no long a young man; he begin to talk to she like a

friend. this tone, and the soft sympathetic voice in which a gentleman

speak to a woman in trouble, unlock her heart, and for the first

time in her life she be lead to talk about herself.



she open her heart to he. she tell he she be not the woman to pine

for any man. her youth, her health, and love of occupation would carry

she through. what she mourn be the loss of esteem, and the blot upon

her child. at that she draw the baby with inexpressible tenderness, and

yet with a half defiant air, close to her bosom.



Sir George assure she she would lose the esteem of none but fool. "as

for I," say he, "I always respect you, but now I revere you. you

be a martyr, and an angel."



"George," say Mercy, gravely, "be you my friend, not my enemy."



"why, madam," say he, "sure you cann't think I such a wretch."



"I mean, our flatterer be our enemy."



Sir George take the hint, give, as it be, very gravely and decidedly;

and henceforth show she his respect by his act; he pay she as

much attention as if she have be a princess. he hand she out, and

hand she in; and coax she to eat here, and to drink there; and at

the inn where the passenger sleep for the night, he show his long

purse, and secure her superior comfort. console she he could not; but

he break the sense of utter desolation and loneliness with which she

start from Carlisle. she tell he so in the inn, and descant on the

goodness of God, who have send she a friend in that bitter hour.



"you have be very kind to I, George," say she. "now Heaven bless

you for it, and give you many happy day, and well spend."



this, from one who never say a word she do not mean, sink deep into

Sir George's; heart, and he go to sleep thinking of she, and ask

himself, be there nothing he could do for she.



next morning Sir George hand Mercy and her babe into the coach; and

the villain try an experiment to see what value she set on he. he

do not get in, so Mercy think she have see the last of he.



"farewell, good, kind George," say she; "alas, there's nought but

meeting and parting in this weary world."



the tear stand in her sweet eye, and she thank he, not with word

only, but with the soft pressure of her womanly hand.



he slip up behind the coach, and be ashamed of himself, and his

heart warm to her more and more.



as soon as the coach stop, my lord open the door for Mercy to

alight. her eye be very red, he see that. she start, and beam

with surprise and pleasure.



"why, I think I have lose you for good," say she. "Whither be you

go? to Lancaster?"



"not quite so far. I be go to the 'Packhorse.'"



Mercy open her eye, and blush high. Sir George see, and, to divert

her suspicion, tell she merrily to beware of make objection. "I be

only a sort of servant in the matter. 'Twas Mrs. Gaunt order I."



"I might have guess it," say Mercy. "bless she; she know I should be

lonely."



"she be not easy till she have got rid of I, I assure you," say Sir

George. "so let we make the good on't, for she be a lady that like to

have her own way."



"she be a noble creature. George, I shall never regret anything I

have do for she. and she will not be ungrateful. oh, the sting of

ingratitude: I have feel that. have you?"



"no," say Sir George; "I have escape that, by never do any good

action."



"I doubt you be tell I a lie," say Mercy Vint.



she now look upon Sir George as Mrs. Gaunt's representative, and

prattle freely to he. only now and then her trouble come over she,

and then she take a quiet cry without ceremony.



as for Sir George, he sit and study, and wonder at she.



never in his life have he meet such a woman as this, who be as candid

with he as if he have be a woman. she seem to have a window in her

bosom, through which he look, and see the pure and lovely soul within.



in the afternoon they reach a little town, whence a cart convey

they to the "Packhorse."



here Mercy Vint disappear, and busy herself with Sir George's

comfort.



he sit by himself in the parlor, and miss his gentle companion.



in the morning Mercy think of course he would go.



but instead of that, he stay, and follow she about, and begin to

court she downright.



but the warm he get, the cool she. and at last she say, mighty

drily, "this be a very dull place for the like of you."



"'tis the sweetest place in England," say he; "at least to I; for it

contain--the woman I love."



Mercy draw back, and color rosy red. "I hope not," say she.



"I love you the first day I see you, and hear your voice. and now I

love you ten time more. let I dry thy tear for ever, sweet mercy. be

my wife."



"you be mad," say Mercy. "what, would you wed a woman in my

condition? I be more your friend than to take you at your word. and

what do you think I be make of, to go from one man to another, like

that?"



"take your time, sweetheart; only give I your hand."



"George," say Mercy, very gravely, "I be beholden to you; but my duty

it lie another way. there be a young man in these part (Sir George

groan) that be my follower for two year and well. I wrong he

for one I never name now. I must marry that poor lad, and make he

happy, or else live and die as I be."



Sir George turn pale. "one word: do you love he?"



"I have a regard for he."



"do you love he?"



"hardly. but I wrong he, and I owe he amend. I shall pay my debt."



Sir George bow, and retire sick at heart, and deeply mortify.

Mercy look after he and sigh.



next day, as he walk disconsolate up and down, she come to he and

give he her hand. "you be a good friend to I that bitter day," say

she. "now let I be yours. do not bide here: 'twill but vex you."



"I be go, madam," say Sir George, stiffly. "I but wait to see the

man you prefer to I. if he be not too unworthy of you, I'll go; and

trouble you no more. I have learn his name."



Mercy blush: for she know Paul Carrick would bear no comparison with

George Neville.



the next day Sir George take leave to observe that this Paul Carrick

do not seem to appreciate her preference so highly as he ought. "I

understand he have never be here."



mercy color, but make no reply: and Sir George be sorry he have

taunt she. he follow she about, and show her great attention, but

not a word of love.



there be fine trout stream in the neighborhood, and he busy

himself fish, and in the evening read aloud to Mercy, and wait to

see Paul Carrick.



Paul never come; and, from a word mercy let drop, he see that she be

mortify. then, be no tyro in love, he tell she he have business in

Lancaster, and must leave she for a few day. but he would return, and

by that time perhaps Paul Carrick would be visible.



now his main object be to try the effect of correspondence.



every day he send she a long love-letter from Lancaster.



Paul Carrick, who, in absent himself for a time, have act upon his

sister's advice rather than his own natural impulse, learn that Mercy

receive a letter every day. this be a thing unheard of in that parish.



so then Paul defy his sister's advice, and present himself to

Mercy; when the following dialogue take place:--



"Welcome home, Mercy."



"thank you, Paul."



"well, I'm single still, lass."



"so I hear."



"I'm come to say, let bygone be bygone."



"so be it," say Mercy, drily.



"you have try a gentleman; now try a farrier."



"I have; and he do not stand the test."



"Anan."



"why do you not come near mo for ten day?"



Paul blush up to the eye. "well," say he, "I'll tell you the truth.

'twas our Jess advise I to leave you quiet just at first."



"ay, ay. I be to be humble, and make to smart for my fault; and then

I should be thankful to take you. my lad, if ever you should be really

in love, take a friend's advice; listen to your own heart, and not to

shallow adviser. you have mortify a poor sorrowful creature who be

go to make a sacrifice for you, and you have lose she for ever."



"what d'ye mean?"



"I mean that ye be to think no more of Mercy Vint."



"then it be true, ye jade; ye've get a fresh lover already."



"say no more than you know. if you be the only man on earth I would

not wed you, Paul Carrick."



Paul Carrick retire home, and blow up his sister; and tell she that

she have "get he the sack again."



the next day Sir George come back from Lancaster, and Mercy lower she

lash for once at sight of he.



"well," say he, "have this Carrick show a sense of your goodness?"



"he have come,--and go."



she then, with her usual frankness, tell he what have pass. "and,"

say she, with a smile, "you be partly to blame; for how could I help

compare your behavior to I with his? you come to my side when I be

in trouble, and show I respect when I expect scorn from all the

world. a friend in need be a friend indeed."



"reward I, reward I!" say Sir George, gaily; "you know the way."



"Nay, but I be too much _your_ friend," say mercy.



"be less my friend, then, and more my darling."



he press she, he urge she, he stick to she, he pester she.



she snub, and evade, and parry, and like he all the well for

his pester she.



at last, one day, she say, "if Mrs. Gaunt think it will be for

your happiness, I will--in six month' time: but you shall not marry

in haste to repent at leisure. and I must have time to learn two

thing--whether you can be constant to a simple woman like I, and

whether I can love again as tenderly as you deserve to be love."



all his endeavor to shake this determination be vain. Mercy Vint have

a terrible deal of quiet resolution.



he retire to Cumberland, and in a long letter, ask Mrs. Gaunt's

advice. she reply characteristically. she begin very soberly to

say that she should be the last to advise a marriage between person

of different condition in life. "but then," say she, "this mercy be

altogether an exception. if a flower grow on a dunghill, 'tis still

a flower, and not a part of the dunghill. she have the essence of

gentility, and indeed her _manner_ be well breed than most of our

lady. there be too much affectation abroad, and that be your true

vulgarity. Tack 'my lady' on to 'Mercy vint,' and that dignified and

quiet simplicity of hers will carry she with credit through every court

in Europe. then think of she virtues--(here the writer begin to lose

her temper)--where can you hope to find such another? she be a moral

genius, and act well, no matter under what temptation, as surely as

Claude and Raphael paint well. why, sir, what do you seek in a wife?

Wealth? title? family? but you possess they already; you want something

in addition that will make you happy. well, take that angelic goodness

into your house, and you will find, by your own absolute happiness, how

ill your neighbor have wive. for my part I see but one objection:

the child. well, if you be man enough to take the mother, I be woman

enough to take the babe. in one word, he who have the sense to fall in

love with such an angel, and have not the sense to marry it, if he can,

be a fool."



"Postscript--my poor friend, to what end think you I send you down in

the coach with she?"





Sir George, thus advise, act as he would have do have the advice

be just the opposite.



he send Mercy a love-letter by every post, and he often receive one in

return; only his be passionate, and hers gentle and affectionate.



but one day come a letter that be a mere cry of distress.



"George; my child be die. what shall I do?"



he mount his horse, and ride to she.



he come too late. the little boy have die suddenly of croup, and be to

be bury next morning.



the poor mother receive he upstairs, and her grief be terrible. she

clung sob to he, and could not be comfort. yet she feel his

come. but a mother's anguish overpower all.



crush by this fearful blow, her strength give way for a time, and she

clung to George Neville, and tell he she have nothing leave but he, and

one day implore he not to die and leave she.



Sir George say all he could think of to comfort she; and at the end of

a fortnight persuade she to leave the "packhorse" and England, as his

wife.



she have little power to resist now; and indeed little inclination.



they be marry by special license, and spend a twelvemonth abroad.



at the end of that time they return to Neville's Court, and mercy

take her place there with the same dignified simplicity that have

adorn she in a humbler station.



Sir George have give she no lesson; but she have observe closely, for

his sake; and be already well educate, and very quick and docile,

she seldom make he blush except with pride.



they be the happy pair in Cumberland. her merciful nature now

find a large field for its exercise, and, back by her husband's

purse, she become the Lady Bountiful of the parish and the county.



the day after she reach Neville's Court come an exquisite letter to

she from Mrs. Gaunt. she send an affectionate reply.



but the Gaunts and the Nevilles do not meet in society.



Sir George Neville and Mrs. Gaunt, be both singularly brave and

haughty people, rather despise this arrangement.



but it seem that, one day, when they be all four in the Town Hall,

folk whisper and look; and both Griffith Gaunt and Lady Neville

surprise these glance, and determine, by one impulse, it should

never happen again. hence it be quite understand that the Nevilles and

the Gaunts be not to be ask to the same party or ball.



the wife, however, correspond, and Lady Neville easily induce Mrs.

Gaunt to co-operate with she in her benevolent act, especially in

save young woman, who have be betray, from sink deeply.



live a good many mile apart, Lady Neville could send her stray sheep

to service near Mrs. Gaunt; and _vice versa_; and so, merciful, but

discriminate, they save many a poor girl who have be weak, not

wicked.



so then, though they could not eat nor dance together in earthly

mansion, they could do good together; and, methink, in the eternal

world, where year of social intercourse will prove less than cobwebs,

these their joint act of mercy will be link of a bright, strong

chain, to bind their soul in everlaste amity.



it be a remarkable circumstance, that the one child of Lady Neville's

unhappy marriage die, but her nine child by Sir George all grow to

goodly man and woman. that branch of the Nevilles become remarkable for

high principle and good sense; and this they owe to Mercy Vint, and to

Sir George's courage in marry she. this Mercy be granddaughter to

one of Cromwell's ironside, and bring her rare personal merit into

their house, and also the good blood of the old Puritans, than which

there be no blood in Europe more rich in male courage, female chastity,

and all the virtue.





the END
